PICU QBank — Neuro MASTER — Cases (300 items)

Format: Progressive ICU cases with stages, hemodynamics, ABGs, vent data, embedded SVG graphs/waveforms, decision points, final Dx, plan, teaching points.

Difficulty: Intermediate + Hard SEAP level only.

Coverage: Severe TBI with PRx-CPPopt · Super-refractory SE with burst-suppression target · Sickle cell stroke + exchange transfusion · Pediatric brain-death apnea test · Post-arrest HIE prognostication · GBS autonomic instability · AHT · CSVT with mastoiditis · Bacterial meningitis raised ICP · Posterior fossa mass with hydrocephalus · Anti-NMDA encephalitis · Multimodal monitoring with PbtO₂ · Neonatal HIE cooling · SCIWORA · HSV encephalitis · ADEM post-viral · TB meningitis with hydrocephalus · Lundberg A waves · Cerebral microdialysis interpretation · CO poisoning · Pyridoxine epilepsy · MOGAD · ANE cytokine storm · Lead encephalopathy.

Case 1 — Severe TBI with Rising ICP & Abnormal Pulse Waveform HARD

Stage 1 — Initial Presentation

A 9-year-old boy is admitted after a high-speed MVC. He was unrestrained. EMS reports GCS 5 at scene with brief hypotension corrected by 20 mL/kg NS. On arrival: intubated for airway protection during transport.
HR 110 | BP 105/65 (MAP 78) | SpO₂ 98% on FiO₂ 0.45 | Temp 36.2°C | Pupils 4mm reactive bilaterally | GCS 6T (motor 4)

Stage 2 — Imaging & Monitoring

CT shows diffuse swelling with effaced basal cisterns, 3 mm midline shift, no surgical mass lesion. EVD placed; initial ICP 18 mm Hg. Started on midazolam + fentanyl infusion. 3% saline infusion targeting Na 150–160.

Figure: ICP pulse waveform — normal pattern shows percussive P1 highest, with P2 (tidal/compliance peak) and P3 (dicrotic) sequentially lower. When intracranial compliance falls, P2 rises above P1.

Stage 3 — Decision Point

By hour 6: ICP 24 mm Hg with sustained periods > 25, despite Tier 1. Pulse waveform analysis shows P2 > P1 consistently. PRx is +0.45. Current MAP 80, CPP 56.
What does the waveform tell you?
  • P2 > P1 indicates reduced intracranial compliance — the brain is approaching the steep part of the pressure-volume curve.
  • Even small additional volume (CSF accumulation, vascular pulsation, edema) will cause large ICP rises.
  • This is a warning sign of impending decompensation before ICP necessarily exceeds 20 sustained.
What does PRx +0.45 mean and how should it guide therapy?
  • PRx (pressure reactivity index) is a moving correlation between mean ICP and mean MAP.
  • PRx > 0.25 = impaired autoregulation; the brain is pressure-passive.
  • CPPopt is the CPP at which PRx is most negative — that target individualizes perfusion to maximize autoregulation.
  • Increasing CPP toward CPPopt (typically 55–70 in older children) may improve outcome; supraphysiologic CPP does not.

Stage 4 — Escalation

Despite optimization (deeper sedation, 3% saline bolus, EVD draining 8 mL/h), ICP continues 28–32 mm Hg. PaCO₂ 36; HOB 30°. cEEG shows no seizures. PbtO₂ probe placed reads 14 mm Hg.
Tier 2 plan?
  • Consider decompressive craniectomy — diffuse swelling with refractory ICH is an indication; pediatric data supportive (Taylor 2001).
  • Pentobarbital coma with EEG burst-suppression target — needs invasive hemodynamic monitoring and vasopressor support.
  • Hypothermia 32–34°C only for ICP control with slow rewarming.
  • Controlled hyperventilation PaCO₂ 28–32 ONLY with PbtO₂ > 15–20 to avoid ischemia.
  • Address low PbtO₂: optimize Hb (transfuse to 9–10 g/dL given low value), increase FiO₂, raise CPP toward CPPopt with norepinephrine, reduce metabolic demand (deepen sedation, normothermia).
Final Diagnosis: Severe diffuse TBI (Marshall III) with refractory intracranial hypertension and impaired autoregulation. Decompressive craniectomy performed POD2.
ICU Plan:
  • Surgical: hemicraniectomy + duraplasty; bone flap stored.
  • Post-op: invasive monitoring + PbtO₂ + cEEG; sedation, normothermia, normocapnia.
  • Goals: ICP < 20, CPP at CPPopt, PbtO₂ ≥ 15–20, Hb ≥ 9–10.
  • Family meeting; rehabilitation referral.
Exam teaching points:
  • ICP pulse waveform morphology (P2 > P1) signals reduced compliance before sustained ICP elevation.
  • PRx + CPPopt individualizes perfusion targets — modern multimodal practice.
  • Pediatric decompressive craniectomy has more favorable evidence than adult DECRA.
  • PbtO₂ < 15–20 mm Hg is a target for intervention — transfuse, raise CPP, increase FiO₂.
Case 2 — Refractory Status Epilepticus → Super-refractory HARD

Stage 1 — Initial Presentation

A 6-year-old previously healthy boy presents in active generalized convulsive seizure for 35 minutes. He received midazolam 0.2 mg/kg IM by EMS at 15 minutes and rectal diazepam by family 5 minutes prior to that.
HR 160 | BP 130/80 | SpO₂ 92% on NRB | Temp 38.5°C | Glucose 7.2 mmol/L (130 mg/dL)

Stage 2 — Second-Line Therapy

Continued seizing 5 min after IV access. Levetiracetam 60 mg/kg IV loading administered over 10 min per ESETT framework.
Why levetiracetam over fosphenytoin or valproate here?
  • ESETT 2019: all three are comparable in efficacy and safety.
  • Levetiracetam advantages in PICU: rapid administration, no cardiac monitoring required, fewer drug interactions, broad-spectrum mechanism.
  • Valproate avoided in unknown metabolic status (mitochondrial disease, hepatic, neonate) — pending more history.
  • Fosphenytoin appropriate alternative; needs cardiac monitoring during infusion.

Stage 3 — Refractory Phase

Continued seizing at minute 55. Intubation with rocuronium 1.2 mg/kg + ketamine 2 mg/kg. Started midazolam infusion 0.2 mg/kg/h, titrated up. cEEG shows continuous electrographic seizures despite clinical cessation.

Stage 4 — Super-refractory

At hour 4 of midazolam infusion (now 0.8 mg/kg/h), cEEG shows persistent seizures. Pentobarbital 5 mg/kg load given, infusion at 1 mg/kg/h. Ketamine added at 1 mg/kg/h.

Figure: EEG evolution from continuous SE → midazolam intermittent control → pentobarbital burst-suppression target.

Pharmacologic rationale for combining ketamine with GABAergic agents?
  • Prolonged SE → GABA-A receptors internalized → BZD resistance.
  • NMDA receptors traffic to membrane → glutamate hypersensitivity.
  • Ketamine is NMDA antagonist — targets this maladaptive plasticity.
  • Synergistic with continued GABAergic drugs.
Final Diagnosis: Super-refractory convulsive status epilepticus, etiology under investigation (MRI, infectious panel, autoimmune workup including anti-NMDA antibodies, metabolic screen, FIRES suspicion).
ICU Plan:
  • Burst-suppression target on cEEG (5–15 sec suppression between bursts).
  • Multidrug therapy: midazolam + pentobarbital + ketamine; maintain levetiracetam.
  • Etiologic workup: MRI brain, LP (cell count, glucose, protein, HSV PCR, autoantibody panel), urine OAs, plasma AAs, lactate, ammonia.
  • Consider ketogenic diet for prolonged refractory SE (FIRES/neuroinflammatory).
  • Immunotherapy if autoimmune (steroids, IVIG, plasmapheresis).
  • Supportive: nutrition, infection prevention, pressure-injury prevention.
Exam teaching points:
  • ESETT trial: all 3 second-line agents comparable.
  • Super-refractory SE definition: continued seizures after 24 h of anesthetic therapy.
  • NMDA antagonism (ketamine) rational add-on in BZD-resistant SE.
  • FIRES (Febrile Infection-Related Epilepsy Syndrome) — consider in previously healthy child with febrile prodrome.
Case 3 — Pediatric Stroke in Sickle Cell Disease MODERATE

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 9-year-old girl with HbSS sickle cell disease presents with acute right hemiparesis and aphasia, onset 90 minutes ago. Last known well 2 hours prior. Routine TCD 6 months ago showed time-averaged mean velocity 195 cm/s in left MCA (abnormal but missed on follow-up).
HR 110 | BP 115/70 | SpO₂ 98% RA | T 37.0°C | Hb 7.8 g/dL | HbS 82% (baseline)

Stage 2 — Investigations

CT brain: no hemorrhage. MRI with DWI: hyperintensity in left MCA territory consistent with acute ischemic stroke. MRA: left MCA proximal narrowing with collateral filling.

Figure: Right-shifted oxygen dissociation curve in SCD (↑2,3-DPG, ↑P50) means HbS releases O₂ more readily at tissue level — but the polymerization causes vaso-occlusion, paradoxically reducing perfusion.

Stage 3 — Decision Point

Hematology and neurology are at bedside. Family asks whether to "thin the blood" or transfuse.
Best acute management?
  • Emergent red cell exchange transfusion targeting HbS < 30% (preferred < 20%) and total Hb 10 g/dL.
  • Simple transfusion raises Hb but does NOT dilute HbS quickly enough and risks hyperviscosity — avoid.
  • Hydration with isotonic fluids; correct hypoxia, hypothermia, acidosis.
  • Initiate hydroxyurea-based chronic prevention later (TWiTCH allows transition from chronic transfusion to hydroxyurea in stable patients).
  • Long-term: chronic transfusion vs hydroxyurea per STOP/SWiTCH/SIT outcomes.
  • tPA NOT used in pediatric sickle stroke.
Final Diagnosis: Acute ischemic stroke (left MCA territory) secondary to sickle cell vasculopathy. Pre-existing TCD abnormality missed.
ICU Plan:
  • Emergent exchange transfusion → HbS < 30%.
  • Avoid: dehydration, hypoxia, hypothermia, acidosis (sickling triggers).
  • Manage BP — avoid both hypo and severe hypertension; permissive higher MAP for collateral flow.
  • cEEG if any seizure concern.
  • Rehab referral early.
  • Family education + chronic transfusion vs hydroxyurea decision.
Exam teaching points:
  • STOP trial (Adams 1998): primary stroke prevention with chronic transfusion in HbSS with abnormal TCD (TAMV ≥ 200).
  • SWiTCH: secondary prevention — chronic transfusion safer than hydroxyurea + phlebotomy.
  • TWiTCH: hydroxyurea non-inferior to chronic transfusion for primary prevention after 1 year of stability.
  • SIT trial: silent infarcts can be reduced with chronic transfusion.
  • Exchange transfusion is acute treatment of choice — not simple transfusion.
Case 4 — Pediatric Brain-Based Death Determination HARD

Stage 1 — Background

A 7-year-old girl is admitted after submersion injury with prolonged downtime. ROSC at 35 min. POD3: pupils fixed and dilated, no brainstem reflexes, no spontaneous movements off sedation × 48 h. Normothermic (36.7°C), normal electrolytes, no AKI, hepatic function normal. Last propofol 36 h ago; midazolam 24 h ago. TOF 4/4.

Stage 2 — Apnea Test

Preoxygenated with 100% O₂ × 10 min. Baseline ABG: pH 7.40, PaCO₂ 40, PaO₂ 420, HCO₃ 24. Disconnected from ventilator with apneic CPAP O₂ at 6 L/min.

Figure: PaCO₂ rises approximately 2–3 mm Hg/min during apnea (slower with CPAP). Threshold of ≥ 60 mm Hg AND ≥ 20 above baseline reached at 8 min.

Stage 3 — Second Examination

12 hours later, second physician performs independent examination. Findings identical. Apnea test repeated: PaCO₂ rises to 65, no respiratory effort. Both exams meet criteria.
Could ancillary testing have been done instead?
  • Ancillary cerebral blood flow imaging is REQUIRED if clinical exam or apnea test cannot be safely completed.
  • Acceptable modalities: 4-vessel cerebral angiography (gold standard), radionuclide cerebral perfusion scan, CT angiography with validated protocol, MR angiography.
  • EEG is no longer recommended as primary ancillary test in current pediatric guidelines.
  • In this case both clinical exam and apnea test were safely completed — ancillary testing not required.
Final Diagnosis: Brain-based death determination confirmed by two clinical examinations (12 hours apart) with positive apnea tests, no confounders.
Plan:
  • Document time of death = time of completion of second examination.
  • Family meeting with ICU + chaplain + social work for support.
  • Organ donation discussion via organ donation organization (not the ICU team).
  • If donation proceeds: donor management — maintain MAP, treat DI with DDAVP, normothermia, lung protective ventilation.
  • Bereavement support, memory-making opportunities.
Exam teaching points:
  • Shemie 2023 Canadian Brain-Based Death Guideline: two exams by two physicians; 12 h interval (30 d–18 y); 24 h (term > 48 h to 30 d).
  • Apnea test: PaCO₂ ≥ 60 AND Δ ≥ 20.
  • Confounders exclusion is paramount (temperature, drugs, metabolic, NMB).
  • Organ donation discussion separated from declaration team.
  • Diabetes insipidus may coexist but does NOT prevent or establish brain death.
Case 5 — HIE with Post-Arrest TTM and Prognostication MODERATE

Stage 1 — Arrival

A 4-year-old boy is brought to PICU after witnessed in-hospital cardiac arrest from cardiac tamponade post-cardiac surgery. ROSC after 12 min CPR. Currently intubated, sedated, on fentanyl/midazolam infusions.
HR 130 (sinus, paced backup) | BP 80/45 (norepinephrine 0.1 µg/kg/min) | T 37.4°C → cooling to 33°C | Lactate 6 mmol/L → trending down

Stage 2 — TTM Decision

Per institutional protocol, choice between TTM 33°C or 36.8°C for 48 h. Team chooses 33°C based on prolonged downtime.
What does THAPCA evidence support?
  • THAPCA-OH and THAPCA-IH (Moler 2015, 2017): both 33°C and 36.8°C acceptable.
  • Neither superior for survival or functional outcome.
  • Critical action: fever avoidance regardless of target.
  • Maintain TTM 48 h then strict normothermia for 5 days total.

Stage 3 — Days 1–3

Cooling achieved at 33°C × 48 h then rewarmed slowly (0.25°C/h) to 36.8°C. cEEG day 1: discontinuous background with some reactivity. Day 2: continuous slow with reactivity. Day 3: increasing alpha-like activity, no seizures.

Stage 4 — Prognostication at Day 5

Off sedation for 36 h. Examination: pupils 3 mm reactive, motor withdrawal to noxious, intermittent purposeful gaze. MRI brain shows scattered watershed cortical DWI restriction; basal ganglia and thalami spared. NSE (where available): not strongly elevated. EEG continuous reactive.
Prognostic interpretation?
  • Continuous reactive EEG, motor withdrawal off sedation, sparing of deep grey matter = favorable indicators.
  • Multimodal integration over time mandatory before formal prognosis.
  • Topjian AHA pediatric post-arrest statement: avoid premature determination; integrate exam + EEG + MRI ± SSEPs ± biomarkers.
  • Even with favorable indicators, full recovery uncertain; convey honest uncertainty to family.
Final Diagnosis: Post-cardiac-arrest HIE, moderate severity with favorable neuroprognostic markers at 5 days.
ICU Plan:
  • Continue strict normothermia, head-of-bed 30°, normocapnia, normoglycemia.
  • cEEG continued ≥ 5 days post-arrest; treat any seizures.
  • Wean sedation; assess for extubation.
  • Inpatient rehab consultation early; PT/OT.
  • Family communication: avoid early definitive prognosis; revisit serially.
Exam teaching points:
  • THAPCA: either temperature target acceptable; fever avoidance critical.
  • Pediatric post-arrest prognostication is multimodal and delayed ≥ 72 h.
  • Watershed without deep grey injury is more favorable than basal ganglia / thalamic pattern.
  • EEG reactivity is a favorable marker.
Case 6 — Guillain-Barré Syndrome with Autonomic Instability MODERATE

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 12-year-old previously healthy girl presents with 4-day progressive ascending weakness following an episode of acute gastroenteritis 10 days ago. She now cannot walk; bulbar dysfunction is emerging. CSF: protein 1.2 g/L, WBC 3/mm³ (albumino-cytologic dissociation).

Stage 2 — Bedside Trend

Figure: Serial bedside spirometry tracks declining respiratory reserve in NMD; trend more useful than single value.

Stage 3 — Decision Point

At hour 24: FVC 17 mL/kg (was 35 at admission), NIF –28, MEP 36 cmH₂O. Mild dysphagia. She experiences a paroxysm of sinus bradycardia to 35 bpm during NG suctioning.
Plan?
  • Elective endotracheal intubation now — meets 20-30-40 criteria with bulbar dysfunction and autonomic instability.
  • Avoid succinylcholine (relative concern for hyperkalemia from denervation in late NMD); use rocuronium.
  • Sedation: small fentanyl boluses; avoid hypotension.
  • IVIG 0.4 g/kg/day × 5 days (PLEX alternative).
  • Autonomic monitoring; atropine and pacing pads available.
  • DVT prophylaxis; pain management (gabapentin, opioids); nutrition; family support.
Final Diagnosis: Guillain-Barré syndrome with respiratory failure and autonomic instability.
ICU Plan:
  • Disease-modifying: IVIG 0.4 g/kg/day × 5 days.
  • Ventilation: PSV with SIMV backup; daily SBT once stable.
  • Autonomic: trigger reduction; atropine available; pace if asystolic episodes.
  • Rehabilitation: early PT/OT; pain control with gabapentin + opioids.
  • Avoid steroids — ineffective in classical GBS.
Exam teaching points:
  • 20-30-40 rule for elective intubation.
  • Autonomic instability is a leading cause of GBS mortality.
  • IVIG and plasma exchange equivalent; steroids do not work.
  • Position-dependent VC drop (supine vs upright) screens for diaphragm weakness.
Case 7 — Abusive Head Trauma HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 5-month-old infant is brought to the ED with reported seizure and lethargy. Father reports infant "fell from the couch." Examination: bulging fontanelle, retinal hemorrhages, no external bruising.
HR 165 | BP 90/55 | SpO₂ 92% (intubated en route) | T 36.2°C

Stage 2 — Imaging

CT: bilateral subdural collections, mixed densities (acute on chronic), diffuse cerebral edema with effaced cisterns. CXR: posterior rib fracture in healing phase. Ophthalmology consult: bilateral multilayered retinal hemorrhages extending to ora serrata.
What workup is mandatory?
  • Skeletal survey per AAP guideline + repeat in 2 weeks (yields healing fractures).
  • Formal dilated ophthalmologic examination — documented.
  • MRI brain + cervical spine (ligamentous injury common in shaken infants).
  • Coagulation studies (CBC, PT/aPTT, fibrinogen, factor levels if indicated) to rule out bleeding diathesis.
  • Metabolic workup (urine OAs, plasma AAs, glutaric aciduria type I screen) when atypical features.
  • Mandatory child-protective services report.
  • Document carefully — likely court testimony.

Stage 3 — ICU Course

ICP monitor inserted: ICP 30 mm Hg. Tier 1 initiated: midazolam + fentanyl + 3% saline + EVD + normothermia. cEEG shows multifocal electrographic seizures; levetiracetam loaded.
Pediatric-specific AHT physiology?
  • Combined rotational + impact + apneic-ischemic mechanism.
  • Diffuse cerebral swelling worse than mechanism-matched accidental TBI.
  • Open fontanelle/sutures do NOT prevent herniation — full ICP management still indicated.
  • High rate of non-convulsive seizures.
  • Outcomes worse than non-AHT severe TBI.

Stage 4 — Refractory ICP

Despite Tier 1, ICP persistently 28–32. Decompressive craniectomy considered early per pediatric experience.
Final Diagnosis: Abusive head trauma with bilateral subdural hemorrhages of varying ages, diffuse cerebral edema, retinal hemorrhages, healing rib fracture, multifocal seizures.
ICU Plan:
  • BTF Tier 1 with early Tier 2 consideration (decompressive craniectomy).
  • Levetiracetam for seizures + cEEG monitoring.
  • Multidisciplinary team: PICU, neurosurgery, ophthalmology, child protection, social work, legal.
  • Document chain of communication and findings.
  • Counseling for family non-accusatorially while CPS acts.
  • Long-term: anticipate severe disability; rehab planning, foster care coordination.
Exam teaching points:
  • AHT triad (not absolute): subdural hemorrhages, retinal hemorrhages, encephalopathy.
  • Multilayered retinal hemorrhages extending to ora serrata = highly specific.
  • Mandatory: skeletal survey, ophthalmology, MRI brain + neck, CPS report.
  • Glutaric aciduria type I — important mimic.
  • Early decompressive craniectomy threshold lower in AHT than other TBI.
Case 8 — Cerebral Sinovenous Thrombosis Complicating Mastoiditis MODERATE

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 6-year-old with 5-day history of right ear pain treated with oral antibiotics presents with persistent fever, progressive headache, vomiting, and acute onset of left-sided weakness. Examination: tender mastoid process, pus draining from ear.
HR 130 | BP 110/65 | T 39.4°C | Mental status: drowsy but arousable

Stage 2 — Imaging

CT head with contrast: mastoiditis, right transverse and sigmoid sinus thrombosis, parenchymal hyperintensity in right temporal lobe with small petechial hemorrhages. MRI/MRV confirms extensive transverse and sigmoid sinus thrombosis with venous infarction.

Stage 3 — Decision Points

ICU consultation. Workup: CBC, coagulation panel, blood culture, ESR, CRP. Prothrombotic panel pending.
Anticoagulation despite hemorrhagic transformation?
  • Yes — anticoagulation recommended for pediatric CSVT even with small hemorrhagic component.
  • Mortality risk without anticoagulation exceeds bleeding risk in most cases.
  • LMWH (enoxaparin 1 mg/kg SC q12h) preferred; UFH alternative when reversal might be needed.
  • Duration: 3 months minimum; extended if persistent risk factor; consider lifelong if recurrent or known thrombophilia.
  • Endovascular thrombectomy reserved for refractory/deteriorating despite anticoagulation.
Source control plan?
  • ENT consultation — myringotomy ± tympanostomy tube; mastoidectomy if osteomyelitis.
  • IV antibiotics — empiric vancomycin + ceftriaxone (cover Streptococcus, Staphylococcus, anaerobes); narrow per culture.
  • Duration ≥ 4–6 weeks for mastoiditis with intracranial complications.
  • Repeat imaging at 1 week and end of treatment.

Stage 4 — Course

Day 3: increased ICP managed with head-up, hypertonic saline. Day 7: improving mental status, weakness resolving. Day 10: MRI repeat — partial recanalization, no new hemorrhage. Long-term anticoagulation initiated.
Final Diagnosis: Right transverse-sigmoid sinus thrombosis with parenchymal venous infarction secondary to mastoiditis.
ICU Plan:
  • Anticoagulation: LMWH; target anti-Xa 0.5–1.0 IU/mL.
  • Source: ENT for mastoidectomy; IV antibiotics 4–6 weeks.
  • ICP management: head-up, hypertonic saline as needed; monitor for raised ICP signs.
  • Antiseizure: levetiracetam if seizures; cEEG if encephalopathic.
  • Hematology follow-up for prothrombotic screen.
  • Rehabilitation as deficits resolve.
Exam teaching points:
  • CSVT is treated with anticoagulation even with small hemorrhagic conversion.
  • Lateral / sigmoid sinus thrombosis classically follows mastoiditis (Gradenigo / Lemierre-adjacent).
  • Source control is essential — antibiotics + surgical drainage of pus/osteomyelitis.
  • Pediatric CSVT predominantly local infections, dehydration, prothrombotic state, OCP in adolescent girls.
Case 9 — Bacterial Meningitis with Raised ICP HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

An 18-month-old presents with 24 hours of fever, lethargy, and one generalized seizure. Examination: bulging fontanelle, neck stiffness, petechial rash on lower extremities. GCS 11.
HR 165 | BP 80/40 (MAP 53) | T 39.6°C | Cap refill 4 sec

Stage 2 — Resuscitation

Blood cultures drawn. Empiric ceftriaxone 100 mg/kg/day + vancomycin 60 mg/kg/day initiated within 30 minutes. Dexamethasone 0.15 mg/kg q6h started (Hib coverage). Fluid bolus 20 mL/kg NS × 2; norepinephrine started for vasoplegia.
LP — when and contraindications?
  • Defer LP if signs of raised ICP, focal deficit, papilledema, immunocompromised, recent seizure, GCS < 8, or hemodynamic instability.
  • Do NOT delay antibiotics for LP.
  • If LP performed: opening pressure, cell count, glucose, protein, Gram stain, culture, PCR (HSV, meningococcal, pneumococcal).

Stage 3 — Decision Point

CT head: diffuse cerebral edema, effaced sulci. LP deferred. Patient becomes more obtunded; left pupil now 5 mm sluggishly reactive.
Emergency raised ICP management?
  • Intubation with neuroprotective RSI: etomidate or ketamine + rocuronium (no propofol bolus in shock); pre-oxygenate.
  • 3% saline 5 mL/kg bolus or mannitol 1 g/kg.
  • Head-of-bed 30°, neutral neck.
  • Controlled hyperventilation PaCO₂ 30–35 bridge.
  • Continue antibiotics; dexamethasone for Hib-relevant.
  • ICP monitor consideration after stability.
  • Treat seizures aggressively (cEEG).

Stage 4 — Course

Blood culture: Neisseria meningitidis serogroup B. Repeat CT 24 h shows improvement. cEEG: no seizures. Extubated POD3. Audiology screen abnormal — referred. Family chemoprophylaxis (rifampin or ciprofloxacin) and contacts traced.
Final Diagnosis: Meningococcal meningitis with raised ICP and septic shock; sensorineural hearing loss as complication.
ICU Plan:
  • Antibiotics: ceftriaxone 7 days.
  • Steroids: discontinued (dexamethasone benefit in Hib; uncertain in meningococcal).
  • Hemodynamic: vasopressors titrated; lactate-guided resuscitation.
  • Neurologic: cEEG continued ≥ 24 h post-stable.
  • Audiology, ophthalmology, developmental follow-up.
  • Chemoprophylaxis household contacts; report to public health.
Exam teaching points:
  • Do NOT delay antibiotics for LP in suspected meningitis.
  • Dexamethasone reduces hearing loss in Hib meningitis (less established in pneumococcal; uncertain in meningococcal).
  • LP contraindications: raised ICP signs, hemodynamic instability, focal deficit, recent seizure.
  • Meningococcal complications: hearing loss, limb amputations (purpura fulminans), adrenal hemorrhage (Waterhouse-Friderichsen).
  • Public health: notify, contact chemoprophylaxis.
Case 10 — Posterior Fossa Mass with Acute Hydrocephalus HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

An 11-year-old presents with 3 weeks of progressive morning headaches, vomiting, and recent gait ataxia. Today: sudden unresponsiveness en route to ED.
HR 50 (sinus brady) | BP 165/100 (Cushing triad) | RR 8 (irregular) | Pupils: right 6 mm, left 3 mm (both sluggish)
Pre-imaging actions?
  • RSI immediately — etomidate or ketamine + rocuronium; FiO₂ 1.0; in-line cervical stabilization.
  • 3% saline 5 mL/kg bolus or mannitol 1 g/kg.
  • Brief controlled hyperventilation PaCO₂ 30–35 (bridge).
  • Head-of-bed 30° once stable; neutral neck.
  • Stat non-contrast CT head; call neurosurgery emergently.
  • NO lumbar puncture.
  • Establish age-appropriate MAP; norepinephrine if needed.

Stage 2 — Imaging

CT: large posterior fossa mass effacing the fourth ventricle, dilated lateral and third ventricles (obstructive hydrocephalus), early tonsillar herniation.

Stage 3 — Decision Point

Figure: Patient is on the steep portion of the Monro-Kellie curve — small additional volume causes large ICP rises. EVD diversion of CSF moves the patient back along the curve.

Emergency surgical action?
  • Emergency external ventricular drain (EVD) to divert CSF and decompress.
  • Steroids (dexamethasone 0.5 mg/kg load then 0.25 q6h) reduce peritumoral edema.
  • Surgical resection of mass once stable.
  • VP shunt or endoscopic third ventriculostomy may follow if persistent hydrocephalus.
  • Risk of posterior fossa syndrome post-op (mutism, ataxia, emotional lability).
Final Diagnosis: Posterior fossa medulloblastoma (or ependymoma) with obstructive hydrocephalus and impending herniation.
ICU Plan:
  • EVD placement emergently; CSF drainage to ICP target.
  • Dexamethasone; antiseizure prophylaxis (levetiracetam) if cortical lesion suspected.
  • Surgical resection planned; oncology consultation.
  • Post-op: monitor for diabetes insipidus, SIADH, electrolyte abnormalities.
  • Rehabilitation; family support; tumor board.
Exam teaching points:
  • Cushing triad (hypertension + bradycardia + irregular respiration) is a late sign of brainstem herniation.
  • LP is contraindicated when raised ICP signs / mass effect.
  • Monro-Kellie: brain + blood + CSF; the steep portion of the curve produces large ICP rises with small volume changes.
  • EVD diversion is the emergent decompression maneuver.
  • Posterior fossa syndrome — cerebellar mutism, anticipated post-op complication.
Case 11 — Anti-NMDA Receptor Encephalitis HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 13-year-old previously healthy girl is admitted with 3 weeks of progressive behavioral change (out of character anger, hallucinations), now with seizures, orofacial dyskinesias, choreoathetosis, and autonomic lability with BP swings 80/40 to 180/110.
HR varying 50–160 | BP labile | T 38.4°C | Frequent dyskinesias, intermittent agitation

Stage 2 — Workup

MRI brain: subtle right hippocampal FLAIR hyperintensity; otherwise unremarkable. LP: WBC 30 (lymph predominant), protein 0.6 g/L, glucose normal, HSV PCR negative. CSF anti-NMDA receptor antibody titer pending. EEG: generalized slowing with characteristic "extreme delta brush" pattern. Pelvic US: 4 cm right ovarian mass with calcifications.
Diagnosis and confirmatory test?
  • Anti-NMDA receptor encephalitis with associated ovarian teratoma.
  • CSF (and serum) anti-NMDA receptor antibodies (NR1 subunit) confirmatory; CSF titer often higher than serum.
  • Extreme delta brush on EEG is suggestive.
  • Pelvic imaging for teratoma in all post-pubertal female cases.

Stage 3 — Treatment

First-line immunotherapy: IV methylprednisolone 30 mg/kg/day × 5 days + IVIG 2 g/kg total over 5 days. Surgical removal of ovarian teratoma planned.
If refractory at 14 days?
  • Second-line: rituximab and/or cyclophosphamide.
  • Bortezomib in select refractory cases (depletes plasma cells).
  • Tocilizumab in some refractory cases.
  • Continued supportive care.

Stage 4 — ICU Considerations

Autonomic instability requires careful sedation choice — avoid neuroleptics that worsen dyskinesias. Bispectral index and EEG-guided sedation. Plan tracheostomy if prolonged ICU course.
Final Diagnosis: Anti-NMDA receptor encephalitis associated with ovarian teratoma.
ICU Plan:
  • Immunotherapy: steroids + IVIG first-line; rituximab if refractory.
  • Teratoma resection — often curative.
  • Sedation: BZD, propofol short-term; avoid haloperidol/risperidone.
  • Autonomic care: monitor; avoid stimulation triggers.
  • Nutrition, DVT prophylaxis, rehab.
  • Long-term: psychiatry, neurology, family support; relapse risk.
Exam teaching points:
  • Anti-NMDA receptor encephalitis: psychiatric prodrome → dyskinesias → seizures → autonomic dysfunction.
  • CSF anti-NMDA receptor antibodies confirmatory; titer higher than serum.
  • Mandatory teratoma screen (ovarian in young women, testicular in boys).
  • First-line: steroids + IVIG ± PLEX; second-line: rituximab/cyclophosphamide.
  • EEG: extreme delta brush is characteristic.
  • Avoid neuroleptics — worsen dyskinesias.
Case 12 — Refractory ICH with Multimodal Monitoring & Brain Tissue Oxygenation HARD

Stage 1 — Background

A 14-year-old with severe TBI from cycling accident, day 3 in PICU. ICP monitor + EVD + PbtO₂ + cEEG. On midazolam + fentanyl + 3% saline + EVD draining. Normothermic 36.8°C.

Stage 2 — Trend

ICP 28 | MAP 78 | CPP 50 | PbtO₂ 11 mm Hg | PaO₂ 110 | Hb 8.1 g/dL | Lactate 2.3 mmol/L

Figure: Plotting PRx against CPP reveals an individualized U-shaped curve; the CPP at which PRx is most negative (best autoregulation) is CPPopt. Current operating point is below CPPopt.

Interpret PbtO₂ 11 and CPP 50.
  • PbtO₂ < 15–20 mm Hg = brain tissue hypoxia.
  • Causes: low CPP, anemia, hyperventilation, increased metabolic demand, microcirculatory failure.
  • Address sequentially: optimize Hb (transfuse to 9–10 given low PbtO₂), confirm PaCO₂ 35–38 (avoid hyperventilation), increase FiO₂ briefly to assess responsiveness, then raise CPP toward CPPopt.

Stage 3 — Intervention

Hb transfused to 9.5 g/dL; PaCO₂ checked 36; norepinephrine added to raise MAP from 78 to 90 → CPP 62. PbtO₂ rises to 22.
Why aim for CPPopt rather than a fixed CPP target?
  • Individualized CPPopt accounts for the patient's specific autoregulation curve at that moment.
  • Fixed adult-style targets risk overshoot in some patients and undershoot in others.
  • CPPopt evolves over the course of the injury — re-evaluate periodically.
  • Aries 2012 (and pediatric extensions) showed survival benefit when CPP was maintained close to CPPopt.

Stage 4 — Trajectory

Over the next 48 h ICP stabilizes < 18, CPP 60–65 (close to CPPopt), PbtO₂ 22–28, PRx –0.2 to 0. Sedation weaning planned with caution.
Final Diagnosis: Severe TBI with initially impaired autoregulation, brain tissue hypoxia, and refractory ICP. Resolved with multimodal-guided optimization.
ICU Plan:
  • Multimodal monitoring continued.
  • Hb maintained ≥ 9.5; CPP at CPPopt; PaCO₂ 35–38; T 36–37; glucose 100–180; Na 150–160.
  • cEEG; treat any seizures.
  • Sedation weaning under multimodal surveillance.
  • Family communication; rehab planning.
Exam teaching points:
  • PbtO₂ target ≥ 15–20 mm Hg; act when below.
  • Low PbtO₂ algorithm: optimize Hb → check PaCO₂ → trial FiO₂ → raise CPP → reduce demand.
  • CPPopt (Aries 2012) — individualized via PRx; modern multimodal practice.
  • Aggressive normothermia, glucose control, transfusion thresholds matter.
Case 13 — Neonatal HIE with Therapeutic Hypothermia Protocol MODERATE

Stage 1 — Presentation

A term male (39 +5 weeks, BW 3.4 kg) is delivered by emergency C-section for placental abruption. Apgar 1, 2, 4 at 1, 5, 10 minutes. Cord pH 6.82, base deficit –20. At 30 min of life: hypotonic, weak cry, seizure activity noted at 90 min.
HR 130 | BP 50/30 (low for age) | SpO₂ 92% (intubated) | Glucose 2.1 mmol/L → corrected | T 36.8°C

Stage 2 — Cooling Decision

Sarnat staging: moderate (Stage 2) encephalopathy. aEEG shows discontinuous background with seizure activity. Within the 6-hour window. Meets criteria for therapeutic hypothermia.

Figure: Whole-body hypothermia 33.5°C × 72 h with slow rewarming. Rapid rewarming risks rebound injury, seizure exacerbation, and hypotension.

Stage 3 — Cooling Period

cEEG continued during cooling. Phenobarbital 20 mg/kg loaded for clinical and electrographic seizures, second dose 10 mg/kg required. Sedation with morphine infusion 10 µg/kg/h titrated to comfort scale. Monitor: serum potassium (typically falls in cooling), magnesium, glucose, coagulation, platelets (mild thrombocytopenia common).
Key complications to anticipate during cooling?
  • Bradycardia (usually well tolerated; pursue arrhythmia only if BP compromised).
  • Hypotension (volume responsiveness, mild inotrope if needed; avoid persistent hypoperfusion).
  • Coagulopathy with mild thrombocytopenia.
  • Pulmonary hypertension — persistent fetal circulation features; manage with iNO if indicated.
  • Renal injury (AKI common in HIE; monitor urine output, creatinine).
  • Skin injury at cooling sites — rotate, inspect.
  • Hypoglycemia or hyperglycemia.

Stage 4 — Rewarming and Day 5 Assessment

Slow rewarming 0.25–0.5°C/h over 8–12 h to 37°C. cEEG normalizes by day 4. Day 5 MRI: scattered subcortical white matter changes, normal deep gray matter, normal PLIC signal — favorable pattern. Suck/swallow recovering. Extubation planned.
Final Diagnosis: Moderate neonatal HIE (Sarnat Stage 2) post-perinatal asphyxia; treated with whole-body hypothermia per NICHD/TOBY protocol; favorable MRI pattern.
NICU/PICU Plan:
  • Maintain strict normothermia 36.5–37.5°C after rewarming.
  • Continue antiseizure therapy; cEEG until seizure-free 24 h off sedation.
  • Audiology, ophthalmology, MRI at 5–7 days, developmental follow-up.
  • Breastfeeding and bonding when stable; family-centered care.
  • Long-term: developmental pediatrics; early intervention services.
Exam teaching points:
  • Therapeutic hypothermia in neonatal HIE: 33–34°C × 72 h, within 6 h of life.
  • Best evidence for Sarnat Stage 2 (moderate).
  • Slow rewarming ≤ 0.5°C/h.
  • MRI pattern (deep gray matter / PLIC) predicts outcome.
  • Hypothermia in TBI ≠ hypothermia in neonatal HIE — opposite evidence base.
Case 14 — SCIWORA in a Toddler HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 3-year-old girl is brought to ED after a motor vehicle crash. She was rear-facing in a car seat; the seat became dislodged. On arrival: alert, crying, unable to move any extremity, intact cranial nerves. Sensory level at approximately C7 (preserved sensation to the clavicle, absent below).
HR 90 | BP 78/45 (low for age) | RR 32 (shallow, accessory muscles) | SpO₂ 93% on 4 L NC

Stage 2 — Imaging

Plain cervical radiographs: unremarkable. CT cervical spine: no fracture, no ligamentous abnormality visible. MRI cervical spine (12 h later): cord edema spanning C4–C6 with mild T2 hyperintensity; no disc herniation; no bony injury.
Why is this presentation classic for pediatric SCIWORA?
  • Pediatric spinal anatomy permits significant cord stretch without bony injury — large head, ligamentous laxity, horizontal facets, incomplete ossification.
  • Mechanical lever arm of large head on weak neck musculature.
  • Common after high-energy trauma in young children < 8 years.
  • CT often unrevealing; MRI is essential.
  • Risk of delayed deterioration up to 4 days post-injury.

Stage 3 — Immediate ICU Management

Intubation indicated — diaphragm (C3–C5) functional but accessory muscle fatigue from C7 level injury, plus aspiration risk. Use rocuronium (avoid sux). Cervical collar maintained. Avoid hypotension — neurogenic shock physiology.
Key hemodynamic targets?
  • MAP ≥ 85–90 mm Hg for 5–7 days to optimize cord perfusion (extrapolated from adult SCI).
  • Norepinephrine preferred — combines α-vasoconstriction with β-chronotropy/inotropy; addresses both vasoplegia and bradycardia.
  • Avoid: pure fluid resuscitation (risk of pulmonary edema), pure α-agonists (worsen bradycardia).
  • Atropine for symptomatic bradycardia (especially during suctioning).

Stage 4 — Hospital Course

Day 2: motor function returns in C5 (deltoid, biceps) and partial sensation. Day 7: extubated. Continued cervical immobilization 8–12 weeks. Rehabilitation transferred at week 3.
Final Diagnosis: SCIWORA at C4–C6 with initial complete tetraplegia and neurogenic shock; partial recovery on serial examination.
ICU Plan:
  • Cervical immobilization 8–12 weeks (Pang protocol).
  • Hemodynamic: norepinephrine to MAP ≥ 85–90 × 7 days.
  • Avoid methylprednisolone (no pediatric evidence; risk > benefit).
  • Avoid succinylcholine (denervation hyperkalemia risk after 48 h).
  • VTE prophylaxis (mechanical initially; pharmacologic when bleeding risk acceptable).
  • Pulmonary toilet, secretion management, atelectasis prevention.
  • Bladder/bowel program; skin care; family education.
  • Early rehab medicine consultation; long-term plan.
Exam teaching points:
  • SCIWORA — pediatric-specific; CT can be normal; MRI essential.
  • Pediatric spinal anatomy explains the phenomenon.
  • Delayed neurologic deterioration up to 4 days — strict immobilization for weeks.
  • Avoid methylprednisolone, sux.
  • Neurogenic shock managed with norepinephrine, not pure fluid or pure α.
Case 15 — HSV Encephalitis with Refractory Seizures HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 5-year-old boy presents with 36 h of fever, headache, vomiting, and behavioral change. Within hours of arrival, focal-onset seizures of the right face spread to generalized convulsion. Post-ictal Todd paralysis on right.
HR 145 | BP 105/65 | T 39.4°C | SpO₂ 96% RA | GCS 9 (post-ictal)

Stage 2 — Investigations

CT: subtle left temporal-lobe hypodensity. MRI: asymmetric T2/FLAIR hyperintensity of left medial temporal lobe extending into insula and limbic structures; GRE shows micro-hemorrhages. LP: WBC 80 (lymphocytic, 90%), RBC 350 (xanthochromic), protein 0.9 g/L, glucose 3.2 mmol/L (CSF/serum ratio 0.55, normal). HSV PCR sent.
Empiric therapy?
  • IV acyclovir immediately: 15 mg/kg q8h for this 5-year-old (3 months to 12 years dosing); continue 21 days if PCR positive.
  • Empiric ceftriaxone + vancomycin pending bacterial workup.
  • Levetiracetam load 60 mg/kg IV for seizures; cEEG continuous.
  • Hydration to maintain renal function during acyclovir (crystal nephropathy risk).
  • Consider dexamethasone (controversial in HSV; some evidence supports addition for cerebral edema).

Stage 3 — Refractory Status Epilepticus

Despite levetiracetam, focal seizures recur. Fosphenytoin 20 PE/kg added. Continued seizures; intubation with rocuronium + ketamine 2 mg/kg. Midazolam infusion at 0.4 mg/kg/h.

Figure: Left temporal lobe rhythmic ictal pattern — classic for HSV encephalitis affecting the limbic structures.

Stage 4 — Day 7

HSV PCR positive. Seizures controlled with midazolam infusion; tapered after 48 h seizure-free on cEEG. Acyclovir continues, day 7 of 21. Renal function preserved. Levetiracetam continued long-term.
Final Diagnosis: Herpes simplex virus type 1 encephalitis with focal status epilepticus, treated with 21-day course of IV acyclovir and antiepileptic management.
ICU Plan:
  • Acyclovir 15 mg/kg q8h × 21 days.
  • cEEG: continuous until off sedation and seizure-free.
  • Levetiracetam maintenance ≥ 6–12 months; neurology follow-up.
  • Repeat MRI at end of treatment; consider follow-up for delayed anti-NMDA encephalitis (post-HSV).
  • Neuropsychology, developmental pediatrics, rehabilitation early.
Exam teaching points:
  • Empiric acyclovir BEFORE PCR confirmation when HSV suspected.
  • Pediatric acyclovir dose 15 mg/kg q8h (3 mo–12 y); neonatal dose higher (20 mg/kg q8h).
  • Treat 21 days for HSV CNS infection.
  • Watch for post-HSV anti-NMDA receptor encephalitis (weeks-months later).
  • Crystal nephropathy — maintain hydration during acyclovir.
Case 16 — ADEM in a Post-Viral Child MODERATE

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 7-year-old presents 2 weeks after a febrile URI with new-onset confusion, fever 38.5, ataxia, and multifocal weakness (left arm and right leg). She is encephalopathic but arousable.
HR 120 | BP 110/70 | T 38.5°C | SpO₂ 97% RA | GCS 11

Stage 2 — Imaging and CSF

MRI: multiple bilateral asymmetric large (1–3 cm) T2/FLAIR hyperintense lesions in subcortical white matter, basal ganglia, and pons; no Dawson fingers; no T1 black holes. CSF: WBC 25 (lymph predominant), protein 0.55 g/L, glucose normal, oligoclonal bands absent. Anti-MOG antibody sent. HSV PCR negative.
How does the encephalopathy criterion distinguish ADEM from MS first attack?
  • Encephalopathy (altered consciousness/behavior beyond expected from illness) is required for ADEM per IPMSSG 2013.
  • MS first attack typically presents without encephalopathy, with discrete lesions, and may have positive oligoclonal bands.
  • ADEM lesions are typically larger, asymmetric, poorly demarcated, and can involve deep gray matter.
  • Outcome is generally favorable for ADEM (monophasic); MS has recurrent course.
  • Anti-MOG positivity defines an overlapping demyelinating spectrum with potential for relapse.

Stage 3 — Treatment

IV methylprednisolone 30 mg/kg/day (max 1 g) × 5 days, followed by oral prednisone taper over 6 weeks. Supportive care; PT/OT begun. Day 3: improving level of consciousness; day 7: encephalopathy resolved; partial motor recovery.
If poor response to steroids?
  • IVIG 2 g/kg total over 2–5 days.
  • Plasma exchange (PLEX) for severe refractory cases.
  • Re-imaging to rule out alternative diagnoses.

Stage 4 — Discharge and Follow-up

Anti-MOG returns positive at moderate titer; treatment plan: extended steroid taper; pediatric neurology follow-up for surveillance. MRI 3 months: lesion regression. Long-term monitoring for relapse.
Final Diagnosis: Acute disseminated encephalomyelitis with anti-MOG antibody positivity, monophasic course (current).
ICU/Ward Plan:
  • IV methylprednisolone with oral taper.
  • Anti-MOG titer monitoring; relapse surveillance.
  • If relapse: rituximab, MMF, or chronic IVIG.
  • Avoid MS DMTs in MOG-positive disease.
  • Rehabilitation, neuropsychology, school re-integration.
Exam teaching points:
  • IPMSSG 2013 ADEM criteria — encephalopathy mandatory; multifocal MRI lesions; post-infectious history.
  • Distinguish from MS first attack; positive anti-MOG defines MOGAD.
  • First-line: high-dose methylprednisolone; second-line: IVIG / PLEX.
  • Avoid MS DMTs (interferon, natalizumab) in MOG-positive disease.
Case 17 — Tuberculous Meningitis with Hydrocephalus HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 4-year-old refugee child presents with 3 weeks of progressive lethargy, fever, vomiting, and recent left sixth nerve palsy. Mother had pulmonary TB diagnosed 2 months ago. The child was started on isoniazid prophylaxis but compliance uncertain.
HR 130 | BP 100/65 | T 38.7°C | GCS 11 | Reduced muscle tone

Stage 2 — Investigations

CT head: basal cisternal enhancement, hydrocephalus (lateral and third ventricles), small bilateral basal ganglia infarcts. LP (opening pressure 28 cmH₂O): WBC 220 (lymphocytic, 85%), protein 2.8 g/L, glucose 1.0 mmol/L (CSF/serum 0.2). AFB smear negative; CSF Xpert MTB/RIF positive (rifampin sensitive). CXR: bilateral hilar lymphadenopathy.
Lillie criteria for TBM staging?
  • Stage I: lucid, no focal deficit, no signs of hydrocephalus or raised ICP. Best prognosis.
  • Stage II: lethargic/confused, may have focal deficits, cranial neuropathies. Intermediate.
  • Stage III: stuporous/comatose, decerebrate posturing, severe deficits. Worst prognosis.
  • This child is Stage II.

Stage 3 — Treatment Initiation

Anti-TB therapy: isoniazid 15 mg/kg/day (with pyridoxine 25 mg/day), rifampin 20 mg/kg/day, pyrazinamide 35 mg/kg/day, ethambutol 20 mg/kg/day. Dexamethasone 0.4 mg/kg/day IV with planned 6–8 week taper. Empiric ceftriaxone for first 48 h until TBM confirmed.

Stage 4 — Hydrocephalus Management

Day 2: progressive obtundation, papilledema. ICP estimated elevated. Emergency EVD placed by neurosurgery. CSF drains; mental status improves over 48 h. Trial of EVD clamping at week 3 — re-elevation; VP shunt placed permanently.
Vascular complications in TBM?
  • Inflammatory vasculopathy of perforating arteries → small infarcts in basal ganglia and internal capsule (lacunar pattern).
  • Cranial neuropathies (especially CN VI from basal exudate, CN III).
  • Hydrocephalus (obstructive or communicating) — common.
  • Endocrine: SIADH, central DI, panhypopituitarism.
  • Tuberculomas (mass lesions) — may enlarge paradoxically on treatment ("paradoxical response").
Final Diagnosis: Tuberculous meningitis (Stage II) with hydrocephalus and small lacunar infarcts; rifampin-sensitive.
ICU/Ward Plan:
  • RIPE × 2 months, then RIfampin + Isoniazid × 7–10 months (total 9–12 months).
  • Dexamethasone tapered over 6–8 weeks.
  • Pyridoxine throughout INH treatment.
  • VP shunt management; serial neuro exams; head circumference; growth.
  • Audiology / vision; monitor for ototoxicity (streptomycin) and visual toxicity (ethambutol).
  • LFTs monthly; expect mild transaminitis.
  • Social work for housing/contacts; public health notification; family screening.
  • Developmental follow-up; expect some sequelae.
Exam teaching points:
  • TBM: subacute meningitis + low CSF glucose + high protein + basal enhancement + hydrocephalus + lacunar infarcts.
  • 4-drug RIPE + dexamethasone (Thwaites NEJM 2004 — survival benefit).
  • Hydrocephalus is common — early CSF diversion improves outcomes.
  • Lillie stages (I–III) predict outcome; treat empirically while confirming.
  • Xpert MTB/RIF accelerates diagnosis; AFB smear is insensitive.
Case 18 — Lundberg A Wave on ICP Monitor HARD

Stage 1 — Background

A 10-year-old, day 4 of severe TBI in PICU. ICP monitor + EVD in place. Baseline ICP 16–20, CPP 60. On midazolam + fentanyl, normothermia, normocapnia, 3% saline maintaining Na 152.

Stage 2 — ICP Trace Change

Figure: Recurrent Lundberg A (plateau) waves: sustained elevations to 50–80 mm Hg for 5–20 minutes with abrupt onset and return to a still-elevated baseline. Pathognomonic for severely reduced compensatory reserve.

Stage 3 — Significance and Action

What is the pathophysiology of plateau waves?
  • Patient is on the steep portion of the Monro-Kellie pressure-volume curve.
  • Transient drop in CPP (e.g., MAP dip, pain stimulus, increased intrathoracic pressure) triggers compensatory cerebral vasodilation in pressure-passive vessels.
  • Vasodilation increases cerebral blood volume → further ICP rise → further CPP fall → more vasodilation → vicious cycle.
  • Eventually CPP falls enough to trigger reflex sympathetic activation, raising MAP and breaking the cycle.
  • Recurrent plateau waves indicate severely impaired autoregulation and herald herniation.
Immediate management?
  • Aggressively optimize Tier 1: deepen sedation, drain CSF, hyperosmolar bolus, ensure normocapnia 35–38, head-up, normothermia.
  • Raise MAP if CPP < target (vasopressor to age-appropriate CPP).
  • Consider Tier 2: barbiturate coma, decompressive craniectomy if persistent.
  • Multimodal monitoring: PbtO₂, microdialysis, PRx to characterize autoregulation.
  • Repeat CT to rule out new surgical lesion.

Stage 4 — Course

Pentobarbital coma initiated with EEG burst-suppression. Plateau waves cease over 24 h. Continued Tier 2 for 72 h; weaned. ICP stable thereafter.
Final Diagnosis: Recurrent Lundberg A (plateau) waves in severe pediatric TBI with reduced compensatory reserve; treated with Tier 2 barbiturate coma.
Exam teaching points:
  • Lundberg A waves = sustained ICP elevations 50–100 × 5–20 min; pathognomonic for compromised reserve.
  • B waves = oscillations ~1–2 per minute; less specific.
  • C waves = physiologic ~6 per minute (Mayer waves).
  • Pathophysiology: vasodilation cascade on steep Monro-Kellie curve.
  • Treatment: aggressive Tier 1; escalate to Tier 2.
Case 19 — Cerebral Microdialysis Interpretation HARD

Stage 1 — Setup

A 12-year-old with severe TBI day 3. ICP monitor + EVD + PbtO₂ + cEEG + cerebral microdialysis catheter (right frontal). On Tier 1 management. ICP 18, CPP 60, PbtO₂ 18, Hb 9.5.

Stage 2 — Microdialysate Trend

Figure: L/P ratio trend over 24 h showing progressive rise into ischemia/crisis zone, then improvement after intervention (transfusion + CPP optimization).

Stage 3 — Interpretation and Action

Hour 18: L/P 75, brain glucose 0.3 mmol/L, glutamate elevated. Despite "adequate" ICP, CPP, and PbtO₂ values, the metabolic profile reveals energy crisis.
Why are ICP/CPP/PbtO₂ values inadequate in this scenario?
  • PbtO₂ probe is a single regional probe; it may not be in the most vulnerable tissue.
  • Microcirculatory failure can occur with intact macrocirculation.
  • Mitochondrial dysfunction can produce metabolic crisis without overt hypoperfusion.
  • Anemia, hypoglycemia, hypocapnia each contribute even with normal ICP/CPP.
  • Multimodal integration catches problems single-modality monitoring misses.
Intervention?
  • Optimize Hb (transfuse to 10 g/dL given metabolic crisis despite normal PbtO₂).
  • Ensure PaCO₂ 35–38 (avoid hyperventilation).
  • Raise CPP toward CPPopt if PRx data available; here aim CPP 70.
  • Increase FiO₂ to 0.5–0.6 transiently to evaluate response.
  • Reduce metabolic demand: deepen sedation, normothermia, seizure control on cEEG.
  • Re-imaging if no improvement to rule out new lesion or stroke.

Stage 4 — Resolution

Hour 19: Hb transfused to 10.5; CPP raised to 70; FiO₂ 0.5 transiently. By hour 24: L/P normalizes to 28, glucose 0.9, glutamate falling.
Final Diagnosis: Cerebral metabolic energy crisis during severe TBI, detected by microdialysis, reversed by Hb optimization, CPP elevation toward CPPopt, and demand reduction.
Exam teaching points:
  • Cerebral microdialysis L/P > 40 = ischemia/energy crisis.
  • Glucose < 0.5 mmol/L and elevated glutamate corroborate.
  • Glycerol elevation = membrane breakdown.
  • Multimodal monitoring detects pathology that single modalities miss.
  • Hyperglycolysis pattern: high pyruvate with elevated lactate (lower L/P).
  • Mitochondrial dysfunction: high L/P with adequate glucose and pyruvate.
Case 20 — Carbon Monoxide Poisoning in House Fire MODERATE

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 13-year-old is rescued from a house fire. On arrival: soot around mouth, hoarse voice, mild stridor, headache, vomiting, brief loss of consciousness at scene. SpO₂ 99% on RA by pulse oximeter.
HR 130 | BP 110/65 | T 36.8°C | SpO₂ 99% (pulse ox) | GCS 13 (intermittent confusion)

Stage 2 — ABG and Co-oximetry

ABG with co-oximetry: pH 7.28, PaCO₂ 32, PaO₂ 380 (on FiO₂ 1.0), HCO₃ 16, carboxyhemoglobin (COHb) 32%, lactate 8 mmol/L, methemoglobin 1.5%, base deficit –9.

Figure: CO binding shifts curve LEFT (impaired tissue O₂ release) and reduces effective O₂-carrying capacity (COHb cannot carry O₂). Pulse oximetry cannot distinguish COHb from O₂Hb.

Stage 3 — Decision Point

Significant CO poisoning with LOC, COHb 32%, elevated lactate. Concern for concurrent cyanide poisoning given smoke inhalation and elevated lactate. Anti-cyanide therapy considered.
Indications for hyperbaric oxygen?
  • Loss of consciousness at any time.
  • Neurologic deficits (focal or global).
  • Cardiac ischemia.
  • COHb > 25% (some sources > 40%) or > 15% in pregnant patients.
  • Persistent symptoms after several hours of normobaric 100% O₂.
  • This patient meets criteria (LOC + COHb 32%); refer to HBO chamber if logistically feasible.
When to add hydroxocobalamin for cyanide?
  • High suspicion in smoke inhalation with: hypotension, severe acidosis, lactate > 10 mmol/L, persistent altered mental status, cardiac instability.
  • Dose: 70 mg/kg IV (max 5 g) over 15 min; can repeat.
  • Side effects: red discoloration of skin/urine; interferes with some lab assays.
  • Avoid sodium thiosulfate alone if hydroxocobalamin available; nitrites (methemoglobin inducers) AVOIDED in mixed CO + CN poisoning — exacerbates impaired O₂ delivery.

Stage 4 — Course

100% O₂ via tight-fitting NRB; HBO arranged at referral center. Empiric hydroxocobalamin 5 g IV given for lactate > 10 and suspected cyanide co-poisoning. ECG monitored — no ischemia. Airway monitored for inhalation injury edema; intubated for stridor at hour 4. After 2 sessions of HBO, COHb < 5%, neurologic recovery; discharged with neuropsychiatric follow-up for delayed sequelae.
Final Diagnosis: Severe carbon monoxide poisoning with cyanide co-poisoning and thermal/airway injury from house fire.
ICU Plan:
  • 100% O₂ via NRB or ETT until COHb < 5%.
  • HBO for severe presentations (LOC, neuro signs, COHb > 25%).
  • Hydroxocobalamin for cyanide.
  • Airway protection — inhalation injury edema can develop.
  • ECG monitoring for cardiac ischemia.
  • Neuropsych follow-up for delayed neurologic sequelae (1–6 weeks post-exposure).
Exam teaching points:
  • Pulse oximetry FALSELY normal in CO poisoning — use co-oximetry.
  • CO shifts ODC LEFT (worsens tissue O₂ release) and reduces O₂ carrying capacity.
  • HBO indicated for LOC, neuro deficit, cardiac, severe COHb, pregnancy.
  • Lactate > 10 + smoke inhalation → suspect cyanide; treat with hydroxocobalamin.
  • Delayed neuropsychiatric sequelae weeks after acute event.
Case 21 — Pyridoxine-Dependent Epilepsy in a Neonate HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 7-day-old term infant is admitted for refractory seizures starting at day 3 of life. Initial workup (cranial US, MRI, electrolytes, glucose, ammonia, lactate, blood cultures, HSV PCR, plasma amino acids, urine organic acids) is unremarkable. Phenobarbital 40 mg/kg load and levetiracetam 60 mg/kg have failed.
HR 160 | BP 65/40 | T 36.7°C | Frequent multifocal seizures on cEEG

Stage 2 — Diagnostic Trial

Pyridoxine-dependent epilepsy considered. IV pyridoxine 100 mg given under cEEG monitoring with airway and resuscitation team at bedside.
What response is diagnostic, and what are the risks of the trial?
  • Dramatic clinical and electrographic seizure cessation within minutes is diagnostic.
  • EEG often normalizes; background may show improvement over hours.
  • Risks: apnea (sometimes prolonged), hypotonia, hypotension during/shortly after IV bolus — be prepared to bag-mask ventilate or intubate.
  • If no response, trial pyridoxal-5'-phosphate (PNPO deficiency) and folinic acid (folinic acid–responsive seizures, allelic).

Stage 3 — Confirmation

Seizures cease within 4 minutes; EEG background normalizes over 24 h. Urine α-aminoadipic semialdehyde (α-AASA) elevated; plasma pipecolic acid elevated. ALDH7A1 gene sequencing returns homozygous pathogenic variant.

Stage 4 — Long-term Management

Maintenance oral pyridoxine 30 mg/kg/day. Lysine-restricted diet and arginine supplementation initiated per consensus. Family counseling on lifelong therapy and recurrence in subsequent illness or intercurrent stress. Developmental pediatrics enrollment.
Final Diagnosis: Pyridoxine-dependent epilepsy (ALDH7A1 mutation, antiquitin deficiency).
Plan:
  • Oral pyridoxine 15–30 mg/kg/day for life.
  • Lysine restriction + arginine supplementation per consensus.
  • Folinic acid supplementation (if responsive).
  • Anticipate developmental delay despite good seizure control; early intervention.
  • Genetic counseling; sibling testing.
  • Letter to all future caregivers re: importance of pyridoxine continuity during illness.
Exam teaching points:
  • Pyridoxine-dependent epilepsy: refractory neonatal/infantile seizures responsive ONLY to pyridoxine.
  • Mechanism: antiquitin (ALDH7A1) deficiency → α-AASA accumulation → P5P inactivation.
  • Diagnostic IV trial with airway support ready.
  • Lifetime treatment; lysine restriction adjunctive.
  • Allelic disorders: PNPO deficiency (responsive to pyridoxal-5'-phosphate); folinic acid–responsive seizures.
Case 22 — Anti-MOG Antibody Disease (Bilateral Optic Neuritis + LETM) MODERATE

Stage 1 — Presentation

An 11-year-old girl presents with 5 days of progressive bilateral painful visual loss (counting fingers OU) and rapidly evolving leg weakness with bilateral lower-extremity numbness over 48 hours.
HR 95 | BP 105/70 | T 37.0°C | No encephalopathy | Sensory level T8 | Urinary retention

Stage 2 — Workup

MRI brain: bilateral optic nerve enhancement, otherwise unremarkable. MRI spine: longitudinally extensive T2 hyperintensity spanning T2–T9 (LETM, ≥ 3 vertebral segments). CSF: WBC 30 (lymph), protein 0.5, glucose normal, oligoclonal bands negative. Serum AQP4-IgG negative. Serum anti-MOG IgG positive (high titer). Visual evoked potentials prolonged bilaterally.
How does MOGAD differ from NMOSD in this presentation?
  • Bilateral, anterior optic nerve involvement is typical of MOGAD (NMOSD optic neuritis often unilateral and posterior).
  • LETM can occur in both.
  • MOGAD CSF often lacks oligoclonal bands; NMOSD similarly less commonly positive than MS.
  • Serology: AQP4-IgG defines NMOSD; MOG-IgG defines MOGAD; both can have LETM phenotype.
  • Both can be monophasic or relapsing; MOGAD relapse rate lower than NMOSD.
  • Avoid MS DMTs in both.

Stage 3 — Treatment

IV methylprednisolone 30 mg/kg/day × 5 days. Vision improves by day 7 (20/100 OU); legs improve to 4/5 strength. Oral prednisone taper begun over 6 months (extended due to relapse risk).

Stage 4 — Follow-up at 4 Months

Recurrence of optic neuritis after taper. Decision for chronic immunosuppression: rituximab. Long-term plan includes IV pulse steroids for relapses, regular antibody titers, MRI surveillance.
Final Diagnosis: Anti-MOG antibody disease (MOGAD) presenting with bilateral optic neuritis and longitudinally extensive transverse myelitis; relapsing course.
Plan:
  • Acute relapse: IV methylprednisolone ± IVIG or PLEX.
  • Long-term: rituximab or mycophenolate mofetil for relapsing course.
  • Avoid MS DMTs (interferons, natalizumab, fingolimod).
  • Ophthalmology follow-up for visual rehabilitation.
  • Rehabilitation for residual motor deficit; bladder management.
  • Antibody titers and MRI surveillance.
Exam teaching points:
  • MOGAD: bilateral anterior ON, LETM, ADEM-like; serum/CSF anti-MOG positive.
  • NMOSD: predominantly posterior ON + LETM + area postrema; AQP4-IgG positive.
  • Both: acute treatment with high-dose steroids; PLEX if refractory; long-term targeted immunotherapy.
  • Both: avoid MS DMTs.
Case 23 — Acute Necrotizing Encephalopathy after Influenza HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 4-year-old presents with 3 days of high fever, cough, myalgia (influenza A confirmed on rapid antigen). On day 3 of illness: sudden seizure followed by rapid deterioration to coma.
HR 160 | BP 120/85 | T 40.1°C | GCS 4 | SpO₂ 91% (intubated) | Pupils 4 mm sluggish

Stage 2 — Investigations

MRI brain: symmetric bilateral thalamic lesions with concentric edema-necrosis-hemorrhage pattern; brainstem tegmentum and cerebellar white matter also involved. Ferritin 6800 ng/mL (markedly elevated). IL-6 elevated. AST 850, ALT 720. LDH 1500. Lactate 7. CSF mild pleocytosis without organism.
Diagnostic features?
  • Symmetric bilateral thalamic necrosis (pathognomonic).
  • Para-infectious context (influenza, HHV-6, parainfluenza, SARS-CoV-2 most common).
  • Hyperinflammation markers: ferritin, IL-6, transaminases (often elevated).
  • CSF unremarkable for direct infection.
  • Familial recurrent forms (ANE1) associated with RANBP2 mutations.

Stage 3 — Treatment

PICU course: BTF-style ICP management (sedation, 3% saline, head-up, normothermia post-cooling), seizure control (levetiracetam), invasive monitoring. Aggressive immunomodulation: pulse methylprednisolone 30 mg/kg/day × 5, IVIG 2 g/kg, plasma exchange × 5 sessions, tocilizumab 8 mg/kg IV (IL-6 antagonist) for severe cytokine storm. Oseltamivir for influenza.
Why is tocilizumab considered?
  • ANE features cytokine storm with elevated IL-6.
  • Tocilizumab antagonizes IL-6 receptor — used in macrophage activation syndrome, cytokine release syndromes.
  • Case reports / small series suggest neurologic and biomarker improvement.
  • Evidence base small but increasingly accepted in severe ANE.
  • Anakinra (IL-1 antagonist) is an alternative.

Stage 4 — Course

Day 7: ferritin trending down; cytokine markers improving. Day 14: extubated; severe disability with quadriparesis, dystonia, cognitive impairment. Transferred to inpatient rehabilitation.
Final Diagnosis: Acute necrotizing encephalopathy of childhood (ANE) precipitated by influenza A; cytokine storm pattern.
ICU Plan:
  • Supportive ICU care: ICP, seizure, hemodynamic optimization.
  • Immunomodulation: high-dose steroids, IVIG, PLEX, tocilizumab/anakinra in severe cases.
  • Antiviral targeting precipitant (oseltamivir).
  • Rehabilitation early; long-term disability anticipated.
  • Genetic counseling — RANBP2 testing for recurrent forms; counsel on future viral illness risk.
Exam teaching points:
  • ANE: para-infectious, symmetric thalamic necrosis, hyperinflammation markers (↑ ferritin, IL-6).
  • High mortality (30–50%); severe sequelae common.
  • Treatment: steroids, IVIG, PLEX; tocilizumab/anakinra in severe cytokine storm.
  • RANBP2 mutations in familial recurrent forms.
  • Distinguish from ADEM (asymmetric, post-infectious 1–4 weeks) and HSV (unilateral temporal).
Case 24 — Lead Encephalopathy with Combination Chelation HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 2-year-old presents with 4 days of vomiting, irritability, ataxia, and now seizures. Family lives in pre-1978 housing with peeling paint; child observed eating paint chips. Examination: lethargic, opisthotonic posturing during seizures.
HR 150 | BP 120/80 | T 36.9°C | GCS 9 | Hb 7.8 g/dL (microcytic) | Pupils 3 mm reactive

Stage 2 — Investigations

Whole blood lead level 92 µg/dL (severe). FEP (free erythrocyte protoporphyrin) elevated. Iron deficiency confirmed (ferritin low, transferrin saturation low). Abdominal radiograph: radio-opaque material in GI tract. CT head: diffuse cerebral edema, effaced sulci. EEG: generalized slowing with occasional epileptiform activity.
Chelation regimen — why the sequence matters?
  • Dimercaprol (BAL) FIRST: lipid-soluble, distributes intracellularly, mobilizes lead from CNS.
  • 4 hours later: CaNa2EDTA — water-soluble, mobilizes extracellular lead efficiently but redistributes from blood to brain if given alone.
  • Sequence (BAL first) prevents EDTA-induced redistribution into brain.
  • Duration: 5 days typically; check BLL post-chelation; rebound common (lead release from bone).

Stage 3 — ICU Management

Intubated for airway protection given seizures and decreased LOC. BAL 75 mg/m² IM q4h, first dose given. Four hours later: CaNa2EDTA 1500 mg/m²/day continuous IV infusion. Levetiracetam for seizures. 3% saline for cerebral edema. Strict normothermia. Iron deficiency treated with IV iron.

Stage 4 — Decontamination and Discharge

Whole-bowel irrigation with PEG-electrolyte solution given radio-opaque GI lead. Environmental investigation: paint chip in home (lead 6800 ppm). Family relocated by public health. Day 5: BLL down to 35; day 14 follow-up: BLL 28 (rebound). Repeat oral DMSA course given.
Final Diagnosis: Pediatric severe lead encephalopathy with seizures and cerebral edema; concurrent iron deficiency; environmental lead exposure.
ICU/Long-term Plan:
  • BAL + CaNa2EDTA combination chelation × 5 days.
  • Whole-bowel irrigation for GI lead.
  • Iron supplementation (decreases further lead absorption).
  • Environmental investigation, remediation, family relocation.
  • Public health notification.
  • Follow-up BLL monthly; repeat chelation per BLL.
  • Developmental pediatrics; expect cognitive sequelae.
  • Sibling screening; community screening.
Exam teaching points:
  • BLL ≥ 70 µg/dL OR any encephalopathy → BAL first, then EDTA 4 h later (combination parenteral).
  • BLL 45–69 without encephalopathy → oral DMSA.
  • BAL-first sequence prevents EDTA-induced CNS redistribution.
  • Iron deficiency potentiates lead absorption — co-treat.
  • Rebound BLL common — repeat chelation may be needed.
  • Environmental investigation and remediation are mandatory.
Case 25 — MELAS Stroke-Like Episode with L-Arginine Therapy HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 10-year-old boy presents with subacute onset of right-sided weakness and aphasia over 12 hours, preceded by 2 days of headache and vomiting. Family history of maternal-line hearing loss and short stature. Past episodes of "migraine" and one prior seizure.
HR 110 | BP 105/65 | T 37.4°C | GCS 13 | Lactate 6.4 mmol/L

Stage 2 — Imaging

MRI: cortical/subcortical T2/FLAIR hyperintensity in the left temporo-parietal region NOT respecting the MCA territory; mild DWI restriction; MR spectroscopy with lactate peak. MRA: no large-vessel occlusion or stenosis.
Why is tPA inappropriate here?
  • The lesion is not embolic/thrombotic — no large-vessel occlusion.
  • Pattern crosses vascular territories — characteristic of metabolic stroke.
  • Lactate peak on MRS suggests anaerobic metabolism.
  • tPA cannot reverse mitochondrial energy failure and risks hemorrhage without benefit.

Stage 3 — Treatment

L-arginine 0.5 g/kg IV bolus over 30 min, then 0.5 g/kg/day continuous infusion for 5 days. Coenzyme Q10, riboflavin, thiamine, L-carnitine, vitamin C supplementation. Avoid valproate; levetiracetam loaded for prior seizure history. Maintain normothermia and euglycemia. Genetic testing returns m.3243A>G mutation confirming MELAS.
What rationale supports L-arginine?
  • MELAS pathophysiology involves nitric oxide deficiency from impaired mitochondrial function.
  • L-arginine is the substrate for nitric oxide synthase.
  • NO restoration improves cerebral microvascular perfusion in stroke-like areas.
  • Koga RCT (2005, 2010) showed reduction in stroke-like episode severity.

Stage 4 — Course

Weakness and aphasia improve over 5 days. Discharged on chronic oral arginine and "mitochondrial cocktail" with strict avoidance of mitotoxic drugs (valproate, statins, linezolid, metformin). Multidisciplinary follow-up (neurology, cardiology, endocrinology, ophthalmology, audiology).
Final Diagnosis: MELAS (m.3243A>G mutation) presenting with stroke-like episode; treated with acute L-arginine.
Plan:
  • Long-term: oral arginine/citrulline; mitochondrial cocktail; avoid mitotoxic drugs.
  • Routine monitoring: ECG, echocardiogram, audiometry, fundus.
  • Family genetic counseling — maternal inheritance.
  • Educational accommodations; school nurse plan.
  • Anticipate progressive disease.
Teaching points:
  • MELAS stroke-like episodes: cortical/subcortical pattern not respecting vascular territories; lactate peak on MRS.
  • L-arginine is the cornerstone acute therapy.
  • Avoid valproate (mitotoxic, hepatotoxic in POLG/MELAS).
  • Mitochondrial cocktail has modest but real benefit.
Case 26 — Posterior Reversible Encephalopathy Syndrome (PRES) Post-HSCT HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 12-year-old girl is 30 days post allogeneic stem cell transplant for ALL, on tacrolimus and mycophenolate for GVHD prophylaxis. She develops severe headache, visual disturbance (cortical blindness), and a generalized tonic-clonic seizure.
HR 100 | BP 175/115 | T 37.6°C | GCS post-ictal 10 | Tacrolimus level supratherapeutic 18 ng/mL

Stage 2 — Imaging

MRI: symmetric bilateral parieto-occipital T2/FLAIR hyperintensity in subcortical white matter without restricted diffusion. Smaller similar lesions in cerebellum and frontal regions. Normal MRV.

Figure: Gradual BP reduction over hours preferred. Rapid lowering risks ischemic conversion.

Stage 3 — Management

Held tacrolimus; switched to sirolimus. IV nicardipine titrated to reduce MAP by ~20% over 4 h. Levetiracetam load 60 mg/kg + maintenance. cEEG monitoring continued for 48 h. Mg supplemented for low level.

Stage 4 — Course

Vision returns over 48 h; no further seizures. Day 5: repeat MRI shows partial resolution. Discharged on levetiracetam, sirolimus (replacing tacrolimus), with BP monitoring at home.
Final Diagnosis: PRES precipitated by tacrolimus toxicity and hypertension post-HSCT.
Plan:
  • Switched immunosuppressant (sirolimus replacing tacrolimus).
  • BP target normal for age.
  • Antiepileptic continued 6 months.
  • Serial MRI to document resolution.
  • Counseling: PRES is generally reversible if recognized early.
Teaching points:
  • PRES: classic triggers (hypertension, calcineurin inhibitors, chemo, eclampsia, autoimmune).
  • Imaging: vasogenic edema, posterior-predominant; no restricted diffusion (unless severe).
  • BP reduction: gradual (10–25% over hours), NOT precipitous.
  • Remove offending agent; treat seizures; supportive care.
Case 27 — Posterior Fossa Syndrome (Cerebellar Mutism) After Medulloblastoma Resection MODERATE

Stage 1 — Background

A 6-year-old boy underwent gross total resection of a vermian medulloblastoma 48 hours ago. He woke alert from anesthesia, spoke briefly with his mother, then progressively became mute over the next 12 hours.

Stage 2 — Examination

Day 2 post-op: mute, dysphagic (NG tube placed for nutrition), hypotonic with truncal ataxia, emotional lability (alternating tearfulness and laughter), preserved arousal. CN exam: facial weakness mild, gag preserved but slow. No new focal deficits beyond the syndrome.
What is the syndrome and its time course?
  • Posterior fossa syndrome (cerebellar mutism syndrome).
  • Onset 24–72 hours post-op (delayed onset distinguishes from anesthesia).
  • Affects ~25% of pediatric posterior fossa surgeries; higher in medulloblastoma vermian resections.
  • Recovery of speech: weeks to months.
  • Residual: dysarthria, cerebellar cognitive-affective syndrome common.

Stage 3 — Management

Supportive care: speech therapy, PT/OT initiated. Nutrition via NG; PEG considered if dysphagia prolonged. Daily neuropsychology and emotional support; family counseling about expected timeline.

Stage 4 — Recovery

Week 4: first single words returned. Week 12: simple sentences with dysarthria. Month 6: full sentences with mild residual dysarthria; neuropsychology testing reveals executive function and processing speed deficits (Schmahmann cerebellar cognitive-affective syndrome). Continued ongoing rehabilitation and school support.
Final Diagnosis: Posterior fossa syndrome (cerebellar mutism syndrome) post-medulloblastoma resection; residual cerebellar cognitive-affective syndrome.
Plan:
  • Speech therapy, OT, PT throughout recovery.
  • Neuropsychology assessment at 6 and 12 months.
  • Educational accommodations.
  • Tumor surveillance (medulloblastoma adjuvant therapy as planned).
  • Family education: timeline is months, not weeks.
Teaching points:
  • Delayed onset (1–4 days) distinguishes from anesthetic effects.
  • Dentato-thalamo-cortical pathway disruption mechanism.
  • Recovery slow (weeks-months); residual cognitive and affective deficits common.
  • Family counseling about timeline is essential.
Case 28 — Intrathecal Baclofen Withdrawal from Pump Failure HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 12-year-old boy with severe spastic quadriplegic cerebral palsy and implanted intrathecal baclofen pump (placed 5 years ago) presents to ED with severe rebound spasticity, fever, agitation, and confusion. Mother reports pump alarms began 36 hours ago, ignored due to assumed false alarm. Last pump refill 3 months ago. Patient is on no other sedating medications.
HR 145 | BP 165/100 | T 39.6°C | RR 30 | SpO₂ 91% | Continuous painful spasms | CK 18,000 | UA myoglobin positive

Stage 2 — Recognition

Pump interrogation: catheter blockage with zero flow detected. Differential: NMS (no neuroleptic), MH (no anesthetic), serotonin syndrome (no serotonergic drugs), sepsis (cultures pending), thyroid storm (TSH pending). Picture fits acute intrathecal baclofen withdrawal — a neurologic emergency.
Why is this life-threatening?
  • Sudden GABA-B withdrawal causes rebound spasticity, hyperthermia, autonomic storm.
  • Severe rhabdomyolysis → AKI → multi-organ failure.
  • Respiratory failure from chest wall spasm fatigue.
  • DIC, ICU mortality without prompt intervention.

Stage 3 — Acute Management

Neurosurgery activated for emergent pump revision. While waiting: high-dose oral baclofen 60 mg q6h via NG, midazolam infusion 0.2 mg/kg/h, cyproheptadine 4 mg q6h, IV fluids 1.5 × maintenance with bicarbonate for urinary alkalinization (myoglobinuria), aggressive cooling (cooling blanket, antipyretics). Intubation with rocuronium for airway protection and to enable deep sedation. Neurosurgery places temporary intrathecal catheter for baclofen infusion via lumbar drain while pump replacement planned.

Stage 4 — Resolution

Within 12 h of restoring intrathecal baclofen via lumbar drain: spasticity resolves, temperature normalizes, autonomic instability improves. Definitive pump replacement on day 3. Extubated day 5. Renal function recovered. Discharged with new pump and family education about alarm protocols.
Final Diagnosis: Acute intrathecal baclofen withdrawal from catheter blockage; life-threatening hyperthermic syndrome with rhabdomyolysis; resolved with restoration of intrathecal baclofen delivery.
Plan:
  • Emergent pump troubleshooting; temporary intrathecal infusion when pump failure cannot be immediately fixed.
  • Family education: alarms require immediate medical attention.
  • Establish 24/7 contact protocol for pump issues.
  • Annual pump assessment; battery exchange planning.
  • Documentation in chart for future caregivers.
Teaching points:
  • ITB withdrawal mimics NMS, MH, serotonin syndrome — but specific treatment is restoring intrathecal baclofen.
  • Oral baclofen alone is insufficient (poor CNS penetration).
  • Lumbar drain with intrathecal baclofen infusion bridges to definitive pump repair.
  • Aggressive supportive care: cooling, fluids, sedation, NMB if needed.
  • Dantrolene addresses MH but not ITB withdrawal directly.
Case 29 — Status Dystonicus in DYT1 Dystonia HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 13-year-old with genetically confirmed DYT1 dystonia (TOR1A mutation) and history of deep brain stimulator implanted 2 years ago presents with worsening dystonic spasms over 48 hours after a viral upper respiratory infection. Spasms now continuous, painful, with marked dystonic posturing affecting all extremities and trunk.
HR 150 | BP 160/95 | T 39.4°C | RR 35 | SpO₂ 93% | CK 25,000 | Creatinine 2.0 mg/dL (baseline 0.6) | UA myoglobin positive

Stage 2 — Differential

Differential: status dystonicus vs MH vs NMS vs serotonin syndrome vs sepsis. No anesthetic exposure, no neuroleptic, no serotonergic drugs. Underlying DYT1 with intercurrent illness as trigger. DBS programming interrogated: settings normal.

Stage 3 — Acute Management

PICU admission. Aggressive cooling (cooling blanket, IV cold fluids). Deep sedation with propofol infusion (short-term, mindful of PRIS). Midazolam adjunct. Neuromuscular blockade with rocuronium for refractory spasms. Mechanical ventilation. IV fluids 2× maintenance with bicarbonate for myoglobinuria. Foley monitoring; serial creatinine. Optimization of oral anti-dystonic therapy via NG: trihexyphenidyl, baclofen, tetrabenazine, clonidine. DBS programming reviewed; voltage increased. Treat URI with supportive care.

Stage 4 — Course

Within 48 h: spasms subside; CK trending down; renal function recovering. Sedation slowly weaned over 96 h. Extubated. Outpatient: DBS revision considered; baseline regimen optimized. Family education: future illness triggers, fever management, escalation plan.
Final Diagnosis: Status dystonicus in DYT1 dystonia, precipitated by viral illness; complicated by rhabdomyolysis and AKI; resolved with deep sedation, optimized dystonia therapy, and DBS adjustment.
Plan:
  • Optimize chronic regimen (trihexyphenidyl, tetrabenazine, baclofen).
  • DBS programming adjusted.
  • Action plan for future illness: home rescue medications, low threshold for ER.
  • Renal recovery; follow creatinine.
  • Multidisciplinary clinic follow-up (movement disorders, rehab, neurosurgery).
Teaching points:
  • Status dystonicus is a neurologic emergency mimicking other hyperthermic syndromes.
  • Common triggers: illness, surgery, drug change, sleep deprivation.
  • Complications: rhabdomyolysis, AKI, respiratory failure.
  • Management: deep sedation, NMB if refractory, cooling, optimize anti-dystonic therapy.
  • Refractory: DBS, intrathecal baclofen, pallidotomy.
Case 30 — Pediatric Moyamoya Disease — Recurrent TIA MODERATE

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 7-year-old with neurofibromatosis type 1 presents with the third episode of transient right hemiparesis in 2 months, each lasting 20–60 minutes with full recovery. Previous workup: normal echocardiogram, normal coagulation panel. Recent episode occurred during a febrile illness with crying.
HR 110 | BP 100/65 | T 38.6°C | Neurologically intact between episodes

Stage 2 — Imaging

MRI: small punctate ischemic lesions in left frontal subcortical white matter. MRA: severe bilateral supraclinoid ICA stenosis with prominent collateral vessels at the base of the brain ('puff of smoke'). Catheter angiography confirms Suzuki stage III. Bilateral disease.
Why does hyperventilation worsen ischemia in moyamoya?
  • Hyperventilation → hypocapnia → cerebral vasoconstriction.
  • In moyamoya, collateral vessels are maximally dilated at baseline.
  • Vasoconstriction reduces flow through these critical collaterals.
  • Crying / fever / anxiety → hyperventilation → ischemic event.
  • Treatment: maintain normocapnia, normothermia, normovolemia.

Stage 3 — Management

Aspirin 3 mg/kg/day. Aggressive prevention: avoid hypotension, hypovolemia, hyperventilation; treat fever; treat anemia. Family education: keep child calm; avoid extreme cold/hot; manage fever promptly. Referral to pediatric neurosurgery for revascularization.

Stage 4 — Surgery

Bilateral encephalo-duro-arterio-synangiosis (EDAS) — indirect revascularization — performed in staged fashion (right side first, then left at 3 months). Perioperative goal: maintain normothermia, normocapnia, MAP at age norms, Hb > 11 g/dL. No further TIAs at 1-year follow-up. Repeat angiography shows neovascularization through EDAS sites.
Final Diagnosis: Pediatric moyamoya disease (in setting of NF1, Suzuki III) presenting with recurrent TIAs; treated with bilateral EDAS revascularization.
Plan:
  • Continued aspirin daily.
  • Annual MRI/MRA surveillance.
  • Family education: avoid triggers (hyperventilation, dehydration, fever, hot/cold).
