  • Schools informed; emergency action plan.
  • Stop OCP if started in adolescence.
  • Genetic counseling.
Teaching points:
  • Moyamoya: bilateral supraclinoid ICA stenosis with characteristic collaterals.
  • Associations: NF1, Down syndrome, sickle cell, post-radiation.
  • TIA in pediatric moyamoya often triggered by hyperventilation.
  • Surgical revascularization (EDAS or STA-MCA bypass) is the standard for symptomatic patients.
  • Perioperative care: avoid hypotension, hypovolemia, hyperventilation, anemia.
Case 31 — Acute Liver Failure with Intracranial Hypertension HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 15-year-old female presents 36 hours after intentional acetaminophen ingestion (estimated 25 g over 48 hours). On arrival: jaundice, AST 12,000, ALT 9,500, INR 5.2, lactate 4.5, ammonia 240 µmol/L, creatinine 2.5. Lethargic with confusion (West Haven grade II encephalopathy).
HR 110 | BP 95/55 | T 37.8°C | GCS 13 → 9 over 6 h | Lactate 4.5 mmol/L

Stage 2 — Progression

N-acetylcysteine initiated despite delayed presentation. Within 12 h: progression to grade IV encephalopathy. Intubated for airway protection (avoided benzodiazepines; used fentanyl + propofol short-term). ICP monitor placed: 28 mm Hg. Liver transplant evaluation activated. Listed status 1A for emergent transplant.

Stage 3 — ICP Management

Standard ICP measures: HOB 30°, 3% saline 5 mL/kg → Na target 150–155, normothermia (cooling blanket), normocapnia 35–38. Ammonia 280 µmol/L → CRRT initiated with high-flux dialyzer for ammonia removal. Lactulose enterally to bowel tolerance. Norepinephrine for MAP support; CPP target ≥ 60. Glucose support D10 maintenance. Coagulopathy not corrected prophylactically (INR is critical transplant criterion); FFP only when surgical procedures planned.
Why avoid prophylactic INR correction?
  • INR is a critical King's College Criteria component for liver transplant listing.
  • Correcting INR with FFP masks disease severity and may delay transplant prioritization.
  • Spontaneous bleeding risk in ALF is paradoxically lower than the INR suggests due to balanced coagulopathy.
  • Correct only for procedures or active bleeding.

Stage 4 — Transplant

Suitable donor identified at hour 36. Bridge to transplant achieved with ICP control (peak ICP 30, controlled with hyperosmolar boluses; CRRT continued; no clinical herniation signs). Successful orthotopic liver transplant on day 4. Post-transplant: rapid resolution of encephalopathy, ICP normalizes, weaned ventilation by day 7. Long-term outcome: full neurologic recovery.
Final Diagnosis: Acute liver failure from acetaminophen overdose with grade IV hepatic encephalopathy and intracranial hypertension; bridged to liver transplant.
Plan:
  • Liver transplant urgency activated based on King's criteria.
  • Acute ICP management: hyperosmolar therapy, CRRT for ammonia, supportive care.
  • Mental health follow-up — intentional ingestion.
  • Long-term immunosuppression and transplant follow-up.
  • Family counseling.
Teaching points:
  • ALF with grade III–IV encephalopathy and intracranial hypertension: high mortality without transplant.
  • Avoid benzodiazepines (prolonged action, worsen encephalopathy).
  • CRRT with high-flux dialyzer for ammonia > 150 µmol/L.
  • Do NOT prophylactically correct INR — it is a transplant prioritization marker.
  • Cerebral herniation is the most common cause of death.
  • NAC indicated even with delayed presentation in acetaminophen toxicity.
Case 32 — Severe Neonatal Hyperammonemia (OTC Deficiency) HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 12-day-old male neonate presents with poor feeding, vomiting × 24 h, then lethargy progressing to coma. Born at term, uncomplicated delivery. Mother had two prior male infants who died in neonatal period of "unknown cause."
HR 180 | BP 65/40 | T 36.5°C | RR 60 (tachypnea then apnea) | GCS 3 | Glucose 4.0 | Lactate 2.0 | Ammonia 1100 µmol/L | Respiratory alkalosis

Stage 2 — Recognition

Severe hyperammonemia in a male neonate with respiratory alkalosis (ammonia stimulates respiratory drive early), family history suggestive of X-linked disorder → urea cycle disorder, likely OTC deficiency. Glucose normal, lactate normal — rules out organic acidemia, mitochondrial. Urine sample sent: elevated orotic acid (confirms distal block — OTC or downstream).

Stage 3 — Emergency Treatment

Stop protein intake (NPO). Start D10 IV continuous + intralipid 2 g/kg/day. Ammonul (Na phenylacetate + Na benzoate) IV bolus 250 mg/kg over 90 min, then 250 mg/kg/day infusion. Arginine HCl 250 mg/kg IV bolus, then 250 mg/kg/day. Carnitine 100 mg/kg IV. Empiric biotin, hydroxocobalamin. Intubation for airway protection. Immediate hemodialysis (more effective than CRRT for rapid ammonia clearance).
Why hemodialysis over CRRT for severe hyperammonemia?
  • Ammonia clearance with hemodialysis is 4–6× faster than CRRT.
  • For ammonia > 500 µmol/L or rising, hemodialysis is preferred.
  • CRRT can be used after acute reduction for ongoing maintenance.
  • Plasma exchange is NOT effective for ammonia removal.

Stage 4 — Course

Hemodialysis × 4 h reduces ammonia from 1100 to 250 µmol/L. CRRT continued. Ammonia trending down on infusions over 48 h. Extubated day 4. Diagnosis confirmed: OTC deficiency on genetic testing. Long-term: protein-restricted formula with essential amino acids, oral nitrogen scavengers (glycerol phenylbutyrate), citrulline supplementation, frequent monitoring of ammonia and plasma amino acids. Family genetic counseling — X-linked inheritance, prior male sibling deaths likely from same disease, mother is obligate carrier.
Final Diagnosis: Ornithine transcarbamylase (OTC) deficiency presenting with severe neonatal hyperammonemia.
Plan:
  • Lifelong protein-restricted diet with essential amino acid supplementation.
  • Glycerol phenylbutyrate as chronic nitrogen scavenger.
  • Citrulline supplementation.
  • Avoid: valproate, steroids, protein loads, dehydration.
  • Emergency plan for illness (Sick Day protocol).
  • Liver transplant evaluation for severe phenotype.
  • Family genetic counseling — X-linked.
  • Neurodevelopmental follow-up (sequelae from hyperammonemia common).
Teaching points:
  • Severe neonatal hyperammonemia is a metabolic emergency.
  • Urea cycle disorders: respiratory alkalosis (ammonia stimulates breathing), low urea.
  • Orotic acid distinguishes OTC (elevated) from CPS1 (normal) and NAGS (normal).
  • Hemodialysis preferred over CRRT for rapid ammonia clearance.
  • Avoid valproate, steroids, protein.
  • X-linked OTC: counsel family; carrier testing for mother and sisters.
Case 33 — Ifosfamide Encephalopathy with Methylene Blue HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 12-year-old with Ewing sarcoma on chemotherapy day 2 of a high-dose ifosfamide cycle (15 g/m² total over 5 days) develops sudden confusion, visual hallucinations, agitation, and a generalized tonic-clonic seizure. Pre-treatment albumin 25 g/L (low), creatinine 1.8 (mild elevation), no other CNS-active medications.
HR 120 | BP 110/65 | T 37.4°C | GCS 11 post-ictal | Glucose normal | Electrolytes normal

Stage 2 — Recognition

Differential: chemotherapy-induced encephalopathy (ifosfamide, methotrexate), CNS metastasis, electrolyte derangement, hypoglycemia, drug toxicity, posterior reversible encephalopathy syndrome. MRI brain: normal (rules out PRES, metastasis). Albumin low + renal dysfunction + ifosfamide = classic risk profile for ifosfamide encephalopathy from chloroacetaldehyde metabolite toxicity.

Stage 3 — Management

Ifosfamide held immediately. IV methylene blue 1 mg/kg q4h (max 50 mg). Thiamine 100 mg IV, B-complex vitamins. Albumin replacement to normalize. Levetiracetam load 60 mg/kg for seizures. Hydration with IV fluids and renal protection. Supportive ICU care; cEEG monitoring.
Mechanism of methylene blue in ifosfamide toxicity?
  • Chloroacetaldehyde (toxic metabolite) inhibits mitochondrial respiratory chain.
  • Methylene blue acts as an alternative electron acceptor.
  • Restores oxidative phosphorylation.
  • Also inhibits monoamine oxidase (reduces neurotoxic metabolites).
  • Most clinically used pharmacologic intervention for ifosfamide encephalopathy.

Stage 4 — Recovery

Encephalopathy resolves over 48 h. No further seizures. Discharged on prophylactic oral methylene blue 50 mg q6h for the next chemotherapy cycle. Albumin corrected before next cycle. Subsequent cycles tolerated without recurrence.
Final Diagnosis: Ifosfamide encephalopathy precipitated by low albumin and mild renal dysfunction; treated with methylene blue, ifosfamide hold, supportive care.
Plan:
  • Prophylactic methylene blue for future cycles.
  • Albumin correction before each cycle.
  • Renal function optimization.
  • Levetiracetam through chemotherapy cycles (until established safe).
  • Document in chart for all future caregivers.
  • Family education on warning signs.
Teaching points:
  • Ifosfamide encephalopathy: ~15% incidence; risk factors include low albumin, renal dysfunction, prior cisplatin, high dose.
  • Methylene blue 1–2 mg/kg q4–6 h is the recognized therapy.
  • Prophylactic methylene blue + albumin correction reduces recurrence.
  • Discontinue ifosfamide; most resolve within 72 h.
  • Differential: PRES, metastasis, sepsis, opioid toxicity.
Case 34 — CAR-T ICANS Grade 3 Post-Tisagenlecleucel HARD

Stage 1 — Background

A 7-year-old with relapsed B-cell ALL received tisagenlecleucel CAR-T cell therapy 6 days ago. On day 3, developed CRS grade 2 with fever and hypotension, treated with tocilizumab. CRS resolving. On day 6: gradually develops confusion, expressive aphasia, then a generalized tonic-clonic seizure. ICE score declining: was 8 at morning round, now 2.
HR 130 | BP 110/65 | T 38.4°C | Fluctuating LOC, post-ictal | Ferritin 4500, CRP elevated, IL-6 elevated

Stage 2 — Grading

ASTCT ICANS grading: ICE 2 (severely decreased), rouses to tactile, seizure occurred (now resolved with intervention). Imaging: MRI shows mild bilateral thalamic and basal ganglia T2 hyperintensity consistent with ICANS. Classification: Grade 3 ICANS.
What is the cytokine pathway difference between CRS and ICANS?
  • CRS: systemic cytokine release primarily affecting hemodynamics; IL-6 elevated; responsive to tocilizumab.
  • ICANS: BBB disruption with cytokines and activated T cells entering CNS; cerebral endothelial activation, edema, neuroinflammation.
  • Tocilizumab does NOT cross BBB to treat established ICANS.
  • Corticosteroids (especially dexamethasone — high CNS penetration) are first-line for ICANS.

Stage 3 — Management

Methylprednisolone 1 g IV daily (high CNS penetration). Levetiracetam load 60 mg/kg + maintenance for seizure prophylaxis. cEEG continuous. Anakinra 100 mg SC daily (IL-1 antagonist) added. PICU level monitoring with frequent ICE reassessment. Tocilizumab continued for residual CRS only. Supportive care: airway monitoring, glucose control, electrolyte management.

Stage 4 — Course

Day 7 (24 h after starting methylprednisolone): ICE improved to 5. No further seizures. Day 9: ICE 8, near-baseline. Methylprednisolone tapered over 7 days. Levetiracetam continued for 4 weeks. Final follow-up: full neurologic recovery. ALL in remission at 6-month CAR-T evaluation.
Final Diagnosis: Grade 3 immune effector cell-associated neurotoxicity syndrome (ICANS) post-CAR-T therapy; resolved with high-dose corticosteroids and supportive care.
Plan:
  • Steroid taper over 7 days.
  • Levetiracetam 4 weeks.
  • cEEG until off sedation and seizure-free.
  • Neuropsychological assessment.
  • Hematology-oncology follow-up for disease surveillance.
  • Family education on ICANS recognition.
Teaching points:
  • ICANS: typically 3–10 days post-CAR-T; may co-occur or follow CRS.
  • ASTCT grading uses ICE score + clinical features (LOC, seizure, motor, ICP).
  • Dexamethasone preferred over methylprednisolone for CNS penetration (some protocols use methylprednisolone for severe).
  • Tocilizumab treats CRS but does NOT treat ICANS (poor BBB penetration).
  • Anakinra in refractory cases.
  • Most patients recover with prompt steroid therapy.
Case 35 — Pediatric AVM Hemorrhage with Sudden Intracranial Hypertension HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 13-year-old previously healthy boy collapses during basketball practice with sudden 'worst headache of life' followed by vomiting and brief LOC. On EMS arrival: GCS 10 (E3V3M4), right hemiparesis. Brought to ED in 15 minutes.
HR 110 | BP 165/95 | T 36.9°C | GCS 10 | Pupils 4 mm equal reactive | Right hemiparesis

Stage 2 — Imaging

CT: left parietal intracerebral hemorrhage with extension into subarachnoid space and mild midline shift (3 mm). CTA: 3 cm parietal AVM with single dominant arterial feeder from MCA branch and superficial venous drainage; small associated flow-related aneurysm on feeding artery. Spetzler-Martin grade 2.

Stage 3 — Stabilization

Intubation with rocuronium + etomidate (BP labile; ketamine alternative). HOB 30°. 3% saline 5 mL/kg → Na 152. BP target MAP 80–90 (avoid hyperension to prevent rebleed but maintain CPP). Norepinephrine titrated as needed. Levetiracetam load 60 mg/kg + maintenance. cEEG continuous. Coagulopathy ruled out (normal INR, platelets). Neurosurgery consulted.

Stage 4 — Definitive Treatment

Day 1: catheter angiography confirms AVM and aneurysm. Day 3: staged endovascular embolization of aneurysm component (reduces rebleed risk). Day 14: microsurgical resection of AVM. Final imaging: complete obliteration. Day 21: discharged to rehabilitation with mild residual right hemiparesis. 6-month follow-up: ambulating independently; school re-integration.
Final Diagnosis: Pediatric AVM rupture (Spetzler-Martin grade 2) with intracerebral hemorrhage; staged embolization of associated aneurysm and microsurgical resection.
Plan:
  • Definitive surgical resection of AVM (Spetzler-Martin grade 2 favorable for surgery).
  • Endovascular treatment of associated aneurysm to prevent rebleed.
  • Levetiracetam continued 6–12 months post-resection.
  • Rehabilitation for hemiparesis.
  • Surveillance angiography at 1 year to confirm obliteration.
  • Family counseling.
Teaching points:
  • Spetzler-Martin grade guides treatment: low grade → surgery; high grade → multimodal/radiosurgery.
  • Associated aneurysm requires urgent attention to prevent rebleed.
  • BP management: avoid hypertension (rebleed) but maintain CPP.
  • Seizure prophylaxis: levetiracetam.
  • Pediatric AVM has higher annual hemorrhage rate than adult (~3%/year).
  • Vasospasm less prominent than aneurysmal SAH; routine nimodipine debated.
Case 36 — Cryptococcal Meningitis in HIV-Infected Adolescent MODERATE

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 16-year-old vertically HIV-infected adolescent (CD4 80, on ART for 2 years with poor adherence) presents with 2 weeks of progressive headache, fever, nausea, and now altered mental status. Family reports gradual cognitive decline.
HR 105 | BP 110/65 | T 38.7°C | GCS 12 | Mild meningismus

Stage 2 — LP and Diagnosis

LP after CT (no focal lesion or shift): opening pressure 35 cmH₂O (markedly elevated), WBC 30 (lymphocytic, 85%), protein 0.7 g/L, glucose 1.5 mmol/L (low). Cryptococcal antigen positive at 1:1024 titer. India ink positive. Fungal culture pending. CSF Gram stain negative. Blood cryptococcal antigen also positive.

Stage 3 — Treatment

Induction: liposomal amphotericin B 5 mg/kg/day + flucytosine 100 mg/kg/day for 2 weeks. Monitor: renal function (amphotericin nephrotoxicity), electrolytes (K, Mg), CBC (flucytosine cytopenia), drug levels. Therapeutic LPs daily for first week (drain to opening pressure < 20 cmH₂O) — main predictor of survival. ART optimized but deferred increase until 4–6 weeks (IRIS risk). HIV-specific care, adherence counseling.
Why is ICP management critical in cryptococcal meningitis?
  • Elevated ICP is the major cause of mortality in cryptococcal meningitis.
  • Cryptococcal antigen and fungal cells in arachnoid villi impair CSF reabsorption.
  • Repeated LPs (or lumbar drain) reduce ICP and improve survival.
  • Target opening pressure < 20 cmH₂O; LP every 1–2 days as needed.
  • Mannitol and steroids are NOT effective for cryptococcal ICP.

Stage 4 — Consolidation and Maintenance

Week 3: consolidation with fluconazole 400 mg/day for 8 weeks. CSF re-evaluated at week 2 (cultures negative). Maintenance: fluconazole 200 mg/day until CD4 > 100 for 6 months on ART with viral suppression. ART intensification started at week 5 (avoid early IRIS); monitored for IRIS development.
Final Diagnosis: Cryptococcal meningitis in advanced HIV/AIDS; treated with amphotericin + flucytosine induction, therapeutic LPs for ICP, then fluconazole consolidation and maintenance.
Plan:
  • Antifungal regimen as detailed.
  • HIV care: ART optimization, adherence support.
  • Family counseling, mental health support.
  • Long-term: continued fluconazole maintenance until immune reconstitution.
  • Monitor for IRIS, opportunistic infections.
  • School re-integration; transition planning for adult HIV care.
Teaching points:
  • Cryptococcal meningitis: subacute meningitis in immunocompromised; CSF often paucicellular but cryptococcal antigen and India ink positive.
  • Induction: amphotericin B (liposomal preferred) + flucytosine × 2 weeks.
  • Consolidation: fluconazole × 8 weeks.
  • Maintenance: fluconazole until immune reconstitution.
  • ICP management with serial LPs is the major modifiable mortality factor.
  • ART timing: defer 4–6 weeks to avoid IRIS.
Case 37 — Cervical Carotid Dissection After Chiropractic Manipulation HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 14-year-old presents with sudden right hemiparesis and aphasia 24 hours after a chiropractic neck manipulation. He had 6 hours of preceding severe left-sided neck pain and Horner syndrome (mild ptosis, miosis). On arrival: GCS 14, right hemiparesis, expressive aphasia.
HR 90 | BP 130/80 | T 36.8°C | Pupils L 3 mm sluggish, R 4 mm reactive | Right hemiparesis (face, arm > leg) | Anosmia

Stage 2 — Imaging

CT/CTA neck and brain: long tapered narrowing of left cervical ICA with flame-shaped distal pseudo-occlusion. Brain: small left MCA infarct in frontal subcortical white matter. T1 fat-sat MRI cervical: crescent of T1 hyperintense intramural hematoma (pathognomonic for dissection).
Why is Horner syndrome a clue?
  • Sympathetic fibers travel along the carotid artery; dissection disrupts these fibers.
  • Postganglionic Horner syndrome (mild ptosis + miosis, intact sweating of forehead).
  • Often presents BEFORE neurologic deficit — recognition allows earlier intervention.
  • Triad: neck pain + Horner + ipsilateral cerebral ischemic symptoms = carotid dissection until proven otherwise.

Stage 3 — Management

Aspirin 5 mg/kg/day (1–5 mg/kg/day acceptable range). BP target: avoid hypertension (worsens dissection) and hypotension (cerebral hypoperfusion). Speech/PT/OT begin early. Connective tissue disease screen sent (Marfan habitus features prompted screen).

Stage 4 — Follow-up

3-month MRA: progressive recanalization. 6 months: complete resolution; aspirin continued 3 more months then stopped. Genetic testing: no Marfan or Ehlers-Danlos identified. Family counseling about lifelong avoidance of chiropractic neck manipulation. Returns to non-contact sports.
Final Diagnosis: Cervical internal carotid artery dissection (post-chiropractic manipulation) with small left MCA infarct.
Plan:
  • Aspirin 3–6 months total.
  • Serial MRA at 3 and 6 months.
  • Avoid chiropractic neck manipulation lifelong.
  • Restrict contact sports temporarily.
  • Rehabilitation for residual deficit.
  • Counseling about future pregnancy / connective tissue disease screen.
Teaching points:
  • Neck pain + Horner + cerebral ischemia = carotid dissection.
  • T1 fat-sat MRI shows pathognomonic intramural hematoma.
  • Antiplatelet vs anticoagulation comparable per CADISS; aspirin first-line in pediatric.
  • Connective tissue disease screen in spontaneous dissection.
Case 38 — Primary Angiitis of the CNS (PACNS) of Childhood HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 14-year-old previously healthy boy presents with 6 weeks of progressive subacute encephalopathy: cognitive decline, fluctuating multifocal neurologic deficits, headaches, and 2 episodes of right-sided weakness lasting hours. Recent admission for 'TIA' work-up was non-diagnostic. Now presents with seizure.
HR 95 | BP 120/75 | T 37.4°C | GCS 12 | Multifocal subtle deficits (mild right pronator drift, decreased visual attention) | ESR 65, CRP elevated

Stage 2 — Investigations

MRI: multifocal cortical and subcortical lesions of various ages bilaterally; some with gadolinium enhancement. CSF: WBC 35 (lymph), protein 1.2 g/L (elevated), glucose normal, oligoclonal bands positive. Infectious workup negative. Autoimmune panel: ANA negative, ANCA negative, anti-NMDA negative. Catheter angiography: multifocal beading and segmental narrowing of distal MCA, ACA, and PCA branches bilaterally — 'string of beads' pattern.
Why is brain biopsy sometimes needed in PACNS?
  • Small-vessel PACNS may have normal angiography but abnormal histology.
  • Biopsy distinguishes from infectious vasculitis, lymphoma, and other mimics.
  • Reserved for diagnostic uncertainty or atypical features.
  • Biopsy site selected based on imaging (most active lesion).
  • Yield 70–80% in carefully selected cases.

Stage 3 — Treatment Induction

High-dose IV methylprednisolone 30 mg/kg/day × 5 days followed by oral prednisone taper. Cyclophosphamide 500 mg/m² IV monthly added for induction (or rituximab as alternative). Levetiracetam for seizures.

Stage 4 — Course and Maintenance

After 6 months induction: clinical and imaging improvement. Switched to maintenance mycophenolate mofetil. Total immunosuppression duration planned: 18–24 months. Sequential MRA at 6 and 12 months: improvement. Cognitive remediation ongoing. Multidisciplinary care.
Final Diagnosis: Medium-large vessel primary angiitis of the CNS (PACNS) of childhood, angiographically diagnosed.
Plan:
  • Induction: high-dose steroids + cyclophosphamide (or rituximab) × 6 months.
  • Maintenance: mycophenolate or azathioprine for 12–18 months.
  • Total immunosuppression 18–24 months.
  • Serial MRA every 6 months.
  • Antiepileptic continuation.
  • Aspirin antiplatelet adjunct.
  • Cognitive remediation.
  • Long-term follow-up; relapse rate ~25% without maintenance.
Teaching points:
  • PACNS: multifocal progressive arteriopathy without systemic features.
  • Medium-large vessel: 'string of beads' on angiography.
  • Small vessel: angiogram-negative; biopsy required.
  • Treatment: induction with steroids + cyclophosphamide/rituximab; maintenance with MMF/azathioprine.
  • Total course 18–24 months minimum.
Case 39 — Silent Cerebral Infarct in Sickle Cell Disease MODERATE

Stage 1 — Screening

A 12-year-old girl with HbSS sickle cell disease undergoes annual MRI brain screening per institutional protocol. She has no acute neurologic complaints but recent report cards show declining grades. Hemoglobin baseline 7.8 g/dL, HbS 80%. TCD 6 months ago time-averaged mean velocity 165 cm/s (conditional).

Stage 2 — Findings

MRI: multiple punctate T2 hyperintense lesions in bilateral frontal and parietal subcortical white matter, predominantly in watershed zones. No acute findings. Neuropsychology testing: mild executive function and processing speed deficits below age expectations.
Why screen and what is the significance?
  • Silent cerebral infarcts in HbSS occur in 20–30% of children by age 14.
  • Associated with cognitive impairment and 14× higher risk of overt stroke.
  • SIT trial (Silent Infarct Transfusion, DeBaun 2014): chronic transfusion reduces progression by ~58%.
  • Screening MRI between 5–18 years; intervals depend on prior findings and risk.
  • Annual TCD for primary stroke risk (STOP/STOP2).

Stage 3 — Treatment Decision

After family discussion, chronic exchange transfusion initiated targeting HbS < 30%, total Hb 10 g/dL. Iron chelation with deferasirox to manage iron overload. Hydroxyurea continued (synergistic). TCD monitored quarterly; remains stable.

Stage 4 — Long-term

5-year follow-up: no new silent infarcts; no overt stroke. Cognitive function stabilized with educational accommodations. Bone marrow transplant evaluation initiated for definitive cure (matched sibling donor available). Family meeting about transplant risks vs continuing chronic transfusion.
Final Diagnosis: Silent cerebral infarcts in HbSS sickle cell disease with cognitive impact; initiated on chronic transfusion per SIT trial evidence.
Plan:
  • Chronic transfusion targeting HbS < 30%.
  • Iron chelation to prevent overload.
  • Hydroxyurea continued.
  • Quarterly TCD; annual MRI.
  • Neuropsychology + educational accommodations.
  • BMT evaluation if matched donor.
  • Family education and shared decision-making.
Teaching points:
  • Silent infarcts cause cognitive impact and predict overt stroke.
  • SIT trial: chronic transfusion reduces progression.
  • TWiTCH allows transition to hydroxyurea after stability.
  • HSCT for definitive cure in selected patients.
  • Multidisciplinary care: hematology, neurology, neuropsychology, education.
Case 40 — Febrile Infection-Related Epilepsy Syndrome (FIRES) — Anakinra Escalation HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 9-year-old previously healthy girl presents 5 days after a viral URI with sudden onset of generalized seizures, then refractory status epilepticus. Within 24 hours: multiple AEDs failed (levetiracetam, fosphenytoin, valproate). Intubated for status. Started on midazolam infusion at 0.2 mg/kg/h, escalated to 1.0 mg/kg/h with persistent electrographic seizures on cEEG.

Stage 2 — Day 3 — Refractory

Pentobarbital coma added with burst-suppression target. Ketamine infusion 1 mg/kg/h added. Continued electrographic seizures despite multidrug therapy. Workup negative: HSV PCR negative, autoimmune encephalitis panel negative, anti-NMDA negative, metabolic studies normal. MRI: subtle bilateral hippocampal T2 hyperintensity. CSF: WBC 15 (lymph), protein 0.4 g/L.

Stage 3 — Day 5 — FIRES Recognition

Diagnosis: FIRES (Febrile Infection-Related Epilepsy Syndrome) — previously healthy child with febrile illness preceding refractory SE. Ketogenic diet initiated via NG (enteral formula). High-dose IV methylprednisolone 30 mg/kg/day × 5 days started.
Why is FIRES considered a cytokine-driven syndrome?
  • Mounting evidence that elevated IL-1, IL-6, and other inflammatory cytokines drive seizures.
  • Anakinra (IL-1 receptor antagonist) increasingly first-line escalation therapy.
  • Tocilizumab (IL-6 antagonist) emerging.
  • Distinguishes from conventional refractory SE pathophysiology.
  • Cytokine-directed therapy can dramatically improve outcomes when initiated early.

Stage 4 — Day 7 — Anakinra and Recovery

Anakinra 5 mg/kg/day SC initiated. IVIG 2 g/kg over 5 days completed. By day 14: seizures controlled on cEEG; sedation weaning slowly. By day 21: extubated; cognitive recovery slow over weeks. Long-term: residual cognitive impairment, refractory epilepsy on multiple AEDs but markedly improved. Continued ketogenic diet.
Final Diagnosis: FIRES; cytokine-driven hyperinflammatory epileptic encephalopathy; responded to ketogenic diet and anakinra-based immunotherapy.
Plan:
  • Ketogenic diet long-term.
  • Multidrug AED therapy.
  • Anakinra continued for several months, then taper.
  • Neuropsychology, special education.
  • Family counseling about lifelong epilepsy and cognitive disability risk.
  • Vagal nerve stimulator considered.
  • Multidisciplinary follow-up (neurology, neurosurgery, rehab, psychology).
Teaching points:
  • FIRES: hyperinflammatory cytokine-driven encephalopathy.
  • Ketogenic diet highly effective (50–70% response).
  • Anakinra emerging as transformative therapy.
  • Most survivors have refractory epilepsy and cognitive sequelae.
  • Distinguish from autoimmune encephalitis and traditional super-refractory SE.
Case 41 — Leigh Syndrome Crisis with Lactic Acidosis HARD

Stage 1 — Background

A 4-year-old with previously diagnosed Leigh syndrome (SURF1 mutation) presents with vomiting, lethargy, and worsening neurologic status during an intercurrent gastroenteritis. Baseline: developmental delay, dystonia, mild ataxia. Now: decreased LOC, brisk reflexes.
HR 130 | BP 90/55 | T 38.6°C | GCS 11 | Lactate 7.5 mmol/L | pH 7.18 | HCO3 12 | Anion gap 22

Stage 2 — Investigations

MRI: progression of bilateral basal ganglia and brainstem T2 hyperintensity from baseline (substantia nigra, periaqueductal gray more pronounced). MR spectroscopy: prominent lactate peak. ABG: severe lactic acidosis. Blood cultures and CSF studies pending. Glucose 5.5 mmol/L. Normal electrolytes apart from acidosis.
Why is intercurrent illness a crisis precipitant?
  • Increased metabolic demand stresses already deficient mitochondrial respiratory chain.
  • Fever increases oxygen consumption (~10% per 1°C).
  • Lactic acidosis worsens; energy failure progresses.
  • Catabolic state mobilizes amino acids and fatty acids, further straining mitochondria.
  • Goal: aggressive supportive care, treat infection, optimize substrate delivery.

Stage 3 — Acute Management

PICU admission. IV hydration with D10 NS to provide carbohydrate substrate (avoid glucose-free fluids). Empiric ceftriaxone for fever workup. Aggressive antipyresis (acetaminophen; cooling blanket). Mitochondrial cocktail continued (CoQ10, riboflavin, thiamine, L-carnitine). Levetiracetam for seizure prophylaxis (avoid valproate). Family meeting to clarify goals of care.

Stage 4 — Course

Infection identified: rotavirus gastroenteritis. With supportive care and dehydration correction, lactate down to 3.5 mmol/L by 48 h. Mental status returns to baseline. Discharged with sick day plan: low threshold for ER, aggressive antipyretics, dextrose-containing fluids, NPO if vomiting beyond 6 h. Family counseling about progressive disease and goals of care (palliative care involved).
Final Diagnosis: Acute decompensation in known Leigh syndrome (SURF1 mutation) precipitated by viral gastroenteritis; recovered with supportive care.
Plan:
  • Sick day plan: aggressive antipyretics, dextrose-containing fluids, low ER threshold.
  • Mitochondrial cocktail continued.
  • Avoid mitotoxic drugs (valproate, statins, metformin, linezolid).
  • Levetiracetam for seizure control.
  • Multidisciplinary care: neurology, palliative care, rehab.
  • Goals of care discussion ongoing — life-limiting illness.
  • Genetic counseling and family support.
Teaching points:
  • Leigh syndrome: characteristic imaging (basal ganglia and brainstem T2 hyperintensity).
  • Lactic acidosis common; not always present.
  • Crisis precipitants: illness, fever, anesthesia, surgery.
  • Supportive care: glucose substrate, antipyresis, infection treatment.
  • Avoid mitotoxic drugs.
  • Life-limiting illness; engage palliative care early.
Case 42 — MCAD Deficiency Presenting as 'Reye-Like' Syndrome HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 3-year-old previously healthy boy presents with vomiting and lethargy progressing to coma after 36 hours of viral gastroenteritis with poor oral intake. No aspirin exposure.
HR 140 | BP 80/45 | T 37.6°C | GCS 7 | Glucose 1.8 mmol/L (severe hypoglycemia) | Ammonia 180 µmol/L | AST 950, ALT 750, INR 1.6 | Lactate 4 | Urine ketones NEGATIVE despite hypoglycemia

Stage 2 — Recognition

Hypoglycemia with INAPPROPRIATELY LOW ketones (hypoketotic hypoglycemia) is the key clue. Suggests fatty acid oxidation defect — most commonly MCAD (medium-chain acyl-CoA dehydrogenase deficiency). Initial 'Reye-like' picture with encephalopathy, hepatic dysfunction, and hyperammonemia following viral illness. Aspirin-related Reye is now rare; MCAD is a leading mimic.
Why hypoketotic hypoglycemia?
  • Fatty acid β-oxidation generates ketones during fasting.
  • MCAD deficiency: medium-chain fatty acids cannot be oxidized → no ketone production.
  • Hypoglycemia + low/absent ketones during fasting/illness is highly suggestive.
  • Confirmation: plasma acylcarnitine profile (elevated C8, C10:1) and ACADM gene sequencing.
  • Newborn screening detects most cases — this child predated comprehensive screening in his birth state.

Stage 3 — Acute Management

D10 IV bolus 5 mL/kg, then D10 NS continuous infusion to maintain glucose 5.5–8 mmol/L. Supplemental carnitine 100 mg/kg IV. Plasma acylcarnitines, urine organic acids, plasma amino acids sent. Ammonia management with lactulose. ICP monitoring not invasive but clinical management. ICU monitoring. Avoid fatty acid administration (NO intralipid).

Stage 4 — Course and Diagnosis

Within 6 hours of D10 infusion: mental status improves. Glucose stabilizes. By 48 hours: normal mental status, AST/ALT trending down, ammonia normalized. Plasma acylcarnitine profile: elevated C8 and C10:1 → MCAD deficiency confirmed. ACADM sequencing pending. Sick day protocol education. Carnitine supplementation. Newborn screening initiated for siblings.
Final Diagnosis: MCAD (medium-chain acyl-CoA dehydrogenase) deficiency presenting as Reye-like syndrome with hypoketotic hypoglycemia.
Plan:
  • Lifelong avoidance of prolonged fasting (no > 8–12 h between meals).
  • Carbohydrate-rich diet, frequent feeds, cornstarch at bedtime.
  • Carnitine supplementation.
  • Sick day plan: D10 IV at home or ER; never withhold dextrose during illness.
  • Avoid MCT oil and fasting tests.
  • Sibling screening; genetic counseling.
  • Genetics and metabolic medicine follow-up.
  • MedicAlert bracelet.
Teaching points:
  • 'Reye-like' presentation: always work up for fatty acid oxidation defects.
  • Hypoketotic hypoglycemia is the hallmark.
  • MCAD is the most common fatty acid oxidation defect.
  • D10 dextrose is the cornerstone of acute and ongoing prevention.
  • Newborn screening catches most cases — assess local screening panel.
Case 43 — Methotrexate Stroke-Like Syndrome HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 10-year-old with ALL on consolidation chemotherapy receives the third intrathecal methotrexate dose at week 6. Eight hours after the dose, the child develops acute confusion, expressive aphasia, and right-sided weakness. No fever; ANC adequate; no recent infection.
HR 105 | BP 110/70 | T 36.9°C | GCS 12 | Right hemiparesis (face, arm > leg) | Expressive aphasia

Stage 2 — Imaging

CT: unremarkable. MRI: bilateral T2 hyperintensity with DWI restriction in centrum semiovale and splenium of corpus callosum — characteristic pattern. No infarct in vascular distribution. No hemorrhage. CSF (delayed): mild lymphocytic pleocytosis, MTX level still measurable.
Why is this pattern recognizable as methotrexate-induced?
  • Bilateral, symmetric, central white matter (centrum semiovale) involvement.
  • Splenium of corpus callosum classically affected.
  • Does NOT respect vascular territories — distinguishes from stroke.
  • Timing: hours to days after MTX administration.
  • DWI restriction (cytotoxic edema) often resolves over days to weeks.
  • Most cases resolve clinically; persistent deficits possible.

Stage 3 — Treatment

Methotrexate held. High-dose leucovorin (folinic acid) rescue 100 mg IV q6h. Dextromethorphan 0.5 mg/kg PO q6h and aminophylline 2.5 mg/kg IV q6h added (purported homocysteine and adenosine modulation; case-series evidence). Levetiracetam 60 mg/kg load + maintenance for seizure prophylaxis. Supportive ICU care.

Stage 4 — Recovery

By day 3: aphasia improving. By day 7: motor function near-normal. Day 14: completely resolved clinically; repeat MRI shows resolution of DWI changes. Discussed with oncology — proceed with continued chemotherapy with caution. Future intrathecal MTX deferred; switched to alternative regimen. No recurrence over 6-month follow-up. ALL in remission.
Final Diagnosis: Acute methotrexate-induced leukoencephalopathy ('stroke-like syndrome'); resolved with high-dose leucovorin, dextromethorphan, aminophylline, supportive care.
Plan:
  • Hold intrathecal methotrexate; consult oncology for chemotherapy modifications.
  • Leucovorin rescue 100 mg IV q6h × 48 h.
  • Levetiracetam through next chemotherapy cycles.
  • Document in chart for future caregivers.
  • Neuropsychology evaluation at 6 months.
  • Surveillance MRI if symptoms recur.
  • Long-term monitoring for cognitive sequelae.
Teaching points:
  • MTX-related neurotoxicity: characteristic bilateral centrum semiovale and splenium DWI restriction.
  • Does not respect vascular territories.
  • Treatment: hold MTX, leucovorin rescue, dextromethorphan/aminophylline adjuncts.
  • Most resolve in days to weeks.
  • Glucarpidase for severe high-dose IV MTX with renal failure.
  • Chronic leukoencephalopathy is a late sequela of cumulative MTX exposure.
Case 44 — Central Pontine Myelinolysis from Rapid Sodium Correction HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 13-year-old presents with severe hyponatremia (Na 105 mmol/L) from primary polydipsia in setting of schizophrenia. Acutely confused. PICU admission. Initial management: hypertonic saline 3% bolus 3 mL/kg × 2, raising Na to 115 over 4 hours. Then continued correction without sufficient caution: Na 130 by 24 hours (rise 25 mmol/L in 24 h).
HR 90 | BP 115/72 | T 36.7°C | Initial GCS 11 → improved with Na rise to 14 by 24 h

Stage 2 — Delayed Deterioration

Day 5: progressive quadriparesis (weakness with hyperreflexia and spasticity), dysarthria, dysphagia, decreased LOC. MRI: symmetric T2 hyperintensity in central pons with restricted diffusion. Extra-pontine involvement of basal ganglia and thalamus also seen. Diagnosis: osmotic demyelination syndrome (central pontine myelinolysis with extra-pontine extension).
Why the delayed onset (typically 2–7 days)?
  • Demyelination occurs after rapid osmotic shifts but takes days to manifest clinically.
  • Oligodendrocyte injury → progressive demyelination.
  • Histologically detectable damage may precede clinical symptoms.
  • This delayed timing distinguishes it from acute stroke or metabolic encephalopathy.

Stage 3 — Re-lowering Attempt

Within 24 h of recognition (still within 48 h of overcorrection): trial of re-lowering Na with DDAVP and D5W to drop Na by 10 mmol/L over 24 h (Na 130 → 120). Controversial efficacy; some evidence of improved outcome in animal models and case reports. Continued supportive care, nutrition (high risk of malnutrition), thiamine and electrolyte replacement.

Stage 4 — Course

Despite re-lowering, residual quadriparesis and dysarthria persist. Inpatient rehabilitation for 6 months. Partial recovery: ambulating with assistance at 12 months. Speech improved but dysarthria persists. Cognitive function recovered. Long-term: psychiatric management for primary polydipsia (CBT, fluid restriction); preventive sodium monitoring. Family counseling about prevention of recurrence (fluid intake, psychiatric care).
Final Diagnosis: Osmotic demyelination syndrome (central pontine myelinolysis with extra-pontine involvement) from rapid sodium correction.
Plan:
  • Inpatient and outpatient rehabilitation (PT, OT, speech therapy).
  • Psychiatric management for primary polydipsia.
  • Fluid intake monitoring and behavioral therapy.
  • Periodic Na monitoring.
  • Family education on prevention.
  • Annual follow-up.
Teaching points:
  • Sodium correction rules: < 8–10 mmol/L/24 h; < 6 in high-risk patients.
  • Chronic hyponatremia (> 48 h) carries highest ODS risk.
  • Delayed onset 2–7 days after rapid correction.
  • Pontine pattern: locked-in syndrome possible.
  • Extra-pontine sites: basal ganglia, thalamus, cerebellum.
  • Re-lowering Na within 24–48 h of overcorrection: controversial efficacy but considered.
  • Prevention is paramount.
Case 45 — Pediatric Post-Arrest Multimodal Prognostication HARD

Stage 1 — Background

A 6-year-old experienced witnessed in-hospital cardiac arrest after cardiac surgery; downtime 18 minutes. Now post-arrest day 5. TTM 36.8°C × 48 h completed; rewarmed; normothermic since. Off sedation × 36 h. Pupils 4 mm, sluggishly reactive bilaterally; no spontaneous movements; motor response to noxious is extensor posturing.

Stage 2 — Multimodal Data Synthesis

ModalityFindingInterpretation
Clinical examPupils sluggish, no spontaneous movements, extensor posturingSeverely depressed; off sedation 36 h
Continuous EEGBurst-suppression with non-reactive background; intermittent generalized periodic dischargesSeverely abnormal
SSEPs at 72 hBilaterally absent N20 cortical responsesHighly specific for poor outcome (adults; pediatric data supportive)
MRI brain (day 5)Extensive cortical and basal ganglia DWI restriction; thalami also involvedSevere global injury pattern
NSE (if available)Markedly elevatedSupportive of severe injury (pediatric thresholds less established)
Why does Topjian AHA statement emphasize multimodal integration?
  • No single modality is reliably predictive of poor outcome.
  • Convergence of multiple unfavorable findings increases certainty.
  • Confounders (sedation, hypothermia, metabolic) can affect individual modalities.
  • Multimodal data over time (72 h to 7 days) is most reliable.
  • Avoid early prognostication; engage palliative care and family throughout.

Stage 3 — Family Meeting

Multidisciplinary meeting: PICU, neurology, palliative care, social work, chaplaincy. Honest communication: 'The data strongly suggests severe brain injury with very limited likelihood of meaningful neurologic recovery. Even with maximal support, the predicted long-term outcome is severe disability with profound dependence.' Family acknowledges grief; questions about timeline; explores goals of care.

Stage 4 — Outcome

After 48 h of family reflection and revisited meetings with extended family present, decision for compassionate withdrawal of life-sustaining treatment with palliative care management. Patient passes peacefully on day 8. Bereavement support continues for family.
Final Diagnosis: Severe pediatric post-arrest hypoxic-ischemic encephalopathy with multimodal evidence of poor prognosis; family elected compassionate withdrawal.
Process:
  • Multimodal prognostication integrated over time (≥ 72 h off sedation).
  • No single modality used in isolation.
  • Family meeting with multidisciplinary team.
  • Honest communication acknowledging uncertainty.
  • Engagement with family values and goals of care.
  • Palliative care involvement throughout.
  • Bereavement support continued.
Teaching points:
  • Topjian AHA pediatric post-arrest statement: multimodal integration ≥ 72 h off sedation.
  • Modalities: exam, cEEG, SSEPs, MRI, biomarkers.
  • Convergence of unfavorable findings increases prognostic certainty.
  • Confounders must be excluded.
  • Palliative care from early in trajectory.
  • Family-centered communication with revisited discussions.
Case 46 — Aneurysmal SAH with Delayed Vasospasm HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 17-year-old presents with sudden 'worst headache of life' followed by transient LOC. CT: subarachnoid hemorrhage in basal cisterns and left Sylvian fissure. CTA: 8 mm left MCA bifurcation aneurysm. Day 1: aneurysm clipped successfully. Day 2: extubated; neurologically intact apart from mild headache.
HR 95 | BP 130/80 | T 37.0°C | GCS 15 | No focal deficits

Stage 2 — Day 7 — Deterioration

Day 7 post-bleed: new onset right-sided weakness and confusion. TCD: left MCA mean velocity 220 cm/s (was 90 on day 2). Lindegaard ratio 4.5 (suggesting vasospasm vs hyperemia). CT perfusion: penumbra in left MCA territory. Diagnosis: symptomatic vasospasm with delayed cerebral ischemia.
Why does vasospasm peak at days 4–14?
  • Blood breakdown products (oxyhemoglobin) and inflammatory cascade peak in this window.
  • Endothelin-1 and nitric oxide imbalance leads to vasoconstriction.
  • Earlier days mainly rebleed risk; later days cortical spreading depolarizations contribute.
  • Up to 30% of aneurysmal SAH develops symptomatic vasospasm.
  • Pediatric AVM SAH less commonly causes vasospasm than aneurysmal.

Stage 3 — Acute Management

Maintained euvolemic (not triple-H — abandoned for harm). Induced hypertension with norepinephrine to MAP 100 (~20% above baseline). Nimodipine 60 mg PO q4h continued (started prophylactically at admission). Hemoglobin maintained 10 g/dL with transfusion. Normothermia, normoglycemia. Improvement initial but partial; emergent endovascular intervention pursued.

Stage 4 — Endovascular Rescue

Catheter angiography: severe MCA vasospasm. Intra-arterial nicardipine bolus and balloon angioplasty of left MCA. Vasospasm resolved acutely. By 12 h post-intervention: neurologic recovery to near baseline. Discharged day 14 with mild residual right-sided weakness. Follow-up imaging at 3 months: full recovery.
Final Diagnosis: Aneurysmal SAH (left MCA bifurcation, clipped) complicated by delayed cerebral ischemia from vasospasm; rescued with induced hypertension and endovascular nicardipine + angioplasty.
Plan:
  • Continued nimodipine 21 days total.
  • Aspirin antiplatelet adjunct.
  • Antiepileptic continued through hemorrhage resolution.
  • Rehabilitation for residual deficit.
  • Vascular imaging surveillance.
  • Lifestyle counseling.
Teaching points:
  • SAH vasospasm peaks days 4–14.
  • TCD monitoring (MCA mean velocity > 120, Lindegaard ratio > 3 = spasm vs hyperemia).
  • Management: euvolemia + induced hypertension (NOT triple-H), nimodipine, normothermia.
  • Endovascular rescue: intra-arterial vasodilators (nicardipine, verapamil), balloon angioplasty.
  • Magnesium and statins NOT effective (CONSCIOUS trials negative).
  • Avoid hypotension, hypovolemia, fever during vasospasm window.
Case 47 — Bickerstaff Brainstem Encephalitis MODERATE

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 11-year-old presents with 2 weeks of progressive bilateral facial weakness, dysphagia, dysarthria, ophthalmoplegia, ataxia, and now decreased level of consciousness. Preceded by gastroenteritis (likely Campylobacter jejuni based on history) 3 weeks ago. Hyperreflexia (4+) throughout.
HR 85 | BP 115/70 | T 37.4°C | GCS 11 | Ophthalmoplegia: complete external | Facial diplegia | Hyperreflexia 4+ | Sensory exam intact

Stage 2 — Investigations

CSF: WBC 25 (lymphocytic), protein 1.4 g/L (elevated), glucose normal. MRI brain and spine: subtle T2 hyperintensity in midbrain and pons; no enhancing lesions. EEG: generalized slowing. NCS: normal. Anti-GQ1b antibody positive at high titer. AQP4-IgG, anti-NMDA negative.
How does Bickerstaff differ from MFS and classical GBS?
  • Miller Fisher syndrome: ophthalmoplegia + ataxia + areflexia, NO encephalopathy.
  • Bickerstaff brainstem encephalitis: same plus encephalopathy and hyperreflexia.
  • Classical GBS: ascending paralysis, areflexia, no encephalopathy.
  • Anti-GQ1b spectrum: MFS, BBE, GBS variants; overlap common.
  • Bickerstaff has central involvement (brainstem encephalitis with MRI changes possible).

Stage 3 — Treatment

IVIG 0.4 g/kg/day × 5 days (2 g/kg total). Airway protection — intubated for declining respiratory status with bulbar dysfunction. Autonomic monitoring; mild dysautonomia (BP swings) managed conservatively. Supportive care with feeding tube, PT/OT.

Stage 4 — Course

By day 5: encephalopathy improving. By day 10: ophthalmoplegia partially resolved. Extubated day 12. By month 3: full neurologic recovery with no residual deficit. Long-term follow-up: no recurrence at 1 year.
Final Diagnosis: Bickerstaff brainstem encephalitis (anti-GQ1b antibody positive), post-Campylobacter; full recovery with IVIG.
Plan:
  • IVIG 2 g/kg total course.
  • Supportive ICU care.
  • Airway monitoring with bulbar dysfunction.
  • Autonomic monitoring.
  • PT/OT/speech therapy.
  • Outpatient neurology follow-up for late recurrence.
Teaching points:
  • Bickerstaff brainstem encephalitis: anti-GQ1b spectrum disease.
  • Features: descending paralysis with cranial nerve involvement, ataxia, encephalopathy, hyperreflexia.
  • Distinguishes from GBS / MFS by encephalopathy and hyperreflexia.
  • Treatment: IVIG or PLEX; favorable prognosis.
  • Anti-GQ1b confirms when positive.
Case 48 — Susac Syndrome with Classic Triad HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 16-year-old girl presents with 6 weeks of progressive cognitive decline (school performance drop), intermittent confusion, severe headaches, and 2 episodes of unilateral visual field defects. Audiology obtained at 4 weeks revealed bilateral low-frequency sensorineural hearing loss. Now presents with new bilateral peripheral visual loss.
HR 80 | BP 115/70 | T 37.0°C | GCS 13 | Multifocal subtle neurologic findings; ophthalmologic examination reveals branch retinal artery occlusions bilaterally

Stage 2 — Triad Recognized

Classic Susac syndrome triad now apparent:
  • Encephalopathy (cognitive decline, confusion, headaches).
  • Branch retinal artery occlusion (BRAO) — bilateral.
  • Sensorineural hearing loss — bilateral, low-frequency.
MRI brain: multifocal T2 hyperintensities including 'snowball' lesions in the body of the corpus callosum (highly characteristic). Spares optic radiations. Fluorescein angiography: arteriolar wall hyperfluorescence (Gass plaques).
How does Susac's corpus callosum pattern differ from MS?
  • Susac: 'snowball' or 'punched-out' lesions in CENTRAL corpus callosum body.
  • MS: Dawson finger lesions at the callosal-septal interface (PERIPHERAL callosum).
  • Susac spares optic radiations; MS may involve them.
  • Susac classically with retinal arteriolar disease and SNHL — MS does not have this combination.
  • Fluorescein angiography findings (Gass plaques) are unique to Susac.

Stage 3 — Treatment

IV methylprednisolone 30 mg/kg/day × 5 days, then oral prednisone taper. IVIG 2 g/kg over 5 days. Rituximab 375 mg/m² weekly × 4 doses. Aspirin 81 mg/day antiplatelet. Hearing aids fitted; ophthalmology and neurology follow-up.

Stage 4 — Long-term

Significant improvement in encephalopathy over 3 months. Hearing loss partially recovered; persistent moderate hearing impairment. No new retinal events. MRI at 6 months: lesion regression. Maintenance immunosuppression with mycophenolate continued for 18–24 months. Educational accommodations; cognitive remediation. Relapse surveillance.
Final Diagnosis: Susac syndrome (autoimmune endotheliopathy) presenting with classic triad of encephalopathy, branch retinal artery occlusions, and sensorineural hearing loss.
Plan:
  • Induction: high-dose corticosteroids + IVIG + rituximab.
  • Maintenance: mycophenolate mofetil 18–24 months.
  • Aspirin antiplatelet adjunct.
  • Hearing aids; cochlear implant evaluation if severe.
  • Ophthalmology monitoring for new BRAOs.
  • Neurology and rheumatology follow-up.
  • Cognitive remediation and educational accommodations.
  • Surveillance for relapse — common in Susac.
Teaching points:
  • Susac syndrome: autoimmune endotheliopathy of brain, retina, cochlea precapillary arterioles.
  • Classic triad — rarely simultaneous at presentation.
  • Characteristic MRI: 'snowball' lesions in corpus callosum body.
  • Fluorescein angiography: Gass plaques.
  • Treatment: corticosteroids + IVIG + rituximab; maintenance 18–24 months.
  • Relapses common; surveillance essential.
Case 49 — Opsoclonus-Myoclonus-Ataxia Syndrome with Occult Neuroblastoma MODERATE

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 22-month-old previously well girl is referred for 6 weeks of irritability, sleep disturbance, and progressively chaotic eye movements. Parents note 'wiggling eyes,' jerky body movements, and inability to sit unaided (regressed from walking 3 months ago).
HR 110 | BP 95/60 | T 37.0°C | Conjugate chaotic eye movements (opsoclonus), generalized myoclonic jerks, truncal ataxia, normal cranial nerves otherwise

Stage 2 — Workup

MRI brain: normal. CSF: normal cell counts and chemistry; oligoclonal bands negative; paraneoplastic antibody panel pending. Urine VMA and HVA: elevated. Abdominal ultrasound: 2.5 cm mass adjacent to right adrenal gland. MIBG scintigraphy: focal uptake matching mass. Confirmed neuroblastoma on biopsy (low-risk, INSS stage 1).
Why is the urine catecholamine screen and MIBG mandatory in OMAS?
  • ~50% of pediatric OMAS associated with occult neuroblastoma.
  • Tumors can be small (millimeters) and missed on physical exam.
  • VMA / HVA + MIBG + cross-sectional imaging together optimize detection.
  • Repeat imaging if initial negative — small occult tumors may emerge.
  • Tumor resection improves outcome.

Stage 3 — Treatment

Tumor resection. Immunotherapy: high-dose pulse methylprednisolone monthly + IVIG monthly. Rituximab added after 3 months for incomplete response. Multidisciplinary care.

Stage 4 — Long-term

Over 6 months: gradual improvement in opsoclonus, myoclonus. Ataxia improves slowly. Long-term: cognitive and behavioral sequelae moderate; receiving early intervention, neuropsychology assessment, ABA therapy. Relapse risk monitored.
Final Diagnosis: Opsoclonus-myoclonus-ataxia syndrome (OMAS) paraneoplastic to low-risk neuroblastoma; treated with tumor resection and multi-agent immunotherapy.
Plan:
  • Tumor resection (complete).
  • Maintenance immunotherapy ≥ 18–24 months.
  • Long-term neurodevelopmental follow-up.
  • Relapse surveillance.
  • Family counseling about cognitive sequelae.
Teaching points:
  • OMAS triad — mandatory neuroblastoma workup.
  • VMA/HVA + MIBG + cross-sectional imaging.
  • Tumor resection + multi-agent immunotherapy.
  • Long-term cognitive and motor sequelae common.
  • Relapse rate ~ 50%.
Case 50 — Alternating Hemiplegia of Childhood (ATP1A3 Mutation) HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 14-month-old presents with 8-month history of recurrent stereotyped episodes: paroxysmal unilateral hemiplegia lasting hours to days, sometimes alternating between sides; dystonic posturing during attacks; abnormal eye movements; episodes triggered by stress, bathing, fatigue; sleep aborts attacks. Developmental delay (not walking, limited words). Multiple ED visits previously labeled as 'seizure' or 'stroke' despite normal imaging.
During attack: pale, distressed, left hemiplegia, dystonic posturing, abnormal conjugate gaze; awake but irritable

Stage 2 — Workup

MRI brain (between attacks): normal. EEG during typical attack: no epileptiform activity. CTA: normal. Metabolic workup: unremarkable. Clinical diagnosis of alternating hemiplegia of childhood (AHC) considered. Gene panel: pathogenic ATP1A3 mutation (de novo).
Why is sleep a clue to AHC?
  • Episodes resolve during sleep — pathognomonic feature.
  • Hemiplegia, dystonia, and eye movements remit immediately on sleep onset.
  • Distinguishes from epilepsy, stroke, and migraine.
  • Inducing sleep can abort severe attacks.

Stage 3 — Management

Flunarizine 5 mg daily initiated (compassionate access; off-label). Trigger avoidance: routine sleep schedule, stress management. Topiramate added for additional effect. Sleep induction (benzodiazepine) used for severe acute attacks. Multidisciplinary referrals: pediatric neurology, developmental pediatrics, PT, OT, speech therapy. Genetic counseling — de novo (low recurrence risk for siblings).

Stage 4 — Long-term

Frequency of attacks reduced 40% with flunarizine. Developmental progress continued slowly. Anticipated long-term: persistent attacks, developmental delay, possible cognitive plateau. Family education and support groups (AHC Foundation). Educational accommodations being planned.
Final Diagnosis: Alternating hemiplegia of childhood (ATP1A3 mutation, de novo); partial response to flunarizine.
Plan:
  • Flunarizine for prevention.
  • Topiramate adjunct.
  • Sleep induction for severe acute attacks.
  • Trigger avoidance.
  • Multidisciplinary developmental care.
  • Family support and education.
  • Research enrollment when available.
Teaching points:
  • AHC: paroxysmal hemiplegia, alternates between sides, sleep resolves.
  • ATP1A3 mutations cause most cases (de novo).
  • Flunarizine — most established preventive.
  • Developmental delay characteristic.
  • Differentiate from epilepsy, stroke, migraine.
Case 51 — Rasmussen Encephalitis with Hemispherectomy HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 7-year-old boy develops focal motor seizures of the right face and arm 18 months ago; over the next 12 months, seizure frequency escalated to multiple daily despite levetiracetam, oxcarbazepine, lacosamide, then perampanel. Progressive right hemiparesis (now 3/5 power in right arm, 4/5 in leg). Cognitive decline noted in school. Now: epilepsia partialis continua (continuous focal motor seizures of right face/arm).
HR 100 | BP 110/70 | T 36.8°C | Continuous focal motor seizures right face/arm; right hemiparesis; subtle aphasia

Stage 2 — Imaging

MRI: progressive left hemispheric atrophy compared to MRI 12 months ago; T2/FLAIR hyperintensity in left perisylvian cortex and adjacent white matter; no contralateral involvement. Diagnostic biopsy: chronic encephalitis with T-cell-dominant infiltrates, microglial nodules; no infectious organism. Bien criteria for Rasmussen encephalitis met.
What is the role of immunotherapy in Rasmussen encephalitis?
  • IVIG, plasma exchange, tacrolimus, rituximab — may slow but rarely halt disease.
  • Evidence quality limited; case series predominantly.
  • Hemispherectomy remains definitive for seizure control.
  • Trial of immunotherapy may delay surgery in selected patients with milder progression.
  • Surgery should not be delayed in cases with established hemiparesis and refractory seizures.

Stage 3 — Surgical Decision

Multidisciplinary epilepsy surgery conference: trial of IVIG had no effect; severe seizure burden and progressive deficits warrant hemispherectomy. Functional hemispherotomy performed (less morbidity than anatomic hemispherectomy). Anticipated outcome discussed with family: improved seizure control, permanent fixed hemiparesis (already developing), preserved or recovered cognition with rehabilitation.

Stage 4 — Post-op Course

Post-op: seizure cessation achieved. Hemiparesis as anticipated; aggressive rehabilitation with PT/OT/speech therapy. By 6 months: ambulating with brace. Cognitive function recovered remarkably over 2 years (typical of pediatric hemispherectomy due to neuroplasticity). AED weaning gradually. Long-term: seizure-free; functional with adaptations.
Final Diagnosis: Rasmussen encephalitis (left hemisphere); treated with functional hemispherotomy with excellent seizure outcome.
Plan:
  • AED slow taper over 2 years.
  • Rehabilitation intensive 6–12 months.
  • Educational accommodations.
  • Neuropsychology assessments at 6, 12, 24 months.
  • Multidisciplinary follow-up.
Teaching points:
  • Rasmussen: progressive unilateral encephalitis with refractory focal epilepsy.
  • Bien criteria for diagnosis.
  • Hemispherectomy / functional hemispherotomy is definitive.
  • Younger patients benefit more from neuroplasticity.
  • Immunotherapy: may slow but rarely halts.
Case 52 — Wilson Disease Neurologic Presentation HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 14-year-old presents with progressive dysarthria, drooling, tremor, and behavioral change over 6 months. School performance has dropped markedly. Mother noted clumsiness and 'staring' episodes. PMH: mild liver enzyme elevation 2 years ago, not pursued. Family history: maternal aunt with 'liver problems.'
HR 75 | BP 110/65 | T 36.8°C | Slurred speech, drooling, parkinsonism with rigidity, mild dystonia of right hand, action tremor, splenomegaly, mild ascites

Stage 2 — Workup

Slit-lamp examination: Kayser-Fleischer rings bilaterally (pathognomonic). Serum ceruloplasmin 90 mg/L (low). 24-h urine copper 8.5 µmol (elevated). AST 95, ALT 110, INR 1.4. Liver ultrasound: cirrhotic appearance. MRI brain: T2 hyperintensity in basal ganglia, midbrain ('face of the giant panda' sign in midbrain). ATP7B sequencing: compound heterozygous pathogenic mutations.
Why is penicillamine sometimes problematic for neurologic Wilson disease?
  • ~50% of neurologic Wilson disease patients worsen initially on penicillamine.
  • Mechanism: rapid copper mobilization from tissues including brain.
  • Worsening can be irreversible.
  • Trientine preferred in many centers for neurologic disease — less initial worsening.
  • Ammonium tetrathiomolybdate emerging — minimal initial worsening.
  • Start low dose and escalate slowly.

Stage 3 — Treatment

Trientine 250 mg PO QID started (preferred over penicillamine for neurologic presentation). Zinc supplementation 50 mg PO TID. Low-copper diet (avoid liver, shellfish, mushrooms, chocolate). Pyridoxine 25 mg daily (cofactor support). Multidisciplinary care: hepatology, neurology, dietitian, psychiatry. Family genetic counseling — sibling screening planned.

Stage 4 — Course

Over 6 months: dysarthria improving; tremor reduced; behavioral symptoms ameliorating; liver enzymes normalizing. Long-term: lifelong therapy and monitoring (urinary copper, AST/ALT, neuro exam). Educational accommodations during recovery. Cousin screening identified asymptomatic ATP7B compound heterozygote — started on zinc as preventive therapy.
Final Diagnosis: Wilson disease (compound heterozygous ATP7B mutations) with neurologic and hepatic involvement; treated with trientine and zinc.
Plan:
  • Lifelong trientine + zinc.
  • Low-copper diet.
  • Quarterly monitoring: urine copper, AST/ALT, neuro exam.
  • Family genetic counseling and sibling screening.
  • Multidisciplinary: hepatology, neurology, psychiatry.
  • Liver transplant if cirrhotic decompensation.
Teaching points:
  • Wilson: ATP7B autosomal recessive; copper accumulation.
  • Pediatric neurologic presentation often adolescent.
  • KF rings + low ceruloplasmin + high urinary copper diagnostic.
  • Trientine preferred for neurologic disease (less worsening than penicillamine).
  • Lifelong therapy; family screening essential.
Case 53 — Sydenham Chorea Post-Streptococcal Infection MODERATE

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 9-year-old girl presents with 2 weeks of progressive irregular movements of arms and face, emotional lability, and academic decline. Mother reports a sore throat 2 months ago with mild fever; no medical visit at that time. Older sibling had rheumatic heart disease.
HR 95 | BP 105/65 | T 37.0°C | Irregular jerky non-stereotyped movements of arms and face (worse with stress, distractible); 'milkmaid grip'; tongue darting; emotional lability; subtle psychiatric symptoms (irritability, OCD)

Stage 2 — Workup

ASO titer 850 IU/mL (markedly elevated); anti-DNase B elevated. Throat culture negative (post-infection). CBC, ESR, CRP mildly elevated. Echocardiogram: mild mitral regurgitation (subclinical carditis). MRI brain normal. CSF: anti-NMDA receptor antibody negative (rules out NMDAR encephalitis mimic).
Why is echocardiography mandatory?
  • Sydenham chorea is one of the major Jones criteria for ARF.
  • ~ 25–50% have associated carditis (may be subclinical).
  • Echocardiogram detects valvulitis (mitral most common).
  • Cardiac involvement modifies duration of penicillin prophylaxis.
  • Cardiac follow-up essential.

Stage 3 — Treatment

Valproic acid 15 mg/kg/day divided BID titrated up for chorea suppression. Penicillin V 250 mg PO BID for 10 days (treat any residual strep). Penicillin G benzathine 1.2 million units IM every 4 weeks for secondary prophylaxis (planned for ≥ 10 years given carditis; lifelong if rheumatic heart disease established).

Stage 4 — Course

Chorea reduced significantly over 4 weeks. Emotional symptoms improving. Valproate continued 6 months then tapered. Repeat echo at 6 months: mitral regurgitation slightly improved. Long-term: ongoing IM penicillin prophylaxis; annual cardiology and rheumatology follow-up.
Final Diagnosis: Sydenham chorea + subclinical carditis (acute rheumatic fever).
Plan:
  • Valproate for chorea suppression (taper over 6 months).
  • Penicillin G benzathine IM every 4 weeks for ≥ 10 years.
  • Echo every 1–2 years; cardiology follow-up.
  • Mental health screening for psychiatric features.
  • Family education about recurrence.
  • Avoid OCP — risk of chorea gravidarum / recurrence.
Teaching points:
  • Sydenham chorea: major Jones criterion for ARF.
  • Latent period 1–6 months post-strep.
  • Antibody-mediated (anti-basal-ganglia).
  • Echo mandatory for carditis screen.
  • Penicillin prophylaxis: ≥ 10 years (lifelong if carditis).
  • Recurrence in pregnancy / OCP use.
Case 54 — Pediatric Brain Abscess with Mass Effect HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 10-year-old with cyanotic congenital heart disease (unrepaired tetralogy of Fallot with prior shunt) presents with 5 days of progressive headache, vomiting, fever, and now lethargy with a focal seizure of the left arm. PMH: chronic right-to-left shunt with baseline SpO₂ 80%.
HR 130 | BP 105/65 | T 38.6°C | GCS 11 | Mild papilledema | Left hemiparesis post-ictal

Stage 2 — Imaging

CT with contrast: 5 cm right frontal ring-enhancing lesion with surrounding edema; 7 mm midline shift; effaced cisterns. MRI with DWI: hyperintense center (pus has restricted diffusion — characteristic; distinguishes abscess from necrotic tumor). No other lesions. Echo: chronic right-to-left shunt confirmed. Blood cultures × 2 obtained.
Why does cyanotic CHD predispose to brain abscess?
  • Right-to-left shunt allows venous bacteria to bypass pulmonary capillary filter.
  • Bacteria reach systemic and cerebral circulation directly.
  • Hematogenous seeding common with bacteremia (dental, otologic, transient).
  • Common organisms: streptococci (especially viridans group), anaerobes.
  • Repair of CHD reduces but doesn't eliminate risk.

Stage 3 — Management

Emergent neurosurgical stereotactic aspiration of abscess (large size + mass effect + neurologic deficit + accessible location). Pus drained, sent for Gram stain, culture, anaerobic culture, fungal stains. Empiric ceftriaxone 100 mg/kg/day + metronidazole 30 mg/kg/day IV started. ICP managed with sedation, 3% saline, head-of-bed 30°. Levetiracetam load + maintenance. cEEG monitoring.

Stage 4 — Course

Cultures grow Streptococcus intermedius. Antibiotic regimen narrowed to penicillin G + metronidazole continued for 6 weeks IV. Weekly imaging shows progressive reduction in abscess size. Hemiparesis improving. Source workup: dental examination identified dental abscess; pediatric dentistry treatment after PICU stabilization. Discharged after 6 weeks IV antibiotics + 2 weeks PO suppression. Long-term: CHD repair scheduling.
Final Diagnosis: Pediatric brain abscess (Streptococcus intermedius) secondary to dental abscess with right-to-left shunt facilitating hematogenous spread; drained surgically with prolonged antibiotics.
Plan:
  • Total 6–8 weeks IV antibiotics post-drainage with response monitoring.
  • Source control (dental).
  • CHD repair to reduce future risk.
  • Antiepileptic continued ≥ 6 months.
  • Serial imaging until resolution.
  • Rehabilitation for residual hemiparesis.
  • Endocarditis prophylaxis for dental procedures.
Teaching points:
  • Pediatric brain abscess: drain + IV antibiotics 6–8 weeks.
  • DWI restriction characteristic of abscess pus.
  • Cyanotic CHD risk factor (right-to-left shunt).
  • Source: dental, sinus, otologic, cardiac.
  • Empiric ceftriaxone + metronidazole; vanco if MRSA risk.
Case 55 — Rhinocerebral Mucormycosis in DKA HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 16-year-old previously undiagnosed with new diabetes presents in DKA (glucose 35 mmol/L, pH 7.10, ketones 6 mmol/L). On day 2 of admission with DKA stabilizing, develops facial pain, swelling around right eye, and a black eschar visible in the right nostril. Right ophthalmoplegia and proptosis emerging.
HR 110 | BP 95/60 | T 38.4°C | Right facial swelling, black eschar nasal turbinate, partial ophthalmoplegia, proptosis | Glucose 18 (corrected) | pH 7.30

Stage 2 — Imaging and Diagnosis

CT sinuses with contrast: aggressive ethmoid/maxillary sinusitis with bony erosion, extension to right orbit, and through cribriform plate to right frontal lobe. MRI: orbital and intracranial extension, cavernous sinus involvement. Nasal biopsy emergent: broad non-septate hyphae with right-angle branching on histology — Mucormycosis confirmed.
Why is DKA a classic trigger for mucormycosis?
  • Hyperglycemia impairs neutrophil function and phagocytic killing.
  • Acidosis releases iron from transferrin (iron is a critical Mucor nutrient).
  • Ketone bodies enhance Mucor growth.
  • Mucor has unique ability to use ketone bodies and free iron — selective advantage in DKA.
  • Other risk factors: immunocompromise, neutropenia, deferoxamine.

Stage 3 — Emergency Management

Emergency surgical debridement by ENT and neurosurgery: extensive debridement of necrotic sinus and palatal tissues; right orbital exenteration considered but deferred initially; multiple repeat debridements anticipated. Liposomal amphotericin B 10 mg/kg/day IV started. Aggressive correction of DKA continued; insulin infusion; strict glucose control. Hydration with normal saline (avoid potassium replacement complications during amphotericin).

Stage 4 — Course

Multiple surgical debridements over 2 weeks (Days 1, 4, 7, 10). Amphotericin B continued at high dose × 4 weeks. Transitioned to oral isavuconazole for step-down therapy. Right orbital exenteration eventually performed (loss of right eye). Frontal lobe involvement controlled. Discharged at 8 weeks IV. Long-term outpatient: isavuconazole 6–12 months total. Diabetes well controlled.
Final Diagnosis: Rhinocerebral mucormycosis precipitated by DKA in newly diagnosed type 1 diabetes; treated with aggressive surgical debridement + amphotericin B + isavuconazole step-down + orbital exenteration.
Plan:
  • Total antifungal course 6–12 months.
  • Diabetes management with endocrinology.
  • Multidisciplinary follow-up: ENT, neurosurgery, infectious disease, endocrinology, oculoplastic for prosthesis.
  • Mental health support.
  • Family / patient education on glucose control as preventive.
Teaching points:
  • Mucormycosis in DKA — high mortality without prompt recognition.
  • Black eschar in nasal turbinate or palate is the alarm sign.
  • Aggressive surgical debridement is the cornerstone (often multiple).
  • Liposomal amphotericin B high dose + isavuconazole step-down.
  • Address underlying — normalize glucose, treat acidosis.
  • Mortality 30–70%; early recognition critical.
Case 56 — Mitochondrial DNA Depletion (MPV17 Hepatocerebral Form) HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 9-month-old presents with progressive failure to thrive, hypoglycemia, mild hypotonia, and developmental delay (no head control). Parents note recent neurologic regression — child no longer recognizes mother. Liver mildly enlarged.
HR 130 | BP 75/45 | T 36.8°C | Hypoglycemia 1.8 (corrected), lactate 5.5, ammonia 110, AST 850, ALT 600, INR 1.6, FTT

Stage 2 — Workup

Plasma amino acids: elevated tyrosine, methionine. Urine organic acids: nonspecific. Acylcarnitine profile: normal (rules out fatty acid oxidation defects). Liver biopsy: marked steatosis with cholestasis and lobular disarray; mtDNA quantification: severe depletion (~ 10% of control). Whole exome sequencing: pathogenic MPV17 mutations confirmed.
Why is liver transplant controversial in MPV17?
  • Liver transplant can correct hepatic phenotype.
  • However, brain disease is progressive and independent of liver.
  • Most MPV17 patients have neurologic deterioration regardless of transplant.
  • Risk-benefit complex; depends on extent of brain involvement at evaluation.
  • Decision is individualized; some centers offer in mild brain involvement.

Stage 3 — Management

Supportive care: D10 IV continuous for hypoglycemia prevention; lactulose for ammonia; high-carbohydrate frequent feeds enterally; mitochondrial cocktail (CoQ10, riboflavin, thiamine, L-carnitine). Avoid mitotoxic drugs (no valproate). Multidisciplinary care: hepatology, neurology, metabolic, palliative care. Family counseling about life-limiting nature of disease.

Stage 4 — Course

Progressive hepatic and neurologic deterioration over 6 months despite supportive care. Liver transplant evaluation deferred due to severe neurologic involvement. Family chose compassionate care at home with hospice support. Patient passed at age 18 months. Family genetic counseling for future pregnancies; preimplantation genetic diagnosis discussed.
Final Diagnosis: Mitochondrial DNA depletion syndrome — hepatocerebral form (MPV17 mutations); life-limiting illness.
Process:
  • Multidisciplinary supportive care.
  • Avoid mitotoxic drugs (valproate fatal).
  • Mitochondrial cocktail.
  • Family counseling: life-limiting illness; engage palliative care early.
  • Genetic counseling and future pregnancy planning.
Teaching points:
  • mtDNA depletion syndromes: nuclear gene mutations affecting mtDNA maintenance.
  • MPV17: hepatocerebral form; progressive liver disease + neurologic regression.
  • Liver transplant controversial.
  • Many forms life-limiting; engage palliative care early.
  • Avoid mitotoxic drugs.
Case 57 — Macrophage Activation Syndrome in Systemic JIA HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

An 8-year-old girl with systemic JIA on tocilizumab presents with new fever spikes (40°C), worsening fatigue, hepatomegaly, and a rash. Blood work shows pancytopenia evolving over 48 hours.
HR 130 | BP 95/55 | T 40.2°C | Hepatosplenomegaly | Ferritin 14,500 ng/mL | AST 1800, ALT 1500 | Fibrinogen 1.2 g/L (low) | Triglycerides 4.5 mmol/L (high) | Platelets 50 | LDH 2200

Stage 2 — Diagnosis

Ravelli MAS criteria for sJIA met: febrile + ferritin 14,500 + platelets 50 + AST 1800 + triglycerides 4.5 + fibrinogen 1.2. Bone marrow biopsy: hemophagocytosis present. Soluble IL-2R elevated. NK cell activity reduced. Diagnosis: Macrophage activation syndrome.
Why does tocilizumab not protect against MAS in this patient?
  • Tocilizumab blocks IL-6 receptor — does not address IL-1 or other cytokine pathways.
  • MAS represents a different cytokine storm phenotype (IL-1, IL-18, IFN-γ predominant).
  • Patients on IL-6 blockade can still develop MAS.
  • Anakinra (IL-1 antagonist) targets the dominant cytokine pathway in MAS.
  • Note: tocilizumab can mask fever and CRP in sJIA, potentially delaying MAS recognition.

Stage 3 — Management

PICU admission. Methylprednisolone 30 mg/kg IV daily × 3 days. Anakinra 5 mg/kg/day SC initiated. Hold tocilizumab. Supportive care: transfusion as needed, monitoring for DIC, hepatic function support. cEEG for monitoring (neurologic features anticipated). Workup for triggers: viral panel (EBV negative), bacterial cultures, drug exposure.

Stage 4 — Course

Within 48 hours of anakinra: ferritin trending down (14,500 → 6,000); fever resolving; platelets stabilizing. Within 1 week: fibrinogen normalizing; liver enzymes improving. Steroids tapered over 6 weeks. Anakinra continued for 6 months then tapered. Tocilizumab not resumed; switched to canakinumab (IL-1β monoclonal antibody) for sJIA control. Long-term: MAS surveillance; ferritin monitoring; family education about warning signs.
Final Diagnosis: Macrophage activation syndrome (MAS) complicating systemic JIA despite tocilizumab; responsive to anakinra and corticosteroids.
Plan:
  • Anakinra 5 mg/kg/day SC, taper after 6 months.
  • Steroid taper over 6 weeks.
  • Switch from tocilizumab to canakinumab for sJIA.
  • MAS surveillance: ferritin, CBC, liver enzymes quarterly.
  • Family education about warning signs.
  • Trigger avoidance (sick day plan).
Teaching points:
  • MAS in sJIA: HLH-like cytokine storm.
  • Ravelli criteria for diagnosis.
  • Anakinra (IL-1 antagonist) increasingly first-line.
  • Tocilizumab does not protect against MAS — different cytokine pathway.
  • Mortality 10–25%; early recognition critical.
Case 58 — Atypical HUS with Neurologic Involvement HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 6-year-old previously well boy presents with 3 days of pallor, fatigue, decreased urine output, and now a generalized tonic-clonic seizure. No diarrhea prodrome. Past medical history negative; maternal cousin had 'kidney failure' as a child.
HR 150 | BP 160/100 (hypertensive) | T 37.0°C | Pallor | Mild edema | Hb 5.5 (was 12 at last visit) | Plt 25 | Cr 4.5 (baseline 0.5) | LDH 4500 | Schistocytes on smear | Stool studies pending

Stage 2 — Diagnosis

Stool studies: STEC PCR and culture negative. Coombs negative (rules out warm AIHA). ADAMTS13 activity 80% (rules out TTP). Complement studies: C3 reduced (0.6, low), C4 normal. Suspected atypical HUS. Anti-Factor H antibodies pending. Genetic complement panel sent.
Why is meningococcal vaccination required before eculizumab?
  • Eculizumab inhibits terminal complement (C5).
  • Terminal complement is critical for defense against Neisseria meningitidis.
  • Patients on eculizumab have 1000–2000× increased risk of meningococcal disease.
  • Vaccinate with quadrivalent (MenACWY) AND serogroup B vaccines BEFORE starting eculizumab.
  • If eculizumab must start urgently, vaccinate concurrently AND give prophylactic antibiotics (penicillin V) until vaccination effective (2 weeks).

Stage 3 — Treatment

Plasma exchange initiated as bridge while obtaining eculizumab approval. Hemodialysis for AKI and fluid overload. Levetiracetam load for seizure prophylaxis. Meningococcal vaccines (ACWY + B) given concurrently with prophylactic penicillin V. Eculizumab loading: 600 mg IV weekly × 4 then 900 mg every 2 weeks (weight-adjusted). MRI brain: bilateral subcortical T2 hyperintensities consistent with PRES-like pattern (vasogenic edema from microvascular injury). BP gradually reduced.

Stage 4 — Recovery

Within 1 week of eculizumab: Hb stabilizing, platelets rising, LDH falling. Hemodialysis weaned over 3 weeks; renal function partial recovery (Cr 1.5). Neurologic recovery near complete. Genetic panel returns: factor H mutation confirmed (familial form). Long-term eculizumab indefinitely; family screening identified maternal aunt with subclinical aHUS history.
Final Diagnosis: Atypical HUS (complement factor H mutation) with neurologic involvement and acute kidney injury; treated with eculizumab.
Plan:
  • Eculizumab indefinitely (or until controlled trials of discontinuation in stable patients).
  • Meningococcal vaccination booster every 5 years.
  • Penicillin V prophylaxis (or amoxicillin) lifelong on eculizumab.
  • Renal function monitoring; antihypertensive therapy.
  • Family genetic counseling.
  • Surveillance for relapse.
Teaching points:
  • aHUS: complement-mediated thrombotic microangiopathy.
  • ~ 25–50% have neurologic involvement.
  • Eculizumab: transformative therapy.
  • Meningococcal vaccination mandatory before eculizumab.
  • Family genetic counseling.
  • Distinguish from STEC-HUS (typical), TTP (ADAMTS13).
Case 59 — Central Diabetes Insipidus After Pituitary Surgery — Triphasic Response MODERATE

Stage 1 — Background

A 12-year-old underwent transsphenoidal resection of a craniopharyngioma 24 hours ago. Successful gross total resection. Recovering in PICU on dexamethasone, antiemetic, normal saline maintenance fluid. Now developing polyuria.
HR 100 | BP 110/65 | T 37.0°C | UOP 12 mL/kg/h | Urine SG 1.002 | Serum Na 152 (rising from 142 in 6 hours) | Glucose 5.5 | Hypovolemic on exam

Stage 2 — Phase 1 (DI)

Central DI confirmed: hypotonic polyuria + rising sodium + low urine SG. DDAVP 0.5 µg IV given; UOP decreases. Free water deficit calculated and replaced cautiously over 48 hours (Na correction < 8 mmol/L per 24 h). Hourly Na monitoring. Anterior pituitary screen: AM cortisol low → empiric stress-dose hydrocortisone added. T4 low → levothyroxine started.
What is the triphasic response and how to manage each phase?
  • Phase 1 (DI, days 1–5): ADH deficiency from posterior pituitary stunning. Manage with DDAVP and careful fluid replacement.
