  • Phase 2 (SIADH, days 5–14): release of stored ADH from dying posterior pituitary cells. Risk of hyponatremia if fluids unrestricted. Stop DDAVP, restrict free water.
  • Phase 3 (DI again, after day 14): permanent DI from posterior pituitary destruction. Long-term DDAVP needed.
  • Not all patients show all phases — monitor closely.

Stage 3 — Phase 2 (SIADH)

Day 7 post-op: UOP decreased to 1 mL/kg/h; serum Na falling (147 → 134 over 24 hours). DDAVP held. Fluid restriction implemented (60% maintenance). Sodium stabilized at 134 with restriction. Phase 2 typically lasts ~ 5–7 days.

Stage 4 — Phase 3 (Permanent DI) and Long-term

Day 14: polyuria resumed, Na rising. Permanent DI confirmed. DDAVP intranasal 5 µg BID established (long-term). Anterior pituitary panhypopituitarism confirmed: lifelong hydrocortisone, levothyroxine, growth hormone replacement (started when stable), and eventually sex hormone replacement at puberty. Family education on sick day management, DDAVP technique, and emergency hydrocortisone administration. Endocrinology long-term follow-up.
Final Diagnosis: Post-pituitary surgery triphasic response with permanent central DI and panhypopituitarism (post-craniopharyngioma resection).
Plan:
  • Lifelong DDAVP intranasal or oral.
  • Hydrocortisone replacement (with stress-dose escalation for illness).
  • Levothyroxine.
  • Growth hormone replacement (when stable).
  • Sex hormone replacement at puberty.
  • Endocrinology follow-up.
  • Family education: sick day rules, DDAVP, emergency steroids.
  • MedicAlert bracelet.
Teaching points:
  • Post-pituitary surgery: triphasic response in ~ 30%.
  • Phase 1: DI; Phase 2: SIADH (~ days 5–14); Phase 3: permanent DI.
  • Hourly Na monitoring during transitions.
  • Anterior pituitary screening essential (cortisol most critical).
  • Free water deficit replaced over 48 h to avoid cerebral edema.
Case 60 — Convexity SAH from Reversible Cerebral Vasoconstriction Syndrome (RCVS) HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 16-year-old girl presents with thunderclap headache reaching maximal intensity in 1 minute. She recently started oral contraceptive pill (OCP) and used pseudoephedrine for cold symptoms. Episode 2 days ago resolved spontaneously; second episode today brought her to ED.
HR 95 | BP 140/85 | T 37.0°C | GCS 15 | Neurologic exam normal

Stage 2 — Imaging

CT: small focal SAH along right central sulcus only — convexity SAH; no basal cistern blood; no parenchymal hemorrhage. CTA: appears normal initially. MRI/MRV: small cortical sulcal SAH confirmed; no thrombosis. Initial catheter angiography: appears normal but slightly irregular caliber of distal MCA branches noted on careful review.
Why repeat imaging in 1 week for suspected RCVS?
  • RCVS vasoconstriction is dynamic and segmental.
  • Initial angiography may appear normal or have only subtle changes.
  • Repeat angiography 1–2 weeks later often shows the classic 'string of beads' multifocal segmental vasoconstriction.
  • Vasoconstriction typically resolves over weeks (hence 'reversible').
  • Helps differentiate from primary CNS vasculitis (persistent inflammation, biopsy positive).

Stage 3 — Management

Hold OCP (vasoactive). Stop pseudoephedrine. Avoid serotonergic agents. Verapamil 80 mg PO TID initiated (calcium channel blocker; controversial efficacy in RCVS). Nimodipine alternative. Levetiracetam for seizure prophylaxis (cortical bleed). BP target: avoid hypertension and hypotension. ICU observation initially.

Stage 4 — Course

No further headache episodes. Repeat MRA at 1 week: now clearly shows multifocal segmental vasoconstriction in distal MCA, ACA, PCA branches bilaterally — 'string of beads' classic for RCVS. Repeat at 4 weeks: complete resolution. Verapamil continued 3 months total. Counseling about avoiding vasoactive drugs (OCP, cocaine, sympathomimetics, triptans, SSRIs). Follow-up neurology for headache management.
Final Diagnosis: Reversible cerebral vasoconstriction syndrome (RCVS) precipitated by OCP and pseudoephedrine, presenting with thunderclap headache and convexity SAH.
Plan:
  • Verapamil 3 months.
  • Lifelong avoidance of OCP, pseudoephedrine, cocaine, triptans (relative).
  • Headache management consultation.
  • Surveillance MRI/MRA at 1, 3, 6 months.
  • Counseling about future pregnancy (risk in postpartum).
Teaching points:
  • RCVS: thunderclap headache + reversible multifocal vasoconstriction.
  • Triggers: OCP, sympathomimetics, postpartum, cocaine, SSRIs.
  • Convexity SAH characteristic complication.
  • Repeat angiography 1–2 weeks if initial normal.
  • Vasoconstriction typically resolves over weeks.
  • Calcium channel blockers used but evidence limited.
Case 61 — Thyroid Storm with Hyperthyroid Encephalopathy HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 16-year-old with Graves disease (recently diagnosed, methimazole started 2 weeks ago, poor compliance) presents with high fever, agitation, vomiting, and chest pain progressing to coma over 12 hours. Recent URI 5 days ago.
HR 200 (atrial fibrillation with rapid response) | BP 175/105 | T 41.0°C | GCS 8 | Tremor, hyperreflexia, exophthalmos | Glucose 9, lactate 4 | TSH undetectable, free T4 markedly elevated

Stage 2 — Diagnosis

Thyroid storm with hyperthyroid encephalopathy. Burch-Wartofsky score > 45 (highly suggestive of storm). Precipitant: infection (URI) plus medication non-compliance. Cardiac: atrial fibrillation with rapid ventricular response, mildly reduced LVEF on bedside echo.
Why is the ORDER of medications critical in thyroid storm?
  • Step 1: Beta-blocker (propranolol or esmolol) — addresses adrenergic symptoms and cardiac.
  • Step 2: Propylthiouracil (PTU) — blocks thyroid hormone synthesis AND inhibits T4→T3 conversion.
  • Step 3: Iodine (Lugol or potassium iodide) — given 1 hour AFTER PTU. If given before PTU, iodine could stimulate organification of thyroid hormones (Jod-Basedow effect — worsens storm).
  • Step 4: Hydrocortisone — blocks peripheral T4→T3 conversion AND treats relative adrenal insufficiency.
  • Step 5: Cooling, fluids, supportive care, treat precipitant.

Stage 3 — Acute Management

Intubation for airway protection (GCS 8, vomiting). Propranolol 0.1 mg/kg IV slow (titrate; or esmolol infusion if heart failure). PTU 5 mg/kg PO/NG load, then 2.5 mg/kg q4h. One hour after PTU: Lugol solution 5 drops PO/NG q6h (or sodium iodide IV). Hydrocortisone 100 mg/m² IV q6h. Aggressive cooling: cooling blanket, ice packs, cold IV fluids; acetaminophen (avoid aspirin — displaces T4 from binding proteins). Norepinephrine carefully for BP. Treat URI with ceftriaxone empirically. cEEG monitoring.

Stage 4 — Course

Within 24 hours of treatment: temperature normalizing; HR controlled with esmolol infusion; mental status improving. Day 3: extubated. Day 5: clinically stable, transitioned to methimazole (after PTU initial therapy — PTU preferred in pregnancy and storm; methimazole otherwise). Endocrinology long-term: discussing definitive treatment with radioactive iodine or thyroidectomy. Cardiology follow-up for atrial fibrillation.
Final Diagnosis: Thyroid storm with hyperthyroid encephalopathy, atrial fibrillation, and high-output heart failure, precipitated by URI and medication non-compliance in Graves disease.
Plan:
  • Continue antithyroid therapy (methimazole maintenance).
  • Beta-blocker until euthyroid.
  • Definitive treatment (RAI or thyroidectomy) planning.
  • Compliance counseling.
  • Cardiology follow-up.
  • Stress-dose hydrocortisone teaching for future illness.
Teaching points:
  • Thyroid storm: high mortality (10–30%).
  • Burch-Wartofsky scoring system.
  • Treatment sequence: beta-blocker → PTU → iodine (1 h after PTU) → hydrocortisone.
  • PTU preferred over methimazole in storm.
  • Avoid aspirin (displaces T4 from binding proteins).
  • Treat precipitant (infection, non-compliance, surgery, trauma, iodine load).
Case 62 — Glutaric Aciduria Type I Encephalopathic Crisis HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 14-month-old (born to consanguineous parents in a region without expanded newborn screening) presents with vomiting, lethargy, and dystonic posturing after 2 days of gastroenteritis. Mother noted his head appeared 'too large' since birth.
HR 150 | BP 90/55 | T 39.0°C | GCS 11 | Macrocephaly (head circumference 99th percentile) | Generalized dystonia | Mild ketosis

Stage 2 — Investigations

MRI brain: bilateral putaminal T2 hyperintensity, frontotemporal atrophy with widened opercular cisterns ('bat-wing' appearance), small bilateral subdural collections. Urine organic acids: markedly elevated glutaric and 3-hydroxyglutaric acids. Plasma acylcarnitines: elevated glutarylcarnitine (C5DC). Diagnosis: Glutaric aciduria type I.
Why does GA-1 mimic abusive head trauma?
  • Macrocephaly and frontotemporal atrophy create wide subarachnoid spaces.
  • Bridging veins are stretched and prone to rupture.
  • Resulting subdural collections of varying ages can mimic AHT.
  • Important to work up metabolic causes when AHT picture is atypical.
  • Both can coexist, but distinct etiologies and management.

Stage 3 — Emergency Management

PICU admission. NPO; protein stopped. D10 IV continuous at 8 mg/kg/min plus intralipid 2 g/kg/day for anti-catabolic effect. IV carnitine 100 mg/kg load then 100 mg/kg/day infusion. Riboflavin 100 mg/day. Aggressive antipyretic management (acetaminophen, cooling blanket). Levetiracetam for seizure prophylaxis (avoid valproate — mitotoxic). Treat gastroenteritis. Bicarbonate for acidosis.

Stage 4 — Course and Long-term

Within 48 hours: encephalopathy resolving, dystonia improving. Discharged on day 7. Long-term: lysine-restricted diet with arginine supplementation, daily carnitine. Sick-day protocol with emergency D10 access. Family genetic counseling — autosomal recessive (siblings 25% risk). Older sibling tested: also affected but milder; both enrolled in metabolic clinic. Educational accommodations for residual mild motor disability.
Final Diagnosis: Glutaric aciduria type I (GCDH deficiency) with encephalopathic crisis precipitated by febrile gastroenteritis.
Plan:
  • Lysine-restricted diet + arginine supplementation.
  • Daily carnitine 100 mg/kg/day.
  • Sick-day protocol with emergency room access.
  • Avoid valproate.
  • Aggressive antipyretic management during illness.
  • Multidisciplinary care: metabolic, neurology, PT/OT, dietitian.
  • Family genetic counseling and sibling screening.
  • MedicAlert bracelet.
Teaching points:
  • GA-1: macrocephaly + striatal injury + frontotemporal atrophy ('bat-wing') after febrile illness.
  • Subdural collections can mimic AHT — always consider metabolic.
  • Emergency: stop protein, D10 + intralipid, carnitine, riboflavin.
  • Avoid valproate (mitotoxic).
  • Newborn screening detects most cases.
Case 63 — Classic Maple Syrup Urine Disease in Neonate HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 5-day-old term male presents with poor feeding (started day 3), irritability, then progressive lethargy and opisthotonus. Mother noticed unusual sweet odor in urine and clothing. Born to consanguineous parents; family from a region without expanded newborn screening.
HR 175 | BP 60/35 | T 36.8°C | Glucose 4 | Ammonia 80 | Lactate 2.5 | Severe ketosis | Maple syrup / burnt sugar odor in urine

Stage 2 — Diagnosis

Plasma amino acids: leucine 2800 µmol/L (markedly elevated; neurotoxic), isoleucine 600, valine 850; alloisoleucine present (pathognomonic for MSUD). Urine organic acids: branched-chain ketoacids. Confirmed BCKDHA mutations. Diagnosis: classic MSUD.
Why is hemodialysis preferred over CRRT for severe MSUD?
  • Leucine is highly diffusible — hemodialysis with high blood flow rates clears leucine 4–6× faster than CRRT.
  • For leucine > 1500 µmol/L or encephalopathy with cerebral edema, rapid clearance is essential.
  • CRRT may be used for ongoing maintenance after initial reduction.
  • Aggressive D10 + intralipid alone insufficient for severe elevations.

Stage 3 — Emergency Treatment

Stop protein intake (NPO). D10 IV continuous + intralipid 2 g/kg/day for anti-catabolic effect; insulin if hyperglycemic. Add isoleucine and valine supplementation (paradoxical but essential — promote protein synthesis to clear leucine into proteins). Thiamine 100 mg/day (trial for thiamine-responsive form — only ~ 10% respond but worth trying). Emergent hemodialysis given leucine > 1500 + encephalopathy. ICU monitoring for cerebral edema (head-up positioning, hyperosmolar therapy ready).

Stage 4 — Course

Hemodialysis × 6 hours reduces leucine from 2800 to 600 µmol/L. CRRT continued for maintenance. Within 48 hours: alert, feeding (BCAA-free formula). Long-term: BCAA-restricted, isoleucine/valine-supplemented diet under metabolic team. Frequent plasma amino acid monitoring. Sick-day protocol. Eventually liver transplant evaluated given severity (would correct hepatic enzyme deficiency). Family genetic counseling: autosomal recessive (siblings 25% risk).
Final Diagnosis: Classic maple syrup urine disease (BCKDH deficiency) presenting in neonatal period; treated with emergent hemodialysis and BCAA-restricted formula.
Plan:
  • Lifelong BCAA-restricted, isoleucine/valine-supplemented diet.
  • Frequent plasma amino acid monitoring.
  • Sick-day protocol with emergency room access.
  • Liver transplant evaluation if severe (corrects hepatic enzyme defect).
  • Multidisciplinary care: metabolic, dietitian, neurology, PT/OT.
  • Family genetic counseling.
  • MedicAlert bracelet.
Teaching points:
  • MSUD: neonatal-onset metabolic emergency.
  • Maple syrup / burnt sugar odor pathognomonic.
  • Plasma alloisoleucine: pathognomonic biomarker.
  • Emergent hemodialysis for leucine > 1500 or severe encephalopathy.
  • Isoleucine/valine supplementation paradoxical but essential.
  • Avoid mitotoxic drugs.
Case 64 — Persistent Hyperinsulinism in Infant HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 3-month-old infant presents to PICU after recurrent severe hypoglycemia episodes. Glucose dropped to 1.2 mmol/L at home; seizure followed. Despite IV D10 + D12.5 PIV running, glucose remains 2.0–2.8 mmol/L. Central line placement planned for higher-concentration glucose. Family history: maternal cousin with similar issues as a baby.
HR 150 | BP 75/40 | T 36.7°C | Glucose 2.4 on D12.5 at 12 mg/kg/min | Suppressed ketones (BHB undetectable) | Detectable insulin 8 µU/mL during hypoglycemia (should be suppressed)

Stage 2 — Diagnosis

Detectable insulin during hypoglycemia + suppressed ketones + suppressed free fatty acids = hyperinsulinemic hypoglycemia. Glucagon challenge: glycemic response > 1.7 mmol/L (confirms hyperinsulinism). Genetic panel: ABCC8 mutation (KATP channel) — diagnoses hyperinsulinism.
Why focal vs diffuse distinction matters?
  • Focal disease (one or two adenoma-like lesions): partial pancreatectomy is curative.
  • Diffuse disease (whole pancreas affected): near-total pancreatectomy may be needed if medically refractory.
  • 18F-DOPA PET/CT distinguishes with high accuracy.
  • KATP mutations (ABCC8, KCNJ11): both focal and diffuse phenotypes.
  • Surgical decision-making depends on PET/CT result and medical response.

Stage 3 — Management Escalation

Central line placed; D20 infusion started, escalated to 18 mg/kg/min. Diazoxide 10 mg/kg/day trial × 5 days — no significant response (KATP mutations typically diazoxide-unresponsive). Octreotide 5 µg/kg/day SC initiated; modest response with glucose 3.0–3.8 mmol/L. Continuous enteral feeds via NG to support hepatic glucose output.

Stage 4 — Imaging and Surgery

18F-DOPA PET/CT: focal uptake at pancreatic head. Surgical exploration with intraoperative biopsy and frozen section confirms focal lesion. Partial pancreatectomy (head resection) curative — glucose normalized post-op with no further hypoglycemia, off all medications and IV glucose. Long-term: normal glucose homeostasis; no diabetes (preserved most of pancreas); annual follow-up for late diabetes risk. Family genetic counseling about ABCC8 inheritance.
Final Diagnosis: Congenital hyperinsulinism (focal, ABCC8 mutation) with diazoxide-unresponsive phenotype; cured by partial pancreatectomy.
Plan:
  • Annual follow-up for late diabetes / hypoglycemia recurrence.
  • Family genetic counseling.
  • Sibling testing.
  • Developmental follow-up (cognitive effects of perinatal hypoglycemia).
  • School accommodations if cognitive impact identified.
Teaching points:
  • Hyperinsulinism: detectable insulin during hypoglycemia + suppressed ketones.
  • Glucose > 3.5 mmol/L essential to protect developing brain.
  • Diazoxide first-line; KATP mutations often unresponsive.
  • 18F-DOPA PET/CT differentiates focal vs diffuse.
  • Focal: cured by partial pancreatectomy.
  • Diffuse refractory: near-total pancreatectomy.
Case 65 — Neuroleptic Malignant Syndrome HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 15-year-old with schizoaffective disorder is admitted to inpatient psychiatry after an episode of agitation. He receives haloperidol 5 mg IM bolus × 2 over 6 hours plus chlorpromazine for ongoing agitation. The next morning: fever, severe rigidity, fluctuating consciousness. Transferred to PICU.
HR 150 | BP 175/100 (labile) | T 41.0°C | GCS 9 | Severe 'lead-pipe' rigidity throughout; hyporeflexia; profuse diaphoresis; tachypnea | CK 32,000 | UA myoglobin positive | WBC elevated; AST 450, ALT 380

Stage 2 — Diagnosis

DSM-5 / DSM criteria for NMS: dopamine antagonist exposure + hyperthermia + rigidity + altered mental status + autonomic dysfunction + elevated CK. Levenson criteria fulfilled. Differential considered and excluded: malignant hyperthermia (no anesthesia trigger), serotonin syndrome (no serotonergic drugs, has hyporeflexia rather than hyperreflexia/clonus), thyroid storm (TSH normal), heat stroke (clinical context), CNS infection (CSF unremarkable).
Why does NMS have hyporeflexia, vs serotonin syndrome with hyperreflexia?
  • NMS: dopaminergic blockade → striatal effects + lead-pipe rigidity from D2 antagonism in basal ganglia and spinal cord; hyporeflexia.
  • Serotonin syndrome: serotonergic excess → hyperreflexia, clonus, especially lower limbs.
  • Same hyperthermic, rigid, autonomic phenotype but opposite reflex pattern.
  • Treatment differs: NMS — dantrolene + bromocriptine; serotonin — cyproheptadine.
  • Clinical history (drug exposure) and reflex examination critical.

Stage 3 — Acute Management

All neuroleptics discontinued. ICU admission. Aggressive cooling: cooling blanket, ice packs, cold IV fluids. IV fluid resuscitation 2× maintenance with normal saline. Urine alkalinization for rhabdomyolysis (bicarbonate target urine pH ≥ 7). Foley catheter; serial creatinine. Lorazepam IV PRN for agitation. Dantrolene 1 mg/kg IV q6h initiated (muscle hypermetabolism). Bromocriptine 2.5 mg PO/NG q6h to restore dopaminergic tone. Mechanical ventilation due to GCS 9 and hyperthermia. cEEG monitoring (rule out seizures).

Stage 4 — Course

Within 48 hours: temperature down to 38°C; rigidity improving; CK trending down (32,000 → 18,000 → 8,000); renal function stable. Sedation weaned over 5 days. Extubated day 6. NMS-specific medications continued × 2 weeks then tapered. Discharged to psychiatric care with no resumption of typical neuroleptics; switched to atypical (clozapine) with carefully escalated dosing. Long-term: NMS susceptibility documented; future neuroleptic use carefully considered with risk-benefit analysis.
Final Diagnosis: Neuroleptic malignant syndrome from haloperidol + chlorpromazine exposure in psychiatric admission; resolved with dantrolene, bromocriptine, supportive care.
Plan:
  • Document NMS history prominently; avoid typical neuroleptics.
  • If future neuroleptic needed: atypical, slow titration, careful monitoring.
  • Psychiatric follow-up for underlying schizoaffective disorder.
  • Family / patient education about warning signs.
  • MedicAlert bracelet.
Teaching points:
  • NMS: dopamine antagonist exposure + hyperthermia + rigidity + altered mental status + autonomic dysfunction.
  • 'Lead-pipe' rigidity with hyporeflexia (vs serotonin syndrome — clonus/hyperreflexia).
  • Treatment: dantrolene + bromocriptine + supportive care.
  • Mortality 5–20%.
  • Avoid restarting offending agent; atypical neuroleptic alternative with caution.
Case 66 — Malignant Hyperthermia Intra-operative HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 9-year-old undergoes elective tonsillectomy and adenoidectomy. Induction with sevoflurane mask induction + propofol. Succinylcholine given for intubation. Family history: maternal uncle had 'a serious anesthesia reaction.' Within 15 minutes of induction: masseter rigidity (couldn't open mouth easily for intubation); within 30 minutes: tachycardia, hypercarbia (EtCO₂ 75 despite ventilator settings), rising temperature.
HR 165 | BP 95/55 | T 40.0°C and rising rapidly | EtCO₂ 85 | SpO₂ 95% on 50% O₂ | Mottled skin | Generalized muscle rigidity progressing | K 6.5 | CK 40,000 within 1 hour | Lactate 8

Stage 2 — Diagnosis

Malignant hyperthermia syndrome — recognized from masseter rigidity + hypercarbia + tachycardia + hyperthermia + acidosis + rigidity. Anesthesiology team activated MHAUS protocol. Family history of unexplained anesthesia reaction supportive of RYR1 mutation.
What is the MHAUS hotline and what role does it play?
  • MHAUS (Malignant Hyperthermia Association of the United States) operates 24/7 hotline (1-800-MH-HYPER) staffed by MH experts.
  • Provides real-time consultation during crisis.
  • Helps clinicians confirm diagnosis, dose dantrolene, manage complications.
  • Coordinates RYR1 testing post-event.
  • Maintains national registry and family screening.

Stage 3 — Emergency Management

Volatile anesthetic STOPPED — surgical procedure aborted. Sevoflurane vaporizer removed; anesthesia machine flushed with high-flow O₂ for 10 minutes. Switched to TIVA (propofol + remifentanil). 100% O₂ at high flow rate. Dantrolene 2.5 mg/kg IV bolus (200 mg in this 80-kg adolescent); repeat dose 1 mg/kg q5 min until controlled — total ~ 10 mg/kg given. Aggressive cooling: cold IV fluids 1 L bolus, ice packs to groin/axillae, cooling blanket, gastric/bladder lavage with cold saline. Treat hyperkalemia: calcium gluconate 30 mg/kg IV, insulin 0.1 U/kg + glucose 0.5 g/kg, sodium bicarbonate 1 mEq/kg. Furosemide for urine output; monitor for compartment syndrome.

Stage 4 — Course

Within 30 minutes of dantrolene: EtCO₂ falling, temperature stabilizing. Within 2 hours: complete resolution of rigidity, normalized EtCO₂. Transferred to PICU for monitoring. Continued dantrolene infusion 1 mg/kg q6h × 24 hours (relapses 25% within 36 hours). Rhabdomyolysis management: aggressive IV fluids, urine alkalinization. CK peaked 60,000 then trended down over 5 days. Renal function preserved. Discharged day 7. RYR1 genetic testing: pathogenic mutation confirmed. Family screening: mother carrier; maternal uncle also positive. MedicAlert bracelet; future anesthesia: non-triggering only (TIVA, locals, regional).
Final Diagnosis: Malignant hyperthermia (RYR1 mutation) triggered by sevoflurane + succinylcholine during pediatric tonsillectomy; resolved with dantrolene and aggressive supportive care.
Plan:
  • RYR1 genetic counseling and family screening.
  • MedicAlert bracelet documenting MH susceptibility.
  • Future surgery: non-triggering anesthesia (TIVA, regional, local).
  • Anesthesiology team notified pre-operatively for any future procedures.
  • Dantrolene available on MH cart.
  • Family education about MHAUS resources.
Teaching points:
  • MH: RYR1/CACNA1S mutations triggered by halogenated volatile anesthetics and sux.
  • Masseter rigidity with sux is sentinel finding.
  • Hypercarbia is the earliest reliable sign.
  • Dantrolene 2.5 mg/kg IV bolus, repeat until controlled (up to 10+ mg/kg).
  • Recurrence in 24–36 hours — continued monitoring and prophylactic dantrolene.
  • MHAUS hotline 1-800-MH-HYPER for real-time support.
Case 67 — Pediatric Psychogenic Nonepileptic Seizures MODERATE

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 15-year-old girl has had 'seizures' for 6 months. Multiple ED visits; treated with escalating AEDs (levetiracetam, lacosamide, lamotrigine) without improvement. Episodes: gradual onset of generalized convulsion-like movements lasting 5–20 minutes, eyes closed with resistance to opening, asynchronous limb movements, no incontinence, no tongue biting, no postictal confusion. Often occur in school nurse's office or family room. PMH: depression, anxiety; reports recent family stress (parental divorce).
HR 90 | BP 110/70 | T 37.0°C | No focal deficits between episodes

Stage 2 — Workup

Video EEG monitoring 72 hours: 3 typical 'seizures' captured, all without electrographic correlate during episodes. Normal interictal EEG. MRI brain: normal. Cardiac monitoring: normal during events. Psychiatric evaluation: major depression with prominent anxiety; high adverse childhood experiences score.
How should diagnosis be communicated to family?
  • Honest, non-stigmatizing language: 'The episodes you are having are real, and your distress is real. The cause is psychological, not electrical. The good news is that the brain is healthy, but the treatment is different — we need to help with anxiety, stress, and depression rather than seizure medications.'
  • Avoid pejorative terms: 'pseudoseizures,' 'fake,' 'attention seeking.'
  • Frame as a recognized medical condition with effective treatment.
  • Engage family in supportive role.
  • Emphasize that this is a body-mind reaction to stress / trauma, not voluntary.
  • Acknowledge that prior AED treatment can cause harm and confusion — wean carefully.

Stage 3 — Management

Multidisciplinary care: pediatric neurology + adolescent psychiatry + psychology. Cognitive behavioral therapy (CBT) initiated weekly. Address triggers: school accommodations, family therapy for parental conflict, individual psychotherapy. AEDs tapered carefully over 8 weeks (one drug at a time with close monitoring for emergence of actual epilepsy). SSRI (sertraline) for depression. Trauma-focused therapy.

Stage 4 — Outcome

Over 6 months: episode frequency reduced from daily to one every 2–3 weeks. AEDs successfully discontinued without recurrence. Returns to school full-time. Long-term: continued mental health follow-up; CBT maintenance. 1-year outcome: only rare brief episodes during stress; functional in school and home.
Final Diagnosis: Psychogenic nonepileptic seizures (functional/conversion disorder) with comorbid major depression and anxiety.
Plan:
  • Multidisciplinary mental health care: psychiatry, psychology, CBT.
  • Address triggers: family therapy, school accommodations.
  • Pharmacotherapy: SSRI for depression/anxiety.
  • AEDs withdrawn (no electrographic basis).
  • Long-term mental health follow-up.
  • Family education and support.
Teaching points:
  • PNES: video EEG with typical event but no electrographic correlate is diagnostic.
  • Adolescent female with depression / anxiety / trauma history common.
  • Honest non-stigmatizing communication essential.
  • Treatment: CBT, psychotherapy, address triggers, treat comorbid mood disorders.
  • Avoid escalating AEDs.
  • 70% improve with appropriate care.
Case 68 — Pediatric Narcolepsy Type 1 with Cataplexy HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 13-year-old girl presents with 6 months of progressive severe daytime sleepiness, falling asleep in class, weight gain of 8 kg, and concerning 'collapse' episodes when laughing or surprised. Initially evaluated for depression and metabolic disorder; misdiagnosed with school refusal. Family history: paternal aunt with narcolepsy.
HR 80 | BP 105/65 | T 36.8°C | BMI elevated; otherwise normal exam

Stage 2 — Workup

Multiple sleep latency test (MSLT): mean sleep latency 4 minutes, 4 sleep-onset REM periods (SOREMPs) — diagnostic. CSF hypocretin-1: 50 pg/mL (markedly reduced; diagnostic threshold ≤ 110). HLA typing: DQB1*06:02 positive. Brain MRI: normal. Diagnosis: narcolepsy type 1 (with cataplexy).
Why is sodium oxybate considered first-line for narcolepsy with cataplexy?
  • Sodium oxybate (γ-hydroxybutyrate) consolidates nighttime sleep architecture.
  • Improves both excessive daytime sleepiness AND cataplexy simultaneously.
  • Approved for pediatric use (≥ 7 years).
  • Dosed at bedtime and 2.5–4 hours later (split nighttime dose).
  • Strict storage and dispensing (controlled substance).
  • Side effects: nausea, nocturnal enuresis, parasomnias, respiratory depression with alcohol or other CNS depressants.

Stage 3 — Management

Behavioral: scheduled naps (mid-morning + after-school 20 min naps), regular sleep schedule (bed at 10 PM, wake 7 AM), good sleep hygiene. Sodium oxybate initiated at low dose (3 g at bedtime, 3 g at 2:30 AM), titrated up to therapeutic over 4 weeks. Modafinil 100 mg AM added for residual EDS. Family education on warning signs and avoidance of alcohol. School accommodations: scheduled nap times, extended testing time, awareness of cataplexy triggers.

Stage 4 — Course

Within 6 weeks: cataplexy episodes reduced from daily to monthly; daytime alertness markedly improved; school performance recovered. Weight stabilized with intentional lifestyle changes. Long-term: ongoing sleep specialist follow-up; routine multidisciplinary care; possible future addition of pitolisant or stimulants based on need. Genetic counseling — autoimmune origin emphasized to distinguish from primary genetic disorder.
Final Diagnosis: Narcolepsy type 1 (with cataplexy) — pediatric onset; treated with sodium oxybate + modafinil + behavioral measures.
Plan:
  • Sodium oxybate at therapeutic dose; modafinil for residual EDS.
  • Scheduled naps and regular sleep schedule.
  • SSRIs/SNRIs for refractory cataplexy.
  • Driving counseling when age-appropriate.
  • School accommodations.
  • Long-term sleep specialist follow-up.
  • Avoid alcohol and CNS depressants with sodium oxybate.
Teaching points:
  • Narcolepsy type 1: EDS + cataplexy + sleep paralysis + hypnagogic hallucinations.
  • Autoimmune destruction of hypocretin neurons.
  • HLA DQB1*06:02 strongly associated.
  • Diagnostic: MSLT showing short sleep latency + ≥ 2 SOREMPs, OR low CSF hypocretin-1.
  • Sodium oxybate: most effective single agent for cataplexy and EDS.
  • Often misdiagnosed (school refusal, depression, ADHD).
Case 69 — Pediatric Neurosarcoidosis with Cranial Neuropathy HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 16-year-old presents with 4 weeks of progressive bilateral facial weakness, fatigue, intermittent fevers, and dry eyes. Examination: bilateral facial nerve palsies, mild parotid swelling, scattered erythematous plaques on arms. Past 3 months: episodes of anterior uveitis treated topically. CXR done for cough: hilar lymphadenopathy.
HR 95 | BP 110/70 | T 38.0°C | Bilateral CN VII palsy (House-Brackmann grade III); mild meningismus; parotid enlargement; cutaneous granulomatous-appearing plaques

Stage 2 — Workup

CSF: WBC 30 (90% lymphocytes), protein 0.9 g/L, glucose normal, oligoclonal bands negative. Serum ACE 95 (elevated). CSF ACE elevated. ANCA negative, ANA negative, AQP4 / MOG negative. MRI brain: leptomeningeal enhancement, multiple small white matter lesions, enhancement of bilateral facial nerves and seventh nerve canals. Bilateral hilar lymphadenopathy on CT chest. Skin biopsy from cutaneous plaque: non-caseating granulomas (diagnostic for sarcoidosis).
Why is biopsy essential rather than just imaging + ACE?
  • ACE elevation is non-specific (30% sensitivity, many false positives and negatives).
  • Imaging findings overlap with MS, NMOSD, TB, lymphoma.
  • Biopsy of accessible site (skin lesion, lymph node, conjunctiva) showing non-caseating granulomas is diagnostic.
  • Excludes infection (mycobacterial, fungal) and lymphoma.
  • Untreated tuberculosis empirically treated as sarcoidosis would be disastrous.

Stage 3 — Treatment

Initial high-dose IV methylprednisolone 30 mg/kg/day × 5 days, then transition to oral prednisone 1 mg/kg/day with slow taper over 6 months. Methotrexate 15 mg/m² PO weekly added as steroid-sparing after 1 month (allow steroid taper while maintaining disease control). Plaquenil for skin lesions. Multidisciplinary care: pediatric rheumatology, pulmonology, ophthalmology, neurology, dermatology. TB testing negative; quantiferon negative.

Stage 4 — Course

Bilateral facial palsy improving by week 4; complete recovery by month 3. Lymphadenopathy regressing. Long-term: methotrexate maintenance for 18–24 months; periodic ACE, imaging. Relapse risk; monitor for recurrence. Family education about chronic immunosuppression; vaccinations updated; calcium and vitamin D supplementation with calcium and 25-OH-D monitoring (sarcoidosis can cause hypercalcemia from active vitamin D production).
Final Diagnosis: Pediatric neurosarcoidosis with bilateral facial palsy, meningitis, and systemic features; biopsy-confirmed non-caseating granulomas.
Plan:
  • Long-term methotrexate maintenance 18–24 months.
  • Periodic ACE and imaging surveillance.
  • Calcium/25-OH-D monitoring (hypercalcemia risk).
  • Multidisciplinary follow-up: rheumatology, pulmonology, ophthalmology, neurology, dermatology.
  • Infliximab if methotrexate-refractory.
  • Vaccinations updated (avoid live vaccines on immunosuppression).
Teaching points:
  • Neurosarcoidosis: multisystem features (lungs, skin, eyes, salivary).
  • Cranial nerve palsies (especially CN VII bilateral) are characteristic.
  • Biopsy of accessible site for non-caseating granulomas is diagnostic.
  • ACE non-specific.
  • Treatment: corticosteroids + steroid-sparing (methotrexate, azathioprine); infliximab for refractory.
  • Long-term immunosuppression with monitoring.
Case 70 — Neuro-Behçet Disease in Adolescent HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 17-year-old presents with 6 weeks of progressive ataxia, diplopia, and dysarthria; preceded by a 1-year history of recurrent painful oral and genital ulcerations and three episodes of anterior uveitis. Family from a Mediterranean background. Past medical history significant for recurrent erythema nodosum.
HR 90 | BP 115/70 | T 37.8°C | GCS 14 | Bilateral horizontal gaze palsy, dysarthria, mild left-sided weakness, ataxia | Active oral aphthous ulcers, healing genital ulcers, pseudofolliculitis

Stage 2 — Diagnosis

MRI brain: brainstem T2/FLAIR hyperintensity involving pons and midbrain with edema; smaller lesions in basal ganglia and white matter. MRV: normal. CSF: lymphocytic pleocytosis (WBC 60), elevated protein 0.7 g/L. HLA-B51 positive. ISG (International Study Group) criteria for Behçet met (oral aphthous ulcers + genital ulcers + uveitis + skin lesions). Neuro-Behçet diagnosis confirmed.
Why avoid cyclosporine in neuro-Behçet?
  • Cyclosporine has been associated with neurologic exacerbation in neuro-Behçet.
  • Mechanism unclear but consistent in case reports.
  • Often controversial — some centers use cautiously.
  • Alternative immunosuppressives: azathioprine, mycophenolate, anti-TNF preferred.
  • Worth discussing with patient and weighing risks/benefits.

Stage 3 — Treatment

Initial high-dose IV methylprednisolone 30 mg/kg/day × 5 days, then oral prednisone 1 mg/kg/day with slow taper. Azathioprine 2 mg/kg/day initiated as steroid-sparing. Avoiding cyclosporine in neuro-Behçet (controversial association with neurologic worsening). For severe / refractory: infliximab 5 mg/kg IV initial loading then q8 weekly. Colchicine 1 mg/day for mucocutaneous features. Topical steroids for uveitis with ophthalmology.

Stage 4 — Course

Brainstem signs improving by week 4; resolved by month 3. Mucocutaneous ulcerations less frequent on colchicine. Maintenance therapy continued: azathioprine + colchicine + topical for skin. Multidisciplinary follow-up: rheumatology, neurology, ophthalmology, dermatology, gynecology (genital ulcers). Long-term relapse surveillance.
Final Diagnosis: Neuro-Behçet disease (brainstem syndrome) in adolescent with full systemic features.
Plan:
  • Long-term azathioprine + colchicine.
  • Anti-TNF (infliximab) escalation for refractory.
  • Avoid cyclosporine.
  • Multidisciplinary care: rheumatology, neurology, ophthalmology, dermatology.
  • Family genetic counseling (HLA-B51 risk allele).
  • Long-term relapse surveillance with MRI.
Teaching points:
  • Behçet: systemic vasculitis with oral and genital ulcers, uveitis, skin lesions.
  • Neuro-Behçet: brainstem syndrome characteristic; basal ganglia involvement.
  • HLA-B51 association.
  • Treatment: corticosteroids + steroid-sparing (azathioprine, mycophenolate, anti-TNF).
  • Avoid cyclosporine in neuro-Behçet.
  • Long-term immunosuppression with relapse surveillance.
Case 71 — Pediatric Optic Neuritis with Anti-MOG Positivity MODERATE

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 12-year-old girl presents with 4 days of progressive painful vision loss in the left eye, particularly with eye movement. Reports preceded by viral illness 2 weeks ago. Fundoscopy: mild optic disc edema. Visual acuity left 20/200, right 20/20. Color desaturation in left eye.
HR 85 | BP 105/65 | T 36.9°C | Left eye 20/200, RAPD positive on left, optic disc edema | Right eye normal | No focal weakness, no other neurologic findings

Stage 2 — Workup

MRI brain and orbits with fat-suppression: enhancing left optic nerve along entire length; one small white matter lesion (non-specific). Visual evoked potentials: prolonged latency left, normal right. CSF: WBC 15 (lymphocytic), protein 0.4 g/L, glucose normal, oligoclonal bands NEGATIVE. AQP4-IgG negative; MOG-IgG positive (moderate titer). Diagnosis: anti-MOG antibody-associated optic neuritis.
Why does anti-MOG positivity affect long-term planning?
  • Anti-MOG associated disease (MOGAD) is distinct from MS and NMOSD.
  • Often monophasic but can relapse.
  • Avoid MS DMTs (interferons, natalizumab, fingolimod) — can worsen MOGAD.
  • Long-term: monitoring for relapse with serial MRI and MOG antibody titer.
  • Maintenance immunosuppression (rituximab, MMF, IVIG) for relapsing course.
  • Generally favorable visual prognosis with prompt treatment.

Stage 3 — Treatment

High-dose IV methylprednisolone 30 mg/kg/day (max 1 g) × 5 days, followed by oral prednisone taper over 6 weeks. Vision recovers to 20/40 by day 5; 20/25 by month 1. Continued surveillance for relapse.

Stage 4 — Long-term

3 months later: new bilateral optic neuritis episode. Re-treated with IV methylprednisolone + IVIG. Now considered relapsing MOGAD; long-term immunosuppression with rituximab 375 mg/m² q6 months initiated. Family education about relapse signs. Ophthalmology and neurology follow-up. School accommodations during recovery periods.
Final Diagnosis: Anti-MOG antibody-associated relapsing optic neuritis (MOGAD).
Plan:
  • Acute relapses: high-dose IV methylprednisolone + IVIG.
  • Long-term immunosuppression: rituximab for relapsing course.
  • Avoid MS DMTs (interferons, natalizumab, fingolimod).
  • Annual ophthalmology examination.
  • Serial MOG antibody titer.
  • Multidisciplinary follow-up.
Teaching points:
  • Pediatric optic neuritis: more often bilateral and MOG-positive than adult.
  • Anti-MOG defines distinct disease (MOGAD).
  • Anti-AQP4 / MOG screening essential.
  • Treatment: high-dose corticosteroids first-line.
  • MOGAD: avoid MS DMTs.
  • Long-term immunosuppression for relapsing course.
Case 72 — Diffuse Intrinsic Pontine Glioma — Multidisciplinary Care HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 7-year-old boy presents with 6 weeks of progressive diplopia, gait imbalance, slurred speech, and recent right-sided weakness. Morning headaches and vomiting over the past 2 weeks. School noticed personality changes.
HR 95 | BP 110/70 | T 36.8°C | GCS 15 | Right CN VI palsy, right CN VII partial weakness, mild ataxia, right hemiparesis (4/5)

Stage 2 — Workup

MRI brain: diffuse expansion of pons with T2/FLAIR hyperintensity; no enhancement; encases basilar artery; minimal mass effect on fourth ventricle. CT chest/abdomen/pelvis (staging): normal. Stereotactic biopsy considered with parents — they opt to proceed for molecular characterization (modern practice). Biopsy returns: high-grade glioma with H3 K27M mutation. Diagnosis: diffuse intrinsic pontine glioma (H3 K27M-mutant diffuse midline glioma, WHO grade IV).
Why is biopsy increasingly performed in DIPG?
  • Modern molecular characterization is essential for clinical trial enrollment.
  • H3 K27M, ACVR1, EZHIP, and other mutations have therapeutic targets in trials.
  • Safety has improved with experienced centers and stereotactic technique.
  • Yield is high.
  • Provides accurate diagnosis (rarely a benign lesion is misdiagnosed).
  • Risk-benefit favors biopsy in established centers.

Stage 3 — Treatment

Multidisciplinary discussion: pediatric oncology, neuro-oncology, neurosurgery, radiation oncology, palliative care, social work, chaplaincy. Family meeting with honest discussion of poor prognosis (median survival 9–12 months) and goals of care. Focal radiotherapy initiated 54 Gy over 30 fractions (provides temporary clinical improvement). Concurrent enrollment in clinical trial of ONC201 (DRD2 antagonist; emerging activity in DIPG). Dexamethasone for peritumoral edema (start 0.5 mg/kg, taper). Palliative care embedded throughout.

Stage 4 — Course

By end of radiation (6 weeks): clinical improvement (regained function, less weakness, fewer headaches). 'Honeymoon period' lasts about 3 months. Disease progression at month 4: new symptoms, MRI shows growth. Continued clinical trial therapy. Discussion with family about transitioning to comfort care as disease progresses. Hospice involvement. Patient dies at home at 10 months from diagnosis. Family supported through bereavement.
Final Diagnosis: Diffuse intrinsic pontine glioma (H3 K27M-mutant diffuse midline glioma, WHO IV); managed with focal radiation, clinical trial, and palliative care.
Process:
  • Multidisciplinary care from diagnosis.
  • Palliative care embedded from outset.
  • Focal radiation as standard.
  • Clinical trial enrollment when available.
  • Steroid for peritumoral edema.
  • Family-centered care with honest communication.
  • Hospice when goals shift to comfort.
  • Bereavement support continued.
Teaching points:
  • DIPG: H3 K27M-mutant diffuse midline glioma; WHO grade IV.
  • Modern biopsy at experienced centers safe and informative.
  • Focal radiation provides temporary improvement but no survival benefit.
  • Clinical trials essential for new therapies.
  • Palliative care from diagnosis.
  • Median survival 9–12 months.
  • Family-centered, honest, multidisciplinary care.
Case 73 — Posterior Fossa Ependymoma in Young Child MODERATE

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 4-year-old presents with 8 weeks of progressive morning headaches, vomiting, and gait imbalance. Family noticed she tilts her head to one side ('torticollis'). Recent decline in fine motor skills. Otherwise developmentally on track previously.
HR 90 | BP 100/65 | T 36.9°C | GCS 15 | Mild bilateral papilledema | Truncal ataxia | Head tilt (torticollis) | Bilateral CN VI palsy mild

Stage 2 — Imaging

CT head: large posterior fossa mass with hydrocephalus (lateral and third ventricles enlarged). MRI: 5 cm posterior fossa mass arising from fourth ventricle floor, extending through foramen of Magendie ('plastic' tumor), heterogeneous enhancement, calcifications, surrounding edema. Hydrocephalus present.
Why is EVD essential before surgical resection?
  • Hydrocephalus is potentially life-threatening; CSF diversion provides immediate decompression.
  • Reduces ICP and provides safe surgical window.
  • Allows for stabilization and pre-operative optimization.
  • May be removed after definitive resection if normal CSF flow restored.
  • Some patients continue to need VP shunt post-resection (residual hydrocephalus from fourth ventricular scarring).

Stage 3 — Surgical Management

Emergent EVD placement at admission for hydrocephalus. After 48 hours stabilization, multidisciplinary planning: pediatric neurosurgery, neuro-oncology, anesthesia, ICU. Surgical resection with goal of gross total resection. Surgery performed: GTR achieved with intraoperative monitoring of cranial nerves. Pathology: ependymoma (WHO grade II; methylation classifier subtype PF-A — younger, worse prognosis). Posterior fossa syndrome (cerebellar mutism syndrome) develops post-op as expected.

Stage 4 — Post-op Course and Adjuvant Therapy

Posterior fossa syndrome: mutism develops on POD2, ataxia, dysphagia, emotional lability. Speech and swallow therapy initiated. Posterior fossa syndrome partially resolves over 4 months. EVD weaned to clamp trial then removed without need for VP shunt. Adjuvant focal radiation 59 Gy/30 fractions completed by month 4 post-op (delayed slightly due to posterior fossa syndrome). Surveillance MRI at month 3, 6, 12 — no recurrence at 12 months. Long-term: regular surveillance, neuropsychology assessments, growth and endocrine monitoring (radiation late effects).
Final Diagnosis: Posterior fossa ependymoma (WHO grade II, methylation classifier PF-A) with hydrocephalus and posterior fossa syndrome post-op; treated with EVD + GTR + focal radiation.
Plan:
  • Surveillance MRI: every 3 months × 1 year, then every 6 months × 4 years, then annually.
  • Neuropsychology assessments at 6, 12, 24 months.
  • Endocrine surveillance (growth hormone, thyroid, pubertal development).
  • Educational accommodations.
  • Audiology surveillance (ototoxicity risk).
  • Multidisciplinary follow-up.
  • Multiple recurrence risk over years — long-term monitoring.
Teaching points:
  • Posterior fossa ependymoma: most common pediatric ependymoma site.
  • Fourth ventricle mass extending through foramina ('plastic' tumor).
  • Hydrocephalus common — emergent CSF diversion.
  • Gross total resection: most important prognostic factor.
  • Adjuvant focal radiation 54–59 Gy.
  • PF-A subtype: younger, worse prognosis.
  • Posterior fossa syndrome anticipated post-op.
  • Late effects of radiation: cognitive, endocrine, secondary malignancy.
Case 74 — Subdural Empyema Complicating Frontal Sinusitis HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 14-year-old presents with 5 days of progressive frontal headache, fever, and 2 days of left-sided weakness. History of recent sinusitis treated with oral amoxicillin without improvement. On arrival: GCS 12, neck stiffness, left hemiparesis.
HR 120 | BP 130/85 | T 39.4°C | GCS 12 | Left hemiparesis, papilledema, neck stiffness

Stage 2 — Imaging

CT: opacification of frontal sinuses with bony erosion. MRI with DWI: thin crescentic fluid collection along right frontal convexity with peripheral enhancement and restricted diffusion (pus pattern). Mass effect on right hemisphere with 4 mm midline shift.
Why is this a neurosurgical emergency?
  • Subdural empyema spreads rapidly along the subdural space.
  • Mortality high without surgical drainage.
  • Antibiotic-alone management fails.
  • Early surgery + IV antibiotics is curative.
  • Source control (ENT for sinus) essential.

Stage 3 — Management

Emergency craniotomy and drainage of subdural empyema. Functional ENT surgery for frontal sinusitis drainage same setting. Empiric IV vancomycin + ceftriaxone + metronidazole; cultures grow streptococci and anaerobes. Levetiracetam for seizure prophylaxis. ICP management. cEEG monitoring.

Stage 4 — Course

By day 5: clinical improvement, ICP normalized, sedation weaning. Day 14: extubated; mild residual left hemiparesis improving. Antibiotics narrowed per cultures to ampicillin + metronidazole. Total IV antibiotic course 6 weeks. Discharged day 21 with continued PT/OT.
Final Diagnosis: Subdural empyema complicating frontal sinusitis; treated with emergency craniotomy + IV antibiotics 6 weeks.
Plan:
  • Total IV antibiotics 6 weeks.
  • ENT follow-up for sinus disease management.
  • Antiepileptic continued 6 months.
  • Rehabilitation for residual hemiparesis.
  • Long-term neurology follow-up.
Teaching points:
  • Subdural empyema: emergent surgical drainage required.
  • DWI distinguishes pus from sterile collection.
  • Source control critical (ENT for sinus / mastoid).
  • Empiric vanco + CTX + metronidazole.
  • Mortality 10–20% with prompt surgery; high without.
Case 75 — Tuberous Sclerosis with Infantile Spasms HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 6-month-old infant presents with clusters of brief flexor jerks during feedings (cluster of 5–10 spasms, 3 cluster groups daily). Family history: father with TSC (skin findings, history of seizures). Examination: hypopigmented macules ('ash-leaf spots'); developmental plateau.
HR 130 | BP 90/55 | T 37.0°C | Hypopigmented macules; subtle infantile spasms during evaluation; mild developmental delay

Stage 2 — Workup

EEG: hypsarrhythmia (chaotic high-voltage slow waves with multifocal spikes — classic pattern in West syndrome). MRI brain: bilateral cortical tubers, subependymal nodules. Skin examination: ash-leaf macules on trunk and limbs. Cardiac echo: bilateral rhabdomyomas. Renal ultrasound: bilateral angiomyolipomas. TSC1/TSC2 sequencing: pathogenic TSC2 mutation (more severe phenotype).
Why is vigabatrin first-line for TSC-associated infantile spasms?
  • Vigabatrin is superior to ACTH in TSC subset (UKISS subgroup analysis and subsequent studies).
  • Acts on GABA-transaminase to enhance GABAergic inhibition.
  • Reduces spasms and underlying epileptic encephalopathy.
  • Long-term cognitive benefit when spasms controlled early.
  • Mandatory ophthalmologic monitoring for irreversible peripheral visual field defects.

Stage 3 — Treatment

Vigabatrin 50 mg/kg/day, titrated to 100 mg/kg/day; baseline ophthalmologic / ERG examination, then q3 months. Within 2 weeks: spasms reduced 70%; EEG showing hypsarrhythmia resolution. Continue 6 months then reassess. Multidisciplinary TSC clinic enrollment (neurology, cardiology, nephrology, dermatology, developmental pediatrics, social work).

Stage 4 — Long-term

Spasms recur at 9 months despite vigabatrin; ketogenic diet added with significant response. By age 3: focal seizures emerge from cortical tubers; pre-surgical workup identifies single dominant tuber → focal resection with seizure freedom achieved. By age 10: SEGA noted on surveillance MRI; everolimus 4.5 mg/m²/day started — SEGA regresses. Cognitive function in average range with educational supports. Family screening: paternal TSC2 mutation confirmed; younger sibling carrier.
Final Diagnosis: Tuberous sclerosis complex (TSC2) with West syndrome (infantile spasms + hypsarrhythmia + developmental regression); managed with vigabatrin → ketogenic diet → focal resection → everolimus for SEGA.
Plan:
  • Vigabatrin with ophthalmology monitoring.
  • Ketogenic diet adjunct.
  • Surgical evaluation for focal seizures from dominant tubers.
  • mTOR inhibitor (everolimus) for SEGA, AML, refractory epilepsy.
  • Multidisciplinary TSC clinic.
  • Family genetic counseling.
Teaching points:
  • TSC infantile spasms: vigabatrin first-line (vs ACTH first-line for non-TSC).
  • Ophthalmologic monitoring for vigabatrin toxicity.
  • mTOR inhibitors (everolimus, sirolimus) — disease-modifying for TSC.
  • Multidisciplinary TSC clinic.
  • Focal resection for seizures from dominant tuber.
Case 76 — X-linked Adrenoleukodystrophy in 8-Year-Old HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

An 8-year-old boy presents with 3 months of declining school performance, irritability, and recent visual / auditory complaints. Parents thought he was being defiant. Recent fatigue with skin hyperpigmentation. Family history: maternal uncle died at 12 of unspecified neurologic disease.
HR 90 | BP 95/55 | T 36.9°C | GCS 15 | Skin hyperpigmentation; mild bilateral hearing loss; visual field defect on confrontation; cognitive testing impaired; orthostatic blood pressure drop

Stage 2 — Workup

Serum cortisol low; ACTH markedly elevated (Addison disease confirmed). Serum VLCFA: C26:0 elevated, C24:0/C22:0 elevated, C26:0/C22:0 elevated (X-ALD pattern). ABCD1 sequencing: pathogenic mutation. MRI brain: posterior parieto-occipital white matter T2 hyperintensity with peripheral enhancement and surrounding edema. Loes score: 8.
Why is the Loes score critical for HSCT timing?
  • Loes score quantifies extent of cerebral demyelination on MRI (range 0–34).
  • Loes ≤ 9 with neurologic function preserved → HSCT candidate with potential to halt progression.
  • Loes > 10 or significant cognitive / motor decline → HSCT less effective; gene therapy emerging.
  • Without treatment: rapidly progressive — typically fatal within 2–5 years.
  • Newborn screening now available in many states catches pre-symptomatic disease.

Stage 3 — Treatment

Allogeneic HSCT planned with matched sibling donor (sibling unaffected). Pre-HSCT: stress-dose hydrocortisone for Addison disease confirmed; fludrocortisone for mineralocorticoid replacement. Adrenal crisis prevention with sick-day protocol. HSCT performed at 9 months post-diagnosis; engraftment successful.

Stage 4 — Long-term

By 1 year post-HSCT: MRI stable (no progression); cognitive function stable; school accommodations. Lifelong hydrocortisone + fludrocortisone for Addison's. Family genetic counseling: mother carrier; sister tested negative; cousin (male) tested with X-ALD genotype but no MRI changes yet — enrolled in newborn screening / surveillance with HSCT planning if MRI changes detected. Long-term ALD clinic follow-up.
Final Diagnosis: X-linked adrenoleukodystrophy (ABCD1 mutation) with childhood cerebral form (Loes score 8) and Addison's disease; HSCT halted neurologic progression.
Plan:
  • Lifelong hydrocortisone + fludrocortisone (adrenal replacement).
  • Sick-day protocol; emergency hydrocortisone at home and school.
  • HSCT follow-up; surveillance MRI.
  • Educational accommodations.
  • Family genetic counseling and cascade testing.
  • Multidisciplinary care: endocrinology, neurology, audiology, ophthalmology, social work.
Teaching points:
  • X-ALD: ABCD1 mutation; VLCFA elevation; X-linked recessive.
  • Loes score quantifies cerebral disease severity.
  • HSCT only effective in early disease (Loes < 10).
  • Gene therapy (elivaldogene autotemcel) emerging for selected patients.
  • Addison disease in 80%; lifelong replacement.
  • Newborn screening allows pre-symptomatic intervention.
Case 77 — Niemann-Pick Type C with Vertical Gaze Palsy HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 5-year-old presents with 1 year of progressive developmental regression, recurrent falls, and clumsiness. Parents note he 'can't look down anymore' and 'collapses when laughing.' Past medical history: prolonged neonatal jaundice; mild hepatosplenomegaly throughout childhood.
HR 90 | BP 100/65 | T 36.9°C | GCS 14 | Vertical supranuclear gaze palsy (downgaze impaired more than upgaze); preserved horizontal eye movements; gelastic cataplexy episodes (laughter triggers brief tone loss); ataxia; mild hepatosplenomegaly

Stage 2 — Workup

MRI brain: mild cerebellar atrophy. Plasma oxysterols (cholestane-3β,5α,6β-triol and 7-ketocholesterol): elevated. Cultured fibroblast filipin staining: positive (free cholesterol accumulation pattern). NPC1 sequencing: compound heterozygous pathogenic mutations. Diagnosis: Niemann-Pick disease type C.
Why does vertical gaze palsy occur in NPC?
  • NPC affects cholesterol trafficking in neurons broadly.
  • Vertical gaze center (rostral interstitial nucleus of medial longitudinal fasciculus) particularly vulnerable.
  • Downgaze impaired first, then upgaze — highly specific clinical sign.
  • Distinguishes from other lysosomal storage disorders without this pattern.
  • Should prompt NPC workup in any pediatric patient with vertical gaze palsy.

Stage 3 — Treatment

Miglustat (substrate reduction therapy with glucosylceramide synthase inhibitor) initiated 200 mg TID (adult dose; pediatric per body surface area). Side effect counseling: GI symptoms, weight loss, tremor. Cataplexy management: clomipramine 25 mg PO daily; counseling about laughter triggers. PT, OT, speech therapy. Educational accommodations. Family genetic counseling.

Stage 4 — Long-term

By 6 months on miglustat: clinical progression slowed; cataplexy reduced with clomipramine. Cognitive decline continues but more slowly than expected. Long-term: continued miglustat; multidisciplinary care; consider gene therapy or arimoclomol trials. Family genetic counseling: autosomal recessive; sibling testing.
Final Diagnosis: Niemann-Pick disease type C (NPC1 compound heterozygous mutations); treated with miglustat substrate reduction therapy.
Plan:
  • Long-term miglustat substrate reduction therapy.
  • Cataplexy management with clomipramine.
  • Multidisciplinary care.
  • Consider emerging therapies (arimoclomol, gene therapy trials).
  • Family genetic counseling.
  • Sibling testing.
Teaching points:
  • NPC: NPC1/NPC2 mutations; intracellular cholesterol trafficking defect.
  • Vertical supranuclear gaze palsy is highly characteristic.
  • Gelastic cataplexy (laughter-induced tone loss).
  • Filipin staining is diagnostic.
  • Miglustat slows neurologic progression.
  • Distinct from NPC-A/B (acid sphingomyelinase).
Case 78 — SMA Type 1 with Pre-symptomatic Treatment HARD

Stage 1 — Newborn Screening Positive

A male neonate is detected by newborn screening (NBS) with bi-allelic SMN1 deletion (no SMN1 copies). SMN2 copy number: 2 copies (associated with SMA type 1 phenotype historically — severe). Examination at day 7: normal tone and reflexes; no respiratory compromise; alert and active.
HR 130 | BP 75/45 | T 36.8°C | Normal tone; normal reflexes; intact suck; no respiratory distress | Genetic: SMN1 deletion homozygous, SMN2 2 copies

Stage 2 — Decision and Treatment

Multidisciplinary discussion: SMA-1 phenotype expected with 2 SMN2 copies; pre-symptomatic treatment offers near-normal developmental trajectory. Three options discussed with family: nusinersen (intrathecal), onasemnogene abeparvovec (single IV gene therapy), risdiplam (oral). Family chooses onasemnogene abeparvovec given single-dose advantage.
What advantages does pre-symptomatic treatment offer?
  • SMA disease progression is irreversible once motor neurons are lost.
  • Pre-symptomatic treatment (before clinical onset) preserves motor neurons.
  • Achieves near-normal motor milestones in studies (NURTURE for nusinersen, SPR1NT for onasemnogene).
  • Newborn screening enables identification before symptom onset.
  • Time window is critical — treat ASAP after diagnosis.

Stage 3 — Onasemnogene Abeparvovec Administration

Pre-treatment workup: weight (3.5 kg — within weight limit), AAV9 antibody screen (negative — eligible). Pre-medication: prednisolone 1 mg/kg/day starting 1 day before infusion through 30 days post (immunomodulation to prevent hepatotoxicity). Onasemnogene abeparvovec single IV infusion 1.1 × 10¹⁴ vg/kg administered. Hepatic function, platelet count, troponin monitored daily × 6 weeks. Mild transient AST/ALT elevation managed with steroid extension. No myocarditis or thrombotic microangiopathy.

Stage 4 — Outcomes

By age 6 months: sits independently. By age 12 months: stands with support. By age 18 months: walks unassisted. Achievement of normal motor milestones — transformed outcome from historical SMA-1 trajectory. Continued physiotherapy, multidisciplinary follow-up. Family education and emotional support. Long-term surveillance for late motor decline.
Final Diagnosis: SMA type 1 (SMN1 deletion, 2 SMN2 copies) detected by newborn screening; pre-symptomatic gene therapy with onasemnogene abeparvovec; achieved normal motor milestones.
Plan:
  • Pre-medication and post-infusion monitoring (AST/ALT, platelets, troponin).
  • Long-term neuromuscular follow-up.
  • Multidisciplinary care: neurology, pulmonology, PT/OT, nutrition, orthopedics.
  • Surveillance for late motor decline.
  • Family education and counseling.
Teaching points:
  • Newborn screening for SMA identifies pre-symptomatic infants.
  • SMN2 copy number predicts phenotype severity (2 copies → SMA-1).
  • Pre-symptomatic treatment achieves near-normal motor outcomes.
  • Three disease-modifying therapies: nusinersen (IT), onasemnogene (IV gene therapy), risdiplam (oral).
  • Onasemnogene: single dose; weight limit; immunomodulation pre/post.
Case 79 — Anti-MuSK Myasthenia Crisis in Adolescent HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 16-year-old girl presents with 3 months of progressive bulbar weakness — dysphagia, dysarthria, neck flexor weakness, mild ptosis. Acute deterioration over 48 hours: cannot swallow secretions, hoarse voice, weakness. ED transferred to PICU. NIF –20 cmH₂O, FVC 18 mL/kg, mild dyspnea.
HR 110 | BP 130/80 | T 37.0°C | GCS 15 | Severe bulbar weakness; respiratory distress; neck flexor 1/5; tongue atrophy

Stage 2 — Diagnosis

Acetylcholine receptor antibody NEGATIVE. Anti-MuSK antibody POSITIVE. Repetitive nerve stimulation: 25% decrement at 3 Hz. Edrophonium test cautious response. CXR normal (no thymoma — less commonly associated with MuSK MG). Diagnosis: anti-MuSK myasthenia gravis with bulbar crisis.
Why is pyridostigmine cautious in MuSK MG?
  • Pyridostigmine often less effective in MuSK MG than AChR MG.
  • Can paradoxically worsen weakness in some MuSK patients.
  • Cholinergic crisis (excess acetylcholine) more concerning.
  • Start low dose; monitor closely; discontinue if worsening.
  • Distinguishes from AChR MG where pyridostigmine is mainstay.

Stage 3 — Acute Crisis Management

Elective intubation for bulbar dysfunction and declining FVC. Pyridostigmine NOT used (MuSK MG caution). IVIG 2 g/kg over 5 days. Plasma exchange 5 sessions every other day (rapid improvement in MuSK MG). Methylprednisolone 1 g IV × 5 days, then oral prednisone 1 mg/kg/day. By day 7: bulbar weakness improving; FVC up to 30 mL/kg; extubated on day 9.

Stage 4 — Long-term

Rituximab 375 mg/m² weekly × 4 doses initiated as steroid-sparing maintenance (especially effective in MuSK MG). By 3 months: prednisone tapered; clinical remission. Long-term: surveillance for relapse; rituximab maintenance every 6 months; periodic MuSK antibody titer. Avoid magnesium, aminoglycosides, fluoroquinolones, β-blockers, neuromuscular blockers (worsen MG). Family education and emergency action plan.
Final Diagnosis: Anti-MuSK myasthenia gravis with bulbar crisis; treated with IVIG + plasma exchange + corticosteroids; rituximab for long-term maintenance.
Plan:
  • Rituximab maintenance every 6 months.
  • Prednisone slow taper.
  • Avoid contraindicated drugs (magnesium, aminoglycosides, certain antibiotics).
  • Emergency action plan (early ER if respiratory or bulbar worsening).
  • Annual neurology follow-up.
  • MedicAlert bracelet.
  • Reproductive counseling for future pregnancy.
Teaching points:
  • MuSK MG: 40% of AChR-negative MG.
  • Bulbar / respiratory predominance.
  • Pyridostigmine often less effective and can worsen.
  • Rituximab particularly effective.
  • Thymectomy less clearly beneficial than in AChR MG.
  • IVIG / plasma exchange for crisis.
Case 80 — Post-Varicella Cerebellitis MODERATE

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 5-year-old presents 12 days after varicella infection with sudden onset truncal ataxia, unable to stand without falling, dysmetria of arms, dysarthria. Alert; intact cognition; no fever now (febrile 1 week ago during varicella).
HR 95 | BP 95/55 | T 36.8°C | GCS 15 | Marked truncal ataxia, intention tremor, dysmetria; intact cranial nerves; no encephalopathy

Stage 2 — Workup

MRI brain: subtle cerebellar T2 hyperintensity with mild cerebellar swelling; no mass effect. CSF: WBC 25 (lymphocytic), protein 0.5 g/L, glucose normal; varicella PCR negative (post-infectious). No tumor; no hemorrhage. Drug screen negative. Differential: post-varicella cerebellitis (most likely), brainstem stroke (rare), tumor (excluded).
What is the risk of cerebellar swelling progression?
  • Rare but life-threatening complication.
  • Massive cerebellar swelling → obstructive hydrocephalus from fourth ventricular compression + brainstem compression.
  • Requires emergent neurosurgical decompression (suboccipital craniectomy and/or EVD).
  • Recognition: declining LOC, signs of raised ICP, focal brainstem signs.
  • Serial neurologic exams + imaging if any concerning change.

Stage 3 — Management

Inpatient observation given marked ataxia and swelling on MRI. Supportive care. Hydration. PT to prevent contractures. Family education. IV methylprednisolone considered but withheld given moderate severity and natural history of recovery. Serial neurologic exams q4h initially.

Stage 4 — Recovery

By day 5: ataxia mildly improved; able to stand briefly. By week 2: walking with support; by week 4: walking unassisted. By month 3: complete recovery clinically. Long-term: no sequelae.
Final Diagnosis: Acute post-varicella cerebellitis; self-limited recovery over weeks.
Plan:
  • Supportive care.
  • PT to maintain function during recovery.
  • Family education about prognosis.
  • Outpatient follow-up.
  • Watch for late post-infectious complications (rare).
Teaching points:
  • Post-varicella cerebellitis: most common pediatric cerebellitis.
  • 1–3 weeks after varicella infection.
  • Post-infectious immune-mediated (not active infection).
  • Most cases self-limited with full recovery.
  • Watch for cerebellar swelling — rare but emergent surgical decompression if obstructive hydrocephalus.
Case 81 — Functional Gait Disorder in Adolescent MODERATE

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 14-year-old girl presents with 3 months of progressive 'unable to walk' — gait variable, sometimes uses wheelchair, sometimes walks normally with distraction. Multiple ED visits without clear diagnosis. AEDs prescribed for suspected seizure-related fall (without clear seizure). Sleep good. Academic decline. Family conflict at home (parental separation).
HR 80 | BP 110/65 | T 36.9°C | GCS 15 | Variable gait — give-way pattern, dramatic falls without injury, normal between episodes | Positive Hoover sign (give-way leg corrects when contralateral leg pushed against resistance) | Inconsistent strength testing | Normal reflexes; no focal deficits when distracted

Stage 2 — Workup

MRI brain and spine: normal. EEG: normal. EMG/NCS: normal. Cardiac monitoring: normal. Bloodwork: normal. Multiple positive clinical signs of functional gait disorder: Hoover sign, distractibility, variability, give-way pattern. Psychiatric evaluation: depression with adjustment disorder; high family stress.
Why are positive clinical signs (Hoover, distractibility) important now?
  • Functional neurologic disorder diagnosis has shifted from 'diagnosis of exclusion' to 'diagnosis with positive features.'
  • Hoover sign: when patient attempts to lift weak leg, normal leg presses down. When patient is asked to push down with normal leg against resistance, the 'weak' leg involuntarily corrects.
  • Variability with distraction is hallmark.
  • Recognizing positive signs avoids prolonged unnecessary investigation.
  • Confident communication of diagnosis to family is key to recovery.

Stage 3 — Management

Multidisciplinary team: pediatric neurology + psychiatry + physiotherapy with motor retraining + family therapy. Honest, non-stigmatizing communication: 'The symptoms are real and disabling, but the cause is a problem of how the brain and body are communicating. The good news is that this is treatable with physical therapy and addressing stress.' AEDs withdrawn carefully. CBT initiated. Family therapy for parental separation. School accommodations during recovery.

Stage 4 — Outcome

Over 6 months: gait recovers; back to normal activities. Episodes of stress-related symptoms still occur but quickly self-managed with CBT skills. Returned to school. Long-term: continued mental health follow-up.
Final Diagnosis: Functional (psychogenic) gait disorder with comorbid depression and family stress.
Plan:
  • Multidisciplinary mental health care.
  • Physiotherapy with motor retraining.
  • Family therapy.
  • School accommodations.
  • Long-term mental health follow-up.
  • Family education about diagnosis and recovery.
Teaching points:
  • Functional neurologic disorders are diagnosed by positive clinical features.
  • Hoover sign, distractibility, variability are key.
  • Honest non-stigmatizing communication essential.
  • Multidisciplinary care with PT motor retraining, CBT, address triggers.
  • Favorable outcomes with appropriate treatment.
Case 82 — Cerebral Aspergillosis in Immunocompromised Patient HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 14-year-old post HSCT for acute lymphoblastic leukemia (day +60) on immunosuppression presents with new focal seizure and headache. Recent prolonged neutropenia (now resolving). Chest X-ray: nodular pulmonary infiltrates. Blood cultures pending.
HR 110 | BP 110/65 | T 38.4°C | GCS 13 post-ictal | Focal motor seizure (right arm)

Stage 2 — Workup

MRI brain: multiple small ring-enhancing lesions in bilateral cortices and basal ganglia with surrounding edema. CT chest: bilateral nodular infiltrates with halo signs (suggestive of angioinvasive fungal). Galactomannan elevated in serum and BAL. Aspergillus fumigatus isolated from BAL. CSF: not done initially (LP deferred for mass effect; bedside US negative for shift).
Why is voriconazole first-line over amphotericin?
  • Voriconazole superior in head-to-head trial against amphotericin B for invasive aspergillosis (Herbrecht NEJM 2002).
  • Better CNS penetration than amphotericin.
  • Oral and IV formulations; bioavailability excellent.
  • Therapeutic drug monitoring (target trough 1–5.5 mg/L).
  • Side effects: hepatotoxicity, visual disturbances (transient), photosensitivity, CYP3A4 interactions.
  • Isavuconazole alternative with less drug interactions and toxicity.

Stage 3 — Management

Voriconazole 9 mg/kg IV q12h × 2 doses then 8 mg/kg IV q12h; therapeutic drug monitoring with target trough 1–5.5 mg/L. Reduce calcineurin inhibitor (interaction with voriconazole via CYP3A4). Levetiracetam load + maintenance for seizures. cEEG monitoring. Optimize immunosuppression — reduce as feasible. Granulocyte colony-stimulating factor for neutropenia.

Stage 4 — Course

Repeat MRI at 4 weeks: lesions stable, edema reduced. By 8 weeks: lesions resolving. Transitioned to oral voriconazole. Total antifungal course planned 6–12 months depending on response. Pulmonary lesions also resolving. Long-term: surveillance for recurrence; maintain antifungal prophylaxis until immune reconstitution.
Final Diagnosis: Cerebral aspergillosis with pulmonary involvement in post-HSCT immunocompromised patient; treated with voriconazole.
Plan:
  • Voriconazole 6–12 months with therapeutic drug monitoring.
  • Antifungal prophylaxis until immune reconstitution.
  • Antiepileptic for 12 months.
  • Multidisciplinary follow-up: infectious disease, oncology, neurology.
  • Watch for fungal IRIS during immune recovery.
  • Surveillance imaging.
Teaching points:
  • Cerebral aspergillosis: severe complication in immunocompromised.
  • Voriconazole is first-line (CNS-penetrating, superior efficacy).
  • Liposomal amphotericin B alternative.
  • Therapeutic drug monitoring essential.
  • Address underlying immunosuppression.
  • Mortality 50–70% despite treatment.
Case 83 — Pediatric MS First Presentation HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 15-year-old girl presents with 5 days of monocular blurry vision, then 2 weeks later, an episode of unilateral hand numbness lasting 3 days resolving spontaneously. No encephalopathy. No prodromal viral illness. Family history: maternal aunt with MS.
HR 80 | BP 105/65 | T 36.9°C | GCS 15 | Mild RAPD left eye; visual acuity 20/40 left, 20/20 right | Otherwise neurologically intact

Stage 2 — Workup

MRI brain: multiple periventricular ovoid T2 hyperintense lesions, some with gadolinium enhancement, oriented perpendicular to ventricles (Dawson fingers); juxtacortical and infratentorial lesions also present. MRI orbits: enhancement of left optic nerve. CSF: WBC 15 (lymphocytic), protein 0.5 g/L, glucose normal, oligoclonal bands positive (CSF-restricted); IgG index elevated. AQP4-IgG negative; MOG-IgG negative. McDonald 2017 criteria: dissemination in space (multiple regions) + dissemination in time (simultaneous enhancing and non-enhancing) + OCB → MS diagnosis.
Why does pediatric MS often have a relapsing-remitting course?
  • Pediatric MS > 95% relapsing-remitting at onset (vs ~ 85% in adults).
  • More relapses than adults but slower disability progression.
  • Longer time to secondary-progressive phase.
  • Cognitive impact is significant even with minimal motor disability.
  • Early disease-modifying therapy is essential.

Stage 3 — Treatment

Acute optic neuritis: IV methylprednisolone 30 mg/kg/day × 5 days. Vision improves to 20/25 within 7 days. Long-term DMT discussion with family: fingolimod (FDA approved ≥ 10 years) chosen. Pre-treatment: VZV serology (positive — no need for vaccination), ECG (PR baseline), ophthalmologic baseline. Started fingolimod 0.25 mg/day; PR interval monitored at first dose (no abnormality). MS clinic enrollment.

Stage 4 — Long-term

On fingolimod: no relapses over 2 years; MRI stable. Cognitive function preserved with school accommodations. Mental health: mild depression managed with counseling. Family planning counseling for future (DMT modification during pregnancy). Multidisciplinary MS clinic care; transition to adult MS care planned at age 18.
Final Diagnosis: Pediatric multiple sclerosis (relapsing-remitting, clinically isolated syndrome with MRI dissemination meeting MS criteria) treated with fingolimod.
Plan:
  • Fingolimod long-term DMT (FDA approved ≥ 10 years).
  • Annual MRI surveillance.
  • Cognitive monitoring; school accommodations.
  • Mental health support.
  • Family planning counseling.
  • Transition to adult MS care at 18.
  • Multidisciplinary MS clinic.
Teaching points:
  • Pediatric MS: McDonald 2017 criteria.
  • Relapsing-remitting predominates.
  • Oligoclonal bands positive in 70–90%.
  • Fingolimod FDA approved ≥ 10 years.
  • Test AQP4 / MOG to rule out NMOSD / MOGAD before MS DMT.
  • Multidisciplinary care; transition to adult care planning.
Case 84 — Pediatric Aicardi-Goutières Syndrome HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 6-month-old presents with regression of motor milestones (was rolling, now flaccid), decreased head circumference (was 50th percentile at birth, now < 5th), irritability, and skin lesions on fingers (chilblains). Initially evaluated for congenital infection.
HR 130 | BP 80/45 | T 37.6°C (intermittent) | Microcephaly; severe hypotonia; chilblains on fingers and toes; mild hepatomegaly

Stage 2 — Workup

CT head: bilateral basal ganglia calcifications, mild white matter abnormalities. MRI: progressive cerebral atrophy. CSF: lymphocytic pleocytosis (WBC 25); negative for CMV, HSV, toxoplasma, EBV. Serum: negative TORCH workup. CSF interferon-alpha activity: elevated (diagnostic clue). Genetic panel: pathogenic compound heterozygous RNASEH2B mutations confirms Aicardi-Goutières syndrome. Type I interferonopathy panel: interferon signature elevated.
Why does AGS mimic congenital infection?
  • Both present with calcifications, white matter changes, CSF inflammation.
  • AGS is genetic (not infectious) — accumulating self nucleic acids trigger inappropriate interferon response.
  • Distinguishes from infection: negative serology and PCR; family history; chilblains; elevated CSF / serum interferon-alpha.
  • Once known, recognize as treatable inflammatory disorder rather than untreatable congenital disease.

Stage 3 — Treatment

Baricitinib (JAK1/2 inhibitor) initiated at 4 mg/m²/day in two divided doses (off-label, compassionate use given approved emerging evidence). Monitoring: monthly CBC, AST/ALT (hepatic monitoring), infection vigilance. Supportive care: PT, OT, nutrition support (G-tube), antiepileptic for seizures, family education. Genetic counseling: autosomal recessive (siblings 25% risk; cascade testing).

Stage 4 — Course

By 6 months on baricitinib: chilblains improved markedly; mild motor gains; CSF interferon activity decreasing. Long-term: continued baricitinib; multidisciplinary developmental care. Sibling testing performed: younger sister also affected (similar genotype); pre-symptomatic baricitinib initiated. Family enrolled in AGS research registry.
Final Diagnosis: Aicardi-Goutières syndrome (RNASEH2B compound heterozygous mutations); type I interferonopathy; treated with baricitinib.
Plan:
  • Long-term baricitinib with monitoring.
  • Supportive care: PT, OT, G-tube, AED.
  • Multidisciplinary care.
  • Family genetic counseling and cascade testing.
  • AGS research registry enrollment.
Teaching points:
  • AGS: type I interferonopathy; mimics congenital infection.
  • RNASEH2B and other nucleic acid sensing/processing gene mutations.
  • Chronic CSF lymphocytic pleocytosis; elevated interferon.
  • Baricitinib (JAK inhibitor) emerging targeted therapy.
  • Distinguishes from STING-associated vasculopathy, CANDLE.
Case 85 — Pediatric Epilepsy Surgery — Functional Hemispherotomy HARD

Stage 1 — Background

A 5-year-old with intractable epilepsy from hemimegalencephaly (right hemisphere) has had ~ 30 seizures daily despite 4 AEDs and ketogenic diet. Severe developmental delay, right hemiparesis (4/5 left arm, 3/5 left leg), homonymous hemianopia on left. MRI: marked right hemispheric enlargement with cortical dysplasia. PET: right hemispheric hypometabolism with preserved left hemisphere. fMRI / DTI: language and motor function lateralized to LEFT hemisphere (unaffected). Pre-surgical conference recommends functional hemispherotomy.

Stage 2 — Surgical Planning

Multidisciplinary epilepsy surgery conference: pediatric neurology, neurosurgery, neuropsychology, social work, family meeting. Discussion with family: anticipated anatomical permanent hemiparesis and hemianopia (already developing); seizure freedom expected ~ 70–80% (Engel class I); cognitive function may be preserved or improved over time with rehabilitation. Family makes informed choice for surgery.
What is the rationale for choosing hemispherotomy over anatomic hemispherectomy?
  • Functional hemispherotomy: disconnect rather than remove the hemisphere.
  • Similar seizure freedom rate as anatomic hemispherectomy.
  • Less surgical morbidity: less blood loss, shorter operative time, less cerebrospinal fluid leak.
  • Lower long-term complication risk (less hemosiderin, less superficial siderosis).
  • Preferred in many centers when feasible.

Stage 3 — Surgery and Post-op

Right functional hemispherotomy performed. Surgical disconnection of cortical-thalamic, callosal, and frontoparietal connections. Operating time 8 hours. Post-op: anticipated worsening of left hemiparesis (resolution back to baseline over weeks). No new visual field loss beyond expected hemianopia. Seizures cease in operating room. Post-op ICU monitoring for ICP, electrolytes (often need careful sodium management post-large craniotomy).

Stage 4 — Outcomes

Day 14: seizure-free; mild ataxia; rehabilitation begins (PT, OT). Day 30: walking with assistance; sleep architecture improving. At 6 months: seizure-free on minimal AED; cognitive function improving with intensive rehabilitation; school re-entry planning. At 2 years: seizure-free; ambulating with mild residual hemiparesis; cognition improved markedly with neuroplasticity; school in special needs program.
Final Diagnosis: Hemimegalencephaly with intractable epilepsy; treated with right functional hemispherotomy with seizure freedom and cognitive recovery.
Plan:
  • AED slow taper over 2–3 years if seizure-free.
  • Intensive rehabilitation: PT, OT, speech.
  • Educational accommodations and special needs program.
  • Neuropsychology assessment at 6, 12, 24 months.
