  • Multidisciplinary epilepsy surgery follow-up.
  • Family support and counseling.
Teaching points:
  • Unilateral hemispheric pathology with intractable epilepsy: hemispherectomy / hemispherotomy is the most effective surgical option.
  • Functional hemispherotomy preferred over anatomic — similar efficacy, less morbidity.
  • Younger patients benefit more from neuroplasticity for cognitive recovery.
  • Anticipate permanent hemiparesis and hemianopia.
  • Multidisciplinary pre-surgical evaluation essential.
Case 86 — Dravet Syndrome with Status Epilepticus HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 9-month-old with febrile illness (T 39.5°C) presents with prolonged convulsive seizure lasting 45 minutes despite midazolam buccal at home. ED: persistent generalized convulsive activity; intubated for airway and seizure control.
HR 180 | BP 90/55 | T 40.0°C | GCS 5 (sedated/post-ictal) | Active convulsion on arrival

Stage 2 — Workup

SCN1A sequencing (sent prior visit): pathogenic missense mutation confirms Dravet syndrome. Past episodes: prolonged febrile seizure age 7 months, multiple subsequent afebrile seizures with developmental plateau.
Why avoid certain AEDs in Dravet?
  • SCN1A haploinsufficiency reduces Nav1.1 function in GABAergic interneurons.
  • Sodium channel blockers (carbamazepine, oxcarbazepine, phenytoin, lamotrigine, vigabatrin) further reduce inhibitory interneuron function.
  • Paradoxically worsen seizures and developmental outcome.
  • Choose valproate, clobazam, stiripentol, cannabidiol, fenfluramine.

Stage 3 — Acute Management

Status epilepticus protocol: midazolam infusion 0.4 mg/kg/h escalated; aggressive cooling for fever (acetaminophen, cooling blanket, ice packs); IV valproate 30 mg/kg load + maintenance. Clobazam continued via NG. Stiripentol started (already on for Dravet management). Phenytoin AVOIDED. cEEG monitoring. Hydration and supportive care.

Stage 4 — Course and Long-term

Within 6 hours: seizures controlled with multidrug regimen + fever control. Extubated day 3. Cannabidiol (Epidiolex) added to chronic regimen. Long-term plan: aggressive fever prevention (acetaminophen scheduled at first sign of illness, cooling), rescue medication at home (rectal diazepam), ER plan, multidisciplinary care.
Final Diagnosis: Dravet syndrome (SCN1A) with febrile status epilepticus.
Plan:
  • Multimodal AED: valproate + clobazam + stiripentol + cannabidiol.
  • Aggressive fever prevention.
  • Rescue medication at home.
  • Avoid sodium channel blockers.
  • Multidisciplinary developmental care.
  • SUDEP risk counseling.
  • Family genetic counseling.
Teaching points:
  • Dravet: SCN1A haploinsufficiency; severe pediatric epilepsy syndrome.
  • Febrile illness triggers prolonged seizures.
  • Avoid sodium channel blockers.
  • Multimodal AED including newer agents (cannabidiol, fenfluramine).
  • SUDEP risk elevated.
Case 87 — LGS with Corpus Callosotomy HARD

Stage 1 — Background

A 9-year-old with LGS (multiple seizure types since age 3) has frequent atonic 'drop attacks' (15–20 daily) causing falls with head injuries. On valproate + lamotrigine + clobazam + cannabidiol; ketogenic diet failed. Helmet worn but bruises and lacerations weekly. Cognitive function moderately impaired.

Stage 2 — Pre-surgical Evaluation

Multidisciplinary epilepsy surgery conference. Video EEG: characteristic LGS pattern with frequent atonic seizures arising from generalized onset. MRI normal. Neuropsychology: moderate ID; reasonable language function. Family meeting: corpus callosotomy palliative option for drop attacks — won't cure all seizures but reduces drops significantly.
Why callosotomy reduces drop attacks?
  • Atonic seizures rapidly spread between hemispheres via corpus callosum.
  • Callosotomy disconnects this rapid bilateral synchronization.
  • Reduces drop attacks by ~ 70% in selected patients.
  • Other seizure types may persist or even worsen briefly.
  • Anterior 2/3 callosotomy preserves some interhemispheric function.
  • Disconnection syndrome (transient) may occur.

Stage 3 — Surgery

Anterior 2/3 corpus callosotomy performed. Post-op: transient disconnection syndrome (alexia without agraphia, left-hand apraxia, mutism) — resolves over days to weeks. ICU monitoring; AEDs continued; seizures monitored on cEEG.

Stage 4 — Outcome

Drop attacks reduced from 15–20/day to 1–2/week — significant injury reduction; helmet can be removed at home. Other seizure types persist but better controlled. Cognitive function preserved / mildly improved with fewer head injuries. Continued ketogenic diet not resumed; AEDs continued. Long-term: surveillance MRI; possible completion callosotomy if drops recur.
Final Diagnosis: LGS with refractory atonic seizures (drop attacks); palliative anterior 2/3 corpus callosotomy with ≥ 90% reduction in drops.
Plan:
  • Continued AED therapy.
  • Possible completion callosotomy if drops recur.
  • Safety measures relaxed (helmet optional).
  • Multidisciplinary follow-up.
  • Continued educational accommodations.
  • Family support.
Teaching points:
  • Corpus callosotomy is palliative for drop attacks in LGS.
  • Anterior 2/3 preserves some interhemispheric function; completion callosotomy if needed later.
  • Transient disconnection syndrome — resolves.
  • Cognitive function often preserved or improved due to fewer injuries.
  • Doesn't cure all seizures but life-changing for injury risk.
Case 88 — Doose Syndrome with Ketogenic Diet Response MODERATE

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 4-year-old previously developing normally presents with new-onset multiple seizure types over 3 months: myoclonic jerks (especially morning), astatic 'drops' causing falls, atypical absence with brief unresponsiveness. Family history: maternal cousin with childhood epilepsy.
HR 95 | BP 100/65 | T 36.9°C | Normal exam between seizures

Stage 2 — Workup

EEG: generalized 2–3 Hz spike-and-wave with brief paroxysms during myoclonic-astatic seizures; normal background between events. MRI brain: normal. Genetic panel: SLC6A1 pathogenic mutation. Diagnosis: myoclonic-astatic epilepsy (Doose syndrome).
What distinguishes Doose from LGS?
  • Doose: better prognosis (~ 50% achieve seizure remission).
  • EEG: 2–3 Hz generalized spike-wave (vs LGS 1.5–2.5 Hz slow spike-wave plus paroxysmal fast).
  • Cognitive impact: often more preserved than LGS.
  • Etiology: often genetic (SLC6A1, SCN1A others) or cryptogenic; LGS often symptomatic of structural lesion.
  • Treatment response: ketogenic diet particularly effective.

Stage 3 — Treatment

Valproate first-line; partial response. Ethosuximide added for absence component. Continued seizures despite combination therapy. Ketogenic diet initiated (classical 4:1 ratio). Within 4 weeks of achieving ketosis (BHB 3.5 mmol/L): seizures reduced ≥ 75%; myoclonic seizures resolved; drops markedly reduced.

Stage 4 — Long-term

Continued ketogenic diet × 2 years; AEDs tapered carefully (valproate continued). At follow-up: seizure-free for 12 months. Slow ketogenic diet wean planned with continued AED. Cognitive function recovered with seizure control. Family education on diet maintenance, sick-day plan, ER protocols.
Final Diagnosis: Doose syndrome (SLC6A1 mutation) with excellent response to ketogenic diet.
Plan:
  • Ketogenic diet × 2 years then slow wean.
  • AED continuation during transition.
  • Long-term follow-up.
  • Cognitive monitoring; school accommodations.
  • Family genetic counseling (SLC6A1).
Teaching points:
  • Doose: myoclonic-astatic epilepsy; better prognosis than LGS.
  • Ketogenic diet particularly effective.
  • SLC6A1 (GABA transporter) is recognized genetic cause.
  • Valproate + ethosuximide + ketogenic diet typical regimen.
Case 89 — Chiari I with Syringomyelia and Decompression MODERATE

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 12-year-old has 1 year of progressive occipital headaches triggered by cough, sneezing, straining. Recent: new left hand atrophy, decreased pinprick sensation in cape distribution (suspended sensory loss), and progressive scoliosis (Cobb angle 35° on recent X-ray).
HR 80 | BP 110/65 | T 36.8°C | Cape sensory loss (C5–T2 distribution); intrinsic hand muscle atrophy; preserved reflexes; left scoliosis

Stage 2 — Imaging

MRI brain: cerebellar tonsils descend 9 mm below foramen magnum (Chiari I malformation). MRI cervical and thoracic spine: large central syringomyelia extending from C2 to T6, expanding cord. Scoliosis X-ray: 35° dextroscoliosis.
Why does syringomyelia cause cape sensory loss?
  • Central cord syrinx affects crossing spinothalamic fibers (pain/temperature) in anterior commissure.
  • 'Suspended' or 'cape' distribution: bilateral arms, shoulders, upper trunk.
  • Dorsal columns (proprioception/vibration) spared because they ascend ipsilaterally without crossing.
  • 'Dissociated' sensory loss is pathognomonic for central cord pathology.
  • Intrinsic hand muscle wasting from anterior horn involvement at C8–T1.

Stage 3 — Surgical Management

Posterior fossa decompression: suboccipital craniectomy with C1 laminectomy and dural opening with duraplasty (cadaveric graft). No direct syringostomy / shunt placed. Post-op ICU monitoring.

Stage 4 — Outcome

Post-op: cough headaches resolved within 2 weeks. By 6 months: syringomyelia reduced to half pre-op size; sensory changes stabilized but did not fully resolve. Scoliosis stabilized (continued bracing). Hand atrophy did not progress further. Long-term: surveillance MRI; orthopedic follow-up for scoliosis; continued PT.
Final Diagnosis: Symptomatic Chiari I with syringomyelia and progressive scoliosis; treated with posterior fossa decompression and duraplasty.
Plan:
  • Surveillance MRI at 6 months and 1 year.
  • Continued scoliosis management with orthopedics.
  • Continued PT.
  • Reassessment if symptoms return or syrinx re-enlarges.
  • Multidisciplinary follow-up.
Teaching points:
  • Chiari I + syringomyelia symptomatic = indication for decompression.
  • Cape sensory loss + intrinsic hand atrophy pathognomonic for cervical syrinx.
  • Posterior fossa decompression often improves syringomyelia and stabilizes scoliosis.
  • Surveillance imaging important.
Case 90 — VP Shunt Malfunction in Adolescent HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 14-year-old with VP shunt (placed at age 2 for aqueductal stenosis) presents with 3 days of progressive headache, vomiting, declining school performance. Mother noticed shunt 'feels different' on palpation — slower refill. Past similar episode 2 years ago resolved with shunt revision.
HR 110 | BP 130/80 | T 37.4°C | GCS 13 | Papilledema bilateral; mild upgaze palsy (Parinaud syndrome — concerning for aqueductal pressure); cushing pattern absent yet

Stage 2 — Workup

CT head: ventriculomegaly increased compared to baseline (lateral ventricles 18 mm vs baseline 8 mm); third ventricle dilated. Shunt series X-ray: catheter integrity intact; no apparent disconnection. ICP elevated estimated 30+ mm Hg given papilledema. Neurosurgery emergently activated for shunt revision.
Why does ventricular size change matter more than absolute size?
  • Baseline ventricular size varies between patients with chronic hydrocephalus.
  • 'Slit ventricles' (collapsed) can occur in over-shunted patients — small but symptomatic.
  • Comparison to patient's baseline ventricular size is key.
  • Absolute ventriculomegaly (Evans index, frontal horn ratio) less reliable than change.
  • Always obtain prior imaging for comparison.

Stage 3 — Management

Hyperosmolar therapy (3% saline 5 mL/kg) and head-of-bed 30° while preparing for OR. Emergent shunt revision: proximal catheter obstructed with choroid plexus tissue; replaced with new ventricular catheter. Operative time 90 minutes. ICP normalized post-op.

Stage 4 — Recovery

Day 2 post-op: headache resolved; papilledema regressing. Discharged day 4. Long-term: family education on shunt malfunction warning signs; low threshold for ER; surveillance imaging; consider endoscopic third ventriculostomy (ETV) at next revision to potentially achieve shunt independence.
Final Diagnosis: VP shunt malfunction (proximal obstruction from choroid plexus); treated with shunt revision.
Plan:
  • Family education on warning signs.
  • Annual surveillance MRI.
  • ETV evaluation at next revision (if aqueductal anatomy favorable).
  • Multidisciplinary follow-up.
  • MedicAlert documenting shunt-dependent.
Teaching points:
  • Shunt malfunction common — investigate every concerning symptom.
  • Ventricular size change from baseline is key.
  • Always have prior imaging for comparison.
  • Family education on warning signs essential.
  • Consider ETV for shunt independence.
Case 91 — VP Shunt Infection with Externalization HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 10-year-old with VP shunt (revised 6 months ago) presents with 3 days of fever, headache, abdominal pain. Examination: shunt tract tender; mild peritoneal signs; intermittent altered mental status.
HR 130 | BP 110/70 | T 39.4°C | GCS 13 | Peritoneal signs; shunt tract erythema; CRP 180; WBC 18000; mild ventriculomegaly on CT

Stage 2 — Workup

Shunt tap by neurosurgery: CSF WBC 1500 (90% neutrophils), glucose 1.0 mmol/L (low), protein 1.8 g/L (elevated). Gram stain: gram-positive cocci in clusters. Cultures growing Staphylococcus aureus (MSSA). Abdominal US: peritoneal pseudocyst around distal catheter. Diagnosis: VP shunt infection with peritoneal pseudocyst.
Why is shunt externalization preferred over IV antibiotics alone?
  • Bacterial biofilm forms on the catheter and valve.
  • Antibiotics cannot penetrate biofilm effectively — relapse rate > 90% without hardware removal/exchange.
  • Externalization converts shunt to EVD, allowing CSF drainage and infection clearance.
  • Alternative: complete shunt removal with separate EVD placement.
  • Reimplant shunt only after CSF sterilization confirmed.

Stage 3 — Management

Emergent shunt externalization (distal catheter removed; ventricular catheter externalized as EVD). Empiric vancomycin + ceftazidime IV; narrowed to nafcillin 200 mg/kg/day IV per culture sensitivity. Peritoneal pseudocyst aspirated for cultures (same MSSA). EVD drainage at appropriate height. Repeat CSF cultures every 2–3 days.

Stage 4 — Reimplantation and Long-term

CSF sterile after 5 days of antibiotics. Maintain antibiotics 7 more days. After 12 days of antibiotic therapy with sterile CSF for 3 consecutive samples, new shunt placed. Total IV antibiotic course 4 weeks (continued post-reimplant). Long-term: family education on warning signs; antibiotic prophylaxis NOT routine; surveillance.
Final Diagnosis: VP shunt infection (MSSA) with peritoneal pseudocyst; treated with externalization + IV nafcillin + reimplantation.
Plan:
  • Total 4 weeks IV antibiotics.
  • Surveillance for shunt malfunction recurrence.
  • Family education.
  • Multidisciplinary follow-up.
Teaching points:
  • VP shunt infection: externalization is the standard.
  • Empiric vancomycin + ceftazidime; adjust per culture.
  • Reimplant after CSF sterilization.
  • Total course 2–4 weeks IV.
  • S. epidermidis most common; S. aureus second; gram-negatives from abdominal source.
Case 92 — Hurler Syndrome with HSCT HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

An 8-month-old presents with developmental delay, increasingly coarse facial features (developing low-set ears, thick eyebrows, depressed nasal bridge), recurrent otitis media, mild hepatosplenomegaly, and dysostosis multiplex on X-ray (claw hand, J-shaped sella, kyphosis). Newborn screening (now available for MPS-I in his state) was positive but family declined initial confirmatory testing.
HR 130 | BP 85/55 | T 37.0°C | Coarse facies developing; hepatosplenomegaly; corneal cloudiness emerging; developmental delay (sitting unsupported but no crawling); cardiac murmur (mitral regurgitation)

Stage 2 — Confirmatory Workup

Urine GAGs: elevated; pattern showing heparan and dermatan sulfate. Leukocyte α-L-iduronidase (IDUA) activity: markedly reduced. IDUA sequencing: confirms severe Hurler genotype (homozygous null). Diagnosis confirmed: Mucopolysaccharidosis Type IH (Hurler syndrome).
Why is HSCT before age 2 ideal for Hurler syndrome?
  • HSCT replaces deficient enzyme by donor leukocytes that cross-correct other cells via mannose-6-phosphate receptors.
  • Penetrates CNS via donor microglia (eventual replacement of recipient microglia).
  • Before significant cognitive decline (which begins around age 2 in untreated Hurler).
  • Preserves cognitive function in many; established cognitive deficits not reversed.
  • Cross-corrects systemic disease (cardiac, hepatic, etc.).
  • Best outcomes when transplanted age 8–18 months.

Stage 3 — HSCT

Matched unrelated donor identified. Enzyme replacement therapy (laronidase 100 U/kg IV weekly) initiated as bridge to transplant — treats systemic features and reduces tissue burden. Pre-HSCT conditioning. HSCT at age 12 months — successful engraftment by day 30; full donor chimerism by day 100. Enzyme activity normalized.

Stage 4 — Long-term

2 years post-HSCT: developmental progress maintained; mild residual cognitive impairment but ambulating and speaking. Hepatosplenomegaly resolved; cardiac valve disease stable (mild mitral regurgitation). Continued multidisciplinary care: cardiology, orthopedics (residual dysostosis), ophthalmology (corneal clouding stable), ENT, neurology. Family genetic counseling for future pregnancies.
Final Diagnosis: Mucopolysaccharidosis Type IH (Hurler syndrome); treated with HSCT at age 12 months with preserved cognitive function.
Plan:
  • Post-HSCT monitoring (chimerism, enzyme activity).
  • Multidisciplinary care: cardiology, orthopedics, ophthalmology, ENT, neurology.
  • Hip dysplasia and kyphosis monitoring; potential orthopedic procedures.
  • Educational support.
  • Family genetic counseling.
  • Sibling cascade testing.
Teaching points:
  • Hurler syndrome: severe MPS-I; cognitive decline begins ~ age 2 untreated.
  • HSCT before age 2 (ideally) preserves cognition.
  • Newborn screening enables early detection.
  • ERT as bridge to transplant.
  • Multidisciplinary lifelong care.
Case 93 — Sanfilippo Syndrome with Palliative Care HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 7-year-old previously diagnosed with Sanfilippo syndrome (MPS-IIIA from SGSH mutations) presents in clinic with progressive behavioral deterioration (severe aggression, sleep disturbance with night wakings, hyperactivity, screaming episodes), declining cognition (lost speech 6 months ago), and developing seizures. Family struggling with quality of life. Mother in tears at appointment.
HR 95 | BP 100/65 | T 36.9°C | Coarse facial features (mild — less than Hurler); behavioral agitation; non-verbal; mild ataxia; weight loss; sleep deprivation evident in both child and family

Stage 2 — Care Planning Discussion

Multidisciplinary care meeting: pediatric neurology, palliative care, social work, child psychiatry. Acknowledged: no disease-modifying therapy currently effective for Sanfilippo; gene therapy trials available but family unable to travel. Focus shifted to quality of life optimization for child and family.
Why is early palliative care engagement so important in Sanfilippo?
  • Life-limiting illness with death typically in teens / early 20s.
  • Severe symptom burden: behavioral problems, sleep disturbance, seizures, dystonia.
  • Family caregiver burden enormous — often siblings also affected.
  • Palliative care manages: symptom control, quality of life, advance care planning.
  • Does NOT mean 'end of life only' — includes years of supportive care.
  • Best initiated at or near diagnosis.

Stage 3 — Symptom Management

Symptom-targeted approach: trazodone for sleep disturbance; risperidone for aggression and severe behavioral issues; levetiracetam for emerging seizures. PT/OT to maintain mobility. Speech therapy for AAC (augmentative communication). Family respite care arranged; sibling support; mental health support for parents. Engagement with national MPS support organization.

Stage 4 — Long-term Trajectory

Over next 2 years: continued cognitive decline; loss of ambulation by age 9; G-tube placement; tracheostomy not pursued (family preference). Continued comprehensive palliative care. Hospice involvement as disease progressed. Patient passed at age 12 with family at home. Long-term family bereavement support continued. Younger sibling cascade testing: negative.
Final Diagnosis: Sanfilippo syndrome MPS-IIIA (SGSH mutation); life-limiting illness; multidisciplinary palliative care.
Process:
  • Multidisciplinary palliative care from diagnosis.
  • Symptom-targeted management (sleep, behavior, seizures).
  • Family respite and mental health support.
  • Sibling support.
  • Advance care planning.
  • Hospice as appropriate.
  • Bereavement support.
  • Connect with MPS Society / advocacy groups.
  • Family genetic counseling and cascade testing.
Teaching points:
  • Sanfilippo: heparan sulfate accumulation; primarily CNS phenotype.
  • Behavioral symptoms predominant over somatic.
  • No disease-modifying therapy currently effective; gene therapy in trials.
  • Engage palliative care from diagnosis — symptom management, family support, advance planning.
  • Multidisciplinary symptom-targeted management.
Case 94 — Rett Syndrome with Trofinetide HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 3-year-old girl presents to clinic with confirmed Rett syndrome (MECP2 mutation, classical Rett). Stage 3 (pseudostationary): lost purposeful hand use, stereotypic hand-wringing, breathing irregularities (hyperventilation), early scoliosis. Recently FDA approved trofinetide is discussed with family.
HR 95 | BP 90/55 | T 36.8°C | Stereotypic hand-wringing; acquired microcephaly; hyperventilation episodes; mild scoliosis; non-verbal; eye contact preserved

Stage 2 — Trofinetide Discussion

Family education on trofinetide (Daybue): FDA approved 2023 for Rett syndrome ≥ 2 years; first disease-modifying therapy. Mechanism: synthetic IGF-1 derivative; modulates astrocyte/glial function, neuroinflammation, synaptic function. Demonstrated modest but significant improvements in clinician-rated and parent-rated outcomes (LAVENDER trial). Side effects: diarrhea (most common, can be severe), vomiting. Cost / insurance considerations addressed.
What outcomes can families expect with trofinetide?
  • Modest improvements in specific domains (hand use, breathing, communication, autonomic).
  • NOT cure; NOT dramatic reversal.
  • LAVENDER trial showed statistically significant but clinically modest improvements.
  • Best results in earlier disease stages.
  • Honest family communication about expectations.
  • Diarrhea can be limiting — proactive management.

Stage 3 — Treatment

Trofinetide initiated at weight-based dose (~ 7 mL BID); titrated up to therapeutic dose over 7 days. Pre-treatment: weight, GI baseline. Monitoring: daily diarrhea log, hydration status, weight, electrolytes. After 4 weeks: mild diarrhea managed with dose adjustment and loperamide PRN.

Stage 4 — Course

3 months on trofinetide: modest improvements noted by family — increased eye contact, slight reduction in hand stereotypies, fewer breathing irregularities. Family pleased with modest gains. Continued multidisciplinary care: orthopedics (scoliosis monitoring), cardiology (QT surveillance), PT/OT/speech, education. Long-term continuation planned.
Final Diagnosis: Classical Rett syndrome (MECP2 mutation); managed with trofinetide and comprehensive multidisciplinary care.
Plan:
  • Continued trofinetide with monitoring.
  • Multidisciplinary care: orthopedics, cardiology, neurology, PT, OT, speech.
  • Scoliosis surveillance.
  • Family support and education.
  • Educational accommodations.
  • Family genetic counseling.
Teaching points:
  • Trofinetide: first disease-modifying therapy for Rett syndrome.
  • Modest improvements (not cure).
  • Diarrhea common side effect.
  • Multidisciplinary care essential.
  • Scoliosis, QT surveillance, breathing irregularities.
Case 95 — Late-Onset OTC Deficiency Unmasked by Steroids HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 14-year-old girl presents to the ED with progressive confusion, vomiting, and lethargy 36 hours after starting high-dose dexamethasone for severe asthma exacerbation. Initial workup unrevealing; differential includes infection, drug effect, metabolic. Family history: mother's brother died at 9 of 'unexplained liver problems.'
HR 110 | BP 110/65 | T 36.9°C | GCS 11 declining | Respiratory alkalosis (pH 7.50, PaCO2 28) | Hypotonia | Ammonia 280 µmol/L (markedly elevated) | Glucose 6.2 | Urea low (1.0)

Stage 2 — Recognition

Hyperammonemia + respiratory alkalosis (ammonia stimulates respiratory drive) + low urea + family history of unexplained male death — suspect urea cycle disorder. Plasma amino acids: elevated glutamine + alanine; low citrulline; normal arginine; pattern suggests proximal urea cycle block (CPS-1, OTC). Urine orotic acid: elevated (consistent with OTC). Diagnosis: late-onset OTC deficiency in heterozygous female carrier, unmasked by steroid-induced catabolism.
Why are female OTC carriers symptomatic?
  • OTC is X-linked.
  • Random X-inactivation in heterozygous females can result in skewed inactivation favoring the mutant allele.
  • Mosaic expression → partial enzyme deficiency.
  • Asymptomatic at baseline but susceptible to decompensation under catabolic stress.
  • Triggers: surgery, valproate, steroids, high-protein meal, fasting, infection.
  • Female carriers can have life-threatening attacks despite being 'heterozygous.'

Stage 3 — Emergency Management

Stop dexamethasone (no longer needed; steroid increased catabolism worsening UCD). NPO; no protein. D10 IV continuous + intralipid for anti-catabolic effect. Ammonul (sodium phenylacetate + sodium benzoate): 250 mg/kg IV load over 90 min, then continuous infusion. Arginine HCl 250 mg/kg IV bolus then 250 mg/kg/day infusion. Carnitine 100 mg/kg IV. Hemodialysis started given ammonia > 250 µmol/L (more effective than CRRT). Intubation for declining GCS. Avoid valproate.

Stage 4 — Recovery and Long-term

Hemodialysis × 8 hours reduces ammonia to 80 µmol/L. CRRT continued. Within 48 hours: alert, extubated; eating special diet. Long-term: protein-moderated diet; oral nitrogen scavengers (glycerol phenylbutyrate); citrulline supplementation; sick-day protocol. MedicAlert bracelet. Family genetic counseling: confirmed OTC mutation; mother is asymptomatic carrier; cascade testing for siblings, aunts. Two female cousins tested: one positive (asymptomatic). All family members educated.
Final Diagnosis: Late-onset OTC deficiency in heterozygous female carrier; unmasked by high-dose dexamethasone for asthma; treated with emergency hyperammonemia protocol + hemodialysis.
Plan:
  • Protein-moderated diet.
  • Glycerol phenylbutyrate oral.
  • Citrulline supplementation.
  • Sick-day protocol.
  • Avoid valproate, steroids (when possible), high-protein loads.
  • MedicAlert bracelet.
  • Family cascade testing and education.
  • Multidisciplinary metabolic clinic.
  • Liver transplant evaluation if recurrent decompensations.
Teaching points:
  • Late-onset urea cycle disorders can present in children / adolescents / adults.
  • X-linked OTC in female carriers can be symptomatic from skewed X-inactivation.
  • Triggers: surgery, anesthesia, valproate, STEROIDS, high-protein, fasting, infection.
  • Family history of unexplained male death is a clue.
  • Hemodialysis preferred over CRRT for severe hyperammonemia.
Case 96 — Acute Intermittent Porphyria Attack HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 16-year-old girl presents with 3 days of severe abdominal pain (10/10, diffuse, no peritoneal signs), vomiting, and now confusion and a generalized tonic-clonic seizure. Mother reports recent menstrual period; recent course of trimethoprim-sulfamethoxazole for UTI. Past episodes of severe abdominal pain attributed to dysmenorrhea, with one prior hospitalization without diagnosis.
HR 130 | BP 165/105 | T 37.4°C | GCS 11 post-ictal | Severe diffuse abdominal pain; no peritoneal signs; tachycardia and labile hypertension (autonomic); hyponatremia Na 122 (SIADH); urine dark red on standing (oxidation of porphobilin)

Stage 2 — Diagnosis

Urine porphobilinogen (PBG): markedly elevated. Urine δ-aminolevulinic acid (ALA): elevated. Plasma porphyrins: elevated. Diagnosis: acute intermittent porphyria (AIP) attack. Triggers identified: menstrual hormonal changes + trimethoprim-sulfamethoxazole (porphyrogenic sulfonamide). HMBS gene sequencing later confirms heterozygous pathogenic mutation.
Why is sulfonamide a porphyria trigger?
  • Sulfonamides (and many other drugs) induce CYP450 enzymes.
  • CYP450 induction depletes hepatic heme pool.
  • Increased heme synthesis drives ALA synthase 1 activity.
  • In AIP (partial HMBS deficiency), upstream substrates (ALA and PBG) accumulate.
  • Neurotoxic intermediates cause attack symptoms.
  • Many drugs porphyrogenic — check the American Porphyria Foundation drug list.

Stage 3 — Emergency Management

Stop offending drug (trimethoprim-sulfamethoxazole). Carbohydrate loading: D10 IV 5 g/kg continuous. IV hemin (heme arginate) 3 mg/kg/day × 4 days (provides negative feedback on ALA synthase 1). Pain control: morphine (opioids safe in porphyria) — NSAIDs and acetaminophen also safe. Treat seizures: levetiracetam IV (safe); AVOID phenytoin, carbamazepine, valproate (porphyrogenic). Manage hyponatremia: water restrict + 3% saline for symptomatic correction (slow). Labetalol IV for hypertension and tachycardia.

Stage 4 — Recovery and Long-term

Within 48 hours of starting hemin: pain dramatically reduced; mental status clearing; HR and BP normalizing. Discharged day 7. Long-term: avoid triggers (drug list at home); givosiran (RNA interference therapy targeting hepatic ALA synthase 1) considered for recurrent attacks — reduces attack frequency. Family genetic counseling: heterozygous AIP; sister tested positive; both cousins on maternal side tested.
Final Diagnosis: Acute intermittent porphyria attack precipitated by sulfonamide + menstrual hormonal changes; treated with IV hemin.
Plan:
  • Givosiran consideration for attack prevention.
  • Drug avoidance list (American Porphyria Foundation).
  • Hormone management consultation (GnRH agonist for cyclic attacks).
  • Family genetic counseling and cascade testing.
  • MedicAlert bracelet.
  • Carry IV hemin information for emergencies.
Teaching points:
  • AIP: HMBS deficiency; autosomal dominant.
  • Acute attacks: abdominal pain + autonomic + neurologic + dark urine + hyponatremia.
  • Triggers: drugs (sulfonamides, CYP inducers, hormones), fasting, alcohol, surgery.
  • Treatment: stop trigger, IV hemin, carbohydrate loading.
  • AVOID phenytoin, carbamazepine, valproate.
  • Givosiran prevents recurrent attacks.
Case 97 — Tethered Cord Re-tethering in Spina Bifida HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 12-year-old with congenital myelomeningocele (repaired neonatally; previously tethered cord released at age 5) presents with 6 months of progressive: declining ambulation (was independent with AFO; now requires walker), urinary symptoms (intermittent retention then incontinence), back pain, and worsening scoliosis (38° from 28°).
HR 80 | BP 110/70 | T 36.8°C | GCS 15 | Increased lower extremity tone with brisk reflexes; declining left lower extremity strength (4/5); sensory level approximately L3; new bladder dysfunction; tethered low conus on prior MRI; worsening scoliosis

Stage 2 — Workup

MRI thoracolumbar spine: re-tethering of conus at L5–S1 fibrous adhesions; cord stretched and angled posteriorly; mild syringomyelia at T10. Urodynamics: detrusor hyperreflexia with low compliance. Scoliosis X-ray: 38° dextroscoliosis. Diagnosis: re-tethering of cord with progressive neurologic deterioration.
Why does cord re-tethering occur even after initial release?
  • Adhesions reform after initial tethering release in 20–40% of patients.
  • Growth-related: spine grows faster than spinal cord in adolescence — cord 'rises' relative to spine if anchored.
  • Scar tissue at prior surgical site re-anchors cord.
  • Symptoms emerge during growth spurts.
  • Imaging may not always show classic tethering; symptoms drive management decision.

Stage 3 — Surgical Management

Neurosurgical untethering: microscopic dissection of L5–S1 adhesions with intra-operative neurophysiologic monitoring (motor evoked potentials, sensory evoked potentials, EMG). Operation tedious but successful release without new neurologic injury. Post-op: prone positioning x 24h; PT and OT consultations.

Stage 4 — Outcome

By 6 weeks: improved ambulation with AFO + walker → AFO only; pain resolved; urinary function partially recovered (still uses CIC). Scoliosis progression slowed; continued bracing. Long-term: multidisciplinary spina bifida clinic; surveillance MRI; potential further untethering if needed.
Final Diagnosis: Cord re-tethering in spina bifida post initial untethering at age 5; symptomatic with deteriorating motor / bladder function; treated with surgical untethering.
Plan:
  • Continued multidisciplinary spina bifida clinic.
  • Surveillance MRI.
  • Continued bracing for scoliosis.
  • Urological management with CIC.
  • PT to maintain function.
  • Sex-related counseling as developmentally appropriate.
  • Transition to adult care planning.
Teaching points:
  • Re-tethering common in spina bifida — 20–40% over time.
  • Adolescent growth spurt is risk period.
  • Symptoms: declining ambulation, new urinary symptoms, back pain, worsening scoliosis.
  • Multidisciplinary spina bifida clinic essential for surveillance.
  • Untethering can be repeated when symptomatic.
Case 98 — Cold-Water Drowning with ECMO Resuscitation HARD

Stage 1 — Pre-hospital

A 6-year-old fell through ice on a frozen pond; rescued by paramedics 28 minutes after submersion (no breathing, no pulse). Bystander CPR initiated 5 minutes after rescue. Core temperature 24°C on arrival. Continuing CPR.

Stage 2 — Resuscitation

'Not dead until warm and dead.' Continued CPR; aggressive rewarming activated. ECMO team alerted given severe hypothermia with arrest. V-A ECMO cannulation in cath lab; flow established 45 minutes after arrival. Core T rises 1°C every 30 min on ECMO. Asystole on monitor through cooling; standard PALS modified (epinephrine spaced, defibrillation deferred until T > 30°C).
Why does pre-hypoxic hypothermia improve outcome?
  • Cold-water drowning: rapid cooling occurs BEFORE significant hypoxic-ischemic injury.
  • Hypothermia reduces cerebral metabolic rate ~ 5–7% per °C drop.
  • 'Diving reflex' shunts blood to vital organs in cold-water immersion.
  • Slows myocardial conduction but myocardium remains viable longer.
  • Outcomes can be remarkable: full recovery reported with downtimes > 1 hour in cold water.

Stage 3 — Post-ROSC

At core T 30°C: defibrillation × 1 restores sinus rhythm. ECMO weaning over 3 days. TTM at 33°C × 48 h then strict normothermia × 5 days total per THAPCA. cEEG ongoing — initially burst-suppression, evolves to continuous reactive by day 5. Off sedation day 7. Begins to localize pain by day 9; tracking visually by day 10.

Stage 4 — Long-term

By 3 months: walking with assistance, speaking single words. By 1 year: significant cognitive deficits but improving with intensive rehabilitation. Family supported throughout with realistic but hopeful communication. Multidisciplinary follow-up: neurology, neuropsychology, rehabilitation, school services.
Final Diagnosis: Hypothermic cardiac arrest after cold-water drowning; resuscitated with ECMO + aggressive rewarming + TTM; significant but recoverable neurologic injury.
Plan:
  • Intensive multidisciplinary rehabilitation.
  • Cognitive remediation.
  • Educational accommodations.
  • Family support and counseling.
  • Long-term neurology follow-up.
Teaching points:
  • Cold-water drowning: 'not dead until warm and dead.'
  • ECMO transformative for severe hypothermic arrest.
  • Pre-hypoxic hypothermia is protective.
  • Continued CPR during rewarming essential.
  • Outcomes can be remarkable.
Case 99 — Organophosphate Poisoning in Farmworker's Child HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 5-year-old child of a migrant farmworker family is brought in with sudden onset of profuse drooling, vomiting, diaphoresis, urination/defecation, bradycardia, and progressive weakness. Father reports child accidentally entered tarp-covered area where pesticide was recently applied (suspected organophosphate).
HR 55 (bradycardic) | BP 80/45 | RR 35 (bronchorrhea, bronchospasm) | SpO2 88% on RA | Pupils miotic (pinpoint); marked bronchorrhea; fasciculations

Stage 2 — Diagnosis

Classic cholinergic toxidrome (SLUDGE + Killer Bs). Provider PPE donned for decontamination. Skin decontamination with soap and water, clothing bagged separately. Activated charcoal if oral exposure confirmed (no clear oral component here). Plasma cholinesterase activity sent; markedly reduced. Diagnosis: severe organophosphate poisoning.
Why is atropine titrated to bronchorrhea / pulmonary compliance, not heart rate?
  • Bradycardia from cholinergic excess is often less life-threatening than bronchorrhea / bronchospasm.
  • Pulmonary effects (bronchorrhea + bronchospasm + respiratory muscle weakness) cause death in OP poisoning.
  • Titrate atropine until secretions clear and ventilation compliance improves.
  • HR not reliable endpoint — may remain tachycardic from sympathetic surge or hypoxia.
  • Doses needed: often 5–20 mg total atropine in severe pediatric cases; adults 50+ mg.

Stage 3 — Management

Intubation for respiratory failure and bronchorrhea management; rocuronium for paralysis (not succinylcholine — fasciculations / NMJ confusion). Atropine 0.05 mg/kg IV repeated every 5 min, titrated to secretion clearance (total 4 mg given over 30 min). Pralidoxime 25 mg/kg IV bolus over 30 min, then continuous infusion 15 mg/kg/h (reactivates AChE before aging completes). Midazolam infusion for seizures and sedation. Aggressive supportive ICU care.

Stage 4 — Course

Within 4 hours: secretions controlled with atropine; bronchospasm resolving; oxygenation improving. Pralidoxime continued × 48 hours. Watch for intermediate syndrome (proximal weakness 1–4 days post-acute — develops in 10–20%; requires continued ventilatory support). Day 5: extubated; recovering. Long-term: pesticide handler safety education for family; community pesticide regulations review.
Final Diagnosis: Severe pediatric organophosphate poisoning from environmental exposure; treated with high-dose atropine + pralidoxime + supportive ICU care.
Plan:
  • Watch for intermediate syndrome × 4 days.
  • Watch for delayed neuropathy 1–3 weeks.
  • Family / community education on pesticide safety.
  • Public health notification.
  • Cholinesterase recovery monitoring.
Teaching points:
  • Cholinergic toxidrome: SLUDGE + Killer Bs.
  • Atropine titrated to secretion clearance, not HR.
  • Pralidoxime before aging completes (variable by agent).
  • Provider PPE essential for decontamination.
  • Intermediate syndrome and delayed neuropathy possible.
Case 100 — Button Battery in Esophagus HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 2-year-old presents 4 hours after suspected button battery ingestion (found her playing with battery compartment of remote control, missing button battery). Now: vomiting, drooling, refusing to eat. CXR confirms button battery lodged in proximal esophagus (double-rim sign on AP, step-off on lateral).
HR 130 | BP 95/55 | T 37.4°C | Distress with swallowing; copious drooling; small amount of blood-tinged emesis

Stage 2 — Recognition and Initial Management

EMERGENCY — button battery in esophagus > 2 hours can cause irreversible damage. Honey 5 mL given to mother to administer every 10 min × 6 doses (only because child > 1 year — coats battery, neutralizes alkaline burns, slows tissue damage while awaiting OR). GI / endoscopy mobilized for emergent removal. NPO; IV access; cardiac monitoring; hemodynamic stability assessed.
Why is honey vs sucralfate vs nothing the right pre-removal strategy?
  • Lithium 3V button batteries cause severe tissue burns via electrolysis (hydroxide generation at negative pole).
  • Honey acidifies and viscously coats battery — reduces local tissue damage.
  • Studies (Anfang/Jatana) show honey markedly reduces esophageal injury in animal models.
  • Sucralfate slurry alternative if honey unavailable.
  • NOT honey in infants < 1 year (botulism risk).
  • Doesn't replace emergent removal — bridges to OR.

Stage 3 — Removal and Evaluation

Emergent endoscopic removal at 5 hours post-ingestion. Battery removed; immediate endoscopic evaluation: focal esophageal mucosal injury (Grade 2 — erythematous, edematous, no perforation). Photographs documented. Repeat endoscopy at 48 hours to assess for progression / fistula. Daily monitoring for vomiting, hematemesis, melena, mediastinitis signs.

Stage 4 — Long-term Monitoring

Day 3: repeat endoscopy shows minimal progression of injury; small ulcer present. NPO continued. PPI initiated. Sucralfate 1 g QID. By day 10: clear liquids tolerated; progression of injury halted. Long-term follow-up at 1, 3, 6 months for stricture surveillance (esophageal stricture risk). Family education on button battery safety. Mother instructed on tape over battery compartments.
Final Diagnosis: Button battery (lithium 3V) esophageal ingestion with Grade 2 mucosal injury; treated with emergent endoscopic removal and supportive care; surveillance for stricture / fistula.
Plan:
  • Repeat endoscopy at 48 hours and as clinically indicated.
  • NPO until tolerating clear liquids and injury improving.
  • PPI + sucralfate.
  • Family education on battery safety.
  • Long-term surveillance for esophageal stricture.
  • Public health awareness.
Teaching points:
  • Button battery in esophagus: emergent endoscopic removal within 2 hours.
  • Honey for > 1 year as bridge to OR.
  • Sucralfate alternative.
  • Double-rim and step-off signs on X-ray.
  • Long-term stricture risk surveillance.
Case 101 — Severe TCA Overdose HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 16-year-old girl found by parents 3 hours after ingesting ~ 50 amitriptyline tablets (intent: suicidal). On arrival: drowsy, tachycardic (HR 145), hypotensive (BP 85/45). Initial ECG: QRS 145 ms (widened), R wave in aVR 5 mm (classic TCA pattern). Mild seizure activity en route.
HR 145 | BP 85/45 | RR 22 | SpO2 96% RA | GCS 11; mydriasis; flushing; dry mucous membranes (anticholinergic); QRS 145 ms; bradyarrhythmia tendency

Stage 2 — Management

Sodium bicarbonate 2 mEq/kg IV bolus immediately for QRS > 100 ms — QRS narrows to 110 ms within 5 min. Bicarbonate infusion (150 mEq in 1 L D5W at 250 mL/h) titrated to keep serum pH 7.50–7.55. Activated charcoal 1 g/kg via NG (intubated for airway protection). Lorazepam 0.1 mg/kg for seizures (continued midazolam infusion). Norepinephrine for hypotension. Cardiac monitoring continuous.
Why is sodium bicarbonate the cornerstone of TCA overdose treatment?
  • TCA toxicity from Na channel blockade (similar mechanism to TCA antiarrhythmic effect).
  • NaHCO3 increases extracellular Na, overcoming channel blockade.
  • Alkalinization (pH 7.50–7.55) reduces TCA binding to channels and increases protein binding.
  • Improves cardiac conduction (QRS narrowing).
  • Also helps hypotension by increasing α-receptor responsiveness.
  • Avoid pH > 7.55 (causes hypokalemia, hypocalcemia).

Stage 3 — Refractory Toxicity

Despite optimization, repeat ventricular tachycardia at hour 6. Cardiac arrest narrowly avoided with bicarbonate boluses and norepinephrine. IV lipid emulsion (20%) given 1.5 mL/kg bolus then 0.25 mL/kg/min × 30 min — 'lipid sink' theory for lipophilic drug binding. Stabilization achieved.

Stage 4 — Recovery and Long-term

By 24 hours: hemodynamics stable, QRS normalized, seizures resolved. Continued ICU monitoring 24 hours. Psychiatric consultation and admission to inpatient psychiatric unit when medically cleared (day 3). Continued mental health treatment; SSRI started for depression; safety plan; family therapy. Long-term: ongoing psychiatric care; cognitive behavioral therapy.
Final Diagnosis: Severe amitriptyline overdose (suicide attempt); treated with sodium bicarbonate, supportive care, and IV lipid emulsion; survived with full recovery.
Plan:
  • Inpatient psychiatric care.
  • SSRI and CBT.
  • Safety plan and family education.
  • Long-term mental health follow-up.
  • Suicide risk monitoring.
  • Reduce access to medications at home.
Teaching points:
  • TCA toxicity: Na channel blockade (cardiac, neuro), α-blockade (hypotension), anticholinergic.
  • Sodium bicarbonate cornerstone for QRS > 100 ms.
  • Norepinephrine preferred vasopressor.
  • AVOID phenytoin (Na channel blocker).
  • Lipid emulsion for severe refractory.
  • Psychiatric admission and follow-up.
Case 102 — Beta-Blocker Overdose with HIE Therapy HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 14-year-old with depression took her grandmother's propranolol (~ 30 tablets of 40 mg each = 1200 mg). Found 4 hours later in stupor. ED arrival: bradycardia 45, hypotension 70/35, hypoglycemia (glucose 2.0 mmol/L), seizure.
HR 45 | BP 70/35 | T 36.5°C | GCS 9 | Glucose 2.0 mmol/L; seizure terminated with midazolam; pupils mid-dilated; wide QRS 130 ms (propranolol Na channel blockade)

Stage 2 — Initial Management

Airway protection / intubation. Glucose D10 IV bolus 2.5 mL/kg then continuous. Sodium bicarbonate for QRS widening. Atropine 0.02 mg/kg — ineffective on HR. Glucagon 0.1 mg/kg IV bolus (5 mg in this teen) then infusion 1 mg/h — modest improvement. Continued hypotension despite norepinephrine + epinephrine. Decision to start high-dose insulin euglycemia therapy (HIE).
What is the mechanism of HIE in beta-blocker overdose?
  • Beta-blockers cause myocardial depression and reduced glucose uptake.
  • Insulin at high doses (1 U/kg/h) acts as potent inotrope — improves cardiac output independent of β-receptors.
  • Insulin also enhances myocardial glucose uptake — energy substrate during shock.
  • Concurrent D10/D20 maintains euglycemia (insulin without glucose = severe hypoglycemia).
  • Effects take 30–60 minutes to manifest — patient may need additional support during transition.

Stage 3 — HIE Therapy

Insulin (regular) 1 U/kg IV bolus, then continuous infusion 0.5 U/kg/h titrated up to 1.5 U/kg/h. Concurrent D20 (or D10 with concentrated dextrose) IV to maintain glucose 4–8 mmol/L — often needs 1.5–2 g/kg/h glucose. Hourly glucose; K replacement (insulin shifts K intracellular). Within 60 minutes of starting HIE: BP improving, HR rising, weaning vasopressors. By hour 3: hemodynamically stable.

Stage 4 — Course

HIE continued 24 hours; vasopressors weaned. IV lipid emulsion considered but not given given clinical improvement. Activated charcoal × 2 doses (4 hours apart) — limited yield given timing. Continued ICU monitoring 48 hours. Psychiatric consultation; transferred to inpatient psychiatric unit. Long-term: ongoing psychiatric care; medication safety review; family therapy.
Final Diagnosis: Severe propranolol overdose (intentional); refractory to standard measures; treated with high-dose insulin euglycemia therapy (HIE) with excellent response.
Plan:
  • Inpatient psychiatric care.
  • Mental health treatment.
  • Medication safety at home.
  • Family education.
  • Long-term follow-up.
Teaching points:
  • HIE: most effective beta-blocker overdose therapy.
  • Insulin 1 U/kg bolus + 0.5–1 U/kg/h infusion + concurrent dextrose.
  • Glucagon helpful but insufficient alone.
  • NaHCO3 for QRS widening (propranolol Na channel).
  • Lipid emulsion for refractory.
  • Psychiatric care after stabilization.
Case 103 — Pediatric Salicylate Poisoning MODERATE

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 15-year-old presents 3 hours after intentional ingestion of ~ 50 aspirin tablets (suicide attempt). Symptoms: severe vomiting, tinnitus, hyperventilation, agitation. Salicylate level 65 mg/dL (rising).
HR 110 | BP 110/65 | T 37.9°C | RR 32 (hyperpnea) | ABG: pH 7.45, PaCO2 25, HCO3 18 (mixed respiratory alkalosis + metabolic acidosis — classic for salicylate); K 3.2; glucose 3.8

Stage 2 — Management

Activated charcoal 1 g/kg PO immediately. K replacement (essential — must replete K before alkalinization works). Glucose IV (target euglycemia — CSF hypoglycemia possible even with normal serum glucose). Sodium bicarbonate IV infusion (150 mEq in 1 L D5W at 200 mL/h) to maintain serum pH ≥ 7.45 (traps salicylate ionized form in serum, preventing redistribution to brain). Urine alkalinization (target urine pH ≥ 7.5) enhances renal excretion. Foley catheter for urine pH monitoring.
Why is urinary alkalinization critical?
  • Salicylate (weak acid; pKa 3) is more ionized in alkaline urine.
  • Ionized form trapped in renal tubular lumen → cannot be reabsorbed → eliminated.
  • Urinary pH 7.5–8.0 increases excretion 10–20-fold compared to acidic urine.
  • Serum pH 7.45–7.55 also reduces redistribution to brain (preventing severe CNS toxicity, cerebral edema).
  • Adequate K essential — hypokalemia prevents bicarbonate alkalinization (kidney can't excrete bicarbonate without K).

Stage 3 — Worsening

Despite optimization, salicylate level continues to rise to 95 mg/dL (delayed absorption from concretions). Patient developing altered mental status. Hemodialysis indications met: severe acidosis, refractory toxicity, AMS, level > 100 in adults. Hemodialysis × 6 hours reduces level to 35 mg/dL. Bicarbonate infusion continued. AMS resolves with reduced level.

Stage 4 — Recovery

By 24 hours: level < 30 mg/dL; tinnitus resolved; mental status normal. Continued bicarbonate infusion to keep urine alkalinized × 24 more hours. Discharged to inpatient psychiatric care day 4. Long-term: psychiatric care; SSRIs; CBT; safety plan; family therapy.
Final Diagnosis: Severe salicylate poisoning (intentional); managed with alkalinization, K replacement, and hemodialysis.
Plan:
  • Inpatient psychiatric care.
  • Mental health treatment.
  • Safety plan and medication access reduction.
  • Long-term psychiatric follow-up.
Teaching points:
  • Salicylate: mixed respiratory alkalosis + metabolic acidosis classic.
  • K depletion limits alkalinization — replete first.
  • Bicarbonate to keep serum pH ≥ 7.45 prevents CNS redistribution.
  • Urine pH ≥ 7.5 increases excretion 10–20-fold.
  • Hemodialysis indications: severe acidosis, AMS, level > 100, AKI, pulmonary edema.
  • Glucose support — CSF hypoglycemia possible.
Case 104 — Methanol Poisoning with Visual Loss HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 15-year-old developed acute encephalopathy and vision loss 18 hours after ingesting an unknown substance (suspected attempted intoxication from windshield-washer fluid). ABG: pH 7.05, anion gap 32, osmolar gap 35. Plasma methanol level: 80 mg/dL (severe).
HR 110 | BP 110/65 | T 36.9°C | GCS 11 | Visual acuity counting fingers bilaterally; fundus: optic disc hyperemia and edema (methanol toxicity); severe HAGMA + osmolar gap

Stage 2 — Diagnosis

Methanol metabolism: alcohol dehydrogenase converts methanol → formaldehyde; aldehyde dehydrogenase → formate. Formate is the toxic metabolite — causes optic toxicity (basal ganglia injury), severe acidosis. Diagnosis confirmed: methanol poisoning.
Why is fomepizole superior to ethanol as ADH inhibitor?
  • Fomepizole (4-methylpyrazole) competitive ADH inhibitor; no CNS depression.
  • Ethanol also blocks ADH but causes CNS depression and unpredictable levels.
  • Fomepizole easier to dose and titrate.
  • No need for level monitoring (vs ethanol needing target level 100–150 mg/dL).
  • Both prevent further toxic metabolite formation.
  • Hemodialysis removes both parent alcohol and existing metabolites (formate).

Stage 3 — Treatment

Fomepizole 15 mg/kg IV bolus, then 10 mg/kg q12h × 4 doses, then 15 mg/kg q12h (alcohol dehydrogenase inhibitor). Folinic acid 1 mg/kg IV q6h (enhances formate metabolism). Sodium bicarbonate for severe acidosis (target pH ≥ 7.3). Hemodialysis emergent given severe acidosis + level > 50 + end-organ toxicity (visual loss). 6 hours hemodialysis; level reduced to 15 mg/dL post-dialysis.

Stage 4 — Course

Acidosis corrected with combined bicarbonate + dialysis. Visual function returns partially over 1 week — final acuity 20/200 in both eyes (permanent partial visual loss from formate-induced optic neuropathy). Survives. Psychiatric admission and follow-up for substance use disorder; family therapy. Counseling about lifelong avoidance of further toxic substances.
Final Diagnosis: Severe methanol poisoning with permanent optic neuropathy; treated with fomepizole + folinic acid + hemodialysis.
Plan:
  • Inpatient psychiatric care for substance use disorder.
  • Mental health treatment.
  • Family therapy.
  • Ongoing ophthalmology for visual rehabilitation.
  • Visual aids and accommodations.
  • Substance use counseling.
Teaching points:
  • Methanol: formate metabolite causes optic neuropathy.
  • HAGMA + osmolar gap + visual symptoms classic.
  • Fomepizole superior to ethanol as ADH inhibitor.
  • Folinic acid enhances formate metabolism.
  • Hemodialysis for severe toxicity.
  • Visual loss may be permanent.
Case 105 — Exertional Heat Stroke in Athlete MODERATE

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 16-year-old wrestler is found unconscious in locker room after final weight-cut workout in hot facility. Had been 'sweating off' 5 kg over 48 hours with restricted water intake. Found 30 minutes after collapse.
HR 165 | BP 80/40 | T 41.5°C core | GCS 7 | Hot, dry skin (despite exertional context — sweating exhausted); CK 65,000; AKI Cr 2.8; lactic acidosis pH 7.15

Stage 2 — Immediate Cooling

Cold water immersion (gold standard for exertional heat stroke) — patient placed in ice water bath while resuscitated with IV fluids. Continuous core temp monitoring (rectal probe). Target: cool to 38.5°C in 30 minutes. IV NS 2 L bolus → 1 L over next hour. Intubation for airway protection given GCS 7. Continuous cardiac monitoring; rhabdomyolysis treatment with urinary alkalinization.
Why is cold water immersion preferred over other cooling methods?
  • Cold water immersion (1–7°C water): cooling rate 0.20–0.35°C/min.
  • Faster than: evaporative cooling (0.07°C/min), cooling blanket (0.05°C/min), ice packs.
  • Survival improved when core T < 40°C achieved within 30 minutes.
  • Limitations: practical setup, monitoring; shivering can occur.
  • Alternative: ice slurry immersion if water unavailable.
  • Continuous monitoring essential — overcooling is risk.

Stage 3 — ICU Management

After cooling to 38.5°C in 25 min, transferred to PICU. AKI from rhabdomyolysis managed with IV fluids and urinary alkalinization (sodium bicarbonate target urine pH ≥ 7). Hepatic dysfunction (AST/ALT > 1000) monitoring. DIC labs trended; mild coagulopathy resolved with supportive care. cEEG: no seizures. Stress ulcer prophylaxis.

Stage 4 — Recovery

By day 3: extubated; renal function recovering (Cr 1.5); hepatic function normalizing. Day 7: discharged. Long-term: medical clearance with cardiac evaluation before return to play (typically 6+ months wait); education on safe weight management; refer for nutritional counseling about weight cuts; sports medicine consultation; school athletic department safety review (cooling stations, water access, acclimatization protocols).
Final Diagnosis: Exertional heat stroke with multi-organ involvement (AKI, rhabdomyolysis, hepatic dysfunction, mild DIC); treated with cold water immersion and supportive ICU care.
Plan:
  • Cardiac evaluation before return to play.
  • Nutritional counseling for safe weight management.
  • Sports medicine follow-up.
  • School athletic safety review.
  • Family / patient education on prevention.
  • Renal and hepatic surveillance.
Teaching points:
  • Exertional heat stroke in adolescents — wrestling weight cuts high risk.
  • Cold water immersion is gold standard — cool to 38.5°C in 30 min.
  • Multi-organ failure common (AKI, rhabdomyolysis, hepatic, DIC).
  • Avoid antipyretics (not effective for heat stroke).
  • Dantrolene NOT effective.
  • Prevention: acclimatization, hydration, safe weight loss, education.
Case 106 — HACE at High Altitude HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 14-year-old on a school trip to Andes mountains (4200 m altitude) develops severe headache, vomiting, ataxia, then confusion by day 2 of arrival. Initially attributed to 'flu.' Group physician recognized HACE; emergency descent attempted.
HR 110 | BP 130/85 | T 37.4°C | GCS 12 | SpO2 70% on RA at altitude | Marked ataxia, dysarthria, papilledema

Stage 2 — Field Management

Descent initiated by helicopter (most effective intervention). Supplemental oxygen (3 L/min via NC). Dexamethasone 8 mg IM/IV. Gamow bag (portable hyperbaric chamber) inflated during descent transit (simulates lower altitude). Within 1 hour at lower altitude (2500 m): mental status improving; ataxia reducing.
Why is dexamethasone effective in HACE but not as primary therapy?
  • HACE involves hypoxia-induced vasogenic edema (BBB disruption).
  • Dexamethasone reduces inflammatory mediators contributing to edema.
  • Reduces ICP and improves symptoms.
  • NOT a substitute for descent — patients still require lower altitude.
  • Adjunct for severe cases or when descent delayed.
  • Acetazolamide for prevention (not acute treatment).

Stage 3 — Hospital Management

Transferred to regional hospital at sea level. Continued dexamethasone 4 mg q6h. MRI brain: vasogenic edema at splenium of corpus callosum (microbleeds on SWI) — characteristic HACE pattern. cEEG: no seizures. Continued supportive care. Watch for HAPE (concurrent in 50%); chest X-ray clear initially but rule out development.

Stage 4 — Recovery

By 48 hours at sea level: symptoms resolved; cognition intact; ataxia resolved. Discharged home with patient and family education about high-altitude travel: acclimatization (gradual ascent, rest days), acetazolamide prophylaxis for future ascents (start day before), warning signs, emergency action plan. Cleared for normal activities; cautioned about future high-altitude travel.
Final Diagnosis: High-altitude cerebral edema (HACE) at 4200 m; treated with descent + dexamethasone + supplemental oxygen; full recovery.
Plan:
  • Education on altitude sickness prevention.
  • Acetazolamide for future high-altitude ascents.
  • Gradual ascent (max 300–500 m gain per night above 3000 m).
  • Adequate hydration; avoid alcohol.
  • Recognize symptoms early; descend if worsening.
  • Emergency action plan for school trips.
Teaching points:
  • HACE: severe form of AMS at altitude; vasogenic edema.
  • Descent is most effective treatment.
  • Dexamethasone + O2 + Gamow bag adjuncts.
  • MRI: splenium of corpus callosum microbleeds characteristic.
  • Often coexists with HAPE.
  • Prevention: gradual ascent, acetazolamide.
Case 107 — Naegleria fowleri Primary Amebic Meningoencephalitis HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 12-year-old presents with 2 days of severe frontal headache, fever, neck stiffness, and now confusion. Recent freshwater swimming in a warm lake (Texas) 5 days ago. Initial workup as bacterial meningitis.
HR 115 | BP 110/70 | T 39.4°C | GCS 11 declining | Meningismus; photophobia; bilateral papilledema; rapid deterioration over hours

Stage 2 — CSF Wet Mount Diagnosis

LP: opening pressure 40 cmH2O (markedly elevated); CSF WBC 800 (PMN predominant — mimics bacterial meningitis); RBC 5000 (hemorrhagic); glucose 1.0 mmol/L (low); protein 3.5 g/L (very high). Gram stain negative. Bacterial cultures negative. WET MOUNT (requested specifically): motile trophozoites identified — DIAGNOSTIC for Naegleria fowleri primary amebic meningoencephalitis (PAM).
Why does PAM diagnosis require specifically requested wet mount?
  • Routine CSF analysis does not include free-living amoeba assessment.
  • Naegleria trophozoites visible on wet mount of fresh CSF.
  • Must request specifically and examine immediately (organisms die quickly).
  • Wright stain shows morphology.
  • PCR (Naegleria-specific) confirmatory but takes time.
  • Initial impression often bacterial meningitis until wet mount reveals amoebae.

Stage 3 — Emergency Treatment

CDC contacted immediately (1-770-488-7100) for guidance and miltefosine compassionate use access. Multi-drug regimen initiated: amphotericin B liposomal 5 mg/kg IV q24h + amphotericin B intrathecal 0.5 mg/24h; miltefosine 50 mg PO q8h (available via CDC); rifampin 10 mg/kg q24h; azithromycin 10 mg/kg IV q24h; fluconazole 10 mg/kg IV q24h. Therapeutic hypothermia 33°C initiated (reported in survivors). Aggressive ICP management (3% saline, head-up, normocapnia).

Stage 4 — Course

Despite aggressive treatment, neurologic deterioration continued — comatose by day 3; brain death determined day 5. Family supported; bereavement counseling. Public health notification (rare reportable disease). Family informed about risk factors and prevention for community.
Final Diagnosis: Primary amebic meningoencephalitis from Naegleria fowleri after freshwater exposure; despite aggressive multi-drug treatment, fatal outcome.
Process:
  • Aggressive multi-drug treatment per CDC guidance.
  • CDC contact essential.
  • Therapeutic hypothermia in survivors.
  • Aggressive ICP management.
  • Family support and bereavement.
  • Public health notification.
  • Community education on prevention.
Teaching points:
  • Naegleria fowleri PAM: freshwater exposure → entry via nose → rapid encephalitis.
  • Wet mount must be specifically requested.
  • Mortality > 95% even with treatment.
  • Multi-drug regimen + CDC consultation + miltefosine.
  • Therapeutic hypothermia reported in survivors.
  • Prevention: nose clips in warm freshwater; sterile water for neti pots.
Case 108 — Severe Synthetic Cannabinoid Toxicity HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 16-year-old male is brought from a party where he smoked 'spice' (synthetic cannabinoid). Now: severe agitation, paranoia, hallucinations, hyperthermia 39.5°C, severe muscle rigidity, autonomic instability.
HR 165 | BP 195/110 | T 39.5°C | GCS 9 | Severe agitation requiring 4 people to restrain; pupils mid-dilated; muscle rigidity

Stage 2 — Management

IV access (difficult given agitation); benzodiazepines (lorazepam 0.1 mg/kg IV repeat × 3) for agitation and sympathetic excess. Aggressive cooling: cold IV fluids, cooling blanket, ice packs to groin/axillae. Cardiac monitoring shows sinus tachycardia without ischemia. CK starting to rise (5000); creatinine baseline. Drug screen sent; comprehensive toxicology pending.
Why are synthetic cannabinoids more dangerous than natural cannabis?
  • Synthetic cannabinoids (K2, Spice, Mojo) are full agonists at CB1 receptor (natural THC is partial agonist).
  • Much higher potency — 100× or more.
  • Unpredictable purity and composition (clandestine production).
  • Severe toxicity: seizures, MI, stroke, rhabdomyolysis, AKI, cardiac arrest.
  • Worse psychiatric effects than natural cannabis.
  • Often laced with other substances (opioids, benzodiazepines, organophosphates).

Stage 3 — Multi-organ Issues

Rhabdomyolysis: CK peaks 35,000 by 24 hours; IV fluids and urinary alkalinization; serial creatinine. AKI mild (Cr 1.5, baseline 0.6). Cardiac monitoring continuous; troponin negative. Hyperthermia resolved with cooling. Psychiatric assessment when stable; substance use counseling. Patient educated about dangers of synthetic cannabinoids.

Stage 4 — Recovery

By 48 hours: hemodynamically stable; rhabdomyolysis improving; AKI resolved. Discharged to outpatient mental health and substance use treatment. Family counseling. School-based education program engaged about synthetic drug awareness. Long-term: ongoing substance use treatment; mental health follow-up.
Final Diagnosis: Severe synthetic cannabinoid toxicity with rhabdomyolysis, AKI, and hyperthermia; treated with supportive care.
Plan:
  • Outpatient mental health and substance use treatment.
  • Family counseling.
  • School-based education.
  • Long-term follow-up.
  • Education about dangers of synthetic drugs.
Teaching points:
  • Synthetic cannabinoids: high potency, unpredictable.
  • Severe toxicity: seizures, MI, stroke, rhabdomyolysis, hyperthermia.
  • Supportive care: benzodiazepines, cooling, IV fluids.
  • Substance use counseling.
  • Public health concern with adolescent recreational use.
Case 109 — Carbamazepine Overdose with Hemodialysis HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 15-year-old with epilepsy on chronic carbamazepine ingested ~ 100 tablets (200 mg each = 20 g) of carbamazepine 6 hours ago. On arrival: somnolent, ataxic, with nystagmus. Repeat seizure on arrival. Level: 45 µg/mL (severe).
HR 130 | BP 95/55 | T 37.4°C | GCS 9 | Horizontal nystagmus; ataxia; mild hyperthermia; QRS 130 ms (widened from baseline 100 ms — Na channel blockade)

Stage 2 — Initial Management

Airway protection / intubation given GCS 9 and seizure risk. Multi-dose activated charcoal initiated: 1 g/kg via NG immediately, then 0.5 g/kg q4h (interrupts enterohepatic recycling). Sodium bicarbonate 1 mEq/kg IV bolus for QRS widening — QRS narrows to 115 ms. Levetiracetam loaded for seizures (AVOID phenytoin which would worsen Na channel toxicity). Continuous EEG monitoring.
Why is multi-dose activated charcoal so important for carbamazepine?
  • Carbamazepine has significant enterohepatic recycling (excreted in bile, reabsorbed in small intestine).
  • Single-dose charcoal binds only what's in the gut at one time.
  • Multi-dose charcoal continuously binds carbamazepine being re-excreted into bile.
  • Effectively shortens elimination half-life.
  • Continue until level < 20 µg/mL or clinical improvement.
  • Requires intact / protected airway.

Stage 3 — Worsening Toxicity

Level peaks at 55 µg/mL at hour 18 (delayed absorption from sustained-release formulation). Hemodialysis with high-flux filter initiated for severe toxicity, refractory cardiovascular instability, and continued elevated level despite MDAC. 6 hours of dialysis reduces level to 25 µg/mL. Hemodynamics stable; QRS narrowed.

Stage 4 — Recovery

By 36 hours: level < 20 µg/mL; mental status clearing; off vasopressors; extubated. ICU discharge day 4. Psychiatric assessment: intentional overdose; psychiatric admission for further care. Long-term: ongoing psychiatric care; epilepsy management with alternative AED (started oxcarbazepine — also Na channel blocker, will need careful titration; or alternative agent); safety plan; family therapy.
Final Diagnosis: Severe carbamazepine overdose with cardiotoxicity, seizures, and CNS depression; managed with multi-dose activated charcoal, sodium bicarbonate, and hemodialysis.
Plan:
  • Inpatient psychiatric care.
  • Mental health treatment.
  • Continued epilepsy management with caution.
  • Safety plan and medication safety at home.
  • Family therapy.
  • Long-term follow-up.
Teaching points:
  • Carbamazepine overdose: Na channel blockade, anticholinergic, CNS depression.
  • Multi-dose activated charcoal crucial (enterohepatic recycling).
  • NaHCO3 for QRS widening.
  • Hemodialysis for severe levels (> 40), refractory cardiotoxicity, hepatic failure.
  • AVOID phenytoin (worsens Na channel toxicity).
  • Psychiatric care after stabilization.
Case 110 — Anti-GABA-B Receptor Encephalitis with Refractory Status Epilepticus HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 12-year-old previously well boy presents with 5 days of behavioural change, insomnia, and increasing irritability, followed by a focal motor seizure that secondarily generalised. EMS notes prolonged convulsive activity (> 15 min) — given IM midazolam. On arrival in the ED he has further focal seizures (right face/arm clonus) recurring every 8–10 min without recovery between events.
HR 138 | BP 132/82 | T 37.9°C | SpO₂ 96% RA → 92% post-seizure | GCS 9 between events; 6 during ictal phase

Stage 2 — Initial Workup

Loaded with levetiracetam 60 mg/kg and fosphenytoin 20 PE/kg; seizures persist. Intubated for airway protection and started on midazolam infusion titrated to 0.3 mg/kg/h. CT head normal. LP: WBC 28 (lymphocytic predominance), protein 0.55 g/L, glucose normal. MRI: subtle T2/FLAIR hyperintensity bilateral mesial temporal lobes, no enhancement.
Differential at this point?
  • Autoimmune encephalitis (anti-NMDAR most common, but anti-GABA-B presents with early prominent seizures and limbic syndrome).
  • HSV encephalitis (cover empirically with acyclovir).
  • Other paraneoplastic / autoantibody encephalitides.
  • Send CSF + serum autoimmune encephalitis panel; check for small-cell lung tumour in adults (rare in children).

Stage 3 — Refractory SE → Super-Refractory

By hour 36: convulsive activity controlled but cEEG shows non-convulsive seizures every 4–6 min from left temporal lead with spread. Failed midazolam + ketamine infusion. Switched to pentobarbital infusion targeting burst-suppression (10–20 sec interburst interval). Started pulse methylprednisolone 30 mg/kg/d × 5d. IVIG 2 g/kg over 5 d initiated.

Stage 4 — Confirmation & Second-Line Immunotherapy

CSF + serum return positive for anti-GABA-B receptor antibodies; NMDAR negative. CT chest/abdomen and testicular US negative for tumour (rare in paediatric GABA-B). Despite steroids + IVIG, breakthrough seizures continue. Plasmapheresis (5 sessions q48h) added; rituximab 375 mg/m² weekly × 4 started.
Why anti-GABA-B is uniquely seizure-prominent?
  • GABA-B receptor is the principal inhibitory metabotropic receptor — antibody-mediated downregulation removes tonic inhibition.
  • Result: severe limbic seizures, often refractory SE as the presenting feature.
  • Behavioural change & memory loss often follow rather than precede seizures.
  • In adults strongly associated with SCLC; paediatric cases are usually non-paraneoplastic.
  • Responds to immunotherapy but seizure burden often dictates outcome.

Stage 5 — Recovery

After 14 days of burst-suppression weaning and 4 doses of rituximab, cEEG clears. Weaned off anaesthetic infusions; extubated day 18. Discharged home day 32 on lacosamide + levetiracetam + prednisone taper. At 6-month follow-up: full cognitive recovery, no further seizures, CD19 still suppressed.
Final Diagnosis: Anti-GABA-B receptor autoimmune encephalitis presenting with super-refractory status epilepticus; treated with anaesthetic burst-suppression and combined first/second-line immunotherapy.
Plan:
  • Continue rituximab maintenance q6 months × 2 y.
  • AED taper at 12 months if seizure-free.
  • Neuropsychology assessment at 6 and 12 months.
  • Annual MRI brain × 2 y.
  • Tumour surveillance not required in paediatric non-paraneoplastic case.
Teaching points:
  • Anti-GABA-B = seizure-dominant autoimmune encephalitis; consider when SE is first symptom.
  • Don't wait for antibody result — start empiric immunotherapy after HSV is covered/excluded.
  • Escalate early to second-line (rituximab ± plasmapheresis) in refractory cases.
  • Anaesthetic burst-suppression buys time but doesn't treat the disease — immunotherapy is curative.
  • cEEG mandatory: clinical seizures often hide non-convulsive activity.
Case 111 — Subacute Sclerosing Panencephalitis (SSPE) in an Adolescent HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 14-year-old boy from a region with low measles vaccination coverage presents with 3 months of progressive academic decline, personality change, and 4 weeks of intermittent myoclonic jerks of the trunk and limbs occurring every 5–10 seconds — initially mistaken for tics. Past medical history: measles at age 18 months (unvaccinated). On exam: bradykinesia, brisk reflexes, periodic head-drops; cognition impaired (MMSE 18/30).
HR 88 | BP 110/68 | T 37.0°C | GCS 14 | Periodic myoclonus q 5–8 sec — synchronous with EEG bursts

Stage 2 — Investigations

EEG: characteristic periodic high-amplitude generalised slow-wave complexes every 4–10 seconds, time-locked to myoclonic jerks (Radermecker complexes). MRI: bilateral cortical and subcortical T2/FLAIR hyperintensity, predominantly posterior; later atrophy. CSF: oligoclonal bands present, IgG index elevated, very high anti-measles IgG titre (CSF:serum ratio ↑↑). Serum anti-measles IgG also markedly elevated. PCR for measles RNA in CSF positive.
How is SSPE diagnosed?
  • Clinical: progressive cognitive decline, myoclonus, motor regression.
  • EEG: characteristic periodic Radermecker complexes.
  • CSF: elevated anti-measles IgG with reduced CSF:serum ratio (i.e., intrathecal synthesis).
  • Imaging: bilateral cortical/subcortical white-matter changes evolving to atrophy.
  • Two of these features in the right clinical context = diagnosis.

Stage 3 — Progression & PICU Admission

Over 6 weeks: rapid decline to stage III (Jabbour). Myoclonus more severe, dysphagia develops with aspiration pneumonia, requires intubation. Spasticity, decerebrate posturing intermittently, autonomic instability with labile BP and temperature.

Stage 4 — PICU Management

Supportive care: ventilation, enteral nutrition via PEG, treatment of aspiration pneumonia with antibiotics. Myoclonus partially controlled with levetiracetam + clonazepam. Intraventricular interferon-α + oral isoprinosine (inosine pranobex) trialled (limited evidence, may slow progression). Autonomic storms managed with clonidine, gabapentin. Family meeting addresses prognosis: median survival 1–3 years from diagnosis.
What is the role of disease-modifying therapy?
  • Isoprinosine: weak evidence for delaying progression; widely used.
  • Intraventricular or intrathecal interferon-α: some studies suggest stabilisation in 30–35%.
  • Ribavirin: investigational; mostly anecdotal.
  • No cure exists — supportive care is mainstay.
  • Prevention via MMR vaccination is the only effective intervention.

Stage 5 — Trajectory

After 10 days, family elects compassionate extubation and transition to comfort care given progression to stage IV (vegetative state) despite therapy. Patient transferred to inpatient hospice; dies 4 months later.
Final Diagnosis: Subacute sclerosing panencephalitis (SSPE), Jabbour stage IV, following measles infection in infancy.
Plan:
  • Comfort care; symptom control.
  • Family bereavement support.
  • Public health notification: missed vaccination case — outbreak risk assessment.
  • Sibling vaccination review.
  • Community education and MMR catch-up campaign.
Teaching points:
  • SSPE = late, fatal complication of measles (latency 6–10 y, risk 1/600 in < 12-month infections).
  • EEG Radermecker complexes are pathognomonic in the right clinical context.
  • Intrathecal anti-measles antibody synthesis confirms.
  • No curative therapy — emphasis on MMR vaccination to prevent.
  • Public health reporting essential.
Case 112 — Second Impact Syndrome in an Adolescent Athlete HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 16-year-old hockey player sustains a second head impact 9 days after a witnessed concussion (he had persistent headache, fogginess, and dizziness but had returned to play against medical advice). After the second hit, he walks off the ice but collapses within 60 seconds. EMS finds him unresponsive with dilated left pupil. Intubated at scene.
HR 52 | BP 175/95 | SpO₂ 94% bag-valve-mask | GCS 3T | Left pupil 6 mm fixed, right 4 mm sluggish | Cushing triad

Stage 2 — ED Workup

CT head: diffuse cerebral oedema with effaced cisterns and incipient uncal herniation; no large mass lesion, no acute haemorrhage of significance (small thin SDH). This out-of-proportion swelling after a relatively minor impact is the hallmark.
What is the pathophysiology of second-impact syndrome?
  • Concussion → metabolic crisis (↑ glutamate, ionic flux, energy demand) and impaired cerebrovascular autoregulation.
  • Within this window (~7–10 d), a second impact triggers catastrophic loss of vascular autoregulation.
  • Cerebral blood flow becomes pressure-passive → diffuse vasogenic + hyperaemic oedema.
  • Rapid rise in ICP → herniation within minutes.
  • Almost unique to adolescents (immature autoregulation, contact sports).

Stage 3 — Neuro-ICU

Tier-1: HOB 30°, head neutral, sedation/analgesia, normothermia. Hyperosmolar therapy: 3% saline 5 mL/kg bolus → continuous infusion targeting Na 150–155, osm 320–340. ICP monitor placed: opening pressure 38 mmHg. Tier 2: pentobarbital infusion, mild hyperventilation (PaCO₂ 32–35) for refractory rises. ICP remains 28–34 mmHg.

Stage 4 — Decompression

After 4 hours of refractory ICP > 25 mmHg despite Tier-2 measures, taken for bilateral decompressive craniectomy with duraplasty. Post-op ICP 14 mmHg. Continued cooling 35°C × 48 h then slow rewarm. Multimodal monitoring: PbtO₂ 22 mmHg (acceptable), CPP > 60 mmHg targeted by age.

Stage 5 — Outcome

After 14 days in PICU, weaned off pentobarbital and extubated to high-flow. Significant residual cognitive deficits, left-sided weakness, expressive aphasia. Transferred to inpatient rehab. At 6 months: GOS-E Peds 5 (severe disability) — independent in basic ADLs but unable to return to school full-time; permanent retirement from contact sports.
Final Diagnosis: Second-impact syndrome with diffuse cerebral oedema and herniation, treated with hyperosmolar therapy and bilateral decompressive craniectomy.
Plan:
  • Long-term inpatient → outpatient neurorehab.
  • Cognitive remediation; school re-entry plan.
  • Permanent retirement from contact sports.
  • Family + team education on concussion return-to-play.
  • Public health: review of school/league concussion protocols.
Teaching points:
  • SIS = catastrophic outcome of premature return-to-play in the post-concussion vulnerability window.
  • Mechanism = autoregulatory failure → malignant cerebral oedema.
  • Almost exclusively a paediatric/adolescent phenomenon.
  • Strict adherence to graded return-to-play protocols (e.g., Berlin/Amsterdam consensus) is mandatory.
  • Survival often comes with severe disability; primary prevention is the only effective strategy.
Case 113 — Cystathionine β-Synthase Deficiency Presenting with Stroke MOD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 14-year-old tall, marfanoid boy with mild intellectual disability and ectopia lentis presents with acute right hemiparesis and aphasia over 90 minutes. He has a history of "growing pains," fair complexion, downward lens dislocation noted at age 6 but no genetic workup. Family history: a maternal uncle died at age 22 of "blood clot."
HR 78 | BP 118/72 | T 36.9°C | GCS 13 | Right facial droop, right arm 1/5, right leg 3/5, expressive aphasia | NIHSS 12

Stage 2 — Imaging & Workup

CT head: hyperdense left MCA sign; no haemorrhage. CT angiography: occlusion of left MCA M1 segment. MRI/MRA: confirmed large vessel occlusion with early ischaemic core in left insula and basal ganglia, large penumbra (DWI/PWI mismatch). Time from onset: 3 h 40 min.
Why is this not a typical childhood stroke?
  • Marfanoid habitus + ectopia lentis + intellectual disability + thrombosis = classic homocystinuria phenotype.
  • Marfan: lens dislocation upward; homocystinuria: downward; both are tall and slender.
  • Homocystinuria patients are at very high lifetime thromboembolic risk from young age.
  • Diagnosis: ↑ plasma homocysteine, ↑ methionine, ↓ cystine; confirm CBS gene mutation.

Stage 3 — Acute Stroke Management

Within 4 hours: IV alteplase 0.9 mg/kg given (criteria met: LVO, no haemorrhage, NIHSS > 6, modified Rankin scale 0 pre-event). Mechanical thrombectomy performed at hour 5 with TICI 2b reperfusion. Admitted to PICU for post-thrombectomy monitoring: BP control (goal 130–160 systolic for 24 h), neuro checks q1h, repeat CT at 24 h shows reperfused MCA territory with small infarct in left basal ganglia.

Stage 4 — Aetiology Workup & Long-term Therapy
