Plasma homocysteine 287 µmol/L (normal < 15). Methionine elevated. Echocardiogram: normal (no PFO). Hypercoagulable panel otherwise unrevealing. Diagnosis: classic homocystinuria (CBS deficiency). Started on:
  • Pyridoxine (B6) 500 mg/d trial — patient is B6-responsive (homocysteine drops to 65 µmol/L).
  • Betaine 6 g/d (alternative remethylation).
  • Folate 5 mg/d and B12 1 mg/d.
  • Methionine-restricted diet.
  • Aspirin for secondary stroke prevention.
At 3 months: residual mild right hemiparesis, aphasia resolving; homocysteine maintained < 50.
Why classify as B6-responsive vs B6-non-responsive?
  • B6-responsive (50% of cases): homocysteine drops by > 30% on pharmacologic B6 — milder phenotype, later presentation.
  • B6-non-responsive: requires diet + betaine ± folate; higher complication rate.
  • All patients require lifelong management; pregnancy & surgery require special VTE prophylaxis.
Final Diagnosis: Acute ischaemic stroke (left MCA) secondary to classic (B6-responsive) homocystinuria.
Plan:
  • Lifelong B6 + betaine + folate + diet; monitor homocysteine q3 months.
  • Aspirin for secondary prevention.
  • Annual ophthalmology for lens.
  • DEXA scan (osteoporosis risk).
  • Genetic counselling; family screening for CBS variants.
  • VTE prophylaxis around any surgery / immobilisation.
Teaching points:
  • Homocystinuria is a classic but under-recognised cause of paediatric/young-adult stroke.
  • Phenotype: marfanoid, ectopia lentis, ID, thromboembolism, osteoporosis.
  • Newborn screening misses ~50% (especially B6-responsive).
  • Test plasma homocysteine in any unexplained child/adolescent stroke.
  • Lifelong therapy & perioperative VTE risk are critical exam points.
Case 114 — Vanishing White Matter Disease — Febrile Crisis HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 5-year-old girl with mild gait ataxia and previously slow but steady development is brought to the ED 24 hours after a minor head bump on the playground. She also developed a febrile URTI 2 days ago (T 39.0°C). Since the head bump and fever, she has become progressively more lethargic, ataxic, with new dysarthria and an episode of focal seizure. Past history: previous "regressions" with prior febrile illnesses, partially recovering.
HR 130 | BP 96/55 | T 38.7°C | SpO₂ 96% | GCS 10 (E3 V2 M5) | Truncal ataxia, dysarthria, hyperreflexia, ankle clonus bilaterally

Stage 2 — Imaging

MRI brain: diffuse, symmetric cerebral white-matter T2/FLAIR hyperintensity with cystic degeneration (signal matches CSF on FLAIR — "vanishing" pattern); cerebellar white-matter spared/less involved; relative sparing of U-fibres; thin corpus callosum. Compared to prior MRI at age 3: dramatic worsening with new cystic change.
What is the diagnostic pattern of vanishing white matter (VWM)?
  • Childhood-onset autosomal recessive leukoencephalopathy, mutations in EIF2B1–5 genes (eukaryotic initiation factor 2B subunits).
  • Triggered/exacerbated by febrile illness, minor head trauma, or fright — episodic worsenings that don't fully recover.
  • MRI: diffuse symmetric WM disease with cystic degeneration; FLAIR-CSF–like signal in affected WM ("rarefaction").
  • MR spectroscopy: low/absent NAA, choline, creatine; lactate may be elevated.
  • Diagnosis confirmed by genetic testing of EIF2B genes.

Stage 3 — ICU Management of Crisis

Admitted to PICU. Aggressive antipyresis (paracetamol + cooling) targeting normothermia. IV fluids, electrolyte correction. AED loading (levetiracetam) for new seizure. Avoid steroids — limited evidence and risk of withdrawal-induced worsening. Empirical broad-spectrum antibiotics + acyclovir until infection workup negative. Avoid avoidable stressors. Family meeting: explain unique vulnerability to stressors.

Stage 4 — Recovery & Prognosis

Over 7 days: gradual recovery to baseline ataxia plus a new mild persistent dysarthria and a new mild right hand weakness — confirming partial recovery from this episode. Discharged home day 14 with comprehensive crisis-prevention plan. Long-term: progressive disease; expected to lose independent ambulation by adolescence; median survival around mid-teens depending on subtype.
Crisis-prevention strategy for VWM?
  • Aggressive antipyresis with any fever (paracetamol, mechanical cooling).
  • Vaccinations including annual influenza and COVID — but plan for fever management post-vaccine.
  • Helmet for outdoor activity; avoid contact sports.
  • Avoid head injury, fright, stressful situations when possible.
  • Low threshold for hospital admission with intercurrent illness.
Final Diagnosis: Vanishing white matter disease (CACH/CLE) — EIF2B5 mutation; episodic worsening triggered by febrile illness and minor head trauma.
Plan:
  • Comprehensive crisis-prevention plan distributed to school, family, GP.
  • Genetic counselling for parents; sibling testing.
  • Annual MRI to track progression.
  • Multidisciplinary care: neurology, rehab, palliative care integration early.
  • Anticipatory guidance about progression.
Teaching points:
  • VWM = unique leukodystrophy with episodic deterioration triggered by stressors.
  • Minor head trauma or fever can precipitate catastrophic decline.
  • "Cystic" appearance on MRI is the key imaging clue.
  • No disease-modifying therapy; antipyresis + avoidance of stressors is the cornerstone.
  • Early palliative care integration is appropriate.
Case 115 — Infantile Alexander Disease with Macrocephaly & Status Epilepticus HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 10-month-old boy presents with new-onset generalised tonic–clonic seizures lasting 25 minutes despite IM midazolam. He has had progressive macrocephaly since 4 months (head circumference now > 99th percentile, OFC crossing percentiles upward), developmental regression (lost ability to sit unsupported), and increasing irritability. Parents are non-consanguineous and healthy.
HR 165 | BP 92/55 | T 37.6°C | SpO₂ 90% RA → 99% intubated | GCS 6T | Macrocephaly (OFC 50 cm at 10 months), spasticity, hyperreflexia, brisk Babinski bilaterally

Stage 2 — Workup

Status terminated with fosphenytoin + levetiracetam. Intubated for airway protection. CT head: large head, no acute haemorrhage, bilateral cerebral white-matter hypodensity prominent in frontal regions. MRI: frontal-predominant T2/FLAIR hyperintensity of cerebral white matter with periventricular rim of T2 hypointensity, contrast enhancement of the periventricular region, basal ganglia and thalami involvement, brainstem involvement. CSF: normal. Genetic testing for GFAP gene (de novo dominant mutations cause infantile Alexander disease).
What MRI features define Alexander disease (Van der Knaap criteria)?
  • Extensive cerebral white-matter abnormality, frontal predominance.
  • Periventricular rim — T2 hypointense, T1 hyperintense.
  • Abnormalities of basal ganglia and thalami (swelling or atrophy with signal change).
  • Brainstem abnormalities, especially medulla and midbrain.
  • Contrast enhancement of various structures (ventricular lining, fornix, optic chiasm, basal ganglia).
  • ≥ 4/5 = definitive diagnosis.

Stage 3 — PICU Course

Recurrent breakthrough seizures controlled with levetiracetam + topiramate. Spasticity managed with baclofen. Feeding difficulties → NG tube → PEG. After 2 weeks, extubated successfully. Worsening hydrocephalus from periventricular involvement requires VP shunt placement on day 18. Family counselled: infantile Alexander has a median survival of 3–5 years from onset; no disease-modifying therapy currently approved (antisense oligonucleotides targeting GFAP in clinical trials).

Stage 4 — Long-term Disposition

Discharged home day 32 on multidrug AED regimen, PEG feeds, baclofen, and integrated palliative care. Genetic testing: confirmed de novo heterozygous GFAP mutation (R79H). Parents counselled regarding very low recurrence risk in subsequent pregnancies.
Final Diagnosis: Infantile Alexander disease — de novo GFAP R79H mutation, presenting with macrocephaly, regression, and status epilepticus.
Plan:
  • Multidisciplinary care: neurology, neurosurgery (shunt), GI (PEG), rehab, palliative care.
  • Antiepileptic management; emergency seizure action plan.
  • Spasticity management; consider ITB pump if oral baclofen ceiling reached.
  • Family bereavement & sibling support.
  • Discussion of clinical trial enrolment if appropriate (ASO trials).
Teaching points:
  • Alexander disease = astrocytopathy from GFAP mutation (Rosenthal fibre accumulation in astrocytes).
  • Infantile form: macrocephaly + regression + seizures + spasticity is the classic triad.
  • MRI Van der Knaap criteria are diagnostic.
  • No disease-modifying therapy yet; supportive care + palliative integration.
  • GFAP-targeted ASOs in clinical trials may change the prognosis.
Case 116 — Pantothenate Kinase-Associated Neurodegeneration (PKAN) with "Eye of the Tiger" Sign MOD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 9-year-old boy with previously normal development presents with 18 months of progressive dystonia (initially right foot, now generalised), dysarthria, mild cognitive decline, and recently severe status dystonicus — sustained, painful, generalised dystonia with rhabdomyolysis (CK 14,000 U/L), hyperthermia (39.5°C), respiratory compromise. Parents are first cousins (consanguineous).
HR 152 | BP 145/95 | T 39.5°C | SpO₂ 91% RA | GCS 13 but cannot answer due to dystonia | Generalised dystonia with opisthotonic posturing, dysphagia, dysarthria

Stage 2 — Imaging

MRI brain T2: bilateral globus pallidus signal — central hyperintensity surrounded by hypointensity ("eye of the tiger" sign). SWI: marked iron deposition (hypointensity) in globi pallidi. No other significant abnormalities. CSF normal. Acanthocytes absent on blood smear. Vitamin E, ceruloplasmin, lactate normal.
What is the "eye of the tiger" sign and what causes it?
  • Bilateral globus pallidus T2: central hyperintensity (gliosis, oedema, neuronal loss) surrounded by hypointensity (iron accumulation).
  • Highly characteristic of classic PKAN (PANK2 mutation) — type of Neurodegeneration with Brain Iron Accumulation (NBIA).
  • Classic PKAN: childhood-onset, rapidly progressive; atypical PKAN: later onset, slower.
  • Other NBIA types (PLAN, MPAN, BPAN) may have iron but not the eye-of-tiger pattern.

Stage 3 — Status Dystonicus Management

Admitted to PICU. Tier 1: discontinue any precipitating drugs (none identified); IV diazepam 0.1 mg/kg q6h; clonidine; trihexyphenidyl up-titrated. Aggressive hydration to protect renal function from rhabdomyolysis (CK trending up to 22,000); urine alkalinisation (bicarbonate infusion targeting urine pH > 6.5). Active cooling. Sedation/intubation when rhabdomyolysis severity and respiratory compromise dictate (Tier 2).

Stage 4 — Refractory Phase

Despite Tier 2, dystonia recurs on weaning sedation. Bilateral globus pallidus internus deep brain stimulation (GPi-DBS) performed under emergency basis given refractory status. Post-DBS: dramatic improvement; able to be extubated 48 h later; dystonia much improved over 2 weeks of stim programming. CK normalises.
Role of DBS in pediatric NBIA?
  • GPi-DBS is the established treatment for medication-refractory generalised dystonia in PKAN and other NBIAs.
  • Best for primary dystonia (mobile dystonia); fixed contractures don't respond.
  • Improvement is typically partial (BFMDRS improvement 30–50% in NBIA, less than in primary dystonia).
  • Particularly indicated emergently in status dystonicus refractory to medication.

Stage 5 — Long-term

Discharged home day 35 on multidrug regimen + active DBS. Slow progressive course expected. Disease-modifying agents in development: fosmetpantotenate (pantothenate substrate replacement); CoA precursors; iron chelators (deferiprone) — modest effect in trials. Enrolled in registry.
Final Diagnosis: Classic PKAN (homozygous PANK2 mutation) presenting with status dystonicus, treated with bilateral GPi-DBS.
Plan:
  • DBS programming and follow-up.
  • Multidisciplinary dystonia clinic.
  • Genetic counselling: 25% recurrence in consanguineous family.
  • Consider deferiprone trial enrolment.
  • Anticipatory guidance about progression; integrate palliative care.
Teaching points:
  • "Eye of the tiger" sign on T2 MRI = classic PKAN; investigate PANK2 mutation.
  • Status dystonicus is a paediatric neurocritical emergency — rhabdomyolysis, AKI, hyperthermia, respiratory failure.
  • Tiered management: meds → sedation/ICU → DBS for refractory cases.
  • NBIA is a spectrum; PKAN is the classical subtype.
  • Iron chelation (deferiprone) under evaluation.
Case 117 — Friedreich Ataxia with Cardiomyopathy in PICU; on Omaveloxolone MOD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 15-year-old girl with known Friedreich ataxia (homozygous GAA repeat expansion, ~ 850/870 repeats; diagnosed age 9) on omaveloxolone for the past 12 months presents with new-onset palpitations, presyncope, and exertional dyspnoea. Past: progressive ataxia, areflexia, dysarthria, scoliosis (corrected at 13). Echocardiogram 1 year ago: mild concentric LVH, EF 58%.
HR 165 (irregular) | BP 88/52 (MAP 64) | SpO₂ 95% RA → 99% on O₂ | T 37.2°C | GCS 14 | Cool peripheries; new S3 gallop; bilateral fine crepitations at bases

Stage 2 — ED Workup

ECG: atrial fibrillation with rapid ventricular response, ~160 bpm, no acute ischaemic changes. CXR: mild pulmonary venous congestion. Troponin: mildly elevated. BNP: markedly elevated. Echo at bedside: LVEF dropped to 32%, severe concentric LVH, dilated LA, no thrombus, no LVOTO.
What cardiac involvement is characteristic of Friedreich ataxia?
  • Hypertrophic cardiomyopathy (often concentric, sometimes asymmetric) is the most common form.
  • Late progression to dilated cardiomyopathy with systolic dysfunction in some patients.
  • Atrial arrhythmias (AF, flutter), supraventricular tachycardia common.
  • Cardiac involvement is the leading cause of death.
  • GAA repeat length correlates with severity and earlier cardiac involvement.

Stage 3 — PICU Management

Rate control with IV amiodarone (avoid β-blockers in significant LV dysfunction without cautious titration; avoid CCBs in HF with reduced EF). Anticoagulation with heparin transitioned to apixaban for CHA₂DS₂-VASc and confirmed AF. Diuresis with IV furosemide. Cardioversion (synchronised, 1 J/kg) attempted after 48 h amiodarone with no thrombus on TEE — successful conversion to NSR. Heart-failure-directed therapy started: low-dose ACEi (enalapril), eplerenone, and gentle β-blocker (carvedilol) up-titration over weeks.

Stage 4 — Disease-Modifying Therapy Review

Continued omaveloxolone 150 mg daily (oral) per FDA-approved indication for FA in patients ≥ 16 (extended off-label in 15-year-old). Cardiology reviewed: omaveloxolone's effect on cardiac involvement is uncertain — primary trial endpoint was mFARS (neurological function), with insufficient data on cardiac progression. Continued under cardiology + neurology co-management.
What does omaveloxolone do?
  • Nrf2 activator → increased expression of antioxidant response element (ARE) genes.
  • FA pathophysiology = frataxin deficiency → mitochondrial iron-sulfur cluster dysfunction → oxidative stress.
  • MOXIe trial: omaveloxolone improved mFARS by ~1.6 points at 48 weeks vs placebo.
  • FDA-approved Feb 2023 for FA patients ≥ 16 years.
  • Cardiac & long-term effects still under investigation.

Stage 5 — Disposition

Discharged home day 12 on stable NSR; HF medications; apixaban; omaveloxolone continued. Long-term: at risk of recurrent AF, progressive cardiomyopathy, and eventual need for ICD/transplant discussions.
Final Diagnosis: Friedreich ataxia with progressive hypertrophic → dilated cardiomyopathy complicated by new atrial fibrillation with rapid ventricular response and acute heart failure.
Plan:
  • Multidisciplinary: neurology, cardiology, electrophysiology.
  • Continue omaveloxolone; surveillance for cardiac response.
  • Repeat echo q6 months; cardiac MRI annually.
  • Discussion of ICD when EF persistently < 30% or sustained VT.
  • Genetic counselling; family screening.
  • Future planning: heart transplant candidacy considerations.
Teaching points:
  • Cardiomyopathy is the leading cause of death in Friedreich ataxia.
  • Watch for HCM → DCM transition with reduced EF.
  • AF is common — anticoagulate; cardiovert when stable.
  • Omaveloxolone (Nrf2 activator) is the first FDA-approved FA therapy (2023) — neurological benefit demonstrated.
  • Combined neuro-cardiac care is essential.
Case 118 — Ataxia-Telangiectasia with Severe Pneumonia HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 7-year-old girl with known ataxia-telangiectasia (homozygous ATM mutation) presents with 5 days of fever and productive cough, now with hypoxia and respiratory distress. Background: progressive cerebellar ataxia from age 2, ocular telangiectasias, history of recurrent sinopulmonary infections. Most recent labs: IgA undetectable, IgG 4.2 g/L (low), low CD4, elevated AFP. On chronic IVIG q4 weekly. Family declined CT imaging "ever" given the well-known radiosensitivity concern.
HR 140 | BP 92/55 | RR 45 | SpO₂ 88% on 6 L NC | T 39.2°C | GCS 13 | Marked accessory muscle use; bilateral crepitations

Stage 2 — Imaging & Initial Care

CXR (limited radiation, mandatory): bilateral lower-lobe consolidation, no effusion. CT thorax deferred — replaced with MRI thorax to avoid ionising radiation in this radiosensitive patient. MRI: bilateral consolidation + early bronchiectasis (chronic). Empirical antibiotics: piperacillin-tazobactam + azithromycin (cover atypicals and chronic colonisation organisms); RSV and influenza PCRs sent. Aerosol drug delivery; chest physio; HFNC escalated to 30 L/min FiO₂ 0.6.
Why is radiation avoidance so important in A-T?
  • ATM gene = master regulator of DNA double-strand break response.
  • Cells from A-T patients have markedly defective DNA repair after ionising radiation.
  • Each CT exposure increases risk of radiation-induced malignancy.
  • Use MRI, US, low-dose CXR or no imaging whenever possible.
  • Therapeutic radiation in A-T cancer patients must be dose-reduced.

Stage 3 — PICU Course

Progressed to acute respiratory failure → intubated on day 2 of admission. Ventilation: protective lung-strategy (Vt 6 mL/kg, PEEP 8, plateau < 30). HSV-1 PCR negative; Streptococcus pneumoniae isolated from BAL with sensitivities to piperacillin-tazobactam. Stress-dose IVIG 0.5 g/kg given on top of routine dosing during severe infection. Nutrition optimised. Avoided unnecessary procedures (radiation exposure, immunosuppressants).

Stage 4 — Recovery

Extubated day 7. Discharged home day 14 on broadened antibiotic course, prophylactic azithromycin to be considered, intensified chest physio. Bronchoscopy planned outpatient.
Long-term considerations in A-T?
  • Recurrent sinopulmonary infection: prophylactic antibiotics, IVIG.
  • Cancer surveillance: lymphoma and leukaemia much more common — clinical surveillance, MRI for screening if needed.
  • Progressive ataxia and dystonia → rehab, mobility aids, eventual wheelchair.
  • Newer therapies (antisense oligonucleotide ATM exon 4 strategies, betamethasone trials) — limited.
  • Bone-marrow involvement and Hodgkin/non-Hodgkin lymphoma require radiation-free protocols.
Final Diagnosis: Severe community-acquired pneumococcal pneumonia with respiratory failure in a child with ataxia-telangiectasia; treated with radiation-free imaging strategy, antibiotics, mechanical ventilation, and IVIG augmentation.
Plan:
  • Intensify IVIG and consider prophylactic azithromycin.
  • Annual MRI surveillance for malignancy in A-T (per local protocol).
  • Multidisciplinary: immunology, neurology, oncology, rehab.
  • Cancer-treatment plan should radiation/chemotherapy be needed: radiation-free protocols, dose-reduced cytotoxics.
  • Family education: hospital alerts, MedicAlert.
Teaching points:
  • A-T = autosomal recessive, multi-system (neuro, immune, cancer, radiation sensitivity).
  • Avoid CT and other ionising radiation whenever possible.
  • Combined immunodeficiency → bacterial & viral infections, IVIG often indicated.
  • Cancer surveillance is lifelong.
  • If cancer occurs, radiation-free protocols and reduced cytotoxics are mandatory.
Case 119 — Non-Ketotic Hyperglycinemia (Glycine Encephalopathy) in a Neonate HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A term male infant, born to consanguineous parents, develops progressive lethargy, hypotonia, hiccups, and intermittent apnoea on day 2 of life. By day 3: refractory myoclonic seizures and intractable hiccups. Apgar at birth was 8/9; no perinatal event. Sibling died at age 6 weeks of "unexplained encephalopathy."
HR 145 | BP 65/40 | RR 25 (with frequent apnoeas) | SpO₂ 86% RA → 100% intubated | T 36.5°C | GCS 5T | Hypotonic, frequent myoclonus, intractable hiccups

Stage 2 — Initial Workup

EEG: burst-suppression pattern. Sepsis workup negative. Glucose, calcium, magnesium, electrolytes normal. Ammonia mildly elevated (90 µmol/L). Lactate normal. CSF glycine markedly elevated; CSF:plasma glycine ratio > 0.08 (very elevated — normal < 0.02). Urine organic acids: no ketones (helps rule out propionic, methylmalonic, isovaleric acidaemias).
What is the differential for neonatal burst-suppression + hiccups + apnoea?
  • Non-ketotic hyperglycinemia (glycine encephalopathy) — defect in glycine cleavage system.
  • Ohtahara syndrome / early infantile epileptic encephalopathy.
  • Pyridoxine-dependent epilepsy (PDE) / pyridoxal-5-phosphate dependency.
  • Sulfite oxidase / molybdenum cofactor deficiency.
  • Severe HIE.
  • Mitochondrial disorders.
  • Hyperammonaemia / urea cycle defects.

Stage 3 — Management

Intubated for apnoea, ventilated. Empirical pyridoxine (B6) 100 mg IV given — no response (rules out PDE). Sodium benzoate 250–750 mg/kg/d started (conjugates glycine via hippurate excretion). Dextromethorphan or ketamine (NMDA antagonist) added — competes with glycine at the NMDA receptor co-agonist site. Despite therapy, severe encephalopathy and burst-suppression persist.
Why use NMDA antagonists in NKH?
  • Glycine is the obligatory co-agonist at NMDA receptors.
  • High CNS glycine → NMDA over-activation → excitotoxicity, seizures.
  • Dextromethorphan, ketamine, felbamate block NMDA — symptomatic benefit.
  • Sodium benzoate lowers plasma glycine; less effect on CNS glycine.
  • Overall, severe (classic neonatal) NKH has very poor prognosis despite therapy.

Stage 4 — Genetic Confirmation & Family Discussion

Genetic testing confirms biallelic GLDC mutation (P-protein deficiency of the glycine cleavage system) — classic severe form. Family meeting: prior sibling almost certainly had same disorder. Severe (neonatal-onset) NKH has > 95% mortality in infancy/early childhood or severe profound disability among survivors. Family elects redirection to comfort care.

Stage 5 — Outcome & Genetic Counselling

Compassionate extubation on day 18. Comfort care; infant dies at home with hospice support on day 24. Genetic counselling: 25% recurrence in consanguineous family. Preimplantation genetic diagnosis or CVS at 11–13 weeks offered for future pregnancies.
Final Diagnosis: Classic neonatal-onset non-ketotic hyperglycinemia (NKH/glycine encephalopathy), biallelic GLDC mutation.
Plan:
  • Family bereavement support.
  • Genetic counselling and reproductive planning.
  • Public health: education about inborn errors in consanguineous communities.
  • Earlier sibling: posthumous diagnosis with confirmation, helpful for closure.
Teaching points:
  • NKH = defect in mitochondrial glycine cleavage system (GLDC most common).
  • Presentation: neonatal encephalopathy, hiccups, apnoea, myoclonus, burst-suppression EEG, NO acidosis or ketosis.
  • Diagnosis: high CSF glycine + elevated CSF:plasma glycine ratio.
  • Therapy: sodium benzoate + NMDA blockade — palliative, not curative.
  • Classic (neonatal) form: very poor prognosis; attenuated forms milder.
  • Recurrence risk 25% in autosomal recessive families.
Case 120 — PANDAS / PANS with Severe Acute-Onset OCD and Restrictive Eating MOD

Stage 1 — Presentation

An 8-year-old girl, previously developmentally typical, develops sudden-onset severe OCD over 48 hours: handwashing every 5 minutes, refusing to eat ("food is contaminated"), accompanied by emotional lability, separation anxiety, tics (head jerking), urinary frequency. Two weeks earlier she had a documented streptococcal pharyngitis treated with amoxicillin. Within 10 days she has lost 4 kg, is severely dehydrated, and presents to ED in acute medical crisis.
HR 132 | BP 86/52 | T 37.0°C | SpO₂ 99% RA | GCS 14 (uncooperative) | Marked dehydration, ketotic breath, scleral icterus absent; new motor tics

Stage 2 — Initial Workup

Bloods: Na 148 (hypernatraemic dehydration), Cr 95 (AKI), urea elevated, glucose 4.5, ketones 4+, lactate normal, normal LFTs, normal TSH, B12/folate normal, ESR 35, ASOT elevated, anti-DNAse B elevated, throat swab negative now. MRI brain: normal. EEG: normal background. Anti-basal ganglia antibodies (research test) sent.
How is PANDAS/PANS defined?
  • PANDAS: Paediatric Autoimmune Neuropsychiatric Disorder Associated with Streptococcus — abrupt OCD or tics, age 3 to puberty, temporal association with GAS, neuro abnormalities (tics, hyperactivity), episodic course.
  • PANS: Paediatric Acute-onset Neuropsychiatric Syndrome — broader, includes any acute-onset OCD or severely restricted food intake, plus ≥ 2 of: anxiety, lability/depression, behavioural regression, deteriorating school performance, sensory/motor abnormalities, somatic symptoms.
  • Both controversial diagnoses — best to think of as a syndrome triggered by infection/immune activation.

Stage 3 — Acute Management

PICU admission for severe dehydration and refeeding risk. IV resuscitation. NG feeds initiated cautiously (refeeding precautions: low-rate, electrolyte monitoring, thiamine). Penicillin V started to eradicate any residual GAS. Sertraline initiated (low dose, slow titration). CBT for OCD planned outpatient. Multidisciplinary involvement: psychiatry, paediatrics, dietitian, OT.

Stage 4 — Immunomodulation Considerations

Given severe presentation, IVIG 2 g/kg over 2 days trialled (PANS Research Consortium criteria for moderate-severe PANS recommend IVIG in selected cases). Course of NSAID (ibuprofen) trialled for mild anti-inflammatory effect. Plasmapheresis reserved for severe/refractory cases. Response monitored using CY-BOCS scale.
What is the evidence for IVIG in PANDAS/PANS?
  • Mixed evidence; not all studies positive.
  • 2017 PANS Research Consortium guidelines recommend IVIG (1.5–2 g/kg) in moderate-severe PANS not responding to standard psychiatric/behavioural therapy.
  • Plasmapheresis for severe cases.
  • Antibiotic prophylaxis (penicillin or azithromycin) considered for recurrent strep-triggered relapses.
  • Newer randomised trial data still developing.

Stage 5 — Outcome

By day 7: significant reduction in obsessive behaviours; eating improved (refeeding completed without metabolic complication); rehydrated. Discharged home day 10 on sertraline, penicillin prophylaxis, and outpatient CBT. At 3 months: CY-BOCS reduced by 50%; tics resolved; back at school full-time. Family counselled about possible exacerbations with future infections.
Final Diagnosis: PANDAS — acute-onset OCD with restrictive eating and tics, temporally related to streptococcal infection; complicated by severe dehydration and AKI; responded to IVIG and SSRI.
Plan:
  • Sertraline + CBT for OCD.
  • Penicillin prophylaxis for 6–12 months given severity.
  • Throat swab any new pharyngitis.
  • Dietitian follow-up.
  • Counsel family about possible flares; have clear plan for IVIG if needed.
Teaching points:
  • Acute-onset OCD or severely restricted food intake in a child should prompt consideration of PANS/PANDAS.
  • Severe restrictive eating can cause life-threatening dehydration and electrolyte disturbance — manage as medical emergency.
  • Refeeding syndrome is the early-treatment risk.
  • IVIG and plasmapheresis are options for moderate-severe disease per consortium guidelines.
  • Multidisciplinary (paeds, psych, dietitian, ID) care is essential.
Case 121 — Paediatric Cardioembolic Stroke from Infective Endocarditis HARD

Stage 1 — Presentation

A 13-year-old with surgically corrected tetralogy of Fallot (TOF repair age 2 with transannular patch; residual mild pulmonary regurgitation) presents with sudden-onset right hemiparesis and global aphasia after 2 weeks of low-grade fever, malaise, and weight loss attributed to a "viral illness." NIHSS 14. Last known well: 75 minutes ago.
HR 105 | BP 130/72 | T 38.3°C | SpO₂ 97% RA | GCS 12 | Right hemiplegia, global aphasia, gaze deviation to the left | New holosystolic murmur LSB

Stage 2 — Acute Imaging

CT head: no haemorrhage; hyperdense left MCA sign. CTA: left M1 occlusion. CT perfusion: large penumbra. Echocardiogram (ED, point-of-care): vegetation on right ventricular outflow tract patch (~ 1.2 cm), no LV dysfunction. Blood cultures × 3 drawn.
How does the embolic source affect acute stroke decisions?
  • Septic embolus from endocarditis ↑ haemorrhagic transformation risk after IV tPA — relative contraindication.
  • Mechanical thrombectomy preferred for LVO when endocarditis suspected.
  • Mycotic aneurysm risk is high — CTA/MRA to look for them before instrumenting.
  • Treat infection promptly; CNS embolic load may be high.

Stage 3 — Treatment

IV tPA withheld due to suspected septic source. Immediate mechanical thrombectomy in interventional neuroradiology: TICI 2b reperfusion at 3.5 h from onset. Empirical IV vancomycin + cefepime started after blood cultures drawn. Admitted to PICU.

Stage 4 — PICU Course

Blood cultures grow viridans group Streptococcus sensitive to penicillin. Antibiotics narrowed to ceftriaxone (4 weeks for native-valve patch endocarditis with embolic complication). Repeat MRI 48 h: small reperfused MCA territory infarct; no haemorrhagic transformation. MRA of intracranial vessels: no mycotic aneurysm. Cardiology MDT discussion: vegetation persistent, recurrent embolic potential, weighing surgical excision vs continued antibiotics. Patient remains haemodynamically stable; no progressive HF; vegetation reduces over 2 weeks on TEE.

Stage 5 — Disposition

Antibiotics completed at 4 weeks; aphasia and right hemiparesis improving with rehab. Cardiology follow-up: continued surveillance imaging; future need for pulmonary valve replacement anticipated and now informed by endocarditis history. Aspirin 3 mg/kg/d started for secondary stroke prophylaxis after antibiotic course.
Final Diagnosis: Acute ischaemic stroke (left MCA) from infective endocarditis on TOF repair patch with septic embolus; treated with mechanical thrombectomy (without tPA) and prolonged antibiotics.
Plan:
  • Aspirin secondary prophylaxis.
  • Stroke rehab (PT, OT, speech).
  • Cardiology surveillance; future pulmonary valve replacement strategy.
  • Dental prophylaxis education; lifelong endocarditis prevention.
  • Genetic counselling reaffirmation for CHD family.
Teaching points:
  • Congenital heart disease (esp. residual right-sided lesions) is a major risk factor for paediatric cardioembolic stroke.
  • Infective endocarditis with septic embolus is a relative contraindication to IV tPA — prefer mechanical thrombectomy.
  • Mycotic aneurysms must be ruled out before/around endovascular work.
  • Treat the source (4–6 wk antibiotics) and consider valve/patch surgery if indicated.
  • Long-term: ASA prophylaxis, endocarditis education, cardiac surveillance.
Case 122 — Adolescent with sudden 'worst headache' and brief LOC HARD
A 15-year-old girl collapses during exercise with a thunderclap headache, brief loss of consciousness, vomiting × 3, and photophobia. On arrival: GCS 13, mild neck stiffness, no focal deficit, BP 168/96, HR 58. No trauma. No prior headache history.

DX

What is the most likely diagnosis, and what is the single best next investigation?
Show model answer
  • Likely diagnosis: aneurysmal subarachnoid haemorrhage (SAH).
  • Best next investigation: non-contrast CT head — sensitivity ≈ 98% within first 6 h of symptom onset.
  • If CT negative but suspicion remains and > 6 h since onset: LP for xanthochromia (requires > 12 h from ictus for accurate interpretation).
  • Vascular imaging (CTA / DSA) to identify the aneurysm once SAH confirmed.

PHYSIOLOGY

Explain the Cushing reflex finding on her vital signs.
Show model answer
  • BP 168/96 with HR 58 in the setting of acutely raised ICP = Cushing response.
  • Mechanism: brainstem ischaemia from raised ICP triggers sympathetic surge (hypertension) and reflex vagal bradycardia via baroreceptor activation.
  • Often accompanied by irregular respirations (third element of Cushing's triad).
  • It is a late sign — herniation may be imminent.

NEXT STEP

Pre-aneurysm-securing BP target and rationale.
Show model answer
  • Target SBP < 140–160 mmHg (avoid both extremes) until aneurysm secured to reduce rebleed risk.
  • Use titratable IV agents (nicardipine or labetalol).
  • Avoid sudden drops that risk cerebral hypoperfusion.
  • After securing: maintain euvolaemia + adequate CPP to prevent vasospasm-related DCI.
Case 123 — Toddler with regression, hand-wringing, and lost speech MOD
A 22-month-old girl had normal development to 12 months, then progressive loss of acquired words, deceleration of head growth, stereotyped midline hand-wringing, episodes of breath-holding and hyperventilation, episodic apnoeas, and now bruxism. Cognitive engagement is reduced. No metabolic abnormalities; MRI brain normal.

DX

What is the most likely diagnosis and the underlying genetic basis?
Show model answer
  • Rett syndrome.
  • Caused by mutations in MECP2 (X-linked dominant); de novo in > 99% of cases.
  • Almost exclusively in girls — most affected males die in utero or in infancy (severe encephalopathy with respiratory failure).
  • Classic four stages: early-onset stagnation → rapid destructive → pseudo-stationary → late motor deterioration.

CRITERIA

Name the main diagnostic criteria and required exclusions.
Show model answer
  • Required: regression followed by recovery or stabilisation.
  • All four main criteria for typical Rett: loss of acquired purposeful hand skills, loss of acquired spoken language, gait abnormalities, stereotypic hand movements.
  • Exclusion criteria include: brain injury secondary to trauma, neurometabolic disease, severe infection causing the picture, or grossly abnormal psychomotor development in first 6 months.
  • Genetic confirmation supports but is not required.

TREATMENT

What disease-modifying therapy is now available and what does it do?
Show model answer
  • Trofinetide (FDA-approved 2023) — synthetic analogue of an N-terminal tripeptide of IGF-1.
  • Shown to improve Rett Syndrome Behaviour Questionnaire and Clinical Global Impression scores in the LAVENDER trial.
  • Side effects: diarrhoea, vomiting.
  • Supportive care still essential: AEDs for seizures, GI management, scoliosis surveillance, breathing irregularities, communication aids.
Case 124 — Schoolchild with stuttering hemiplegia after varicella HARD
A 6-year-old boy had a confirmed varicella infection 4 months ago. He now presents with 2 days of episodic right-sided weakness, each lasting 20–40 minutes with full recovery, then a persistent right hemiparesis with mild expressive aphasia. CT plain: no haemorrhage. MRI DWI: small left lenticulostriate infarcts. MRA: irregular focal narrowing of the left distal ICA / proximal M1.

DX

What is the most likely arteriopathy?
Show model answer
  • Post-varicella focal cerebral arteriopathy (FCA-i, inflammatory subtype).
  • Unilateral, non-progressive (by definition stabilises within 6 months) arteriopathy of distal ICA/proximal MCA/ACA.
  • Often follows varicella by weeks to months.
  • Distinct from progressive moyamoya (bilateral, smoke-puff collaterals) and dissection (flame-shaped tapering with intramural haematoma).

TREATMENT

What are the components of acute and secondary-prevention management?
Show model answer
  • Acute: aspirin 3–5 mg/kg/day after haemorrhage excluded.
  • Adjunct corticosteroids (e.g., prednisolone 2 mg/kg/day taper) in inflammatory FCA per VIPS observational data — biologically rational; pending RCT confirmation.
  • Acyclovir if active VZV reactivation suspected (vesicular eruption, VZV PCR / antibody).
  • Long-term aspirin for 2 years; serial MRA at 3, 6, 12 months.

DEFINITION

Define 'focal cerebral arteriopathy of childhood' per international consensus.
Show model answer
  • A unilateral arteriopathy of intracranial ICA/proximal MCA/ACA confirmed on neurovascular imaging in a previously healthy child with arterial ischaemic stroke.
  • Must be monophasic / non-progressive — stabilises or improves within 6 months.
  • Excludes moyamoya (progressive), dissection, vasculitis, cardiogenic causes.
  • Subtypes: FCA-i (inflammatory, often post-VZV) and FCA-d (dissection).
Case 125 — Infant with seizures and 'inverted-V' lactate-glucose pattern HARD
A 4-month-old infant presents with myoclonic seizures since the neonatal period, severe developmental delay, microcephaly developing, and acquired epileptic encephalopathy on EEG. Plasma glucose 4.2 mmol/L; CSF glucose 1.4 mmol/L; CSF lactate normal/low; CSF-to-plasma glucose ratio 0.33.

DX

What is the diagnosis and the genetic basis?
Show model answer
  • Glucose transporter type 1 deficiency syndrome (GLUT1 DS) — De Vivo disease.
  • Caused by heterozygous (mostly de novo) loss-of-function mutations in SLC2A1.
  • Diagnostic hallmark: low CSF glucose (hypoglycorrhachia) with normal blood glucose and normal/low CSF lactate.
  • CSF:plasma glucose ratio < 0.45 (often ≤ 0.4) is highly suggestive.

TREATMENT

Name the first-line therapy and its physiologic rationale.
Show model answer
  • Ketogenic diet (classic or modified Atkins).
  • Rationale: ketone bodies (β-hydroxybutyrate, acetoacetate) bypass GLUT1 — they cross the blood-brain barrier via monocarboxylate transporter 1 (MCT1) to fuel the brain.
  • Often produces dramatic seizure control and improved movement disorder symptoms.
  • Triheptanoin (UX007) shown to reduce paroxysmal movement disorders in some patients.
  • AVOID phenobarbital, valproate, and other agents that further depress GLUT1.
Case 126 — Adolescent on long-stay sedation with refractory hypoactive delirium MOD
A 14-year-old has been mechanically ventilated for 12 days for severe pneumonia. Sedation: midazolam infusion 0.3 mg/kg/h + fentanyl. Off paralytics. He is hypoactive, intermittently agitated, not tracking, refusing eye contact, sleep-wake disturbance, and CAPD score 12. No fever now; CT head normal.

DX

What is the diagnosis and which scale is used to identify it?
Show model answer
  • Paediatric ICU delirium — mixed or hypoactive subtype.
  • Cornell Assessment of Pediatric Delirium (CAPD) is a validated 8-item observational tool; score ≥ 9 suggests delirium.
  • Includes items on eye contact, awareness, purposefulness, communication, agitation, inconsolability, hypoactivity, and response to interaction.
  • Hypoactive delirium is the most common subtype in PICU and the most missed.

TREATMENT

What is the bundled approach to management?
Show model answer
  • Non-pharmacologic first: family presence, normal day/night cues, minimise sedation interruption, early mobility, restoration of sleep.
  • Reduce deliriogenic drugs — especially benzodiazepines and anticholinergics.
  • Switch sedation toward dexmedetomidine or opioid-sparing strategy if appropriate.
  • Pharmacologic adjuncts only for severe distress or self-harm risk: low-dose atypical antipsychotic (quetiapine or risperidone).
  • Treat pain, withdrawal, sleep deprivation, restraints, constipation.

PHYSIOLOGY

Why are benzodiazepines particularly deliriogenic?
Show model answer
  • Potent positive allosteric modulators of GABA-A — broad CNS depression including cortical and limbic regions.
  • Disrupt sleep architecture (suppress REM, reduce slow-wave sleep).
  • Cause anterograde amnesia — patients can't form ICU memories, increasing disorientation.
  • Particularly disruptive to circadian rhythm.
  • Causally linked to delirium in PICU studies (e.g., Smith et al.); reduction is a key bundle element.
Case 127 — Boy with progressive cognitive decline, adrenal crisis HARD
A 7-year-old boy is brought to the ED in shock with hyponatraemia (Na 121), hyperkalaemia (K 6.2), hypoglycaemia (glucose 2.5 mmol/L), and hyperpigmentation of buccal mucosa and palmar creases. The family also reports 6 months of declining school performance, behavioural changes, and recent visual difficulties. MRI brain: symmetric posterior parieto-occipital white matter T2/FLAIR hyperintensity with peripheral contrast enhancement.

DX

What is the unifying diagnosis?
Show model answer
  • X-linked adrenoleukodystrophy (X-ALD), childhood cerebral form, with simultaneous adrenal insufficiency (Addisonian crisis).
  • Caused by mutations in ABCD1 (Xq28) → impaired peroxisomal β-oxidation of very-long-chain fatty acids (VLCFA).
  • Classic MRI pattern: posterior cerebral white-matter demyelination starting in splenium of corpus callosum, with peripheral garland-like contrast enhancement at the active edge.

NEXT STEP

Immediate management priorities for this presentation?
Show model answer
  • Treat the Addisonian crisis FIRST: IV hydrocortisone 50–100 mg/m² bolus then maintenance + isotonic saline + glucose correction.
  • Lifelong glucocorticoid (and mineralocorticoid if aldosterone-deficient) replacement after stabilisation.
  • Confirm X-ALD with elevated plasma VLCFA (C26:0, C24:0/C22:0 and C26:0/C22:0 ratios) and ABCD1 sequencing.
  • Calculate the Loes MRI score for severity.

TREATMENT

What is the principal disease-modifying option and its eligibility criteria?
Show model answer
  • Allogeneic haematopoietic stem cell transplant (HSCT) — the only therapy proven to halt cerebral disease progression.
  • Must be performed EARLY, while neurologically asymptomatic or minimally affected (Loes score ≤ 9, no major neurological deficits).
  • Once advanced, HSCT does not reverse and may not halt disease.
  • Gene therapy (elivaldogene autotemcel, FDA-approved 2022) is an alternative for early cerebral ALD.
  • Lorenzo's oil does NOT prevent cerebral disease in symptomatic patients.
Case 128 — Adolescent with refractory headache and persistent vomiting MOD
A 13-year-old presents with 8 weeks of progressive morning headaches that wake her from sleep, vomiting on rising, and recently diplopia from CN VI palsies bilaterally. Fundoscopy: bilateral papilloedema with venous engorgement and peripapillary haemorrhages. CT head: 4 cm enhancing posterior fossa midline mass arising from the 4th ventricle with marked supratentorial hydrocephalus.

DX

What is the most likely diagnosis given location and demographics?
Show model answer
  • Most likely medulloblastoma — embryonal posterior fossa tumour, peak age 4–9 years, but seen into adolescence.
  • Arises from cerebellar vermis floor of 4th ventricle in classic location.
  • Other differentials: ependymoma (often projects through foramina, with rosettes histologically), pilocytic astrocytoma (more lateral cerebellar hemisphere, cyst with mural nodule), atypical teratoid/rhabdoid tumour (very young children).
  • Molecular subgroups: WNT (best), SHH, Group 3 (worst), Group 4.

NEXT STEP

What are the immediate management priorities?
Show model answer
  • Treat hydrocephalus: emergency external ventricular drain (EVD) or endoscopic third ventriculostomy (ETV).
  • Avoid lumbar puncture (herniation risk with obstructive hydrocephalus).
  • Dexamethasone to reduce peri-tumoural oedema.
  • Stage with full spinal MRI before surgery if possible (CSF dissemination present in ~30% of medulloblastoma).
  • Definitive treatment: gross-total resection + craniospinal radiation (in children > 3 years) + chemotherapy.
Case 129 — Teenager with rapidly progressive ascending weakness post-Campylobacter HARD
A 16-year-old, 10 days after a self-limited bloody diarrhoeal illness, develops bilateral leg weakness ascending over 4 days. Reflexes are absent. She now has bilateral facial weakness and decreasing forced vital capacity from 60 → 32 mL/kg over 12 hours; NIF –22. Mildly elevated BP, HR fluctuations, urinary retention.

DX

Diagnosis and the most likely antibody target.
Show model answer
  • Guillain–Barré syndrome (GBS), most likely the acute motor axonal neuropathy (AMAN) variant given the Campylobacter jejuni trigger.
  • Antibodies target GM1 and GD1a gangliosides — molecular mimicry from C. jejuni LPS.
  • AMAN: pure motor, axonal NCS, more common after Campylobacter, often more severe.
  • Other variants: AIDP (demyelinating, most common in Europe/N America), Miller Fisher (ophthalmoplegia + ataxia + areflexia, anti-GQ1b), Bickerstaff (encephalopathy added).

CRITERIA

What are the indications for ICU admission and intubation in GBS?
Show model answer
  • FVC < 20 mL/kg, NIF less negative than –30 cmH₂O, or 30% reduction from baseline.
  • Bulbar dysfunction with aspiration risk.
  • Severe autonomic instability.
  • Inability to lift head off bed (proxy for diaphragm/neck weakness).
  • Rapid progression (peak weakness within 7 days).
  • Use the EGRIS score (Erasmus GBS Respiratory Insufficiency Score) for risk stratification.

TREATMENT

Which immunotherapy options are evidence-based and when is each preferred?
Show model answer
  • IVIG 2 g/kg total over 2–5 days OR plasma exchange (5 sessions over ~10 days) — equivalent efficacy.
  • Combining IVIG + PLEX has NO additional benefit and is not standard.
  • Steroids alone are NOT effective in GBS (contrast with CIDP).
  • IVIG generally preferred for ease/availability in paediatrics.
  • Supportive care: airway/ventilatory support, autonomic management, VTE prophylaxis, pain control, physiotherapy.
Case 130 — Toddler with macrocephaly and progressive seizures after febrile illness HARD
A 2-year-old previously well boy develops febrile illness with influenza A. Within 24 hours: rapid neurologic deterioration, multifocal seizures, decreased LOC to GCS 7. MRI: symmetric bilateral thalamic and brainstem T2 hyperintensity with petechial haemorrhage and necrosis. Cerebellum involved. Serum ferritin 8200, AST 1500, no shock, ammonia normal.

DX

What is the diagnosis and the key MRI feature?
Show model answer
  • Acute necrotising encephalopathy of childhood (ANE).
  • Key MRI: symmetric, multifocal lesions in BILATERAL THALAMI (always), with brainstem tegmentum, putamen, cerebral and cerebellar white matter, often haemorrhagic/necrotic.
  • Strongly associated with influenza A/B, HHV-6, mycoplasma, and recently SARS-CoV-2.
  • Familial / recurrent forms linked to RANBP2 mutations.
  • Cytokine storm (very high IL-6, TNF, IL-10) is the proposed pathophysiology.

TREATMENT

Outline the multimodal acute management.
Show model answer
  • Aggressive ICU support including ICP management.
  • High-dose IV methylprednisolone (early, ideally within 24 h).
  • IVIG 2 g/kg.
  • Plasma exchange or therapeutic plasma filtration for severe cases.
  • Tocilizumab (anti-IL-6) increasingly used in severe / refractory disease.
  • Treat underlying viral infection (oseltamivir for influenza).
  • Avoid hyperthermia; aggressive antipyresis.
  • Prognosis poor — mortality ~30%, severe disability common in survivors.
Case 131 — Infant with bulging fontanelle, persistent vomiting, sun-setting eyes MOD
An 11-month-old infant has macrocephaly (head circumference > 99th centile, crossing percentiles upward over 3 months), bulging anterior fontanelle, sun-setting eyes (impaired upgaze with lid retraction), irritability, and vomiting. Fundoscopy normal (open sutures decompress papilloedema). CT: triventricular hydrocephalus with patent 4th ventricle.

DX

Where is the obstruction, and what is the most likely cause?
Show model answer
  • Obstruction at the cerebral aqueduct (aqueduct of Sylvius) — triventricular dilatation with NORMAL 4th ventricle is the diagnostic clue.
  • Most likely cause in this age group: congenital aqueductal stenosis (X-linked L1CAM mutations in some, with corpus callosum agenesis and adducted thumbs in male infants); or post-haemorrhagic from IVH of prematurity; or tectal plate glioma.
  • Other obstructive causes: posterior fossa tumour, vein of Galen malformation, intraventricular cysts.

TREATMENT

What surgical options exist and how do you choose?
Show model answer
  • Two principal options: ventriculoperitoneal (VP) shunt OR endoscopic third ventriculostomy (ETV).
  • ETV preferred for non-communicating hydrocephalus (aqueductal stenosis) in children > 1 year — avoids shunt dependence; ETV success score predicts likelihood.
  • ETV success score uses: age, aetiology, prior shunt status. Higher scores favour ETV.
  • ETV + choroid plexus cauterisation (ETV/CPC) in infants < 1 year (Warf protocol) improves success.
  • VP shunt always an option, but lifetime infection (5–15%) and malfunction risk are substantial.

PHYSIOLOGY

Explain 'sun-setting eyes' (Parinaud-like sign).
Show model answer
  • Caused by pressure on the dorsal midbrain/tectum from a dilated suprapineal recess or third ventricle pushing on the pretectal area.
  • Compromises the vertical-gaze centre (rostral interstitial nucleus of MLF, posterior commissure).
  • Results in impaired upgaze + lid retraction (Collier sign) ± convergence-retraction nystagmus ± light-near dissociation = Parinaud syndrome.
  • In infants with raised ICP, manifest as tonic downgaze (sun-setting).
Case 132 — Adolescent post-tonsillectomy with painful neck and Horner HARD
A 14-year-old had a tonsillectomy 3 days ago. He now presents with right-sided neck and head pain, partial ptosis with miosis on the right (anisocoria more marked in dim light), and 6 hours of intermittent left-arm weakness culminating in fixed left hemiparesis. No fever. CT head: subtle right MCA territory hypodensity. CTA: tapered narrowing of the right cervical ICA with a long string sign.

DX

What is the diagnosis and the explanation for the Horner sign?
Show model answer
  • Right cervical internal carotid artery dissection with stroke from artery-to-artery embolisation.
  • Horner syndrome (partial — ptosis + miosis without anhidrosis of face): caused by disruption of the postganglionic sympathetic fibres that run on the surface of the cervical ICA en route to the dilator pupillae and Müller muscle.
  • Painful Horner = dissection until proven otherwise.
  • Trigger: surgical positioning / neck manipulation / hyperextension during anaesthesia.

NEXT STEP

What is the best imaging modality to confirm, and what specific feature is diagnostic?
Show model answer
  • MRI of the neck with T1 fat-suppressed sequences is most sensitive.
  • Pathognomonic finding: crescent of T1-hyperintense intramural haematoma in the arterial wall (within first 2 weeks, methaemoglobin signal).
  • CTA shows the lumen — tapered narrowing or 'string sign' or pseudo-occlusion, sometimes a flap.
  • Catheter angiography rarely needed; reserved for surgical planning or unclear imaging.

TREATMENT

What is the acute and longer-term antithrombotic strategy?
Show model answer
  • Antiplatelet (aspirin 1–5 mg/kg/day) OR anticoagulation (LMWH/UFH bridged to warfarin) — both are acceptable per CADISS for adults; choose based on infarct size, bleeding risk, intraluminal thrombus.
  • Continue for 3–6 months with repeat imaging at 3 months.
  • Avoid further neck manipulation and contact sports.
  • Endovascular stenting reserved for refractory haemodynamic compromise.
  • Long-term: aspirin until vessel healed.
Case 133 — Infant with episodic stiffening, eye deviation, and head movements MOD
A 4-month-old former 38-week infant presents with brief episodes of bilateral arm flexion and head bobbing occurring in clusters of 20–40 spasms over a few minutes on awakening, several times daily. EEG between clusters: hypsarrhythmia. MRI brain: multiple supratentorial tubers and subependymal nodules. Skin exam: 4 ash-leaf macules on Wood lamp.

DX

Name the seizure type, the syndrome, and the underlying genetic condition.
Show model answer
  • Seizure type: infantile epileptic spasms (formerly West syndrome triad of spasms + hypsarrhythmia + developmental arrest/regression).
  • Syndrome: infantile epileptic spasms syndrome (IESS) per ILAE 2022 classification.
  • Underlying condition: tuberous sclerosis complex (TSC) — TSC1 or TSC2 mutations, cortical tubers and subependymal nodules + skin findings.
  • ~ 80–90% of TSC infants develop epilepsy; ~ 40% present with spasms.

TREATMENT

What is the first-line treatment in TSC-related spasms and why?
Show model answer
  • Vigabatrin is FIRST-LINE for spasms in TSC (more effective than ACTH/steroids in this population — TSC patients have hyperactive mTOR signalling and vigabatrin's GABA-T inhibition is particularly effective).
  • Typical dose 100–150 mg/kg/day; up-titrate over a few days.
  • Monitor for irreversible visual-field constriction (ERG / visual fields where feasible).
  • If no response within 2 weeks, switch to ACTH or oral steroids.
  • Pre-emptive vigabatrin from infancy (EPISTOP trial 2021) reduces drug-resistant epilepsy in TSC infants with epileptiform EEG.

PHYSIOLOGY

How does vigabatrin work, and what is the mTOR connection in TSC?
Show model answer
  • Vigabatrin is an irreversible inhibitor of GABA transaminase (GABA-T) → increases brain GABA concentration.
  • In TSC, loss of TSC1/TSC2 function disinhibits the mTORC1 complex → cortical malformations and hyperexcitable networks.
  • Vigabatrin's GABAergic effect counter-balances cortical hyperexcitability.
  • mTOR inhibitors (everolimus, sirolimus) directly target the pathway and are used for TSC-associated SEGA, refractory seizures (everolimus FDA-approved for TSC focal seizures > 2 years), and skin/renal lesions.
Case 134 — Child with sudden monocular vision loss + later limb weakness HARD
A 9-year-old girl presents with 3 days of painful right-eye vision loss (vision 20/200, RAPD, optic disc swelling). One week later she develops bilateral leg weakness with a sensory level at T8 and urinary retention. MRI: optic nerve enhancement on right; longitudinally extensive T2 hyperintensity in spinal cord spanning T2–T9. Serum AQP4-IgG positive.

DX

What is the diagnosis and what defines the lesion length?
Show model answer
  • Neuromyelitis optica spectrum disorder (NMOSD), AQP4-IgG-positive.
  • Longitudinally extensive transverse myelitis (LETM) is defined as a spinal cord T2 lesion spanning ≥ 3 contiguous vertebral segments.
  • LETM is characteristic of NMOSD/MOGAD/sarcoidosis — NOT typical MS (which has shorter, peripheral cord lesions).
  • Other red flags for NMOSD: simultaneous bilateral or sequential optic neuritis, area postrema syndrome (intractable hiccups/vomiting), brainstem syndromes, narcolepsy-like hypothalamic features.

TREATMENT

Acute and long-term treatment principles?
Show model answer
  • Acute attack: high-dose IV methylprednisolone 30 mg/kg/day × 3–5 days, then oral taper.
  • If inadequate response: plasma exchange (5–7 sessions every other day) — earlier PLEX correlates with better outcomes.
  • IVIG is alternative if PLEX unavailable.
  • Long-term immunotherapy: rituximab, eculizumab, satralizumab, or inebilizumab (all FDA-approved for AQP4-IgG NMOSD).
  • AVOID interferon-β and natalizumab — worsen NMOSD.

CRITERIA

List the IPND 2015 diagnostic criteria for AQP4-IgG positive NMOSD.
Show model answer
  • 1) At least ONE core clinical characteristic.
  • 2) Positive AQP4-IgG by best available test (cell-based assay preferred).
  • 3) Exclusion of alternative diagnoses.
  • Core clinical characteristics: optic neuritis, acute myelitis, area postrema syndrome, acute brainstem syndrome, symptomatic narcolepsy/diencephalic syndrome with NMOSD-typical MRI lesions, symptomatic cerebral syndrome with NMOSD-typical brain lesions.
  • For AQP4-IgG-negative or unknown status: need 2 core features (with at least one of ON/myelitis/area postrema) and additional MRI requirements.
Case 135 — Child with limp gait and increasing reflexes after viral illness MOD
A 7-year-old presents 10 days after a viral URI with subacute-onset bilateral lower limb weakness, areflexic at the knees but hyperreflexic at the ankles with up-going plantars, sensory level at T6, and bladder retention. MRI cord: T2 hyperintensity from T3 to T8 with mild cord swelling. Brain MRI normal. CSF: WBC 35 lymph, protein 0.6, OCB negative. AQP4-IgG negative; MOG-IgG negative.

DX

Most likely diagnosis and key features distinguishing from NMOSD?
Show model answer
  • Idiopathic acute transverse myelitis (parainfectious), seronegative.
  • Distinguishing features from NMOSD: no optic involvement, no area postrema syndrome, AQP4-IgG negative, no recurrent attacks expected (monophasic).
  • Need to also rule out MOG-IgG (negative here), spinal cord ischaemia, infection (HSV, enterovirus, schistosomiasis in endemic areas), sarcoidosis, compressive lesions.
  • Pattern of mixed UMN/LMN findings reflects 'spinal shock' early then evolving UMN as inflammation resolves.

NEXT STEP

What is the immediate management?
Show model answer
  • High-dose IV methylprednisolone 30 mg/kg/day × 5 days then oral taper.
  • If poor response: PLEX or IVIG.
  • Supportive care: bladder management (catheterisation), bowel programme, pressure-area care, VTE prophylaxis, physiotherapy.
  • Surveillance: relapse may signal underlying NMOSD or MS — repeat serology and brain MRI at 6–12 months.
  • Counsel family: most children with idiopathic TM make substantial recovery; some have residual deficits.
Case 136 — Newborn with respiratory failure and tongue fasciculations HARD
A 6-week-old infant presents with progressive hypotonia, poor head control, weak cry, frog-leg posture, paradoxical (abdominal) breathing, and tongue fasciculations on quiet examination. Cognitive engagement is preserved with eye tracking and bright smile. Reflexes absent. Creatine kinase normal. EMG: fibrillations + reduced motor unit recruitment in lower-motor-neuron pattern.

DX

Most likely diagnosis and the genetic test?
Show model answer
  • Spinal muscular atrophy (SMA), most likely type 1 (severe, infantile-onset).
  • Caused by homozygous deletion or compound heterozygous mutations of SMN1 on 5q13.
  • Severity modified by number of SMN2 copies (more SMN2 = milder phenotype).
  • Send: SMN1/SMN2 dosage analysis (multiplex ligation-dependent probe amplification, MLPA).
  • Preserved cognition with profound LMN weakness + tongue fasciculations + normal CK is highly suggestive.

TREATMENT

What disease-modifying therapies exist and how do they differ mechanistically?
Show model answer
  • Three FDA-approved disease-modifying therapies:
  • 1) Nusinersen — antisense oligonucleotide given intrathecally; modifies SMN2 splicing to include exon 7, increasing functional SMN protein.
  • 2) Onasemnogene abeparvovec (Zolgensma) — single IV dose AAV9 gene therapy delivering an SMN1 gene copy; approved < 2 years.
  • 3) Risdiplam — oral small molecule splicing modifier of SMN2.
  • Pre-symptomatic treatment (detected by newborn screening) yields the best outcomes.
  • Supportive: respiratory (BiPAP, cough assist), nutrition, orthopaedics, anticipatory care.

PHYSIOLOGY

Why do these therapies need to be given early?
Show model answer
  • SMA causes progressive motor neuron degeneration in the anterior horns — once neurons are lost, they cannot be regenerated.
  • Each motor unit lost = permanent functional deficit.
  • Pre-symptomatic infants treated before symptom onset can develop near-normal motor function.
  • Symptomatic infants treated late may stabilise but rarely recover lost milestones.
  • This is why newborn screening for SMN1 deletion (now standard in many regions) is critical.
Case 137 — Athletic teen with collapse and ICU course HARD
A 16-year-old long-distance runner collapses during a midday race in 32 °C heat. EMS: GCS 8, T 41.6 °C rectal, HR 152, BP 88/52, profuse sweating absent. Transferred to PICU. Now: cooled to 38 °C with cold-water immersion, but develops AKI (Cr 280), CK 65 000, hyperkalaemia, lactic acidosis, deranged coagulation (INR 2.1), and persistent encephalopathy.

DX

What is the diagnosis and the threshold defining it?
Show model answer
  • Exertional heat stroke.
  • Defined by core body temperature > 40 °C with CNS dysfunction (encephalopathy, seizures, ataxia, behavioural change) in the context of strenuous physical exertion.
  • Classic heat stroke (passive, in elderly / chronic disease) is distinguished by lack of exertion and often present in heat waves.
  • Exertional heat stroke has high lactate, more rhabdomyolysis, more DIC risk.

TREATMENT

What is the gold-standard cooling method and the target?
Show model answer
  • Cold water immersion (CWI) — most rapid cooling method, gold standard for exertional heat stroke.
  • Target core temperature 38.5–39 °C within 30 minutes of presentation.
  • Stop cooling at 38–38.5 °C to avoid overshoot/hypothermia.
  • Alternative methods if CWI unavailable: ice packs to neck/axillae/groin, cool mist + fans (evaporative).
  • Antipyretics (paracetamol/NSAIDs) are NOT effective and may worsen hepatic/renal dysfunction.

PHYSIOLOGY

What is the multi-organ injury cascade in heat stroke?
Show model answer
  • Direct cytotoxic effects of heat: protein denaturation, mitochondrial dysfunction, cellular apoptosis above ~40 °C.
  • Splanchnic hypoperfusion → endotoxin translocation → SIRS cascade.
  • Endothelial damage → DIC, microvascular thrombosis.
  • Rhabdomyolysis from muscle injury → AKI from myoglobin and pre-renal injury.
  • Hepatic injury → coagulopathy, hypoglycaemia.
  • CNS: cerebral oedema, hippocampal/cerebellar vulnerability — long-term deficits possible.
Case 138 — Toddler with fever and intermittent dystonic posturing HARD
A 22-month-old presents with 3 days of fever, decreased oral intake, and now intermittent painful dystonic posturing of all limbs lasting 3–10 minutes, recurring every 30 min, T 40.4 °C, HR 180, BP 145/95, profuse sweating. CK rising (12 000). Already on multiple AEDs from prior diagnosis of GNAO1 movement disorder. Pump-baclofen previously initiated but malfunctioned 24 h ago.

DX

Diagnosis and the genetic/pharmacologic context.
Show model answer
  • Status dystonicus — a paediatric neurocritical emergency: increasingly severe, generalised dystonia with autonomic instability, hyperthermia, rhabdomyolysis, AKI, and respiratory compromise.
  • Common precipitants: medication change/withdrawal (especially intrathecal baclofen pump failure), infection, dehydration, fatigue.
  • GNAO1-related dystonia: severe, refractory dystonia from de novo mutations in GNAO1 (Gα-o subunit); often early-onset, severe movement-disorder phenotype.

TREATMENT

Outline the tiered ICU management.
Show model answer
  • TIER 1: high-dose oral/IV anti-dystonic agents — trihexyphenidyl, baclofen (replace IT delivery emergently), tetrabenazine, clonidine, benzodiazepines.
  • TIER 2: deep ICU sedation — propofol or midazolam infusion; clonidine or dexmedetomidine adjunct; intubate if not already.
  • TIER 3: neuromuscular blockade with full sedation.
  • TIER 4: deep brain stimulation (GPi-DBS) for refractory cases — increasingly used early.
  • Throughout: aggressive cooling, IV fluids and urinary alkalinisation for rhabdomyolysis, monitor renal function, electrolytes.
  • Restore intrathecal baclofen delivery immediately if pump failure is the trigger.
Case 139 — Child with chronic seizures and new acute-onset ataxia MOD
A 9-year-old with focal epilepsy on phenytoin and lamotrigine presents acutely with 2 days of ataxia, nystagmus, dysarthria, and a slow tremor. No fever. Drug levels: phenytoin 38 mg/L (target 10–20). Lamotrigine 11 mg/L.

DX

What is the diagnosis and the mechanism of toxicity?
Show model answer
  • Phenytoin toxicity — cerebellar dysfunction at supratherapeutic levels.
  • Phenytoin metabolism follows zero-order (saturation) kinetics at therapeutic levels — small dose increases can lead to disproportionate increases in serum concentration.
  • Cerebellar signs (ataxia, nystagmus, dysarthria) typically appear at > 30 mg/L.
  • Other toxicities: gum hyperplasia, hirsutism, megaloblastic anaemia (folate inhibition), DRESS syndrome, Stevens-Johnson (HLA-B*15:02 in Asian populations).

TREATMENT

What is the management approach?
Show model answer
  • Hold or reduce phenytoin; recheck level in 24–48 hours.
  • Supportive care: prevent falls, monitor airway and aspiration risk.
  • Activated charcoal NOT useful (chronic toxicity, late presentation).
  • Hemodialysis only for severe toxicity (level > 100 mg/L, refractory cardiotoxicity, status epilepticus from toxicity).
  • Adjust dosing using the equation: New dose = Current dose × (Target / Current level) × (Vmax / (Km + Target)) — or simpler: small downward dose adjustment with level monitoring.

PHYSIOLOGY

Define zero-order vs first-order kinetics with phenytoin as the example.
Show model answer
  • First-order: rate of elimination is proportional to drug concentration; constant fraction (half-life) eliminated per unit time. Most drugs.
  • Zero-order: constant AMOUNT eliminated per unit time regardless of concentration — happens when enzymes are saturated.
  • Phenytoin: shows first-order kinetics at low concentrations and zero-order at higher (therapeutic) concentrations due to saturation of hepatic CYP2C9/CYP2C19.
  • Consequence: small dose changes near therapeutic level can cause large concentration changes; predicting half-life is unreliable.
  • Other classic zero-order drugs: ethanol, salicylates at high doses, theophylline at high doses.
Case 140 — Adolescent with new-onset psychosis and movement disorder HARD
A 14-year-old girl is admitted to psychiatry with new behavioural change, paranoia, auditory hallucinations, agitation, insomnia. Over the next 10 days she develops orofacial dyskinesias, oromandibular dystonia, autonomic storms (BP 90/50 ↔ 180/110, T 38 °C, HR 60 ↔ 150), reduced LOC, and a generalised tonic-clonic seizure. CSF: WBC 18 lymph, protein normal, oligoclonal bands present. MRI brain: unremarkable.

DX

Diagnosis and the antibody target.
Show model answer
  • Anti-NMDA receptor encephalitis.
  • IgG antibodies against the GluN1 (NR1) subunit of the NMDA receptor.
  • Antibodies cause internalisation of NMDA receptors → reversible hypofunction (similar to ketamine/PCP physiology).
  • Underlying tumour found in ~ 50% of females (ovarian teratoma) overall; lower rates in children < 12 years (10–25%) and males.

CRITERIA

Apply the Graus 2016 diagnostic criteria.
Show model answer
  • PROBABLE anti-NMDAR encephalitis: all 3 must be met → (a) rapid onset (< 3 months) of ≥ 4 of 6 categories: abnormal behaviour/psychiatric symptoms, speech dysfunction, seizures, movement disorder/dyskinesias, decreased LOC, autonomic dysfunction/central hypoventilation; (b) at least one of abnormal EEG, CSF pleocytosis/oligoclonal bands; (c) exclusion of other disorders.
  • DEFINITE anti-NMDAR encephalitis: same as probable PLUS positive IgG anti-GluN1 in CSF (preferred over serum — false negatives in serum).
  • Test serum AND CSF; CSF antibody has higher specificity.

TREATMENT

First-line and second-line therapy + tumour search.
Show model answer
  • FIRST-LINE: high-dose IV methylprednisolone (30 mg/kg/day × 5 d) + IVIG (2 g/kg) and/or plasma exchange.
  • Concurrent tumour search: pelvic US ± MRI in females (look for ovarian teratoma); testicular US in males; chest/abdomen imaging if no pelvic teratoma. Repeat surveillance at 6–12 months if initial workup negative.
  • SECOND-LINE (if no improvement at 2 weeks or severe disease): rituximab 375 mg/m²/week × 4 + cyclophosphamide 500–750 mg/m²/month.
  • Tumour removal is critical for paraneoplastic cases — faster and better recovery.
  • Long-term immunosuppression to prevent relapse (~ 12–20% relapse rate).
Case 141 — Schoolchild with first-time seizure and behavioural cluster MOD
A 7-year-old child presents to the ED in active convulsion. EMS gave one dose of intranasal midazolam at home. On arrival: ongoing right-sided tonic-clonic activity, has been seizing for 15 minutes total. He receives IV lorazepam 0.1 mg/kg. Seizure continues.

DEFINITION

Define status epilepticus in terms of T1 and T2 (ILAE 2015).
Show model answer
  • T1 = the time at which seizure activity exceeds the duration considered the natural cessation point — the 'operational' definition for when emergency treatment should be initiated. For generalised tonic-clonic seizures, T1 = 5 minutes.
  • T2 = the time at which prolonged seizure activity causes long-term consequences (neuronal injury, network alteration). For convulsive SE, T2 = 30 minutes.
  • Other seizure types have different T1/T2: focal SE with impaired awareness — T1 = 10 min, T2 = 60 min; absence SE — T1 = 10–15 min, T2 unclear.

TREATMENT

What is the next best step now and the second-line agents per ESETT?
Show model answer
  • Second dose of benzodiazepine if not given recently — many guidelines allow 2 doses then move on.
  • Then second-line: fosphenytoin 20 mg PE/kg IV OR levetiracetam 60 mg/kg IV OR valproate 40 mg/kg IV — ESETT (NEJM 2019) showed all three are equally effective (~ 50% termination).
  • If still seizing: intubate and start continuous infusion of midazolam (loading 0.2 mg/kg then 0.05–2 mg/kg/h) → ketamine → pentobarbital for super-refractory SE.
  • Continuous EEG to confirm electrographic termination.
  • Concurrent investigations: glucose, electrolytes, Ca/Mg, AED levels, toxin screen, infection workup, neuroimaging.
Case 142 — Adolescent with acute hemiparesis and cortical visual symptoms HARD
A 13-year-old girl on combined oral contraceptive develops severe headache for 24 h, then sudden left arm weakness with cortical sensory loss, agraphesthesia, and a left homonymous hemianopia. CT plain: hyperdensity along the right superior sagittal sinus and a small parasagittal venous infarct without mass effect.

DX

What is the diagnosis and the top three predisposing factors in this child?
Show model answer
  • Cerebral venous sinus thrombosis (CVST) involving the right superior sagittal sinus, with venous infarction.
  • Common prothrombotic factors in adolescents: combined oral contraceptive use, dehydration/infection (especially sinusitis/mastoiditis/otitis), inherited thrombophilia (factor V Leiden, prothrombin G20210A, protein C/S/antithrombin deficiency).
  • Other: nephrotic syndrome (urinary AT loss), L-asparaginase therapy, IBD flare, COVID-19.
  • Stop the OCP; check thrombophilia screen, antiphospholipid antibodies, complete blood count.

TREATMENT

What is the anticoagulation strategy even with venous infarction?
Show model answer
  • Anticoagulation is indicated EVEN with venous infarction and mild haemorrhagic transformation — propagation/extension risk outweighs bleeding risk.
  • Acute: therapeutic LMWH (enoxaparin 1 mg/kg q12h) or UFH.
  • Bridge to oral anticoagulation (warfarin or DOAC) for 3–6 months minimum; longer if prothrombotic state persists.
  • Endovascular thrombolysis/thrombectomy reserved for deteriorating patients despite anticoagulation or impending death.
  • Treat raised ICP, manage seizures aggressively (high seizure rate with cortical venous infarcts).

NEXT STEP

What confirmatory imaging is needed and what timing for follow-up?
Show model answer
  • MRV (preferred — no radiation, sensitive) or CTV to confirm extent of sinus involvement.
  • MRI brain to assess infarct and exclude differential.
  • Follow-up MRV at 3–6 months to assess recanalisation (~ 50% complete recanalisation).
  • Persistent occlusion does not necessarily mandate prolonged anticoagulation if asymptomatic.
Case 143 — Post-arrest child being prognosticated HARD
A 6-year-old had a witnessed in-pool drowning with submersion ~10 minutes. ROSC after 12 minutes of CPR. Initial pH 6.9, lactate 14. TTM 36 °C × 48 h. Day 4 post-arrest, off sedation 24 h: pupils 4 mm sluggish, intermittent decorticate posturing to noxious, absent gag, no spontaneous breaths over ventilator. cEEG: discontinuous background with rare reactivity. MRI day 4: extensive bilateral cortical and basal ganglia DWI restriction with normal brainstem.

CRITERIA

What are the core principles of multimodal prognostication in pediatric post-arrest?
Show model answer
  • NEVER prognosticate on a single modality.
  • Wait ≥ 72 hours off sedation/paralytics with normothermia and corrected confounders (metabolic, hypotension, sedatives).
  • Integrate: neuro exam (motor response, pupils, brainstem reflexes), EEG background continuity/reactivity, SSEPs (bilaterally absent N20 → highly specific for poor outcome), MRI DWI severity, biomarkers (NSE — less validated in children).
  • Always include family in decisions; involve neurology and palliative care.
  • Avoid self-fulfilling prophecy — premature withdrawal of life support biases data.

PHYSIOLOGY

Why is the basal ganglia particularly vulnerable in HIE?
Show model answer
  • Basal ganglia (putamen, globus pallidus), thalamus, and hippocampus are highly metabolic regions with limited collateral supply — 'selectively vulnerable' to hypoxic-ischemic injury.
  • Energy failure → membrane depolarisation → glutamate release → NMDA/AMPA over-activation → calcium influx → mitochondrial failure, apoptosis, excitotoxic death.
  • Secondary energy failure 6–24 h later (the 'therapeutic window' for hypothermia in neonates).
  • Watershed cortex (parietal/occipital boundary zones) vulnerable to global hypoperfusion.
  • Selective vulnerability explains the typical MRI pattern of severe HIE.
Case 144 — Adolescent with recurrent stroke-like episodes and short stature HARD
A 14-year-old of short stature (height < 3rd centile), sensorineural hearing loss, and chronic headaches with vomiting presents with sudden right hemiparesis and cortical blindness. MRI: cortical T2/FLAIR hyperintensity in left occipital cortex NOT respecting any vascular territory. Plasma lactate 5.8. MR spectroscopy in the lesion: lactate doublet (inverted at TE 144 ms).

DX

What is the most likely diagnosis and the genetic basis?
Show model answer
  • MELAS syndrome — Mitochondrial Encephalopathy, Lactic Acidosis, Stroke-like episodes.
  • Most common mutation: m.3243A>G in MT-TL1 (mitochondrial tRNA Leu).
  • Other mutations: m.3271T>C, m.13513G>A.
  • Maternally inherited; heteroplasmy explains variable expression.
  • Phenotype: stroke-like episodes, lactic acidosis, hearing loss, short stature, diabetes, cardiomyopathy, migraine-like headaches.

TREATMENT

What is the acute and chronic pharmacotherapy?
Show model answer
  • ACUTE stroke-like episode: IV L-arginine 0.5 g/kg bolus over 30–60 min, then 0.5 g/kg/day infusion × 3–5 days.
  • Rationale: L-arginine is precursor to nitric oxide → improves microvascular endothelial dysfunction (impaired NO bioavailability is a key MELAS pathology).
  • Aggressive seizure control with levetiracetam (AVOID valproate — mitotoxic).
  • CHRONIC: oral L-arginine + citrulline; coenzyme Q10, riboflavin; avoid mitotoxic drugs (statins, linezolid, aminoglycosides).
  • Treat hearing loss (cochlear implant), diabetes, cardiomyopathy.

PHYSIOLOGY

Why don't MELAS lesions follow vascular territories?
Show model answer
  • MELAS lesions reflect regions of selective neuronal metabolic vulnerability — not arterial occlusion.
  • Pathology = mitochondrial dysfunction with energy failure in metabolically active cortex, plus microvascular endothelial dysfunction and impaired NO signalling.
  • Hence lesions cross arterial boundaries, often spare deep white matter (which is less metabolically active), and show characteristic gyriform cortical T2/FLAIR signal.
  • MR spectroscopy shows the lactate peak (anaerobic metabolism) — sensitive and specific.
  • Distinguishes from arterial ischaemic stroke (sharp vascular boundary, DWI restriction across white matter, no metabolic markers).
Case 145 — Toddler with diffuse encephalopathy and bilateral pupil dilation HARD
A 2-year-old is found with empty pill bottles. T 38.6 °C, HR 160, BP 110/60, dry skin, dry mucous membranes, mid-dilated pupils (poorly reactive), urinary retention, agitation alternating with somnolence, picking at the air, mumbling. ECG: sinus tachycardia, QRS 92 ms, QTc 460 ms.

DX

Identify the toxidrome and the most likely class of agent.
Show model answer
  • Anticholinergic toxidrome.
  • Mnemonic: 'mad as a hatter, hot as a hare, red as a beet, dry as a bone, blind as a bat, full as a flask, the heart runs alone'.
  • Classes: antihistamines (diphenhydramine, doxylamine), TCAs (often with cardiotoxicity — wide QRS), antipsychotics, antimuscarinic eye drops, scopolamine, atropa belladonna, jimsonweed (Datura).
  • Distinguishing from sympathomimetic toxidrome (cocaine, amphetamines): sympathomimetic patients SWEAT; anticholinergic patients have DRY skin.

TREATMENT

What is the role of physostigmine and the contraindications?
Show model answer
  • Physostigmine (anticholinesterase, crosses BBB) 0.02 mg/kg slow IV (max 0.5 mg) for severe central anticholinergic features (severe agitation, seizures, dangerous hyperthermia not controlled by sedation).
  • Contraindications: known TCA ingestion (risk of asystole), QRS > 100 ms, bradyarrhythmias, bronchospasm/asthma, intestinal/bladder obstruction.
  • Have atropine ready (to reverse cholinergic excess if overdose).
  • Mainstay: benzodiazepines for agitation and seizures, IV fluids, active cooling (cold water immersion if T > 40 °C), Foley for urinary retention, supportive care.
  • Activated charcoal if recent ingestion (< 1 h) with protected airway.
Case 146 — Adolescent with confusion after dental anaesthetic MOD
A 15-year-old develops cyanosis and confusion 20 minutes after a benzocaine spray for endoscopy. SpO₂ 84% on FiO₂ 1.0 (does not rise with O₂). ABG: PaO₂ 290 (normal — oxygen IS being carried, just abnormally), 'chocolate-brown' arterial blood.

DX

What is the diagnosis and the relevant haemoglobin chemistry?
Show model answer
  • Methemoglobinemia, induced by topical benzocaine (or prilocaine, dapsone, nitrites).
  • Iron in haemoglobin is oxidised from Fe2+ to Fe3+ (met-Hb) → cannot bind O₂ AND causes a left shift of the dissociation curve of the remaining normal Hb → effective hypoxia.
  • SpO₂ reads ~ 85% regardless of true PaO₂ (pulse ox cannot distinguish met-Hb from deoxy-Hb).
  • Co-oximetry (or methaemoglobin-specific assay) is needed for accurate diagnosis.
  • Levels: < 15% asymptomatic, 20–50% cyanosis/headache, > 70% coma/death.

TREATMENT

What is the antidote and the key contraindication?
Show model answer
  • Methylene blue 1–2 mg/kg IV over 5 min for symptomatic metHb > 20% or any metHb > 30%.
  • Mechanism: methylene blue + NADPH (via NADPH-methaemoglobin reductase / G6PD-dependent pathway) → leukomethylene blue, which reduces metHb back to Hb.
  • CONTRAINDICATED in G6PD deficiency (cannot generate NADPH; can paradoxically cause haemolysis AND fail to work).
  • In G6PD deficiency: exchange transfusion or hyperbaric oxygen.
  • Stop offending agent. Repeat dose at 1 hour if needed; max 7 mg/kg total.
Case 147 — Infant with seizures and skin findings on Wood's lamp MOD
