A 7-month-old infant presents with cluster of flexor spasms and on examination has three hypopigmented macules visible with the naked eye and four more on Wood's lamp examination. Echocardiogram: small left ventricular rhabdomyoma. MRI: multiple cortical tubers and subependymal nodules.

CRITERIA

List the diagnostic criteria for tuberous sclerosis complex (TSC).
Show model answer
  • Genetic criteria: pathogenic TSC1 or TSC2 mutation = definitive diagnosis (even without clinical features).
  • Clinical criteria (2012 revised): need 2 major OR 1 major + ≥ 2 minor.
  • MAJOR: hypomelanotic macules (≥ 3, ≥ 5 mm), angiofibromas (≥ 3) or fibrous cephalic plaque, ungual fibromas (≥ 2), shagreen patch, multiple retinal hamartomas, cortical dysplasia (≥ 3, tubers or migration lines), subependymal nodules (≥ 2), subependymal giant cell astrocytoma (SEGA), cardiac rhabdomyoma, lymphangioleiomyomatosis, renal angiomyolipoma (≥ 2).
  • MINOR: confetti skin lesions, dental enamel pits (> 3), intraoral fibromas (≥ 2), retinal achromic patch, multiple renal cysts, non-renal hamartomas.

TREATMENT

Name the mTOR-targeted therapy and its principal pediatric indications.
Show model answer
  • Everolimus (FDA-approved) — oral mTOR inhibitor.
  • Indications in pediatric TSC: SEGA > 1 cm not surgical candidate; renal angiomyolipoma > 3 cm; refractory focal seizures (≥ 2 years).
  • Sirolimus: topical formulation for facial angiofibromas; systemic for lymphangioleiomyomatosis.
  • Side effects: mucositis, infections, hyperlipidaemia, growth concerns, immunosuppression.
  • Vigabatrin remains first-line for TSC-related infantile spasms; everolimus is adjunctive.
Case 148 — Adolescent boy with progressive movement disorder and behavioural changes MOD
A 14-year-old has 8 months of declining school performance, dysarthria, drooling, parkinsonian rigidity, dystonic posturing of arms, depression, and a recent tremor of the hands. Liver enzymes mildly elevated. Slit-lamp: rust-coloured pigment in the limbus of both eyes.

DX

What is the diagnosis and the metallurgical/genetic basis?
Show model answer
  • Wilson disease (hepatolenticular degeneration).
  • Autosomal recessive mutations in ATP7B (chromosome 13q14) → defective biliary excretion of copper → copper accumulates in liver, brain (basal ganglia), cornea (Kayser-Fleischer rings), kidneys.
  • Neuropsychiatric Wilson disease typically presents in adolescents/young adults; Kayser-Fleischer rings present in > 95% of neurologic Wilson.
  • Laboratory: low ceruloplasmin (< 20 mg/dL), elevated 24-h urinary copper (> 100 µg), elevated hepatic copper on biopsy (> 250 µg/g dry weight) is gold standard.

TREATMENT

What are the principal chelators and key precautions in neurological Wilson?
Show model answer
  • Trientine — preferred initial chelator in NEUROLOGIC Wilson disease (lower risk of paradoxical worsening than penicillamine).
  • D-Penicillamine — effective but can paradoxically worsen neurological symptoms in 10–50% (mobilises copper). Start low, go slow.
  • Zinc — induces metallothionein in enterocytes → blocks copper absorption. Useful for maintenance or pre-symptomatic.
  • Low-copper diet (avoid shellfish, organ meat, chocolate, nuts).
  • Liver transplant for fulminant hepatic failure or end-stage cirrhosis.
  • Lifelong therapy mandatory; monitor with urinary copper, ceruloplasmin levels, clinical exam.
Case 149 — Newborn with seizures unresponsive to phenobarbital and benzodiazepines HARD
A term newborn presents day 3 with refractory clonic seizures. Phenobarbital 40 mg/kg has not stopped seizures. Glucose, calcium, magnesium, electrolytes all normal. CSF normal. MRI normal. EEG: burst-suppression. CSF pipecolic acid elevated; α-aminoadipic semialdehyde (α-AASA) elevated.

DX

What is the diagnosis and the genetic basis?
Show model answer
  • Pyridoxine-dependent epilepsy (PDE).
  • Autosomal recessive ALDH7A1 mutations → antiquitin (α-AASA dehydrogenase) deficiency.
  • Accumulation of α-AASA → reacts with pyridoxal-5-phosphate (active vitamin B6) → depletes B6 cofactor.
  • Pipecolic acid and α-AASA in CSF/plasma/urine elevated — biomarkers.
  • Variant: PNPO deficiency requires pyridoxal-5-phosphate (PLP) rather than pyridoxine.

TREATMENT

Diagnostic trial and chronic therapy?
Show model answer
  • IV pyridoxine 100 mg with continuous EEG and crash cart at bedside — apnoea risk during the loading dose.
  • Seizure cessation within minutes (and EEG normalisation within hours) is diagnostic.
  • If no response to pyridoxine, trial pyridoxal-5-phosphate (PLP) 30 mg/kg/day to identify PNPO deficiency.
  • Lifelong oral pyridoxine 15–30 mg/kg/day (max 500 mg/day).
  • Adjunctive lysine-restricted diet ± L-arginine supplementation reduces α-AASA buildup and may improve cognitive outcomes.
Case 150 — Child with subacute psychomotor regression and movement disorder after measles HARD
A 12-year-old immigrant child had measles at age 11 months (no MMR vaccine). Now 11 years later: 6 months of progressive cognitive decline, personality change, intermittent myoclonic jerks (now occurring every 5–8 s in clusters), and slowly progressive spasticity. EEG: periodic high-amplitude generalised slow-wave complexes every 4–10 seconds (Radermecker complexes).

DX

What is the diagnosis and the pathologic agent?
Show model answer
  • Subacute sclerosing panencephalitis (SSPE).
  • Caused by a defective (matrix protein-defective) measles virus persisting in CNS neurons after initial infection.
  • Latency 6–10 years from infection.
  • Risk higher when measles occurred < 2 years of age, or in unvaccinated populations.
  • Four clinical stages (Jabbour): I behavioural/cognitive; II myoclonus + motor decline; III vegetative + rigidity; IV stupor → death.

CRITERIA

How is SSPE diagnosed?
Show model answer
  • Two of the following four features in the right clinical context:
  • 1. Characteristic progressive clinical course.
  • 2. Periodic EEG Radermecker complexes.
  • 3. Elevated measles IgG in CSF with intrathecal synthesis (CSF measles antibody disproportionately elevated relative to serum).
  • 4. Typical brain biopsy or pathology (rarely needed in life).
  • Measles RNA PCR in CSF supportive but not always positive.

TREATMENT

What palliative/disease-modifying therapies exist?
Show model answer
  • No cure. Oral isoprinosine (inosine pranobex) and intraventricular or oral interferon-α may slow progression in some patients.
  • Ribavirin investigational.
  • Supportive: AEDs for myoclonus/seizures, spasticity management, nutrition, palliative care.
  • PRIMARY PREVENTION via MMR vaccination is the only effective intervention.
  • Median survival ~ 1–3 years from diagnosis; some prolonged stable states reported.
Case 151 — Adolescent with severe TBI and persistent ICP issue HARD
A 14-year-old after a high-speed MVC. GCS 6 at scene. CT: diffuse brain swelling, effaced cisterns, no surgical lesion. EVD placed; ICP 25 mm Hg. PRx +0.5. CPP currently 58, MAP 73. Tier-1 in place: HOB 30°, normothermia, sedation/analgesia, CSF drainage, paralysis, hyperosmolar (3% saline infusion Na 152, osm 318), normoglycaemia, normocapnia.

DEFINITION

Define PRx and explain how PRx +0.5 should change your management.
Show model answer
  • PRx (pressure reactivity index) = moving correlation coefficient between slow waves of arterial BP and ICP over a ~5-minute window.
  • Reflects vasomotor reactivity of cerebral vessels.
  • Interpretation: PRx < 0 = intact autoregulation (ABP and ICP inversely related — vessels constrict when BP rises). PRx > 0.2–0.3 = impaired autoregulation (pressure-passive flow).
  • PRx +0.5 = severely impaired autoregulation — brain is fully pressure-passive.
  • Use to plot CPP-optimal (CPPopt) curve and target individualised CPP.
  • In this child, do NOT just push MAP up — pressure-passive flow risks hyperaemic oedema. Target CPPopt instead.

NEXT STEP

With refractory ICP despite optimised tier 1, what tier-2 options exist and in what order?
Show model answer
  • Tier 2 (BTF 2019): hyperosmolar therapy intensification (target Na 150–155, osm 320–340), moderate hyperventilation (PaCO₂ 30–35), mild hypothermia 35–36 °C, neuromuscular blockade if not already.
  • Tier 3 (last-resort): barbiturate coma (pentobarbital infusion to EEG burst-suppression), decompressive craniectomy (especially in young children, evidence stronger than adult RescueICP-extrapolation), deep hypothermia 32–34 °C (controversial — Cool Kids showed no benefit prophylactically).
  • Always rule out surgical lesion with repeat imaging before tier 3.
  • Concurrent multimodal monitoring (PbtO₂, microdialysis where available, NIRS) to guide tier escalation.
Case 152 — Child with new-onset facial palsy and rash MOD
A 9-year-old child returned from a summer camp in Connecticut 6 weeks ago. Now: bilateral peripheral facial nerve palsy (cannot wrinkle forehead bilaterally), one episode of arthritis of the left knee, and a recent rash described by parents as a 4-cm expanding red ring on his thigh that has now faded.

DX

Diagnosis and stage?
Show model answer
  • Lyme disease — early disseminated stage (stage 2).
  • Caused by Borrelia burgdorferi, transmitted by Ixodes ticks in endemic areas.
  • Stage 1 (early localised): erythema migrans (the expanding ring); 3–30 days after bite.
  • Stage 2 (early disseminated, days–weeks): cranial neuropathies (especially unilateral or BILATERAL CN VII palsy — particularly characteristic in children), lymphocytic meningitis, AV block, multiple erythema migrans lesions.
  • Stage 3 (late, months–years): Lyme arthritis (large joints), chronic neuroborreliosis.

TREATMENT

What is the regimen for neurologic Lyme disease in a child?
Show model answer
  • IV ceftriaxone 50–75 mg/kg/day (max 2 g/day) × 14–28 days for neuroborreliosis with meningitis, radiculopathy, or cardiac involvement.
  • Recent IDSA 2020 guidelines suggest oral doxycycline (4 mg/kg/day in 2 divided doses, max 200 mg/day) is acceptable for many neuroborreliosis presentations in children > 8 years (no longer routinely contraindicated for shorter courses; tooth discolouration risk minimal at this age).
  • Bell's palsy of Lyme: oral doxycycline 14–21 days for children > 8 years, amoxicillin or cefuroxime for younger.
  • Bilateral CN VII palsy in endemic area = Lyme until proven otherwise.
Case 153 — Newborn with persistent hyperammonaemia and respiratory alkalosis HARD
A 4-day-old term boy presents with poor feeding, lethargy, vomiting. Ammonia 950 µmol/L. Glucose 4.2 mmol/L. pH 7.51 with primary respiratory alkalosis (PaCO₂ 22). Urea low. Urinary orotic acid markedly elevated. Mother says her brother died in infancy with similar symptoms.

DX

Most likely diagnosis given the inheritance pattern and orotic acid finding?
Show model answer
  • Ornithine transcarbamylase (OTC) deficiency — X-linked partial dominance.
  • The maternal-uncle history is a classic clue for X-linked inheritance.
  • OTC is the second step of the urea cycle (in mitochondria) → defect → carbamoyl phosphate spills into pyrimidine pathway → orotic acid accumulates and appears in urine (hallmark biochemical finding).
  • Other UCDs: CPS1 (no orotic acid), NAGS (no orotic acid), citrullinaemia type 1 (ASS1 deficiency — citrulline elevated, orotic acid elevated), ASA lyase (citrullin + argininosuccinate elevated).

PHYSIOLOGY

Why does hyperammonaemia cause respiratory alkalosis?
Show model answer
  • Ammonia crosses the blood-brain barrier and is converted to glutamine in astrocytes.
  • Glutamine accumulation causes astrocyte swelling and cerebral oedema.
  • Stimulation of brainstem respiratory centres by ammonia and central irritation → hyperventilation → primary respiratory alkalosis.
  • This is opposite to most metabolic encephalopathies (which acidose); urea-cycle defects are nearly unique in causing primary respiratory alkalosis.
  • A respiratory alkalosis pattern in a sick neonate without fever or sepsis should always prompt an ammonia level.

TREATMENT

Outline the emergency management of severe hyperammonaemia.
Show model answer
  • STOP all protein intake immediately.
  • Reverse catabolism: D10 + insulin if needed for normoglycaemia; intralipid as caloric source.
  • Nitrogen scavengers IV: Sodium phenylacetate + sodium benzoate (Ammonul) load + maintenance.
  • Arginine HCl (or citrulline) IV for citrullinaemia/ASA lyase/OTC — replenishes urea cycle.
  • Hemodialysis (preferred over CRRT for rate) if ammonia > 250 µmol/L, rising despite scavengers, or in coma — most rapid clearance.
  • Treat cerebral oedema (head up, careful fluid management); avoid mannitol which doesn't address the underlying problem and can worsen.
  • After stabilisation: low-protein diet, scavenger oral (phenylbutyrate or glycerol phenylbutyrate), arginine, transplant evaluation in severe forms.
Case 154 — Schoolchild with episodic right hemiplegia and contralateral migraine MOD
A 9-year-old has 18 months of episodes that begin with visual aura, progress to right-arm and right-leg weakness, then severe left-sided pounding headache with nausea/vomiting, lasting 4–8 hours, with full recovery. His father has identical episodes. CTA and MRA normal. CT brain normal.

DX

Most likely diagnosis and the genes typically involved?
Show model answer
  • Familial hemiplegic migraine (FHM).
  • Autosomal dominant; three main genes: CACNA1A (FHM1; Ca channel), ATP1A2 (FHM2; Na/K ATPase), SCN1A (FHM3; Na channel).
  • All three genes affect ion homeostasis — alter cortical spreading depression susceptibility.
  • Attacks: visual aura → motor aura (hemiplegia) → severe headache. Some have ataxia, encephalopathy, seizures.
  • Distinguish from stroke: stereotyped recurrence, family history, complete recovery, normal vascular imaging, age of onset.

TREATMENT

Acute and preventive therapy?
Show model answer
  • Acute: IV fluids, antiemetics (metoclopramide, prochlorperazine — watch for dystonia), acetaminophen, NSAIDs.
  • AVOID triptans and ergotamines historically (theoretical vasoconstrictive concern in hemiplegic migraine — evidence has softened, but caution preferred in pediatrics).
  • Avoid β-blockers (can prolong aura).
  • Preventive: verapamil (especially CACNA1A), lamotrigine, acetazolamide, flunarizine.
  • Genetic counselling for autosomal dominant family.
Case 155 — Infant with macrocephaly and microcephaly cousins (paradox) MOD
A 7-month-old presents with macrocephaly (HC +3 SD), large fontanelle, mild developmental delay, head lag. MRI: diffuse swollen subcortical white matter T2 hyperintensity, sparing of cortex, ventricles slit-like, brainstem and cerebellum involved. MRS in white matter: huge N-acetylaspartate (NAA) peak.

DX

Diagnosis and the underlying enzyme deficiency?
Show model answer
  • Canavan disease — autosomal recessive leukodystrophy.
  • Aspartoacylase (ASPA) deficiency on chromosome 17 — failure to hydrolyse N-acetylaspartate (NAA).
  • Accumulated NAA → spongiform white-matter degeneration ('spongy degeneration of infancy').
  • Higher carrier frequency in Ashkenazi Jewish population (~ 1/40).
  • Imaging hallmark: macrocephaly + diffuse white-matter T2 hyperintensity + MRS NAA peak (instead of the typical reduced NAA in other neurodegenerative disease — pathognomonic).

TREATMENT

Therapeutic options and prognosis?
Show model answer
  • Currently NO disease-modifying therapy of proven efficacy.
  • Supportive care: nutrition (often PEG), seizure management, physiotherapy, anticipatory care.
  • Gene therapy with AAV-delivered ASPA in clinical trials (e.g., BBP-812) — early results promising.
  • Early palliative care integration; survival usually limited to first decade in the classic infantile form.
  • Family genetic counselling — recurrence 25%; carrier screening offered for Ashkenazi couples.
Case 156 — Toddler with intermittent ataxic episodes and triggers MOD
A 4-year-old has recurrent episodes of acute cerebellar ataxia and dysarthria lasting hours to days, triggered by stress and febrile illness, with normal interictal exam. Father had similar attacks as a child. MRI normal. Acetazolamide given prophylactically reduced attacks dramatically.

DX

Diagnosis and the channel involved?
Show model answer
  • Episodic ataxia type 2 (EA2) — autosomal dominant CACNA1A mutations.
  • CACNA1A encodes the α1A subunit of P/Q-type voltage-gated calcium channel.
  • Same gene also mutated in familial hemiplegic migraine type 1 and spinocerebellar ataxia type 6 (CAG repeat) — allelic disorders with different phenotypes.
  • EA2: longer episodes (hours-days), interictal nystagmus (downbeat), ACETAZOLAMIDE-RESPONSIVE.
  • EA1 (KCNA1) — shorter episodes (minutes), interictal myokymia.

TREATMENT

Why acetazolamide?
Show model answer
  • Acetazolamide is a carbonic-anhydrase inhibitor; its precise mechanism in EA2 is uncertain but likely involves mild metabolic acidosis altering neuronal excitability and possibly direct effects on neuronal pH-sensitive channels.
  • Effective in many EA2 patients — reduces frequency and severity of attacks.
  • 4-aminopyridine (potassium channel blocker) — alternative or adjunctive (small trials show benefit).
  • Avoid triggers; treat fever aggressively.
  • Genetic counselling; cascade testing of relatives.
Case 157 — Adolescent on biologic with new-onset diplopia and worsening headache HARD
A 16-year-old with Crohn disease on infliximab presents with 3 days of progressive headache, then double vision and a 6th nerve palsy on the right. Subsequently he develops progressive lethargy. MRI brain: confluent multifocal asymmetric white-matter T2 hyperintensities, particularly subcortical, no enhancement, no mass effect, with sparing of U fibres.

DX

Most likely diagnosis and pathogen?
Show model answer
  • Progressive multifocal leukoencephalopathy (PML).
  • Caused by reactivation of JC polyomavirus (JCV) in oligodendrocytes.
  • Occurs in immunocompromised patients: HIV/AIDS, post-HSCT, on natalizumab/rituximab/fingolimod (most TNF-α inhibitors carry lower but real risk).
  • MRI: asymmetric subcortical T2 hyperintensities sparing cortex, no enhancement typically (enhancement may appear with immune reconstitution).
  • Diagnosis: CSF JCV PCR + characteristic MRI; brain biopsy if CSF PCR negative and suspicion remains.

TREATMENT

Management and the risk of treatment for it?
Show model answer
  • No proven antiviral therapy specific for JCV.
  • Restore immune function: stop/reduce immunosuppressive therapy; in HIV, optimise antiretroviral therapy.
  • In natalizumab-associated PML: stop natalizumab + plasma exchange to remove drug + supportive care.
  • Pembrolizumab (anti-PD-1) reported in case series — restores T-cell immune surveillance.
  • Filgrastim or IL-7 experimental.
  • Beware IRIS — immune reconstitution inflammatory syndrome can paradoxically worsen brain injury when immunity is restored; managed with corticosteroids if severe.
Case 158 — Boy with hypopigmented hair and severe seizures from infancy HARD
A 4-month-old boy presents with intractable myoclonic seizures from day 14 of life. Examination: pale skin and blond hair (parents are dark-haired), eczematous rash, musty body odour, severe global developmental delay, mild microcephaly. Newborn screen reportedly 'misplaced'.

DX

What is the diagnosis and the enzyme deficient?
Show model answer
  • Phenylketonuria (PKU) — classic, untreated.
  • Autosomal recessive phenylalanine hydroxylase (PAH) deficiency on 12q23.
  • Accumulation of phenylalanine → toxic to developing brain.
  • Phenotype: severe intellectual disability, microcephaly, seizures, hypopigmentation (Phe inhibits tyrosinase and hence melanin synthesis), musty odour (from phenylacetate metabolite), eczema.
  • Universal newborn screening prevents this clinical picture in resource-rich settings — this presentation indicates missed/delayed screening.

TREATMENT

What is the management and the genetic counselling for the family?
Show model answer
  • Strict low-phenylalanine diet for life — limit natural protein; use Phe-free medical formula.
  • Sapropterin dihydrochloride (BH4) — synthetic cofactor; ~ 30% of patients are BH4-responsive and can liberalise diet.
  • Pegvaliase (phenylalanine ammonia lyase) — enzymatic substitute, FDA-approved for adults.
  • Plasma Phe target 120–360 µmol/L (180–360 in older patients).
  • Maternal PKU: women with PKU must maintain strict diet preconceptionally and during pregnancy to avoid maternal PKU syndrome (microcephaly, congenital heart disease, ID) in offspring.
  • Genetic counselling: AR with 25% recurrence; carrier screening if family history.
Case 159 — Adolescent with subacute cognitive decline and 14-3-3 protein in CSF HARD
A 16-year-old has 4 months of progressive cognitive decline, ataxia, myoclonus, and a recent psychotic episode. He has no known exposures. MRI: cortical ribbon T2/FLAIR hyperintensity (involves the caudate and putamen — 'hockey stick' sign). EEG: periodic sharp wave complexes at ~ 1 Hz. CSF: 14-3-3 protein and tau elevated; RT-QuIC positive.

DX

Diagnosis and pathogenic mechanism?
Show model answer
  • Sporadic Creutzfeldt-Jakob disease (sCJD) — rare in adolescents but possible.
  • Caused by misfolded prion protein (PrPSc) that templates conversion of normal PrPC → progressive neuronal vacuolation and death.
  • Variants: sporadic (most common), familial (PRNP mutations), iatrogenic (corneal transplant, dura mater, growth hormone — historical), variant (bovine spongiform encephalopathy exposure).
  • Imaging: cortical ribbon hyperintensity on DWI + 'hockey stick' (basal ganglia involvement) is highly suggestive.
  • RT-QuIC (real-time quaking-induced conversion) is the most specific diagnostic test (now CSF-based, replaces brain biopsy in many cases).

TREATMENT

Management and counselling?
Show model answer
  • No disease-modifying therapy available; uniformly fatal.
  • Supportive care; palliative care early integration.
  • Genetic counselling — familial forms (~ 10–15% of CJD) require family testing.
  • Public health notification for surveillance.
  • Infection control during procedures: prions resistant to standard sterilisation — single-use instruments for any procedure; specific decontamination protocols required.
Case 160 — Toddler with isolated downgaze and visual impairment after surgery MOD
A 5-year-old had a posterior fossa medulloblastoma resected 6 days ago. Today: bilateral lid retraction, impaired upward gaze, convergence-retraction nystagmus on attempted upgaze, light-near dissociation (pupils react to near but not light).

DX

Name the syndrome and the lesion location.
Show model answer
  • Parinaud syndrome (dorsal midbrain syndrome / pretectal syndrome).
  • Caused by lesion in the pretectal area / dorsal midbrain.
  • Classical tetrad: (1) impaired vertical gaze (especially upgaze), (2) convergence-retraction nystagmus on attempted upgaze, (3) light-near dissociation (pupils don't react to light but constrict on accommodation), (4) lid retraction (Collier sign).
  • In paediatric oncology: pineal region tumours (germinoma, pineoblastoma), aqueductal stenosis with sudden decompression, posterior fossa surgery, midbrain stroke.

NEXT STEP

What investigations should follow?
Show model answer
  • Urgent post-op MRI to assess for residual tumour, oedema around midbrain, hydrocephalus.
  • Check shunt/EVD function — sudden CSF dynamics changes can cause upward herniation through tentorium with midbrain compression.
  • If recurrence or new pineal lesion suspected, image whole neuraxis.
  • Ophthalmology referral for prism management if persistent.
  • Most post-operative Parinaud resolves over weeks if cause is transient oedema; persistent signs warrant further investigation.
Case 161 — Adolescent with episodic flaccid paralysis and low potassium MOD
A 16-year-old presents with sudden severe weakness of all four limbs upon waking after a heavy carbohydrate meal the prior evening. He has had previous similar episodes lasting hours. ECG: U-waves; serum K 2.1 mmol/L; thyroid function normal. Family history: maternal uncle has similar episodes.

DX

Diagnosis and the principal channel involved?
Show model answer
  • Hypokalaemic periodic paralysis (HypoPP) — autosomal dominant.
  • Mutations in CACNA1S (DHP receptor, calcium channel) or SCN4A (sodium channel) — alter ion fluxes during depolarisation, causing inappropriate sarcolemmal hypoexcitability.
  • Triggers: large carbohydrate meal (insulin shifts K into cells), strenuous exercise followed by rest, stress, cold.
  • Episode: severe flaccid weakness, often spares respiratory muscles, lasts hours–day.
  • Differential: thyrotoxic periodic paralysis (commoner in Asian males with thyrotoxicosis), Andersen-Tawil syndrome (KCNJ2 — also has cardiac arrhythmia + dysmorphism).

TREATMENT

Acute and prophylactic therapy?
Show model answer
  • ACUTE: oral KCl 0.5–1 mEq/kg, repeat every 30 min monitoring ECG and serum K — avoid IV K if possible (rebound hyperkalaemia common).
  • Avoid glucose-containing fluids (worsens hypokalaemia).
  • Continuous cardiac monitoring; bedside swallow.
  • PROPHYLAXIS: acetazolamide or dichlorphenamide (FDA-approved carbonic-anhydrase inhibitor for periodic paralysis), low-carb low-Na diet, avoid triggers.
  • Spironolactone or eplerenone in selected cases.
  • Genetic counselling autosomal dominant.
Case 162 — Child with ptosis and progressive weakness with diurnal variation MOD
An 8-year-old has 3 months of intermittent bilateral ptosis (worse at end of day), diplopia, neck flexor weakness when tired, dysphagia of solids. Exam at end of day: ptosis after sustained upgaze 60 sec, fatiguable shoulder abduction, normal reflexes, normal sensation. Anti-acetylcholine receptor antibody positive.

DX

Diagnosis and the autoantibody specifics.
Show model answer
  • Juvenile myasthenia gravis (JMG).
  • Two main subtypes by antibody:
  • Anti-AChR (acetylcholine receptor) — 80–90% of generalised JMG.
  • Anti-MuSK (muscle-specific tyrosine kinase) — 10–15%, especially female adolescents with prominent bulbar involvement, neck and respiratory weakness, may be seronegative for AChR.
  • Anti-LRP4 (newer) — small fraction.
  • Seronegative MG: ~ 5–10% — diagnose clinically and electrophysiologically (decremental response on RNS).

TREATMENT

Outline the stepwise treatment.
Show model answer
  • 1) Symptomatic: pyridostigmine (acetylcholinesterase inhibitor) — oral, titrated; less effective in MuSK MG.
  • 2) Immunosuppression for inadequately controlled disease: prednisone with calcium/vitamin D; steroid-sparing agents (azathioprine, mycophenolate, rituximab for MuSK).
  • 3) Acute exacerbation / crisis: IVIG or plasma exchange (equivalent efficacy).
  • 4) Thymectomy: indicated for AChR-positive generalised MG and thymoma; for ocular MG controversial. Less effective in MuSK MG.
  • 5) Avoid drugs that worsen MG: aminoglycosides, fluoroquinolones, telithromycin, β-blockers, magnesium, neuromuscular blockers (use carefully — increased sensitivity).

CRITERIA

What is the difference between myasthenic crisis and cholinergic crisis?
Show model answer
  • Myasthenic crisis: severe weakness, respiratory failure, bulbar dysfunction — caused by undertreatment or worsening disease.
  • Cholinergic crisis: weakness AND excessive cholinergic activity (miosis, bradycardia, hypersalivation, lacrimation, bronchorrhoea, diarrhoea, fasciculations) — caused by excessive anticholinesterase therapy.
  • Edrophonium (Tensilon) test historically used to distinguish — improvement with edrophonium = myasthenic; worsening = cholinergic. Rarely used today.
  • Modern approach: hold pyridostigmine, treat with IVIG/PLEX + steroids + supportive ventilation.
Case 163 — Toddler with rapid hyperventilation and chest wall retraction MOD
A 14-month-old presents with episodic hyperventilation (RR 80) followed by apnoea lasting 30–60 seconds, recurring through the day, associated with stereotyped hand-wringing. Developmental regression noted around 12 months. Head circumference dropping percentiles.

DX

What syndrome is suggested and how does the breathing abnormality fit?
Show model answer
  • Rett syndrome — typical breathing dysrhythmia.
  • Characteristic respiratory pattern: paroxysmal hyperventilation, breath-holding/apnoea, Valsalva manoeuvres, air swallowing.
  • Believed due to brainstem dysregulation (medullary autonomic centres) related to MECP2 dysfunction.
  • Often associated with QT prolongation and risk of arrhythmia.
  • Episodes are wakeful — breathing typically normalises in sleep (distinguishes from central apnoea/Ondine's curse).

NEXT STEP

What investigations and surveillance are essential?
Show model answer
  • MECP2 genetic testing — confirms diagnosis (also screen for CDKL5 and FOXG1 mutations for atypical Rett variants).
  • ECG — measure QTc (cardiac monitoring; arrhythmia is a leading cause of sudden death in Rett).
  • EEG — high seizure burden expected.
  • Polysomnography if sleep-related symptoms.
  • Bone density (osteoporosis common from immobility + MECP2 effects).
  • Scoliosis surveillance every 6 months from age 4.
Case 164 — Preadolescent with progressive cognitive decline post-streptococcal infection MOD
A 9-year-old previously well child develops sudden-onset severe OCD with food refusal (claiming food is 'contaminated'), motor tics, urinary frequency, separation anxiety, and emotional lability, over 1 week. 3 weeks ago she had a strep throat culture-positive infection treated with amoxicillin. ASO titre rising. Anti-DNase B elevated.

DX

Most likely diagnosis and its broader classification?
Show model answer
  • PANDAS — Pediatric Autoimmune Neuropsychiatric Disorders Associated with Streptococcal infections.
  • Falls within broader PANS (Pediatric Acute-onset Neuropsychiatric Syndrome) — abrupt-onset OCD or severely restricted food intake plus > 2 of: anxiety, lability/depression, regression, school decline, sensory/motor abnormalities, somatic symptoms.
  • PANDAS-specific criteria: prepubertal onset, temporally related to GAS infection, episodic course.
  • Controversial entity — diagnostic criteria debated; many experts now prefer PANS framework.

TREATMENT

What is the treatment principle?
Show model answer
  • Treat documented GAS infection: penicillin V or amoxicillin × 10 days.
  • Prophylactic antibiotics (penicillin or azithromycin) for recurrent strep-triggered relapses — controversial in long-term use.
  • SSRIs (sertraline, fluoxetine) for OCD — start LOW because children with PANS may be sensitive to side effects.
  • CBT for OCD.
  • Immunomodulation for moderate-severe disease: IVIG (2 g/kg) per PANS consortium guidelines; plasma exchange for severe / refractory.
  • NSAIDs (ibuprofen) trialled with modest evidence.
  • Manage refeeding precautions for restrictive eating; multidisciplinary care.
Case 165 — Newborn with severe encephalopathy and intractable hiccups HARD
A term boy presents on day 2 with progressive lethargy, hypotonia, severe myoclonic seizures unresponsive to phenobarbital, and intractable hiccups. Apnoeic episodes occur. Glucose, calcium, electrolytes normal. CSF glycine markedly elevated; CSF:plasma glycine ratio 0.12. EEG: burst-suppression pattern.

DX

Diagnosis and the underlying enzyme defect?
Show model answer
  • Non-ketotic hyperglycinemia (NKH) / glycine encephalopathy.
  • Autosomal recessive defect in the glycine cleavage system — most often GLDC (P protein, 70–75%); also AMT (T protein) or GCSH (H protein).
  • Glycine accumulates in CSF and brain.
  • Glycine acts as co-agonist at NMDA receptors → over-activation → excitotoxicity, seizures, encephalopathy.
  • Glycine also inhibitory at brainstem glycine receptors → hiccups, apnoea.
  • CSF:plasma glycine ratio > 0.08 is characteristic (normal < 0.02).

TREATMENT

Therapies and prognosis?
Show model answer
  • No cure; supportive care.
  • Sodium benzoate (250–750 mg/kg/day): conjugates glycine for renal excretion as hippurate; lowers plasma glycine, less effect on CSF glycine.
  • NMDA antagonists: dextromethorphan (5–15 mg/kg/day), ketamine — reduce excitotoxicity.
  • Felbamate (anti-NMDA mechanism) used by some centres.
  • Ketogenic diet emerging.
  • Severe neonatal form: poor prognosis (most die in infancy or have severe disability).
  • Attenuated form (later-onset, milder GLDC variants): better outcomes.
  • Family genetic counselling (25% recurrence in carrier parents).
Case 166 — Child with persistent unexplained eye movements after trauma MOD
A 4-year-old presents 2 weeks after a febrile illness with rapid, chaotic conjugate eye movements in all directions (opsoclonus), myoclonic jerks of trunk and limbs, severe truncal ataxia, irritability, sleep disturbance. No fever now.

DX

Diagnosis and mandatory workup?
Show model answer
  • Opsoclonus-myoclonus-ataxia syndrome (OMAS) — 'dancing eye syndrome'.
  • Pathognomonic triad: opsoclonus + myoclonus + ataxia (often with behavioural/sleep disturbance).
  • Up to 50% of pediatric cases are PARANEOPLASTIC — usually neuroblastoma (often occult, early-stage).
  • Mandatory workup: urine catecholamines (VMA, HVA), MIBG scan, MRI/CT chest-abdomen-pelvis to find occult neuroblastoma.
  • Other causes: post-infectious (parainfectious; VZV, EBV, mycoplasma, viral), idiopathic, anti-Hu in adults rare in children.

TREATMENT

What is the immunotherapy approach?
Show model answer
  • If neuroblastoma found: resect/treat per oncology.
  • First-line immunotherapy: corticosteroids (prednisone or pulsed methylprednisolone) + ACTH + IVIG (combination — synergistic).
  • Second-line for refractory: rituximab; cyclophosphamide.
  • Long-term immunotherapy often needed for 12–24 months to prevent relapse.
  • Neurocognitive sequelae are common (50–80%) even with treatment — behavioural problems, ADHD, intellectual impairment — long-term neuropsychological follow-up.
  • Multidisciplinary care; school support.
Case 167 — Schoolchild with optic neuritis and cerebral lesions MOD
A 9-year-old boy presents with acute painful right monocular vision loss, then 5 days later confusion, ataxia, and a generalised tonic-clonic seizure. MRI: large fluffy bilateral cortical and subcortical T2/FLAIR lesions with patchy enhancement, plus enhancement of the right optic nerve. CSF: 25 lymphocytes, normal glucose, mildly elevated protein, no oligoclonal bands. AQP4-IgG negative. MOG-IgG strongly positive at 1:1000.

DX

Diagnosis and how it differs phenotypically from MS and NMOSD?
Show model answer
  • MOG antibody-associated disease (MOGAD).
  • Antibodies target myelin oligodendrocyte glycoprotein (surface of oligodendrocytes).
  • Pediatric MOGAD often presents as ADEM-like illness, bilateral optic neuritis, or longitudinally extensive transverse myelitis.
  • Distinguishing features: typically MONOPHASIC or relapsing-remitting (~ 40% relapse), excellent steroid response, OCBs usually NEGATIVE (vs MS), AQP4-IgG NEGATIVE (vs NMOSD), MOG-IgG positive at titre ≥ 1:160 (must be cell-based assay).
  • MRI: fluffy, cloud-like cortical/juxtacortical T2 lesions, less periventricular than MS, optic nerve enhancement often anterior with disc swelling.

TREATMENT

Acute and relapse-prevention strategies?
Show model answer
  • ACUTE attack: high-dose IV methylprednisolone 30 mg/kg/day × 3–5 days then prolonged oral taper (10–12 weeks) — abrupt steroid taper triggers relapse in MOGAD.
  • PLEX or IVIG for severe attacks unresponsive to steroids.
  • Long-term immunotherapy if relapsing: rituximab, IVIG monthly, mycophenolate, azathioprine; tocilizumab and inebilizumab emerging options.
  • Distinguishing from NMOSD matters: eculizumab/satralizumab for NMOSD are NOT used in MOGAD.
  • AVOID interferon-β and natalizumab (worsens MOGAD as it does NMOSD).
  • Repeat MOG-IgG at 6 months; persistently positive favours relapsing disease.

CRITERIA

Apply the 2023 international consensus criteria for MOGAD.
Show model answer
  • 2023 criteria require BOTH:
  • 1) MOG-IgG positive by cell-based assay (clear positive: serum titre ≥ 1:100 if dilution assay, or clearly positive on flow cytometry; lower titres need supporting features).
  • 2) Compatible clinical/radiological event: optic neuritis, myelitis, ADEM, cerebral monofocal/polyfocal deficits, brainstem/cerebellar deficits, or cortical encephalitis with seizures.
  • PLUS exclusion of better alternative diagnoses (MS, NMOSD).
  • Low-titre MOG-IgG positivity (1:10–1:100) is non-specific and requires strong supporting clinical features.
Case 168 — Adolescent with bulbar weakness and respiratory failure post-URI HARD
A 17-year-old developed sore throat 12 days ago. Now: 5 days of progressively worsening diplopia, ptosis, dysarthria, dysphagia leading to aspiration, and progressive ascending paralysis. Today: respiratory failure requiring intubation; mild encephalopathy with brisk reflexes preserved (unusual for GBS). Anti-GQ1b antibody positive.

DX

Diagnosis and how it differs from classic GBS and Miller Fisher syndrome?
Show model answer
  • Bickerstaff brainstem encephalitis (BBE) — part of the anti-GQ1b antibody spectrum.
  • Distinguishing features from classic GBS: BRAINSTEM involvement (cranial neuropathies, ophthalmoplegia, bulbar dysfunction) PLUS encephalopathy/altered consciousness PLUS hyperreflexia (vs areflexia in GBS).
  • Miller Fisher syndrome (MFS): ophthalmoplegia + ataxia + areflexia, NO encephalopathy, also anti-GQ1b positive.
  • BBE and MFS are part of a continuum — central + peripheral involvement.
  • Often follows Campylobacter, Haemophilus influenzae, or other respiratory infection.

TREATMENT

Acute management priorities?
Show model answer
  • Airway protection — early intubation if bulbar dysfunction or respiratory failure.
  • IVIG 2 g/kg over 5 days OR plasma exchange (5 sessions) — equivalent efficacy.
  • Supportive ICU care: aspiration prevention, autonomic monitoring (BP swings, arrhythmias), nutrition (NG/PEG).
  • Treat any identified infection (Campylobacter — azithromycin).
  • Most patients recover with appropriate treatment; long-term sequelae uncommon.
  • Continuous EEG if persistent altered consciousness — exclude non-convulsive seizures.
Case 169 — Adolescent with neuromyotonia, insomnia, hyperhidrosis HARD
A 16-year-old over 4 months develops progressive insomnia (sleeping 1–2 h/night for weeks), autonomic features (excessive sweating, lacrimation, hypertension, dysuria), continuous muscle twitching at rest (myokymia, neuromyotonia on EMG), cognitive change with vivid hallucinations, and weight loss. CSF anti-CASPR2 antibody positive.

DX

Diagnosis and the antibody target?
Show model answer
  • Morvan syndrome — autoimmune syndrome with anti-CASPR2 antibodies (sometimes co-positive for anti-LGI1).
  • CASPR2 (contactin-associated protein-like 2) is part of the voltage-gated potassium channel (VGKC) complex; antibodies disrupt nerve and CNS function.
  • Triad: peripheral nerve hyperexcitability (neuromyotonia / myokymia / fasciculations) + autonomic dysfunction + encephalopathy (often with severe insomnia).
  • Differential within VGKC-complex spectrum: anti-LGI1 (limbic encephalitis with faciobrachial dystonic seizures and hyponatraemia), Isaacs syndrome (peripheral hyperexcitability only without encephalopathy).

TREATMENT

Treatment strategy and tumour screen?
Show model answer
  • First-line immunotherapy: high-dose IV methylprednisolone + IVIG and/or plasma exchange.
  • Second-line: rituximab, cyclophosphamide.
  • Symptomatic: AEDs / membrane stabilisers (carbamazepine, lacosamide) for neuromyotonia; benzodiazepines for sleep; antihypertensives for autonomic.
  • Screen for thymoma — Morvan syndrome strongly associated with thymoma (especially in adults; less so in adolescents); also screen for SCLC in older patients.
  • Treat tumour if found — often improves neurologic disease.
  • Long-term immunotherapy maintenance to prevent relapse.
Case 170 — Adolescent with painful muscle spasms triggered by sound MOD
A 15-year-old developed progressive stiffness over 6 months. Now: continuous painful muscle stiffness of axial muscles with superimposed spasms triggered by sudden sounds or being touched. Severe lumbar hyperlordosis. Anti-GAD65 antibody positive at very high titre.

DX

Diagnosis and the antigen target?
Show model answer
  • Stiff person syndrome (SPS).
  • Anti-GAD65 antibodies (against glutamic acid decarboxylase 65, the enzyme producing GABA) — high titres differentiate from those seen in type 1 diabetes (where titres are much lower).
  • Reduced GABAergic inhibition → continuous motor unit activity → axial rigidity and stimulus-triggered spasms.
  • Variants: classic SPS (axial), stiff-limb syndrome, SPS-plus (with cerebellar/oculomotor features), PERM (progressive encephalomyelitis with rigidity and myoclonus — anti-glycine receptor more common in PERM).
  • Pediatric SPS very rare; consider anti-glycine receptor and PERM in this age group.

TREATMENT

Symptomatic and disease-modifying treatments?
Show model answer
  • Symptomatic: high-dose oral benzodiazepines (diazepam up to 80–100 mg/day in adults; pediatric dosing lower), baclofen (oral or intrathecal for severe), tizanidine.
  • IVIG 2 g/kg monthly — proven efficacy in randomised trials.
  • Rituximab for refractory disease.
  • Plasma exchange in acute severe presentations.
  • Screen for associated autoimmune conditions: type 1 diabetes, thyroiditis, pernicious anaemia.
  • Long-term immunotherapy generally needed.
Case 171 — Toddler with deep puncture wound and trismus HARD
A 3-year-old, never vaccinated, sustained a deep puncture wound from a rusty nail 8 days ago. Now: jaw stiffness (cannot fully open mouth), risus sardonicus (grimace), opisthotonic spasms triggered by stimuli, severe BP swings, profuse sweating, tachycardia.

DX

Diagnosis and pathogen?
Show model answer
  • Generalised tetanus.
  • Caused by Clostridium tetani (anaerobic spore-forming Gram-positive bacillus); spores in soil germinate in anaerobic wound environment.
  • Tetanospasmin toxin → cleaves synaptobrevin (VAMP) → blocks inhibitory neurotransmitter release (GABA, glycine) at spinal interneurons → uncontrolled motor neuron firing.
  • Generalised tetanus: trismus (lockjaw, ~ 75% present feature), risus sardonicus, opisthotonus, generalised spasms, autonomic instability (most lethal feature in modern ICU care).
  • Cephalic tetanus (head wounds, CN palsies), neonatal tetanus (umbilical stump contamination in unvaccinated mothers).

TREATMENT

Outline the multi-pronged management.
Show model answer
  • 1) NEUTRALISE circulating toxin: human tetanus immune globulin (HTIG) 3000–6000 IU IM (some advocate intrathecal route). Does NOT reverse bound toxin.
  • 2) ERADICATE organism: surgical wound debridement; metronidazole 30 mg/kg/day IV (preferred over penicillin in some guidelines — fewer GABA antagonist effects).
  • 3) ACTIVE IMMUNISATION: tetanus toxoid (the disease does not produce immunity; vaccination still required).
  • 4) CONTROL SPASMS: heavy sedation with benzodiazepines (diazepam continuous), magnesium sulfate infusion (also helps autonomic instability), propofol, intrathecal baclofen.
  • 5) MANAGE AUTONOMIC STORM: magnesium first-line; α + β blockers (labetalol), clonidine; AVOID isolated β-blockade (unopposed alpha can worsen).
  • 6) Mechanical ventilation, NMB if needed; ICU for weeks.
  • Mortality 10–50% even with optimal care.
Case 172 — Adolescent with sudden encephalopathy and DKA on day 2 HARD
A 14-year-old in DKA being treated for new-onset T1DM presents on day 2 with worsening headache, then sudden facial pain and dark eschar over the right nasal turbinate and palate. CT/MRI sinuses: aggressive sinus disease with bony erosion and intracranial extension into the right frontal lobe.

DX

Diagnosis and the key pathogen group?
Show model answer
  • Rhinocerebral mucormycosis (zygomycosis).
  • Caused by Mucorales fungi — Rhizopus oryzae (most common), Mucor, Rhizomucor, Cunninghamella, Lichtheimia.
  • Angio-invasive — invades blood vessels causing thrombosis and tissue necrosis (the black eschar).
  • Risk factors: DKA (low pH + iron availability favours fungal growth), neutropenia, immunosuppression, deferoxamine therapy, COVID-19 (during pandemic surge).
  • Rapidly fatal without aggressive treatment — invasion into orbit, brain, cavernous sinus.

TREATMENT

What is the multimodal treatment?
Show model answer
  • Surgical debridement — aggressive, often disfiguring, repeated.
  • Antifungal: liposomal amphotericin B 5–10 mg/kg/day (high-dose).
  • Step-down to isavuconazole or posaconazole.
  • Reverse predisposing factors: aggressive correction of DKA (lower glucose, correct acidosis), discontinue deferoxamine, reverse immunosuppression where possible.
  • Hyperbaric oxygen adjunctive in some centres.
  • Long-term antifungal therapy.
  • Mortality remains high (40–80%) even with optimal treatment.
Case 173 — Schoolchild with progressive headache and ring-enhancing lesion MOD
A 9-year-old with cyanotic congenital heart disease (uncorrected tetralogy of Fallot) presents with 5 days of fever, headache, vomiting, focal left-arm seizures, and now drowsiness. CT: 4 cm ring-enhancing lesion in the right frontal lobe with surrounding oedema and 6 mm midline shift.

DX

What is the diagnosis and the predisposition mechanism?
Show model answer
  • Pyogenic brain abscess.
  • In cyanotic CHD: right-to-left shunt bypasses the pulmonary capillary filter → bacteria from systemic circulation reach cerebral arteries directly → seeded in brain parenchyma.
  • Other predisposing factors: contiguous spread from otitis/sinusitis (frontal, temporal), penetrating trauma, post-neurosurgery, IE, immunocompromise.
  • Common organisms in pediatric brain abscess: Streptococcus (viridans, milleri group — anginosus most common), Staphylococcus aureus, anaerobes (Bacteroides, Fusobacterium), Enterobacteriaceae; polymicrobial in > 30%.

NEXT STEP

Indications for surgical drainage vs medical therapy alone?
Show model answer
  • Surgical drainage (aspiration or excision) indicated for: abscess > 2.5 cm, neurological deterioration, mass effect with midline shift, multiloculated abscess, posterior fossa location, gas-containing abscess, failure to respond to antibiotics, immunocompromised host.
  • This case has all the indications (size 4 cm, shift, drowsiness, focal seizures) → drain urgently.
  • Empiric antibiotics: ceftriaxone + metronidazole; add vancomycin for staph coverage (especially post-neurosurgery, MRSA risk); broader Gram-negative coverage if immunocompromised.
  • Continue antibiotics 6–8 weeks IV total; can transition to oral if responding.
  • Image (MRI with contrast) at 2 weeks, monthly, then 3-monthly for response.

PHYSIOLOGY

Stages of brain abscess maturation?
Show model answer
  • Stage 1 — Early cerebritis (days 1–3): poorly defined area of inflammation, oedema.
  • Stage 2 — Late cerebritis (days 4–9): central necrosis begins, peripheral inflammation.
  • Stage 3 — Early capsule formation (days 10–13): collagen deposition begins, ring enhancement appears on imaging.
  • Stage 4 — Late capsule formation (≥ 14 days): mature capsule with central necrosis, well-defined ring enhancement, surrounding oedema.
  • Treatment varies by stage: early stages may respond to antibiotics alone if small; mature abscesses generally need drainage.
Case 174 — Child with subacute fever and basal cisternal enhancement MOD
A 7-year-old recent immigrant from a high-TB-prevalence region presents with 3 weeks of low-grade fever, weight loss, persistent headache, vomiting, and now bilateral CN VI palsies. CSF: WBC 220 (lymphocyte predominant 80%), protein 2.8 g/L, glucose 1.0 (CSF:plasma ratio 0.2). MRI: basal cisternal enhancement, hydrocephalus, small lacunar infarcts in basal ganglia. Tuberculin skin test 18 mm.

DX

Diagnosis and the typical lesion pattern?
Show model answer
  • Tuberculous meningitis (TBM).
  • Three pathological hallmarks: basal exudate (causes CN palsies and hydrocephalus), vasculitis (particularly perforating vessels — causes lacunar infarcts in basal ganglia/thalamus), tuberculomas.
  • British Medical Research Council staging: Stage 1 (alert, no deficits), Stage 2 (lethargy, focal deficits), Stage 3 (stupor/coma) — outcome strongly stage-dependent.
  • CSF: lymphocytic pleocytosis, high protein, very low glucose — classic 'cold' pattern.
  • Sensitivity of Xpert MTB/RIF Ultra higher than culture, but culture remains gold standard.

TREATMENT

Outline the standard antitubercular regimen and adjunctive steroid use.
Show model answer
  • Intensive phase × 2 months: isoniazid + rifampicin + pyrazinamide + ethambutol (RIPE) — high dose for CNS penetration (especially rifampicin per recent trials).
  • Continuation phase × 7–10 months: isoniazid + rifampicin.
  • WHO recommends total ≥ 9–12 months for TBM.
  • Adjunctive DEXAMETHASONE (Thwaites 2004 NEJM): improves survival in TBM regardless of HIV status — 0.4 mg/kg/day IV tapered over 6–8 weeks.
  • Pyridoxine to prevent isoniazid neuropathy.
  • Monitor for hepatotoxicity (especially rifampicin/INH/PZA), drug interactions, IRIS.
  • Manage hydrocephalus: VP shunt vs ETV depending on type (communicating vs obstructive).
Case 175 — Adolescent post-HSCT with progressive cognitive decline HARD
A 14-year-old, 6 months post-allogeneic HSCT for AML, on tacrolimus and mycophenolate for GvHD prophylaxis, develops 4 weeks of progressive cognitive decline, ataxia, right hemiparesis. MRI: bilateral, asymmetric, multifocal subcortical white-matter T2 hyperintensities with relative sparing of U-fibres; no enhancement; no mass effect. CSF JCV PCR positive.

DX

Diagnosis and confirmation method?
Show model answer
  • Progressive multifocal leukoencephalopathy (PML).
  • Caused by reactivation of latent JC virus (JCV, polyomavirus 2) in oligodendrocytes in immunocompromised hosts.
  • JCV PCR in CSF — high specificity but sensitivity ~ 70–90%; if negative and suspicion high, brain biopsy (oligodendroglial inclusions, lytic infection).
  • MRI typical: asymmetric multifocal T2 hyperintensities, subcortical predominantly with relative U-fibre sparing, no enhancement (enhancement may appear in immune reconstitution).
  • Differential: ADEM (more enhancement, deep grey involvement), MS (periventricular, OCBs in CSF), CMV ventriculitis (periventricular enhancement).

TREATMENT

What is the principal therapeutic strategy and risk of IRIS?
Show model answer
  • Reverse / reduce immunosuppression: taper tacrolimus and mycophenolate where possible; in HIV/AIDS, optimise ART.
  • No proven specific antiviral.
  • Investigational: pembrolizumab (anti-PD-1) — restores T cell function (case series), mirtazapine (5-HT2A blocker, reduces JCV entry), filgrastim (G-CSF for immune reconstitution).
  • IRIS — immune reconstitution inflammatory syndrome: when immunity restored, robust inflammatory response to JCV-infected cells can cause clinical worsening with new enhancement on MRI. Manage with steroids if severe.
  • Prognosis poor in many — mortality high without immune reconstitution.
  • Manage symptoms; involve palliative care early.
Case 176 — Infant with sudden lethargy and metabolic acidosis after illness HARD
A 6-month-old infant, previously well, presents with sudden onset lethargy, vomiting, and an episode of generalised tonic-clonic seizure during a viral URI 12 hours ago. ABG: pH 7.21, lactate 8, glucose 1.4 mmol/L (hypoglycaemia), small ketones in urine (insufficient for degree of hypoglycaemia). NH3 normal. Plasma carnitine low; acylcarnitine profile: elevated C8-C10 species (medium-chain).

DX

Diagnosis and the metabolic defect?
Show model answer
  • Medium-chain acyl-CoA dehydrogenase deficiency (MCADD).
  • Most common fatty acid oxidation disorder; autosomal recessive ACADM gene mutations.
  • Cannot oxidise medium-chain (C6-C10) fatty acids during fasting/illness → energy deficit + hypoketotic hypoglycaemia + accumulation of C8 (octanoylcarnitine) and C10 dicarboxylic acids.
  • Classic presentation: previously well infant 3 months–2 years, hypoglycaemic event during intercurrent illness or fasting, hypoketotic hypoglycaemia (CRITICAL CLUE — ketosis is INAPPROPRIATELY LOW for the hypoglycaemia), hepatomegaly, encephalopathy, rarely sudden death.
  • Newborn screening detects this; most countries now screen — this child's may have been missed.

TREATMENT

Acute and long-term management?
Show model answer
  • ACUTE: IV dextrose (D10) to maintain glucose > 4 mmol/L — provides alternative fuel and suppresses lipolysis.
  • L-carnitine 100 mg/kg/day if levels are low (controversial — may not change outcome).
  • Aggressive treatment of intercurrent illnesses with early IV glucose if oral intake compromised.
  • LONG-TERM: avoid prolonged fasting (sick-day rules: short fasting limits — 4 hours in infants, 8 hours in toddlers, 10–12 hours in school-age children).
  • Frequent feedings, complex carbohydrates at bedtime (uncooked cornstarch in older children).
  • Family education and emergency action plan; medical alert bracelet.
  • Other FAODs to know: VLCAD (long-chain), LCHAD (long-chain hydroxy with maternal HELLP risk), CPT1, CPT2, SCAD.
Case 177 — Schoolchild after ICU admission with persistent unresponsiveness HARD
A 7-year-old, 9 days post-arrest from in-hospital cardiac event. Has been off sedation for 36 hours, normothermic, normometabolic, normoxia, normocapnia. Now: GCS 3, no pupillary reflex (8 mm fixed bilaterally), no corneal, no oculocephalic, no oculovestibular (cold caloric), no gag, no cough. Spontaneous respirations absent. Apnoea test performed: pre-test PaCO₂ 38, after 8 min off vent PaCO₂ 64, pH 7.10 — no respiratory effort.

CRITERIA

List the prerequisites that must be met before pediatric brain-death examination.
Show model answer
  • 1. Identifiable cause of brain injury sufficient to explain irreversible loss of all brain function.
  • 2. Exclusion of reversible confounders:
  • - Hypotension (MAP > 5th percentile for age, on minimal vasopressors).
  • - Hypothermia (core T ≥ 35 °C — preferably 36 °C).
  • - Severe metabolic disturbance (electrolytes, glucose, pH within reasonable range).
  • - CNS depressant drugs cleared (especially barbiturates — need levels sub-therapeutic).
  • - Neuromuscular blockade reversed (train-of-four 4/4).
  • 3. Observation period: 24 h for term newborns–30 days; 12 h for > 30 days–18 years (per AAP/SCCM 2011).
  • 4. Two examinations by two different attending physicians, separated by the observation period.
  • 5. Apnoea test included in EACH examination.

DEFINITION

Define a positive apnoea test in paediatric brain-death determination.
Show model answer
  • Pre-test prerequisites: core T ≥ 35 °C, SBP > 5th percentile, euvolaemia, eucapnia (PaCO₂ 35–45), oxygenation (PaO₂ > 200 on FiO₂ 1.0 for 10 min before disconnection).
  • Procedure: disconnect from ventilator; apnoeic O₂ via T-piece into endotracheal tube; observe 5–10 min for spontaneous respirations.
  • POSITIVE test (apnoea consistent with brain death) requires ALL of:
  • 1. NO respiratory effort during the entire observation.
  • 2. Final ABG showing PaCO₂ ≥ 60 mm Hg AND ≥ 20 mm Hg above baseline.
  • 3. pH typically < 7.30.
  • If chronic CO₂ retainer, may need higher threshold (e.g., > 60 mm Hg above patient's baseline).
  • Abort and repeat if SBP drops > 20% from baseline, SpO₂ < 85%, or arrhythmia.

NEXT STEP

If apnoea test cannot be completed safely, what ancillary tests can be used?
Show model answer
  • Ancillary tests confirm absent cerebral blood flow when clinical or apnoea test is impossible.
  • Acceptable ancillary tests (per consensus):
  • - 4-vessel catheter cerebral angiography (gold standard but invasive).
  • - Radionuclide cerebral perfusion scintigraphy (Tc-99m HMPAO) — 'hollow skull' sign.
  • - CT angiography (CTA) with absence of intracranial filling at level of M4/A4 branches (criteria still being refined in paediatrics).
  • - Transcranial Doppler showing reverberating or absent diastolic flow (operator dependent).
  • EEG is no longer recommended as the sole ancillary test (can show electrocerebral silence with preserved brain function in deep barbiturate coma — gives false confirmation).
  • Ancillary tests do not replace clinical exam — they supplement when clinical exam can't be completed.
Case 178 — Adolescent on ECMO with new pupillary asymmetry HARD
A 15-year-old has been on veno-arterial ECMO for 8 days for fulminant viral myocarditis. Sedation has been minimised over the past 24 hours. Bedside exam: right pupil 6 mm fixed, left 3 mm reactive. No spontaneous movement of right side. INR 1.8 (heparin gtt running). Platelets 78. CT impossible because of transport risk; transcranial Doppler suddenly shows reduced right MCA diastolic flow with high pulsatility index.

DX

What are the most likely cerebrovascular complications on ECMO and which one fits here?
Show model answer
  • This presentation (sudden new fixed pupil + lateralising signs + reduced MCA diastolic flow with high PI) is most consistent with right intracranial haemorrhage (ICH) — likely right hemispheric large bleed causing herniation.
  • Differential: large ischaemic stroke (often left hemispheric with VA ECMO due to retrograde aortic flow — 'watershed' phenomenon), cerebral oedema with raised ICP.
  • ECMO neurologic complications: 10–20% of children on ECMO — ICH (especially neonates), ischaemic stroke (carotid cannulation, low-flow watershed), seizures, hypoxic-ischaemic encephalopathy.
  • VA ECMO predisposes to left-sided thromboembolic events (cardiac/aortic retrograde flow); VV ECMO higher risk of bleeding.

NEXT STEP

Immediate bedside management before any imaging?
Show model answer
  • 1. Optimise CPP at bedside: head midline, elevate HOB 30°, ensure adequate MAP, treat agitation/pain.
  • 2. Reduce anticoagulation as far as feasible: reduce or stop heparin; reverse with protamine if necessary (balance against ECMO circuit clotting); transfuse platelets to > 100, FFP/PCC to correct coagulopathy.
  • 3. Hyperosmolar therapy: 3% saline 3–5 mL/kg or mannitol 0.5–1 g/kg as bridge.
  • 4. Hyperventilate transiently (PaCO₂ 30–35) as bridge to imaging/intervention.
  • 5. Arrange URGENT CT head with appropriate sedation, paralysis, and intra-hospital transport precautions; many centres do bedside CT or portable CT on ECMO when available.
  • 6. Multidisciplinary discussion: neurosurgery for possible decompression, ECMO team for circuit management, family for prognosis.
Case 179 — Newborn with congenital heart failure and audible cranial bruit HARD
A 6-day-old term infant presents with progressive high-output congestive heart failure. On examination: a loud continuous cranial bruit audible over the anterior fontanelle. Echocardiogram: dilated right heart with elevated PVR and reverse diastolic flow in descending aorta. Brain MRI: large midline venous pouch with multiple feeding arteries from posterior cerebral and choroidal vessels.

DX

Diagnosis and the principal differential?
Show model answer
  • Vein of Galen aneurysmal malformation (VGAM).
  • Embryologic vascular malformation: persistence of median prosencephalic vein of Markowski with arteriovenous shunting from choroidal and posterior cerebral arteries.
  • Two main types: choroidal (multiple small feeders to wall of vein — most common in neonates) and mural (single large fistula).
  • Differential of cardiac failure + cranial bruit in neonate: arteriovenous fistula, dural sinus malformation.
  • Neonatal presentation: high-output cardiac failure, pulmonary hypertension; later infancy: macrocephaly + hydrocephalus + developmental delay; adolescents: headache + seizures.

TREATMENT

Management timing and approach?
Show model answer
  • 1) Acute stabilisation of cardiac failure: diuretics, afterload reduction, sometimes high-dose milrinone; supplemental O₂.
  • 2) Definitive treatment is staged endovascular embolisation — transarterial or transvenous; goal is gradual flow reduction.
  • 3) Timing: ideally deferred to age 4–6 months when feasible (better outcomes); urgent intervention in neonates with refractory cardiac failure despite medical management.
  • 4) Bicêtre score (Lasjaunias) guides decision-making: clinical scoring of cardiac, cerebral, hepatic, renal, respiratory function — informs whether intervention is appropriate vs supportive care alone.
  • 5) Surgical resection rarely possible — endovascular only.
  • 6) Long-term: monitor for hydrocephalus (often from progressive venous hypertension or compression of aqueduct); neurodevelopmental follow-up.
Case 180 — Adolescent with positional headache after minor trauma MOD
A 14-year-old developed daily severe headache 3 weeks ago after a minor fall during gymnastics. The headache is dramatically worse when upright (within 15 minutes of standing) and resolves when lying flat. Some posterior neck pain. MRI brain: diffuse smooth pachymeningeal enhancement, descent of cerebellar tonsils 4 mm below the foramen magnum, brain 'sagging' appearance.

DX

Diagnosis and the imaging hallmark?
Show model answer
  • Spontaneous intracranial hypotension (SIH) from CSF leak.
  • Postural headache (worse upright, better supine, classic feature) often follows trivial trauma or even no identifiable trigger.
  • MRI brain hallmarks (SEEPS mnemonic): Subdural fluid collections, Enhancement of pachymeninges (diffuse smooth), Engorgement of venous structures, Pituitary hyperaemia, Sagging of the brain (with tonsillar descent often mimicking Chiari I).
  • Diagnostic gold standard for localising leak: CT myelography or MR myelography; sometimes radionuclide cisternography.
  • Common leak sites in young patients: ventral dural tears (from disc osteophytes), nerve root sleeve cysts, CSF-venous fistulas.

TREATMENT

Stepwise management approach?
Show model answer
  • 1) Conservative: strict bed rest, hydration, caffeine, oral analgesics. Effective in < 30% of cases.
  • 2) Epidural blood patch (EBP) — autologous blood injected into epidural space — first-line intervention. Often performed lumbar level first, then targeted to identified leak if EBP fails.
  • 3) Targeted EBP at leak site (after imaging localisation) for refractory cases.
  • 4) Fibrin glue patch for refractory leaks.
  • 5) Surgical repair for persistent leak refractory to multiple patches, identified by myelography.
  • 6) Treat any secondary subdural haematoma carefully — do NOT drain first if SIH suspected; treat the leak and SDH often resolves spontaneously.
Case 181 — Obese adolescent with chronic daily headache and vision threat MOD
A 16-year-old BMI 35 girl presents with 3 months of daily headache, transient visual obscurations (greying for seconds when bending over), pulsatile tinnitus. Now: gradually decreasing peripheral vision over 2 weeks. Fundoscopy: bilateral severe papilloedema with cotton-wool spots and haemorrhages. Visual fields: enlarged blind spot and inferior arcuate defects, snellen acuity 20/40 OD, 20/60 OS. MRI/MRV: empty sella, prominent perioptic CSF, no thrombosis, normal ventricles. LP opening pressure 42 cmH₂O.

DX

Diagnosis and key red flag?
Show model answer
  • Idiopathic intracranial hypertension (IIH / pseudotumor cerebri) with VISION-THREATENING course.
  • Modified Dandy criteria for diagnosis: symptoms/signs of raised ICP only, no localising signs except CN VI palsy, awake/alert, normal CSF composition, normal imaging (no mass/ventriculomegaly/venous sinus thrombosis), elevated opening pressure (> 25 cm H2O adults, > 28 cm H2O in non-sedated obese children per Friedman criteria).
  • Red flag here: visual field loss with cotton-wool spots and decreased acuity = imminent permanent visual loss → emergency intervention required.

TREATMENT

Acute and definitive management?
Show model answer
  • Medical: acetazolamide (high-dose if vision threatened — up to 4 g/day in adults, weight-based in pediatrics); furosemide adjunct.
  • Weight loss is the only disease-modifying intervention for sustained remission (5–10% weight reduction often resolves disease).
  • Serial lumbar punctures for temporary relief while planning definitive intervention.
  • SURGICAL options for vision-threatening disease:
  • - Optic nerve sheath fenestration (ONSF) — preserves vision; less effective for headache.
  • - CSF diversion (VP shunt or LP shunt) — addresses both vision and headache; more shunt-related complications.
  • - Venous sinus stenting — for documented stenosis (especially transverse sinus); emerging option.
  • - In this patient with rapid visual decline: emergency ONSF or shunt within days — visual loss can become permanent.
  • Serial ophthalmology, visual fields, and OCT for surveillance throughout.
Case 182 — Toddler with witnessed cardiac arrest from drowning MOD
A 2-year-old recovered from a domestic pool. Submerged for ~5 minutes (witnessed by parents). CPR started by family immediately. EMS confirms ROSC after 8 minutes of resuscitation. On admission: GCS 6, intubated, normothermic, no spontaneous movement, sluggish pupils.

DX

What is the syndrome and what determines outcome?
Show model answer
  • Anoxic / hypoxic-ischaemic brain injury from drowning.
  • Outcome determinants (Quan et al., Wintemute):
  • - Submersion duration (single strongest predictor): < 5 min good outcomes likely; 5–10 min variable; > 25 min very poor.
  • - Water temperature: cold water (< 6 °C) protective due to diving reflex + cerebral metabolic suppression.
  • - Time to first CPR.
  • - Time to ROSC: > 25 min strongly predicts poor outcome.
  • - Initial pH and lactate; neurologic status at 24 hours.
  • - Pupillary reactivity at presentation.

TREATMENT

Targeted temperature management in pediatric post-cardiac arrest?
Show model answer
  • THAPCA-OH and THAPCA-IH trials (NEJM 2015, 2017): hypothermia 33 °C vs normothermia 36.8 °C, both followed by strict normothermia × 5 days total.
  • Neither showed survival-with-good-outcome benefit of 33 °C over 36.8 °C in pediatric out-of-hospital or in-hospital arrest.
  • BUT — both arms had aggressive fever AVOIDANCE — strongly suggests that the key is PREVENTING fever, not necessarily hypothermia.
  • Current guideline: choose 33 °C OR 36.8 °C × 48 h, then strict normothermia × 5 days total. Avoid fever (> 37.5 °C).
  • Adjunctive: cEEG monitoring (treat seizures), avoid hypoxia/hypotension, optimise CPP, minimise sedation post-cooling for neuroprognostication ≥ 72 h off sedation.
Case 183 — Adolescent with recurrent TIAs and unilateral arm numbness MOD
A 13-year-old with sickle cell disease HbSS has had 3 episodes of right-sided arm and face weakness with dysarthria, each lasting 30–45 minutes with full recovery, over 2 months. Most recent episode persisted for 6 hours. MRI/MRA: bilateral terminal ICA narrowing with collateral 'puff of smoke' vessels at the base of the brain. TCD time-averaged mean velocity in left MCA 220 cm/s (abnormal).

DX

Diagnosis and the underlying mechanism?
Show model answer
  • Sickle cell-associated moyamoya syndrome.
  • Moyamoya refers to progressive stenosis of bilateral distal ICAs/proximal MCA/ACA with collateral network development at base of brain ('moyamoya' = 'puff of smoke' in Japanese, describing angiographic appearance).
  • Pathophysiology: progressive smooth muscle hyperplasia in ICAs, induced by chronic vascular injury from sickling, hypoxia, repeated micro-occlusions.
  • Differential: idiopathic moyamoya DISEASE (often bilateral, RNF213 mutations especially in East Asian populations) vs moyamoya SYNDROME (secondary to underlying condition — SCD, NF1, Down syndrome, radiation-induced, autoimmune).
  • Suzuki staging: angiographic progression from stage 1 (ICA narrowing) → stage 6 (no major vessels, collaterals only).

TREATMENT

Combined management approach?
Show model answer
  • 1) Acute stroke management as usual (no tPA in pediatric SCD stroke — bleeding risk and lack of efficacy data).
  • 2) Reduce HbS < 30% via emergent EXCHANGE transfusion; maintain HbS < 30% chronically with regular exchange or simple transfusion.
  • 3) Antiplatelet aspirin 1–5 mg/kg/day.
  • 4) Optimise hydration; avoid hypotension and dehydration; treat anaemia.
  • 5) Surgical revascularisation: indirect (encephaloduroarteriosynangiosis EDAS, pial synangiosis) or direct (STA-MCA bypass — limited by pediatric arterial size) — definitive treatment to develop new pial collaterals.
  • 6) Bone marrow transplant: curative for SCD; may halt moyamoya progression if performed before severe vasculopathy.
  • 7) Pre-, intra-, peri-operative care critical: HbS < 30%, avoid hypotension, normothermia, normocapnia.
Case 184 — Schoolchild with progressive ataxia after viral illness MOD
A 5-year-old developed varicella infection 10 days ago (now resolving). Today: acute onset truncal ataxia, gait widening, mild dysarthria, intention tremor, but normal mental status, no fever, no headache. MRI: subtle T2 hyperintensity in the cerebellar hemispheres bilaterally; normal otherwise.

DX

Diagnosis and prognosis?
Show model answer
  • Acute cerebellitis (post-infectious cerebellar ataxia).
  • Most common cause of acute ataxia in children < 5 years.
  • Often follows varicella (most classic), but also EBV, mycoplasma, influenza, COVID-19, post-vaccination.
  • Typically MONOPHASIC — recovery over weeks to months.
  • Differential to exclude: posterior fossa tumour, drug intoxication, ADEM, mass lesion with herniation, opsoclonus-myoclonus, GBS variant (MFS).

NEXT STEP

Management approach?
Show model answer
  • Most cases: supportive only — physical therapy, mobility aids during recovery.
  • Severe cases: high-dose IV methylprednisolone (consider in severe disability, atypical features, slow recovery, or significant brainstem involvement).
  • IVIG for atypical or severe cases.
  • Investigations: MRI brain (rule out tumour, ADEM), CSF (mild pleocytosis acceptable), urine drug screen (especially in pre-school ages — toxic ingestion can mimic), viral serology if guided by clinical context.
  • Most children recover fully within 4–8 weeks; persistent ataxia > 3 months warrants more extensive workup.
Case 185 — Adolescent with painless rapidly progressive visual loss bilaterally HARD
A 16-year-old boy presents with painless central vision loss in the right eye over 2 weeks, then identical loss in the left eye 6 weeks later. No pain on eye movement. Fundoscopy: relatively normal optic discs initially, later mild pseudoedema and then optic atrophy. No other neurological symptoms. Maternal uncle had similar bilateral vision loss as a young adult.

DX

Diagnosis and the inheritance pattern?
Show model answer
  • Leber hereditary optic neuropathy (LHON).
  • Mitochondrial disease — three primary mtDNA mutations: m.11778G>A (most common, ~50%), m.14484T>C, m.3460G>A. All affect complex I.
  • MATERNAL inheritance (mitochondrial); heteroplasmy and incomplete penetrance — only some carriers develop the disease.
  • STRONG male predominance (~ 80–90% of affected; modifier loci on X chromosome implicated).
  • Classic presentation: subacute painless central vision loss, sequential eyes (weeks-months apart), typically in adolescence/young adulthood.
  • Differential: bilateral optic neuritis (painful, demyelinating workup), nutritional/toxic optic neuropathy (methanol, ethambutol), other hereditary optic neuropathies.

TREATMENT

Therapeutic options and counselling?
Show model answer
  • Idebenone (synthetic ubiquinone analogue) — approved in Europe (Raxone); evidence for partial improvement, particularly with the m.11778 mutation.
  • Gene therapy (lenadogene nolparvovec, AAV-delivered ND4 in subretinal injection) — in clinical trials.
  • AVOID smoking, alcohol, mitotoxic medications (linezolid, aminoglycosides, certain antiretrovirals).
  • Coenzyme Q10, vitamin supplementation often advised though evidence weak.
  • Counselling: maternal-line relatives at risk; visual rehabilitation, low-vision support; many patients learn to function with partial residual vision (especially peripheral).
Case 186 — Infant with sudden severe head asymmetry and lethargy HARD
A 4-month-old previously well infant is found unresponsive in his crib. CT: large right-sided subdural haematoma with midline shift, multiple retinal haemorrhages on fundoscopy, no external injury except a small bruise on left ear. Skeletal survey: posterior rib fractures of different ages, healing right tibial corner fracture. Coagulation screen normal. Parents report a fall from a low couch 2 days ago.

DX

Diagnosis and the constellation of findings?
Show model answer
  • Abusive head trauma (AHT) — specifically the triad of subdural haematomas + retinal haemorrhages + encephalopathy with disproportionate injury severity for reported mechanism.
  • Posterior rib fractures (especially when bilateral) and corner / metaphyseal lesions are highly specific for inflicted injury — they are uncommon in accidental trauma at this age.
  • Stated mechanism (low couch fall) is incompatible with the severity of injury — a major red flag.
  • This is a CHILD-PROTECTION emergency requiring statutory reporting in all jurisdictions.

NEXT STEP

Comprehensive workup and reporting?
Show model answer
  • Stabilise child first (airway, ICP management, neurosurgical evaluation).
  • Imaging: skeletal survey (& repeat at 2 weeks for healing fractures), MRI brain + cervical spine (cervical injury, retinoschisis on MRI of optic nerves), dilated fundoscopy by ophthalmology (multilayered retinal haemorrhages extending to ora serrata = highly specific).
  • Bloods: coagulation, CBC, platelet function, urine organic acids (rule out glutaric aciduria type 1 which can mimic with subdural collections), copper/ceruloplasmin (Menkes disease).
  • Mandatory CHILD-PROTECTION REPORT to local authorities.
  • Social work, hospital child-protection team, multidisciplinary case review.
  • Sibling safety assessment.
  • Document findings meticulously; consider photographic documentation; preserve specimens.

DEFINITION

What is glutaric aciduria type 1 and why is it a critical mimic?
Show model answer
  • Autosomal recessive deficiency of glutaryl-CoA dehydrogenase.
  • Accumulation of glutaric and 3-hydroxyglutaric acids — neurotoxic to striatum.
  • Phenotype: macrocephaly, characteristic frontotemporal atrophy with widened opercular cisterns ('bat-wing' appearance), subdural collections (from bridging vein stretching across enlarged cisterns), acute striatal injury during catabolic stress.
  • The subdural collections in GA1 CAN BE SPONTANEOUS — important AHT mimicker.
  • Distinguishing: characteristic MRI brain findings (bat-wing), elevated urinary glutaric/3-hydroxyglutaric acids, plasma acylcarnitine showing C5DC.
  • Treat with low-lysine diet + L-carnitine; aggressive emergency protocols during illness.
  • This is the reason urine organic acids should be part of AHT workup — to avoid wrongly accusing parents.
Case 187 — Adolescent with progressive headache and worsening papilloedema during chemo MOD
A 12-year-old with ALL on induction chemotherapy (including L-asparaginase 5 doses ago) develops new-onset severe headache, photophobia, projectile vomiting, and bilateral 6th nerve palsies over 3 days. MRI/MRV: thrombosis of superior sagittal sinus + bilateral transverse sinuses; small cortical venous infarcts.

DX

Diagnosis and the chemotherapy mechanism?
Show model answer
  • Cerebral venous sinus thrombosis (CVST) related to L-asparaginase therapy.
  • L-asparaginase depletes asparagine — necessary for normal hepatic synthesis of many proteins, including anticoagulants (antithrombin III, protein C, protein S) and procoagulants (fibrinogen, plasminogen).
  • Net effect = hypercoagulable state (especially when antithrombin drops disproportionately).
  • Incidence of thrombosis with L-asparaginase: 1–5% (peak in adolescents on dose-intensive protocols).
  • Other thrombotic risks during ALL therapy: central lines, asparaginase, dexamethasone, immobility.

TREATMENT

Anticoagulation despite ongoing chemotherapy?
Show model answer
  • Anticoagulate with LMWH (enoxaparin therapeutic dose) for at least 3 months — propagation/extension risk outweighs bleeding risk even with thrombocytopenia from chemo.
  • If thrombocytopenia < 50, transfuse to keep platelets ≥ 30–50 while anticoagulated; pause LMWH if platelets < 20.
  • Consider AT III replacement (FFP or AT concentrate) during asparaginase therapy to maintain levels.
  • Continue asparaginase if essential for cure unless severe complications — discuss with oncology.
  • Treat raised ICP, seizures (high seizure rate with cortical CVST).
  • Repeat MRV at 3 months; longer anticoagulation if persistent occlusion or new events.
Case 188 — Schoolchild with strep infection followed by jerky movements MOD
An 11-year-old previously well girl developed sudden-onset irregular jerking movements of arms, face, and tongue over 3 weeks. Movements are non-rhythmic, flow into one another, worsen with stress, disappear in sleep. She also has new emotional lability, obsessive behaviour, and a faint mid-systolic murmur. ASO 850, anti-DNase B elevated. Echocardiogram: mild mitral regurgitation.

DX

Diagnosis and the broader syndrome it belongs to?
Show model answer
  • Sydenham chorea — a major manifestation of acute rheumatic fever (ARF).
  • Antibodies generated against group A Streptococcus cross-react with basal ganglia neurons (molecular mimicry).
  • Triad to remember in Sydenham chorea: motor (chorea), psychiatric (OCD, lability, anxiety), exam (chorea, hung-up reflexes, milkmaid grip, hypotonia).
  • Falls under PANDAS spectrum but is its own well-defined entity.
  • Major Jones criteria for ARF: carditis, polyarthritis, chorea, erythema marginatum, subcutaneous nodules.

CRITERIA

List the modified Jones criteria for ARF.
Show model answer
  • Evidence of preceding GAS infection (positive throat culture, rapid antigen test, elevated/rising ASO or anti-DNase B titre).
  • PLUS:
  • • 2 major criteria, OR
  • • 1 major + 2 minor.
  • MAJOR (revised 2015 for low-incidence vs moderate/high-incidence populations): carditis (subclinical OK), arthritis (or monoarthritis in high-prevalence settings), chorea, erythema marginatum, subcutaneous nodules.
  • MINOR: arthralgia, fever ≥ 38.5 °C (or 38 in high prevalence), ESR or CRP raised, prolonged PR interval (in absence of carditis as major).
  • Chorea is sufficient alone to make ARF diagnosis (does NOT need other criteria) when other causes excluded.

TREATMENT

Acute and long-term treatment plan?
Show model answer
  • Treat acute GAS: penicillin V or amoxicillin × 10 days (even if no longer culture-positive).
  • Symptomatic chorea: valproate (avoid if liver concerns), carbamazepine, haloperidol or risperidone (use cautiously — extrapyramidal side effects).
  • Steroids / IVIG / PLEX for severe disabling chorea or pediatric autoimmune neuropsychiatric features — used selectively.
  • Carditis: bed rest, anti-inflammatories, treat heart failure if present.
  • Long-term: SECONDARY PROPHYLAXIS with benzathine penicillin G every 4 weeks (or oral penicillin V daily) for years — duration depends on severity (chorea alone — 5 years or until 21; carditis with valvulopathy — until 40 or longer).
  • Echo surveillance for valvular sequelae.
Case 189 — Toddler with port-wine birthmark and status epilepticus HARD
