A 3-year-old has a large port-wine stain involving the right forehead, eyelid, and cheek (V1 + V2 trigeminal distribution). Today she presents in convulsive status epilepticus with left-sided focal motor seizures. CT head: extensive right hemispheric atrophy with gyriform cortical calcifications.

DX

Diagnosis and the genetic basis?
Show model answer
  • Sturge-Weber syndrome (encephalotrigeminal angiomatosis).
  • Caused by somatic mosaic mutations in GNAQ (R183Q most common) — postzygotic — explains segmental distribution.
  • Triad: facial port-wine stain (V1 distribution; V1 involvement is the critical risk factor for neurological/ophthalmologic complications), leptomeningeal angiomatosis (often unilateral, posterior parietal-occipital), glaucoma.
  • Brain: leptomeningeal angiomatosis → chronic venous stasis → cortical hypoperfusion → progressive cortical atrophy with characteristic gyriform 'tram-track' calcifications and gliosis.
  • Seizures often start in infancy; ipsilateral hemiparesis often progressive.

TREATMENT

Acute SE and long-term management?
Show model answer
  • Acute SE: standard pediatric SE algorithm (benzodiazepine → fosphenytoin/LEV/VPA → midazolam infusion → pentobarbital if super-refractory).
  • Aggressive antipyretic management — fever worsens SWS strokes.
  • LOW-DOSE ASPIRIN (3–5 mg/kg/day) chronically — reduces stroke-like episodes by mitigating venous stasis thrombosis (off-label but standard of care).
  • Ensure adequate hydration, avoid hypotension and dehydration (precipitate strokes).
  • Long-term seizure management with AEDs — often refractory.
  • Hemispherectomy (functional or anatomical) for medically refractory focal epilepsy with established hemiparesis — can be transformative for seizure control and cognitive trajectory.
  • Ophthalmology: lifelong glaucoma surveillance.
  • Cosmetic: pulsed dye laser for port-wine stain.
Case 190 — Schoolchild with progressive weakness of mouth-opening HARD
A 9-year-old has 4 weeks of trismus, dysarthria, dysphagia. No fever. No trauma. Lumbar puncture normal. Now: prominent ophthalmoplegia, ptosis, increasing respiratory weakness, and finally generalised weakness. CK normal. EMG: incremental response to repetitive nerve stimulation at HIGH frequencies (50 Hz). Anti-VGCC antibody pending.

DX

Diagnosis and the pathophysiology?
Show model answer
  • Lambert-Eaton myasthenic syndrome (LEMS).
  • Antibodies target presynaptic P/Q-type voltage-gated calcium channels (VGCC) on motor nerve terminals → reduced calcium influx during depolarisation → reduced acetylcholine release.
  • Hallmark: weakness that IMPROVES with sustained or repeated effort (in contrast to MG, where weakness worsens with effort).
  • EMG: low CMAP amplitude at baseline that INCREASES (decrement-increment pattern) with high-frequency RNS (10–50 Hz) or after sustained voluntary contraction (post-exercise facilitation > 100%).
  • Autonomic features common: dry mouth, constipation, impotence (uncommon in young children).
  • In adults: ~ 50% paraneoplastic (SCLC); pediatric LEMS rarely paraneoplastic.

TREATMENT

Symptomatic and immunomodulatory treatment?
Show model answer
  • Symptomatic: 3,4-diaminopyridine (amifampridine, FDA-approved) — blocks presynaptic K channels, prolongs depolarisation, increases ACh release.
  • Pyridostigmine adjunctive (less effective alone than in MG).
  • Immunomodulation if symptoms severe or refractory: prednisone, azathioprine, mycophenolate, rituximab.
  • IVIG or plasma exchange for acute exacerbations or pre-surgical preparation.
  • Search for underlying tumour (rare in children but neuroblastoma, lymphoma reported).
  • Long-term follow-up; cancer surveillance in adolescents.
Case 191 — Adolescent with rapidly progressive hyperkinesis after starting antipsychotic MOD
A 14-year-old psychiatric inpatient was started on haloperidol 2 mg orally last night. This morning she has acute torticollis, oculogyric crisis (eyes deviated upward and stuck), trismus, dysarthria, anxiety. No fever, no rigidity, vital signs stable.

DX

Diagnosis and the typical timing?
Show model answer
  • Acute dystonic reaction (drug-induced dystonia).
  • Caused by acute D2 dopamine receptor blockade with consequent striatal cholinergic excess.
  • Typically occurs within hours to days of initiation of dopamine antagonist (typical antipsychotic, metoclopramide, prochlorperazine).
  • Pediatric/adolescent males at higher risk than adult women.
  • Manifestations: torticollis, oculogyric crisis, trismus, laryngospasm, opisthotonus.
  • Distinguish from neuroleptic malignant syndrome (NMS) — NMS has hyperthermia, rigidity, autonomic instability, hours-days to weeks course, very different management.

TREATMENT

Acute treatment?
Show model answer
  • IV / IM ANTICHOLINERGIC — first-line: benztropine 1–2 mg IV, OR diphenhydramine 1 mg/kg IV (max 50 mg).
  • Response typically within 5–20 minutes — diagnostic and therapeutic.
  • Continue oral anticholinergic (benztropine or trihexyphenidyl) for 24–48 hours to prevent recurrence.
  • Stop or reduce offending agent; consider switch to atypical antipsychotic (lower EPS risk).
  • Reassure patient — frightening but not life-threatening usually (unless laryngospasm).
  • Document the reaction; avoid re-exposure to same agent.
Case 192 — Immunocompromised teen with chronic headache and slowly progressive confusion HARD
A 15-year-old, on chronic prednisone for SLE flare, develops 4 weeks of progressive daily headache, low-grade fevers, mental status changes. LP opening pressure 38 cmH₂O. CSF: WBC 25 (lymphs), protein 0.7, glucose 1.8. India-ink positive; cryptococcal antigen titre 1:1024 in CSF and serum.

DX

Diagnosis and the principal pathogen subtypes?
Show model answer
  • Cryptococcal meningitis — yeast infection of CNS.
  • Two main species: Cryptococcus neoformans (worldwide, immunocompromised especially HIV/AIDS, transplant, steroids); Cryptococcus gattii (tropical/subtropical, can affect immunocompetent hosts, often pulmonary nodules and cryptococcomas).
  • Pediatric presentation often subacute/chronic with raised ICP and meningeal signs.
  • Diagnosis: India-ink, CSF cryptococcal antigen (lateral flow assay), CSF culture (gold standard).
  • High opening pressure is a critical complication — drives morbidity/mortality.

TREATMENT

Outline the three-phase antifungal regimen and ICP management.
Show model answer
  • INDUCTION (≥ 2 weeks): liposomal amphotericin B 3–6 mg/kg/day + flucytosine 100 mg/kg/day. Combination superior to monotherapy.
  • CONSOLIDATION (≥ 8 weeks): fluconazole 10–12 mg/kg/day.
  • MAINTENANCE: fluconazole 6 mg/kg/day for ≥ 1 year (or until immune recovery / CD4 > 200 sustained).
  • ICP management is CRITICAL: serial therapeutic LPs to remove CSF (target opening pressure < 20 cmH₂O); lumbar drain if recurrent; rarely VP shunt for refractory.
  • DO NOT use corticosteroids routinely (worsen outcomes).
  • Investigate underlying immunocompromise; restore immune function where possible.
Case 193 — Pre-teen with rapidly progressive coma after travel to endemic area HARD
A 10-year-old child returns from a 4-week visit to sub-Saharan Africa. Three days after return: high fever, headache, vomiting. Within 24 hours: declining LOC to GCS 7, intermittent decorticate posturing, brief generalised tonic-clonic seizures, retinal haemorrhages on fundoscopy. Blood smear: ring forms with 4% parasitaemia.

DX

Diagnosis and key prognostic features?
Show model answer
  • Cerebral malaria from Plasmodium falciparum.
  • WHO criteria for severe malaria include: impaired consciousness (GCS < 11), prostration, multiple seizures, acidosis, hypoglycaemia, severe anaemia, renal impairment, jaundice, pulmonary oedema, abnormal bleeding, shock, hyperparasitaemia (≥ 10%).
  • Malarial retinopathy (haemorrhages, vessel changes, retinal whitening) is highly specific — distinguishes malaria coma from other causes.
  • Pathophysiology: parasitised erythrocytes sequester in cerebral microvasculature → sequestration, cytoadherence, endothelial dysfunction, blood-brain barrier disruption.
  • Mortality 15–25% in African children even with treatment; long-term neurological sequelae in ~ 25% of survivors.

TREATMENT

First-line antimalarial and adjunctive ICU care?
Show model answer
  • IV ARTESUNATE 2.4 mg/kg at 0, 12, 24 hours then once daily — first-line for severe malaria (superior to quinine per SEAQUAMAT and AQUAMAT trials).
  • Once stable and able to tolerate orally: complete with 3-day artemisinin-based combination therapy (ACT).
  • Watch for post-artesunate delayed haemolysis (1–3 weeks after treatment).
  • ICU support: airway and ventilation, glucose monitoring and replacement (hypoglycaemia common), seizure management with benzodiazepines (avoid phenobarbital prophylaxis — increased mortality), transfusion if severe anaemia.
  • AVOID over-hydration (risk of fluid overload, pulmonary oedema); FEAST trial showed bolus fluids worsen outcomes in African children with severe sepsis/malaria.
  • Treat any superinfections.
  • Do NOT use corticosteroids (no benefit, possibly harmful).
Case 194 — Toddler with constipation, weak cry, and progressive flaccid weakness HARD
A 5-month-old infant who has been fed honey by grandmother for 3 weeks presents with 4 days of poor feeding, weak cry, constipation, then descending flaccid weakness: ptosis, dilated pupils, weak head control, decreased gag, progressive respiratory weakness. Stool sample sent. Tone severely decreased; deep tendon reflexes diminished.

DX

Diagnosis and pathogenic mechanism?
Show model answer
  • Infant botulism (Clostridium botulinum).
  • Pathogenesis: ingestion of C. botulinum SPORES (not pre-formed toxin) → colonisation of infant intestine (immature flora) → in vivo toxin production → systemic absorption.
  • Honey (and corn syrup, environmental dust) are recognised spore sources — avoid honey in infants < 12 months.
  • Toxin cleaves SNARE proteins (SNAP-25, syntaxin, synaptobrevin depending on serotype) at presynaptic motor terminals → blocks ACh release → flaccid paralysis.
  • Classic infant presentation: 'floppy infant' with constipation, weak cry, poor feeding, hypotonia, descending paralysis, ptosis, autonomic features (dilated sluggish pupils, dry mouth, decreased gut motility).

TREATMENT

Specific antitoxin and supportive care?
Show model answer
  • Human-derived botulism immune globulin INTRAVENOUS (BabyBIG) — single infusion specifically licensed for infant botulism (< 1 year).
  • Reduces hospital stay by ~ 3 weeks if given early.
  • EQUINE antitoxin (HBAT — heptavalent botulism antitoxin) is for foodborne and wound botulism in OLDER patients (not first-line in infants — risk of serum sickness/anaphylaxis).
  • DO NOT use antibiotics routinely (release of more toxin from lysed bacteria); aminoglycosides specifically contraindicated (potentiate neuromuscular blockade).
  • Supportive care: meticulous airway management, mechanical ventilation if respiratory failure, NG/PEG feeding, prevention of complications during the often prolonged paralysis (weeks-months).
  • Full recovery is the rule with optimal supportive care.
Case 195 — Teen with seizures and lethargy after suicide attempt HARD
A 16-year-old presents 6 hours after intentionally ingesting ~ 100 tablets of aspirin (about 30 g). On arrival: confused, hyperventilating (RR 38), nausea, tinnitus. ABG: pH 7.41, PaCO₂ 18, HCO₃ 13 — mixed primary respiratory alkalosis + primary metabolic acidosis with elevated anion gap. Salicylate 75 mg/dL (toxic).

DX

Explain the mixed acid-base picture and identify the diagnosis.
Show model answer
  • Salicylate toxicity.
  • Salicylate directly stimulates brainstem respiratory centre → primary respiratory ALKALOSIS (PaCO₂ ↓).
  • Simultaneously, salicylate uncouples oxidative phosphorylation → increased anaerobic metabolism + accumulation of ketoacids/lactate → primary metabolic ACIDOSIS with elevated anion gap.
  • Net pH may be near-normal early (mixed picture), or alkalemic, or acidemic depending on relative magnitudes.
  • In CHILDREN: tend to develop metabolic acidosis predominance more quickly; adults more often have prolonged respiratory alkalosis phase.
  • Toxicity stages: tinnitus, nausea, hyperventilation → confusion, hyperthermia → coma, seizures, cerebral oedema, pulmonary oedema, death.

TREATMENT

Key management priorities including the critical pH target?
Show model answer
  • 1) Aggressive ALKALINISATION with IV sodium bicarbonate — TARGET SERUM pH ≥ 7.45 to keep salicylate ionised in blood (less crossing into CNS) and TARGET URINE pH ≥ 7.5 to enhance renal excretion (5-fold increase in clearance per unit pH rise).
  • 2) Replete K (alkalinisation requires adequate K to maintain urine alkalinisation — hypokalaemia thwarts the strategy).
  • 3) Glucose-containing fluids (D5W with bicarbonate) — neuroglycopenia common even with normal serum glucose; CNS glucose depleted.
  • 4) HEMODIALYSIS for severe toxicity — indications: level > 100 mg/dL acute (or > 60 chronic), severe acidosis (pH < 7.2 or worsening), AMS, pulmonary/cerebral oedema, renal failure, refractory to alkalinisation.
  • 5) DO NOT INTUBATE casually — patient's compensatory hyperventilation is protective; iatrogenic hypoventilation (with sedation/paralysis) causes catastrophic acidemia and rapid death. If you MUST intubate, match the minute ventilation aggressively and pre-treat with bicarbonate.
  • 6) Activated charcoal if early presentation (< 2 hours).

PHYSIOLOGY

Why does CSF salicylate distribution matter and what is the 'ion trapping' principle?
Show model answer
  • Salicylate exists in ionised (anionic, water-soluble) and non-ionised (protonated, lipid-soluble) forms.
  • Only the non-ionised form crosses the blood-brain barrier and cell membranes.
  • Lower blood pH (acidemia) → more salicylate is protonated (non-ionised) → more crosses into CNS → more brain toxicity.
  • Higher blood pH → more ionised in blood, less crossing into CNS = neuroprotective.
  • Conversely in urine: higher urine pH → salicylate ionised once excreted → cannot be reabsorbed by tubule → enhanced elimination.
  • This is why ALKALINISATION (raising serum AND urine pH) is the therapeutic cornerstone — same principle ('ion trapping') applies to phenobarbital, methotrexate.
Case 196 — Family with multiple sick members after barbecue HARD
A 12-year-old and his 5-year-old sibling both present with headache, nausea, dizziness, weakness, and confusion after spending the night in a cabin heated by a poorly-maintained gas heater. The infant in the family is also lethargic. Pulse oximetry 100% on RA in all. SpO₂ does NOT improve with O₂ in proportion to expected from clinical picture.

DX

Most likely diagnosis and why pulse oximetry is misleading?
Show model answer
  • Carbon monoxide (CO) poisoning — multiple family members with similar symptoms after exposure to a combustion source = CO until proven otherwise.
  • Carboxyhaemoglobin (COHb) — CO binds Hb with ~ 250× the affinity of O₂ → displaces O₂ from Hb (reducing carrying capacity) AND shifts the O₂-Hb dissociation curve to the LEFT (reduces O₂ unloading at tissues).
  • Pulse oximetry CANNOT distinguish COHb from O₂Hb — both absorb similar wavelengths — so SpO₂ reads falsely normal.
  • Need CO-oximetry (multi-wavelength) to measure COHb directly — arterial or venous sample.
  • Severity: < 10% (often asymptomatic in non-smokers), 10–20% (mild flu-like), 20–30% (headache, confusion), 30–50% (severe — seizures, coma), > 50% (often fatal).

TREATMENT

Indications for hyperbaric oxygen and why?
Show model answer
  • Immediately: 100% O₂ via tight-fitting non-rebreather mask or ETT — accelerates COHb dissociation.
  • Half-life of COHb: ~ 4–5 h on room air; ~ 80 min on 100% normobaric O₂; ~ 20–30 min on hyperbaric O₂ at 2.4–3 ATA.
  • Hyperbaric O₂ (HBO) indicated for: loss of consciousness (any duration), severe neurological deficits, seizures, cardiac ischaemia, severe metabolic acidosis, COHb > 25% (some sources > 25%, others > 40%), pregnancy with COHb > 15%, or persistent symptoms despite normobaric O₂.
  • Continue 100% O₂ until COHb < 5% AND symptoms have resolved.
  • Watch for delayed neuropsychiatric sequelae (DNS) — appear 2–40 days after exposure with cognitive, personality, movement abnormalities. HBO may reduce DNS incidence.
  • Test all exposed individuals; investigate and remediate source.
Case 197 — Adolescent intoxicated with multiple toxidromes HARD
A 16-year-old is found unconscious at a party. Pinpoint pupils, RR 6, hypotonia, hypotension. Naloxone administered with partial response — RR increases to 12 but patient remains drowsy. Later he develops fluctuating mentation. ECG: QTc 510 ms. Friends mention 'somebody gave him several pills'.

NEXT STEP

Approach the multi-drug overdose systematically.
Show model answer
  • 1) ABCs: secure airway if GCS unable to protect, support ventilation, IV access, monitor.
  • 2) Universal antidotes / cocktails: dextrose for hypoglycaemia, thiamine pre-glucose if malnourished/EtOH history, NALOXONE titrated to respiratory effort (partial response in this case suggests opioid + something else).
  • 3) Toxidrome assessment after stabilisation: pupils, vital signs, mental status, skin, bowel sounds, reflexes.
  • 4) Send: comprehensive urine drug screen (limited, miss many drugs); paracetamol level (CRITICAL — silent killer); salicylate level; glucose; electrolytes (including Ca, Mg, anion gap); osmolality (compare with calculated for toxic alcohol screen); ABG; ECG (QTc, QRS for Na channel blockers, terminal R aVR).
  • 5) Activated charcoal only if < 1 h since ingestion AND airway protected AND useful agent.
  • 6) Consult toxicology / poison centre early.

DX

What is the prolonged QTc telling you and which agents to consider?
Show model answer
  • Prolonged QTc > 500 ms = high risk for torsades de pointes — needs immediate attention.
  • Drugs that prolong QTc: methadone, antipsychotics (haloperidol, quetiapine, risperidone, olanzapine), antidepressants (SSRIs, especially citalopram; TCAs), antiemetics (ondansetron — especially IV high dose), antibiotics (macrolides, fluoroquinolones), antihistamines (older), antimalarials.
  • Methadone is particularly concerning given the partial naloxone response AND opioid features — methadone is long-acting (t½ 24–36 h), causes prolonged respiratory depression refractory to single naloxone doses, AND prolongs QTc.
  • Continuous naloxone infusion may be needed for long-acting opioids (methadone, sustained-release oxycodone).
  • Correct hypokalaemia, hypomagnesaemia; consider IV magnesium for prophylaxis or torsades.
Case 198 — Teen with seizures and CN deficits after smoke inhalation HARD
A 14-year-old rescued from a house fire. On arrival: soot around face and mouth, hoarse voice, expired CO 10%. Initial COHb 35%. Despite 100% O₂ and partial improvement of COHb, the patient remains comatose with seizures, severe lactic acidosis (lactate 14), and 'pink' skin colour without cyanosis even with desaturation.

DX

Second diagnosis to consider and the metabolic clue?
Show model answer
  • Concurrent CYANIDE poisoning from inhaled smoke (combustion of plastics, wool, polyurethane releases hydrogen cyanide).
  • Cyanide blocks cytochrome oxidase (complex IV) → cellular hypoxia → anaerobic metabolism → severe lactic acidosis.
  • Classic clues: severe lactic acidosis disproportionate to other findings, narrow A-V O₂ difference (tissues can't extract O₂), 'pink' skin colour from saturated venous blood, neurological + cardiovascular collapse, smell of bitter almonds (only 40% of population can detect).
  • Always co-suspect cyanide in fire victims with severe acidosis + altered mental status, especially when CO levels do not match severity of illness.

TREATMENT

Specific antidotes and their mechanisms?
Show model answer
  • HYDROXOCOBALAMIN (Cyanokit) 70 mg/kg IV — first-line in pediatric fire victims.
  • Mechanism: binds cyanide directly forming cyanocobalamin (vitamin B12), excreted in urine. Safe even when cyanide poisoning uncertain (doesn't reduce O₂ carrying capacity, doesn't cause hypotension, can be used in CO co-poisoning).
  • Side effects: red/orange discolouration of urine and skin (cosmetic), transient hypertension, may interfere with lab tests.
  • ALTERNATIVE/older — sodium thiosulfate (provides sulfur for rhodanese to convert cyanide to thiocyanate, excreted renally; slower onset).
  • AVOID nitrites (sodium nitrite, amyl nitrite) in fire victims with CO co-poisoning — induce methaemoglobinaemia which compounds the inability to carry oxygen on top of existing carboxyhaemoglobinaemia.
  • Continue supportive care: 100% O₂, treat acidosis, manage seizures, ICU support.
Case 199 — Child with old housing exposure and lead paint HARD
A 3-year-old from an older home (pre-1978 housing with peeling paint) presents with 2 weeks of irritability, anorexia, abdominal pain, constipation, and now a focal seizure. Blood lead level 95 µg/dL (very high). Hb 9.2 with basophilic stippling on smear. X-ray long bones: dense metaphyseal lines (lead lines).

DX

Diagnosis and the principal CNS pathology?
Show model answer
  • Lead encephalopathy (severe pediatric lead poisoning).
  • Blood lead level > 70 µg/dL with symptoms = lead encephalopathy in pediatrics.
  • Pathophysiology: lead disrupts heme synthesis (inhibits ALA dehydratase and ferrochelatase) → anaemia with basophilic stippling; competitive antagonism of calcium → multiple CNS effects (decreased BBB integrity, NMDA receptor disruption, decreased neurotransmitter release, mitochondrial dysfunction).
  • CNS: cerebral oedema (the principal acute danger), seizures, encephalopathy, raised ICP.
  • Chronic: developmental delay, behaviour problems, lower IQ — no 'safe' level of lead exposure in children.

TREATMENT

Outline the combined chelation regimen for severe encephalopathy.
Show model answer
  • COMBINATION chelation for symptomatic encephalopathy or BLL > 70 µg/dL:
  • 1) Dimercaprol (BAL) 75 mg/m² IM q4h (DEEP IM injection in peanut oil; do not use in peanut-allergic patients).
  • 2) BEGIN BAL FIRST. Then 4 HOURS LATER start CaNa₂EDTA 1500 mg/m²/day IV infusion (24-h infusion or split q4h doses).
  • 3) The 4-hour delay is critical — starting EDTA before BAL can paradoxically redistribute lead into brain (worsen encephalopathy).
  • 4) Continue combination for 5 days; reassess BLL.
  • 5) Then succimer (DMSA) oral 30 mg/kg/day × 5 days, then 20 mg/kg/day × 14 days; reassess.
  • 6) PICU admission MANDATORY for severe encephalopathy — ICP management, seizure control (avoid mannitol initially — fluid balance considerations).
  • 7) Identify and remediate lead source — child cannot return home until environment is decontaminated.
  • 8) Iron-deficiency commonly co-exists — replete iron (without giving more lead access).
  • 9) Report to public health.
Case 200 — Adolescent with severe abdominal pain and ataxia after recreational use MOD
A 17-year-old college student presents with 2 episodes of generalised seizures and gait ataxia. Friends report he has been chronically using 'whippits' (nitrous oxide canisters) for the past 6 months — recently up to 50 canisters per day. Examination: distal symmetric sensory loss in stocking-glove distribution, Romberg positive, hyperreflexia in legs with bilateral Babinski signs, megaloblastic anaemia on CBC.

DX

Diagnosis and the biochemical mechanism?
Show model answer
  • Nitrous oxide (N₂O) abuse-induced subacute combined degeneration of the spinal cord and peripheral neuropathy.
  • N₂O irreversibly oxidises the cobalt centre of vitamin B12 (cobalamin) from Co1+ to Co3+, inactivating it.
  • Functional B12 deficiency develops even with normal serum B12 levels initially.
  • B12-dependent reactions impaired: methylation of homocysteine to methionine (low methionine → demyelination of dorsal columns/lateral corticospinal tracts = SCD), conversion of methylmalonyl-CoA to succinyl-CoA (elevated methylmalonic acid).
  • Classic features: dorsal column dysfunction (loss of vibration/proprioception, Romberg), corticospinal involvement (spasticity, hyperreflexia, Babinski), peripheral neuropathy, megaloblastic anaemia.

TREATMENT

Management approach?
Show model answer
  • 1) Immediate STOP of nitrous oxide use — definitive.
  • 2) Parenteral vitamin B12 (cyanocobalamin or hydroxocobalamin) — 1000 µg IM daily for ~ 1 week, then weekly, then monthly. Some advocate higher initial doses and longer courses.
  • 3) Methionine and folate supplementation may help.
  • 4) Avoid further N₂O exposure (including medical/anaesthetic use; nitrous oxide is contraindicated in patients with B12 deficiency).
  • 5) Substance use counselling and psychiatry; underlying psychosocial factors.
  • 6) Investigate other reasons for B12 deficiency (pernicious anaemia, dietary, GI surgery).
  • 7) Neurological recovery variable — early treatment generally good outcome; delayed treatment may leave permanent deficits.
  • 8) Check homocysteine and methylmalonic acid as functional markers.
Case 201 — Young child with seizures and cherry-red macula MOD
A 14-month-old of Ashkenazi Jewish ancestry developed normally until 5 months, then regression: lost ability to sit, lost grasp, exaggerated startle to sound (hyperacusis), progressive weakness, blindness. Fundoscopy: bilateral cherry-red spot at the macula. Now seizures and progressive macrocephaly.

DX

Diagnosis and the enzyme deficient?
Show model answer
  • Tay-Sachs disease (GM2 gangliosidosis type B) — autosomal recessive.
  • β-Hexosaminidase A (HEXA gene) deficiency → accumulation of GM2 ganglioside in lysosomes of neurons → progressive neurodegeneration.
  • Ashkenazi Jewish, French-Canadian (Quebec), and Cajun populations have higher carrier frequencies.
  • Cherry-red spot at the macula = retinal ganglion cell accumulation around the fovea; foveola itself is spared (no ganglion cells there) so the choroidal red shows through against the otherwise pale retina.
  • Other lysosomal disorders with cherry-red spot: Sandhoff disease (HEXA+HEXB), Niemann-Pick A, sialidosis, GM1 gangliosidosis, metachromatic leukodystrophy.

TREATMENT

Therapy options?
Show model answer
  • No disease-modifying therapy currently approved.
  • Substrate reduction therapy (miglustat — glucosylceramide synthase inhibitor) tried with limited efficacy.
  • Gene therapy and enzyme replacement in clinical trials.
  • Mainstay: supportive care, palliative care, family support.
  • Seizure management often challenging — multiple AEDs needed.
  • Carrier screening for at-risk populations is standard preconception care (Ashkenazi panel testing).
  • Prenatal diagnosis via CVS or amniocentesis available for at-risk pregnancies.
  • Family genetic counselling — autosomal recessive, 25% recurrence; cascade testing of siblings/relatives.
Case 202 — Adolescent with unexplained subarachnoid blood and normal angiogram HARD
A 16-year-old presents with sudden thunderclap headache. Non-contrast CT: focal sulcal subarachnoid haemorrhage along the right frontal convexity only (NOT in basal cisterns). CT angiogram and 4-vessel catheter angiogram: NORMAL. Repeat angiogram at 1 week: now shows multifocal segmental narrowing of distal cerebral arteries.

DX

Diagnosis and the typical triggers?
Show model answer
  • Reversible cerebral vasoconstriction syndrome (RCVS).
  • Characterised by recurrent thunderclap headaches and reversible multifocal segmental cerebral artery vasoconstriction over 1–3 months.
  • Often manifests with convexity (cortical) SAH (rather than basal cistern, ruling out aneurysm).
  • Triggers: post-partum, exposure to vasoactive substances (SSRIs, triptans, ergots, nasal decongestants, cannabis, cocaine, amphetamines), Valsalva (sex, exercise, defecation, swimming).
  • Angiography may be normal initially — characteristic 'string of beads' pattern develops later (peak 2–3 weeks after onset).
  • Differential: ruptured aneurysm (basal SAH pattern), primary CNS vasculitis (more progressive, CSF abnormal, doesn't reverse).

TREATMENT

Management approach?
Show model answer
  • 1) Stop offending agents (vasoactive drugs).
  • 2) Calcium channel blockers — nimodipine 60 mg PO q4h (extrapolated from SAH evidence; some retrospective benefit in RCVS).
  • 3) Avoid triggers (Valsalva manoeuvres) during the active phase.
  • 4) Treat severe BP (controlled reduction to MAP within autoregulation range; avoid both extremes).
  • 5) Magnesium for refractory cases.
  • 6) Steroids generally NOT helpful (and may worsen).
  • 7) Headache management with analgesics, avoid triptans/ergots.
  • 8) Most cases resolve completely within 1–3 months.
  • 9) Repeat imaging (MRA, CTA) to confirm resolution.
  • 10) Counsel against future use of vasoactive substances.
Case 203 — Newborn with absent suck and lethargy in setting of maternal substance use MOD
A term newborn (mother used opioids and benzodiazepines throughout pregnancy) is initially well at birth but develops, over 24–72 hours: high-pitched cry, jitteriness, hypertonia, exaggerated Moro, poor sleep, poor feeding, nasal flaring, diarrhoea, frequent yawning and sneezing, low-grade fever, brief seizure.

DX

Diagnosis and the assessment scale?
Show model answer
  • Neonatal abstinence syndrome (NAS), now called Neonatal Opioid Withdrawal Syndrome (NOWS) — specifically opioid withdrawal with concurrent benzodiazepine component.
  • Onset: usually 24–72 hours after birth for short-acting opioids; can be delayed up to 5–10 days for methadone/buprenorphine.
  • Assessment: Finnegan Neonatal Abstinence Scoring System (or modified versions like the ESC — Eat, Sleep, Console — non-pharmacologic-focused alternative).
  • Finnegan score > 8 three consecutive scores (or > 12 two consecutive) typically triggers pharmacologic treatment.
  • Eat, Sleep, Console approach assesses functional outcomes; if infant can EAT > 1 oz/feed, SLEEP > 1 h, be CONSOLED within 10 min, non-pharmacologic care continues.

TREATMENT

Stepwise treatment approach?
Show model answer
  • 1) Non-pharmacologic FIRST: skin-to-skin, breastfeeding if appropriate (not contraindicated with methadone or buprenorphine if maternal HIV negative and not using illicit drugs concurrently), low stimulation environment, swaddling, rooming-in.
  • 2) Pharmacologic for refractory symptoms:
  • - First-line for opioid NAS: oral morphine OR methadone OR buprenorphine (newer evidence supports buprenorphine — BBORN trial showed shorter LOS).
  • - Adjunct for severe / polysubstance withdrawal: clonidine, phenobarbital (especially if benzodiazepine component).
  • - Wean slowly per protocol.
  • 3) Multidisciplinary follow-up: neonatology, developmental paediatrics, social work, addiction medicine for mother.
  • 4) Long-term neurodevelopmental surveillance — increased risk of behavioural, attention issues.
  • 5) Public health: reporting requirements vary by jurisdiction; focus on supporting maternal recovery while ensuring infant safety.
Case 204 — Newborn with seizures and bilateral basal ganglia injury post-jaundice HARD
A term male infant, breastfed, was sent home at 24 h despite total bilirubin 12 mg/dL. At day 5: lethargy, opisthotonic posturing, retrocollis, high-pitched cry. Total bilirubin now 28 mg/dL; direct bilirubin normal. MRI at 1 week: bilateral globus pallidus and subthalamic nuclei T1 hyperintensity.

DX

Diagnosis and the key risk factors for it?
Show model answer
  • Kernicterus / acute bilirubin encephalopathy (ABE).
  • Caused by severe hyperbilirubinaemia with unconjugated bilirubin crossing BBB → deposits preferentially in globus pallidus, subthalamic nuclei, hippocampus, brainstem auditory nuclei.
  • MRI: bilateral T1 hyperintensity in globus pallidus and subthalamic nuclei is pathognomonic; T2 hyperintensity develops in chronic kernicterus.
  • Risk factors: prematurity, breastfeeding (in setting of poor intake/dehydration → exaggerated jaundice), haemolysis (ABO/Rh incompatibility, G6PD deficiency, hereditary spherocytosis), East Asian ancestry, family history, sepsis, hypoalbuminaemia, drugs displacing bilirubin from albumin (sulfonamides, ceftriaxone).
  • Phases: phase 1 (lethargy, hypotonia, poor suck), phase 2 (hypertonia, retrocollis, opisthotonus, fever, high-pitched cry), phase 3 (chronic kernicterus — choreoathetosis, sensorineural deafness, gaze paresis, dental dysplasia).

TREATMENT

Acute management?
Show model answer
  • 1) Aggressive phototherapy (multiple banks, intensive, exposed skin maximally).
  • 2) DOUBLE-VOLUME EXCHANGE TRANSFUSION immediately when bilirubin reaches exchange threshold (varies with gestational age, postnatal age, risk factors per Bhutani nomogram / AAP 2022 updated thresholds).
  • 3) IVIG 0.5–1 g/kg if haemolytic disease and rapidly rising bilirubin (reduces immune-mediated haemolysis).
  • 4) Aggressive hydration to dilute bilirubin and reduce enterohepatic circulation.
  • 5) Treat underlying cause (sepsis workup, ABO/Rh testing, Coombs test, G6PD level — but do not delay treatment).
  • 6) Long-term sequelae monitoring: ABR for hearing, developmental assessment, MRI follow-up.
  • 7) PREVENTION: pre-discharge bilirubin screening, early follow-up for breastfeeding jaundice, parental education.
Case 205 — Infant with seizures shortly after circumcision HARD
A 7-day-old male newborn, after routine circumcision, develops persistent oozing from the surgical site over 24 h, then seizures, then bilateral subdural haematomas on imaging. Coagulation studies: prothrombin time markedly prolonged (INR 4.5), normal aPTT initially (later prolonged). Family is recent immigrants who declined the newborn vitamin K injection.

DX

Diagnosis and the underlying deficiency?
Show model answer
  • Late-onset (classical-late) vitamin K deficiency bleeding (VKDB), formerly called haemorrhagic disease of the newborn.
  • Three forms:
  • - Early VKDB (within 24 h of birth) — usually maternal medications inhibit vitamin K (warfarin, anticonvulsants).
  • - Classic VKDB (days 2–7) — inadequate intake, no prophylaxis given.
  • - Late VKDB (2–12 weeks) — typically exclusively breastfed infants (breast milk low in vitamin K), no prophylaxis, often with underlying cholestasis or fat-malabsorption (cystic fibrosis, biliary atresia).
  • Vitamin K is cofactor for γ-carboxylation of factors II, VII, IX, X, and proteins C and S.
  • Late VKDB has high rate of intracranial haemorrhage (50%) and high mortality/morbidity.

TREATMENT

Acute management and prevention?
Show model answer
  • 1) IV vitamin K (phytonadione) 1–2 mg slow IV bolus — corrects coagulopathy within 6–12 h.
  • 2) Fresh frozen plasma (FFP) 10–15 mL/kg for immediate factor replacement while waiting for vitamin K to work — needed for active major bleeding.
  • 3) Prothrombin complex concentrate (4-factor PCC) for life-threatening haemorrhage.
  • 4) Neurosurgical evaluation for SDH/ICH — depends on size and clinical status.
  • 5) Investigate for underlying cholestasis/malabsorption (signs of biliary atresia, CF screen, fat-soluble vitamins).
  • 6) PREVENTION: universal IM vitamin K 1 mg single dose at birth — gold standard. Oral regimens are LESS effective (require multiple doses, missed doses, poor absorption with cholestasis); inferior in preventing late VKDB.
  • 7) Counsel families who refuse prophylaxis about late VKDB risk; provide oral alternative if absolutely refusing IM (multiple oral doses needed; not as effective).
Case 206 — Premature infant with sudden deterioration on day 3 MOD
A 26-week premature infant (now day 3 of life) has been on conventional mechanical ventilation. Today: sudden bulging fontanelle, drop in haematocrit by 8 points without blood loss elsewhere, hypotension, brief tonic posturing, metabolic acidosis. Bedside cranial ultrasound: bilateral germinal matrix-intraventricular haemorrhage (GMH-IVH) with ventricular dilation. Largest clot on left fills the ventricle with parenchymal extension.

CRITERIA

Grade the intraventricular haemorrhage per Papile classification.
Show model answer
  • Papile classification (1978) of GMH-IVH:
  • - Grade I: GMH only (subependymal haemorrhage) — confined to the germinal matrix.
  • - Grade II: IVH without ventricular dilation — blood within ventricles but no dilation.
  • - Grade III: IVH with ventricular dilation.
  • - Grade IV: IVH with periventricular haemorrhagic infarction (parenchymal extension) — formerly 'Grade IV', now often referred to as PVHI.
  • This case = Grade IV (IVH with parenchymal extension).
  • Risk factors: extreme prematurity (most haemorrhages in < 30 weeks), hypotension, hypercapnia, rapid volume infusion, PDA, pneumothorax, mechanical ventilation, perinatal asphyxia.
  • Most occur in the first 72 hours of life; rare after day 7.

TREATMENT

Acute and longer-term management?
Show model answer
  • 1) Stabilise: gentle ventilation (avoid hypercarbia/hypocarbia), gentle handling, minimise stimulation, maintain normotension, transfusion to maintain Hb, treat metabolic acidosis carefully.
  • 2) Seizure management with phenobarbital.
  • 3) NO acute role for surgical drainage of acute IVH itself.
  • 4) Monitor for POST-HAEMORRHAGIC HYDROCEPHALUS (PHH) — develops in 25–50% of severe IVH; serial cranial US and head circumference.
  • 5) Management of PHH: serial LPs or ventricular taps for early reduction in CSF volume; temporary external ventricular drains; ventricular reservoirs; ETV; ultimately VP shunt when infant has reached weight/stability for surgery.
  • 6) Drug-Resistant Encephalopathy of Prematurity (DREAMS) trial and clinical practice: early intervention (DRIFT — drainage, irrigation, fibrinolytic therapy) used in some centres for severe PHH, but standard remains controversial.
  • 7) Prognosis: Grade IV IVH carries high risk of cerebral palsy (60–80%) and intellectual disability.
  • 8) Family counselling, multidisciplinary follow-up, early intervention services.

PHYSIOLOGY

Why does the germinal matrix bleed in premature infants?
Show model answer
  • Germinal matrix is a richly vascular, transient embryonic structure providing neurons and glia to developing brain (until ~ 32 weeks, then involutes).
  • Vessels are immature: thin-walled, no muscularis, fragile, lack autoregulation.
  • Located in subependymal region between caudate nucleus and thalamus.
  • Cerebral autoregulation is impaired in premature brain — pressure-passive flow makes vessels vulnerable to BP fluctuations.
  • Fluctuations from positive-pressure ventilation, volume infusion, hypoxia-reoxygenation, pneumothorax all stress these vessels.
  • Hence delivery and stabilisation strategies focused on minimising BP fluctuations and avoiding extremes of CO₂ to reduce GMH-IVH risk.
Case 207 — Schoolchild with episodes of falling and emotional dysregulation MOD
A 7-year-old boy has 12 months of episodic 'falling out of chair' — brief sudden falls without warning, no loss of consciousness, no incontinence, no postictal phase, sometimes with arm extension. Now also has occasional 'spacing out' episodes lasting 5–10 seconds. EEG: generalised slow spike-wave 1.5–2.5 Hz with paroxysmal fast activity in sleep. MRI: normal. Developmental delay noted.

DX

Diagnosis and the seizure types observed?
Show model answer
  • Lennox-Gastaut syndrome (LGS).
  • Triad: (1) multiple seizure types including ATONIC drop attacks, TONIC seizures (especially in sleep), and ATYPICAL ABSENCE; (2) characteristic EEG (slow spike-wave 1.5–2.5 Hz + paroxysmal fast activity, especially in sleep); (3) cognitive impairment.
  • Onset typically 3–8 years.
  • Aetiology: structural (cortical malformations, post-injury), genetic (numerous), or unknown.
  • Often evolves from West syndrome (infantile spasms).
  • Differential to consider: Doose syndrome (myoclonic-atonic — better prognosis), Dravet syndrome (febrile triggers, SCN1A).

TREATMENT

Drug therapy and rescue approach?
Show model answer
  • Broad-spectrum AEDs preferred: valproate, lamotrigine, topiramate, rufinamide (FDA-approved for LGS), clobazam (FDA-approved for LGS), cannabidiol (Epidiolex — FDA-approved for LGS and Dravet), fenfluramine (newly approved for LGS).
  • Felbamate effective but reserved due to aplastic anaemia/liver failure risk.
  • Ketogenic diet — highly effective for many LGS patients.
  • Non-pharmacologic: vagal nerve stimulator (VNS) for refractory disease, corpus callosotomy for drop attacks, focal resection if focal lesion identified.
  • Avoid carbamazepine, oxcarbazepine, vigabatrin (can worsen LGS seizures).
  • Helmets for drop attacks to prevent injury.
  • Cognitive support, special education, family support.
Case 208 — Adolescent with sudden severe right facial drooping after wedding MOD
A 16-year-old girl is brought to ED after sudden onset of right-sided facial weakness — she cannot raise her right eyebrow, close her right eye fully, or smile on the right. Forehead is involved. No limb weakness. Speech and swallow are normal. The night before, she attended a wedding outdoors in Connecticut. No tick bite noticed but she remembers itching her scalp.

DX

Diagnosis and the critical examination feature?
Show model answer
  • Peripheral (lower motor neuron) right facial nerve palsy (Bell's palsy) — to be considered Lyme neuroborreliosis until proven otherwise in this endemic exposure history.
  • Critical exam feature: FOREHEAD INVOLVEMENT (cannot wrinkle forehead) = LOWER motor neuron lesion.
  • In an UPPER motor neuron lesion (cortical or corticobulbar stroke), the forehead is SPARED due to bilateral innervation of the upper face from both cortices.
  • If forehead involved → think LMN: Bell's, Lyme, Ramsay Hunt (varicella zoster reactivation — vesicles in ear canal/external ear, taste loss, deafness), parotid mass, otitis media, mastoiditis, neurosarcoidosis, Guillain-Barré (Miller Fisher/AIDP).

NEXT STEP

Workup and management?
Show model answer
  • 1) Detailed otoscopy (Ramsay Hunt vesicles in ear), parotid exam, mastoid tenderness.
  • 2) In endemic Lyme area: Lyme serology (ELISA → confirmatory immunoblot) + consider empiric treatment.
  • 3) Imaging only if atypical features: bilateral, recurrent, progressive over weeks, other CN involvement, hearing loss → MRI brain with contrast and IAC to rule out tumour, demyelination.
  • 4) BELL'S PALSY (idiopathic, no other cause): oral prednisone 1 mg/kg/day × 7 days started WITHIN 72 hours of onset improves recovery rate (Sullivan 2007 NEJM).
  • 5) ANTIVIRAL adjunct (acyclovir/valacyclovir) controversial — likely benefit only in severe Bell's palsy (House-Brackmann 5-6) per meta-analyses.
  • 6) LYME NEUROBORRELIOSIS: doxycycline (oral) 4 mg/kg/day for > 8 years; amoxicillin or cefuroxime for younger; 14–21 days.
  • 7) EYE CARE: lubrication (artificial tears every 2 h, gel at night, tape closed at night) — corneal exposure injury is the major morbidity.
  • 8) Bilateral CN VII palsy → consider Lyme, GBS, sarcoidosis, leukaemia.
Case 209 — Adolescent with sleep attacks and cataplexy MOD
A 14-year-old has 6 months of progressive excessive daytime sleepiness, falling asleep in class, and recently 'collapse' episodes when laughing or surprised — knees buckle, jaw drops, transient inability to speak, but consciousness preserved. He also describes vivid dream-like experiences when falling asleep, occasional sleep paralysis. CSF hypocretin-1 (orexin-A) very low (5 pg/mL; normal > 110).

DX

Diagnosis and the pathology?
Show model answer
  • Narcolepsy type 1 (NT1) — with cataplexy and hypocretin deficiency.
  • Caused by selective autoimmune destruction of hypocretin (orexin)-producing neurons in the lateral hypothalamus.
  • HLA-DQB1*06:02 association (necessary but not sufficient — > 95% of NT1 carry this allele, but it's also common in unaffected populations).
  • Cataplexy = sudden bilateral loss of muscle tone triggered by strong emotions (especially positive — laughter, surprise) with preserved consciousness; pathognomonic feature.
  • 2010 H1N1 vaccination (Pandemrix) associated with increased NT1 incidence in Scandinavia/Europe.
  • Other features: sleep paralysis, hypnagogic/hypnopompic hallucinations, fragmented night sleep.
  • Diagnosis: CSF hypocretin-1 < 110 pg/mL OR polysomnography + MSLT (sleep latency < 8 min, ≥ 2 sleep-onset REM periods).

TREATMENT

Stepwise pharmacotherapy?
Show model answer
  • EXCESSIVE DAYTIME SLEEPINESS:
  • - Modafinil / armodafinil (first-line) — wakefulness-promoting.
  • - Methylphenidate, dextroamphetamine (second-line if inadequate).
  • - Pitolisant (H3 receptor inverse agonist; newer non-stimulant).
  • - Solriamfetol (D/NE reuptake inhibitor).
  • CATAPLEXY:
  • - Sodium oxybate (γ-hydroxybutyrate) — gold standard, addresses BOTH EDS and cataplexy and consolidates night sleep; FDA-approved.
  • - TCAs (clomipramine, imipramine) or SSRIs/SNRIs (venlafaxine, fluoxetine) — REM suppression.
  • SCHEDULED NAPS during the day.
  • SLEEP HYGIENE: regular schedule, avoid alcohol/sedatives.
  • School accommodations, driving safety counselling, mental health support.
  • Genetic counselling — multifactorial; no Mendelian transmission.
Case 210 — Child with chronic dietary restriction and severe gait abnormality MOD
A 10-year-old child on a strict vegan diet without supplementation for 4 years presents with 6 months of progressive bilateral lower-limb weakness, paraesthesias, ataxia, hyperreflexia with bilateral Babinski signs, loss of vibration and proprioception. Megaloblastic macrocytic anaemia. Serum B12 88 pg/mL (low). Homocysteine and methylmalonic acid elevated.

DX

Diagnosis and key location of cord involvement?
Show model answer
  • Subacute combined degeneration (SCD) of the spinal cord from vitamin B12 deficiency.
  • Affects DORSAL COLUMNS (loss of vibration and proprioception) AND LATERAL CORTICOSPINAL TRACTS (UMN signs — hyperreflexia, Babinski, spasticity).
  • Posterior columns affected first (vibration loss, sensory ataxia, Romberg positive), then lateral columns (spasticity, weakness).
  • Peripheral neuropathy also common.
  • B12 deficiency in pediatrics: dietary (strict vegan without supplementation, including breastfed infants of vegan mothers), malabsorption (terminal ileum disease, post-resection), Imerslund-Gräsbeck syndrome, transcobalamin II deficiency, pernicious anaemia (rare in children).
  • Functional deficiency: nitrous oxide abuse (inactivates B12).

TREATMENT

Replacement strategy?
Show model answer
  • Parenteral vitamin B12 (cyanocobalamin or hydroxocobalamin) — oral can work if intake/absorption issue is resolved, but parenteral preferred for severe deficiency with neurological involvement.
  • Typical regimen: 1000 µg IM daily × 1 week → weekly × 4–8 weeks → monthly for maintenance (or for life if absorption defect).
  • Folate supplementation alongside B12 — but NEVER give folate alone in B12 deficiency (can worsen neurologic disease without correcting it, by 'consuming' methionine synthesis machinery).
  • Address underlying cause: dietary counselling (B12-fortified foods, supplements), investigate malabsorption.
  • Neurological recovery variable — early treatment good prognosis; delayed treatment may leave permanent deficits (especially if symptoms > 6 months).
  • Iron supplementation if concurrent iron deficiency.
  • Family education and screening of vegan family members for similar deficiencies.
Case 211 — Child with sudden left arm weakness and history of cardiac surgery HARD
A 14-year-old with surgically palliated single ventricle (post-Fontan completion 2 years ago) presents with sudden onset left arm weakness and dysarthria 3 hours ago. CT plain: no haemorrhage. CT angiogram: occlusion of right MCA M1 segment. He had been off aspirin for 1 month for a dental procedure.

DX

Diagnosis and the embolic mechanism?
Show model answer
  • Acute ischaemic stroke from cardioembolic source in Fontan circulation.
  • Fontan physiology: systemic venous return → directly to pulmonary arteries (no subpulmonary pump). Chronically elevated systemic venous pressure.
  • Multiple thrombosis risks: stasis in systemic venous channels, prothrombotic state (factor abnormalities, protein-losing enteropathy), arrhythmia (especially atrial tachyarrhythmias), shunts/fenestrations creating right-to-left flow.
  • Lifetime stroke risk in Fontan patients is substantial — antithrombotic therapy (typically aspirin or warfarin) is standard.
  • This patient was 1 month off aspirin → likely contributed.

TREATMENT

Acute stroke management considerations?
Show model answer
  • 1) Time-critical: assess if within window for reperfusion.
  • 2) IV thrombolysis (tPA) — limited paediatric data; extrapolated from adult evidence for LVO; consider on case-by-case basis with risk-benefit weighed (paediatric stroke trials limited).
  • 3) MECHANICAL THROMBECTOMY — increasingly performed in older children/adolescents with large vessel occlusion. Time window for adolescents similar to adults (extended window to 24 h with perfusion mismatch). Demonstrated benefit in case series and now in registries.
  • 4) Search for embolic source: echocardiogram (TTE + TEE if not contraindicated, with bubble study), evaluate Fontan circulation, atrial thrombus, vegetation.
  • 5) Antithrombotic optimisation: restart aspirin acutely; consider switch to warfarin or DOAC long-term depending on Fontan anatomy and risk profile.
  • 6) Manage CPP, normothermia, normocapnia, glucose 4–10 mmol/L, BP avoiding both hypertension (haemorrhagic transformation) and hypotension.
  • 7) Multidisciplinary: paediatric stroke team, cardiology, intervention.
  • 8) Rehabilitation early.
Case 212 — Newborn with focal seizures on day 2 and normal MRI MOD
A term newborn (uncomplicated delivery) presents on day 2 of life with focal clonic seizures of the right arm. Initial workup: normal glucose, calcium, magnesium, electrolytes. CSF normal. MRI on day 2: normal. Family history: father had febrile seizures resolving by age 5; paternal grandfather had brief seizures in infancy that resolved.

DX

Most likely diagnosis given the family history?
Show model answer
  • Benign familial neonatal epilepsy (BFNE) — autosomal dominant with high penetrance.
  • Caused by mutations in KCNQ2 or KCNQ3 (potassium channel genes; M-current subunits).
  • Onset typically days 2–8 of life with focal seizures (often migrating, sometimes generalising).
  • Seizures self-limited — resolve within weeks-months.
  • Normal development in most.
  • Some KCNQ2 variants cause severe encephalopathy (KCNQ2-encephalopathy) instead of benign course — phenotype-genotype distinguishes.
  • Related entity: benign familial neonatal-infantile epilepsy (SCN2A); benign familial infantile epilepsy (PRRT2).

TREATMENT

Management and prognosis?
Show model answer
  • 1) Confirm with genetic testing (KCNQ2, KCNQ3 panel).
  • 2) AEDs to control seizures during acute period:
  • - Phenobarbital, levetiracetam, or sodium-channel blockers (carbamazepine, oxcarbazepine) — channel-blockers like carbamazepine may be PARTICULARLY effective in KCNQ2 disorders (and the related KCNQ2 encephalopathy).
  • 3) Continue AED for 6–12 months typically, then attempt wean once seizure-free.
  • 4) Reassure family — typical benign course with normal long-term development.
  • 5) Genetic counselling: AD with high penetrance; 50% recurrence in offspring.
  • 6) Cascade testing of family members may identify previously unrecognised cases.
  • 7) Distinguish from KCNQ2-encephalopathy (more severe phenotype with abnormal MRI/EEG, persistent seizures, developmental delay) — different prognosis and management.
Case 213 — Schoolchild with worsening visual loss and uveitis HARD
A 14-year-old presents with 6 weeks of progressive visual loss bilaterally, bilateral chronic anterior uveitis, parotid swelling, fevers, and now CN VII palsy on the right. CSF: WBC 80 (lymph), elevated ACE level. CXR: hilar lymphadenopathy. Serum ACE elevated.

DX

Diagnosis and the histologic feature?
Show model answer
  • Neurosarcoidosis (CNS involvement of systemic sarcoidosis).
  • Heerfordt syndrome (Heerfordt-Waldenström syndrome): parotid enlargement + uveitis + facial nerve palsy + fever = highly suggestive of sarcoidosis.
  • Histology: non-caseating granulomas.
  • CNS involvement in 5–15% of sarcoidosis cases; can affect cranial nerves (CN VII most common, then optic), meninges (basilar predilection), parenchyma, hypothalamic-pituitary axis.
  • Differential: TB meningitis (caseating granulomas), other granulomatous diseases (Wegener — granulomatosis with polyangiitis, fungal), CNS lymphoma.
  • Diagnosis often requires biopsy of accessible tissue (parotid, lymph node, skin, conjunctiva) showing non-caseating granulomas.

TREATMENT

Treatment principles?
Show model answer
  • 1) FIRST-LINE: corticosteroids — high-dose IV methylprednisolone for severe disease, then oral prednisone taper over months.
  • 2) STEROID-SPARING agents: methotrexate, azathioprine, mycophenolate mofetil for long-term immunosuppression.
  • 3) REFRACTORY DISEASE: TNF-α inhibitors (infliximab, adalimumab) — increasingly used.
  • 4) Cyclophosphamide for severe refractory disease.
  • 5) Treat complications: hydrocephalus (shunt), seizures (AEDs), hormone replacement for hypothalamic-pituitary involvement.
  • 6) Long-term follow-up with neurology, ophthalmology, rheumatology, pulmonology.
  • 7) Surveillance for systemic disease (especially pulmonary, cardiac, ocular involvement).
  • 8) ACE level monitoring of limited utility (not sensitive or specific enough alone).
Case 214 — Toddler with developmental regression and 'butterfly' MRI pattern HARD
A 16-month-old who walked at 13 months presents with 4 months of progressive gait deterioration, dysphagia, dystonia, and seizures. Lactate 4.8 mmol/L (elevated). MRI: bilateral symmetric T2/FLAIR hyperintensities in putamen, caudate, and brainstem (substantia nigra, periaqueductal grey) — 'butterfly' appearance. MR spectroscopy: lactate doublet.

DX

Diagnosis and the underlying defect category?
Show model answer
  • Leigh syndrome (subacute necrotising encephalomyelopathy).
  • Heterogeneous mitochondrial disorder — > 75 known genetic causes — mtDNA mutations (m.8993T>G in MT-ATP6 most common single mutation) AND nuclear mutations (SURF1, PDHA1, MT-ATP6, NDUFV1, etc.).
  • Common defects: complex I deficiency, complex IV (COX) deficiency, pyruvate dehydrogenase deficiency.
  • Classic MRI: bilateral symmetric basal ganglia + brainstem (substantia nigra, periaqueductal grey) T2 hyperintensities.
  • Onset usually 3–24 months; some adolescent/adult forms.
  • Presentation: psychomotor regression, hypotonia → dystonia, ataxia, ophthalmoplegia, optic atrophy, respiratory abnormalities.
  • MR spectroscopy: lactate peak in affected areas (sensitive marker).

TREATMENT

Supportive measures and what to AVOID?
Show model answer
  • No curative therapy — supportive only.
  • Mitochondrial cocktail (variable evidence): coenzyme Q10, riboflavin, thiamine, biotin, levocarnitine, creatine, idebenone. Largely empirical.
  • Pyruvate dehydrogenase deficiency: ketogenic diet (provides alternative substrate bypassing PDH bottleneck) + thiamine — sometimes dramatic response.
  • Aggressive treatment of intercurrent illnesses (catabolic decompensation).
  • Seizure management with levetiracetam (preferred); seizures often refractory.
  • Multidisciplinary care: neurology, metabolism, GI/nutrition, cardiology (cardiomyopathy surveillance), ophthalmology (optic atrophy), audiology.
  • AVOID mitotoxic drugs:
  • - VALPROATE (especially POLG-related Leigh — fulminant hepatic failure).
  • - Aminoglycosides (sensorineural hearing loss).
  • - Linezolid (peripheral and optic neuropathy).
  • - Statins (myopathy).
  • - Certain nucleoside analogue antiretrovirals.
  • - Propofol prolonged infusion (PRIS).
  • Genetic counselling per mode of inheritance; reproductive options including mitochondrial donation in some jurisdictions.
Case 215 — Teen with severe anaemia, neurological signs, and metallic exposure MOD
A 15-year-old apprentice in a metal-recycling shop (exposed to fumes) presents with 2 months of headache, abdominal pain, weakness, paraesthesias, ataxia, and an episode of seizure. Hb 9 with prominent basophilic stippling on smear. Abdominal X-ray: dense lines at long-bone metaphyses, dense flecks visible in colon. Blood lead level 65 µg/dL.

DX

Most likely diagnosis and the workplace context?
Show model answer
  • Occupational lead poisoning (plumbism) from inhalation and/or ingestion in the recycling/smelting context.
  • Sources include: lead-acid battery recycling, metal smelting, paint manufacturing, jewellery making, certain pottery glazes, herbal/ayurvedic medicines.
  • BLL 65 µg/dL is symptomatic but pre-encephalopathy range (encephalopathy usually > 70 µg/dL).
  • Characteristic findings: anaemia with basophilic stippling, lead lines on X-ray (in growing bones), abdominal pain ('lead colic'), peripheral neuropathy (wrist drop, foot drop classically), encephalopathy with seizures at higher levels.
  • Children absorb 30–50% of ingested lead vs ~10% in adults.

TREATMENT

Chelation indications and oral regimen?
Show model answer
  • For symptomatic BLL ≥ 45 µg/dL but no encephalopathy: oral chelation with succimer (DMSA) 30 mg/kg/day × 5 days, then 20 mg/kg/day × 14 days.
  • For BLL ≥ 70 µg/dL or encephalopathy: combination IV/IM chelation as in younger child (BAL + EDTA).
  • D-Penicillamine has been used orally but more side effects; succimer preferred.
  • NEW EXPOSURES MUST STOP — remove from workplace, OSHA/equivalent occupational health notification, public health follow-up.
  • Iron deficiency commonly co-exists — replete iron after chelation initiated (iron and lead share absorption pathways).
  • Family/co-worker screening — others may also be affected.
  • Long-term cognitive and behavioural surveillance.
  • BLL takes time to normalise — bone stores release lead slowly over months-years; persistent low-level exposure even after acute treatment.
Case 216 — Adolescent with new-onset OCD and brisk tics after viral illness MOD
A 12-year-old previously well girl developed an upper respiratory infection 5 weeks ago. Now: sudden-onset severe obsessive-compulsive symptoms (counting rituals, contamination fears), prominent motor tics (eye blinking, head jerking), separation anxiety, urinary frequency, rapid cognitive deterioration. ASO titre rising; throat culture now negative; mycoplasma serology pending.

DX

Diagnosis and the broader spectrum it belongs to?
Show model answer
  • PANS (Pediatric Acute-onset Neuropsychiatric Syndrome).
  • Broader than PANDAS — defined by sudden-onset OCD or severe food restriction PLUS ≥ 2 of: anxiety, mood lability/depression, behavioural regression, school decline, sensory/motor abnormalities, somatic symptoms (sleep, urinary, motor).
  • Possible triggers beyond strep: mycoplasma, influenza, EBV, viral, COVID-19, autoimmune/inflammatory states.
  • PANDAS = subset specifically temporally related to GAS infection.
  • Diagnosis is clinical — both PANS and PANDAS lack a definitive biomarker.
  • Differential: tic disorder/Tourette, autism (clearer onset distinguishes), anti-NMDAR encephalitis (more severe encephalopathy and dyskinesias), Sydenham chorea (chorea specifically with rheumatic heart disease).

TREATMENT

Tiered treatment per consortium guidelines?
Show model answer
  • 1) Treat any active infection (antibiotics for GAS, mycoplasma; antiviral if applicable).
  • 2) PSYCHIATRIC SYMPTOMS: SSRI for OCD (start LOW, go SLOW — children with PANS often have heightened side effect sensitivity); CBT/ERP for OCD.
  • 3) NSAIDs (ibuprofen) — first-line anti-inflammatory adjunct.
  • 4) Oral corticosteroids — short pulse for moderate-severe.
  • 5) IVIG (1.5–2 g/kg) — for moderate-severe per consortium 2017 guidelines.
  • 6) PLASMA EXCHANGE — for severe/refractory.
  • 7) Rituximab/mycophenolate for chronic refractory.
  • 8) Antibiotic prophylaxis (penicillin or azithromycin) for recurrent strep-triggered relapses — controversial duration.
  • 9) Multidisciplinary care: psychiatry, paediatrics, ID, neurology; family support; school accommodations.
  • 10) Avoid stigmatising — PANS/PANDAS criteria evolving; some experts skeptical; balance reasonable workup and treatment trials with avoiding over-medicalisation.
Case 217 — Infant with apnoeic spells and laughing fits HARD
A 7-month-old infant presents with brief paroxysmal episodes of inappropriate laughter (gelastic seizures) occurring 8–10 times daily, sometimes triggered by gentle stimulation. Now also having brief stiffening of the body. MRI: well-circumscribed non-enhancing T1-isointense, T2-hyperintense midline lesion attached to the hypothalamus near the tuber cinereum.

DX

Diagnosis and the seizure semiology?
Show model answer
  • Hypothalamic hamartoma (HH) with gelastic seizures.
  • Gelastic seizures: brief outbursts of inappropriate or mirthless laughter that the patient does not control or remember; pathognomonic for HH.
  • HH = congenital non-neoplastic malformation of grey matter and hypothalamus.
  • May progress with multiple seizure types, precocious puberty, behavioural problems, cognitive decline.
  • Associated with Pallister-Hall syndrome (GLI3 mutation — HH + polydactyly + imperforate anus).

TREATMENT

Therapeutic options?
Show model answer
  • AEDs typically ineffective for gelastic seizures.
  • Surgical/ablative options (definitive):
  • - MR-guided laser interstitial thermal therapy (LITT) — minimally invasive, increasingly first-line.
  • - Stereotactic radiosurgery (gamma knife).
  • - Endoscopic disconnection.
  • - Open microsurgical resection (more morbid).
  • Treat precocious puberty (GnRH agonist if present).
  • Behavioural and cognitive support.
  • Multidisciplinary epilepsy surgery team.
Case 218 — Child with progressive ataxia and frequent infections MOD
A 6-year-old has had progressive gait ataxia since age 2, oculocutaneous telangiectasias (conjunctival, ears, neck), recurrent sinopulmonary infections, and recently a chest X-ray showing bronchiectasis. Serum IgA absent; IgG2 subclass low; α-fetoprotein elevated.

DX

Diagnosis and the gene involved?
Show model answer
  • Ataxia-telangiectasia (A-T) — autosomal recessive.
  • Mutations in ATM gene (11q22) — encodes ATM kinase (master regulator of DNA double-strand break repair).
  • Cells unable to detect/repair DNA damage → predisposition to cancer (lymphoma, leukaemia — 10–25% lifetime risk) and radiation hypersensitivity.
  • Features: progressive cerebellar ataxia (starts age 1–4), oculocutaneous telangiectasias (appear later), combined immunodeficiency (low IgA most consistent; low IgG2, IgG4; impaired T cell function), elevated AFP, radiation sensitivity, increased malignancy risk.
  • Endocrine: insulin-resistant diabetes, hypogonadism.

NEXT STEP

Management priorities and what to AVOID?
Show model answer
  • 1) Immunoglobulin replacement (IVIG/SCIG) for hypogammaglobulinaemia and infection prophylaxis.
  • 2) AGGRESSIVE infection management and prevention; vaccinations including pneumococcal, influenza (use live vaccines cautiously).
  • 3) AVOID IONISING RADIATION when possible (X-rays, CT) — A-T cells are radiosensitive, increased malignancy risk from each exposure. Use MRI/ultrasound when feasible.
  • 4) Avoid radiomimetic chemotherapy where possible; reduced doses used for malignancy.
  • 5) Cancer surveillance — annual physical, lymph node exam; investigate any unusual symptoms aggressively.
  • 6) Pulmonary care: chest physiotherapy, prophylactic antibiotics, treat bronchiectasis.
  • 7) Multidisciplinary: neurology, immunology, oncology, pulmonology, rehabilitation, genetic counselling.
  • 8) Disease-modifying therapies in trials (antisense oligonucleotides for specific mutations, betamethasone for ataxia symptoms — modest, controversial).
Case 219 — Adolescent with progressive ataxia and cardiomyopathy MOD
A 14-year-old has 4 years of progressive gait ataxia, dysarthria, scoliosis, foot deformities (pes cavus). Now: cardiomyopathy diagnosed on routine echo (concentric LVH, LVEF 50%), and impaired glucose tolerance. Neurological exam: absent lower-limb reflexes, extensor plantars, sensory ataxia.

DX

Diagnosis and the mutation type?
Show model answer
  • Friedreich ataxia (FRDA) — autosomal recessive.
  • GAA TRINUCLEOTIDE REPEAT EXPANSION in intron 1 of FXN gene (chromosome 9q21) — reduces frataxin expression.
  • Frataxin = mitochondrial protein involved in iron-sulfur cluster assembly; deficiency → mitochondrial iron accumulation, oxidative stress, energy failure.
  • Repeat size correlates with severity (larger repeats → earlier, more severe).
  • Features: progressive ataxia (cerebellar + sensory — both spinocerebellar tracts and dorsal columns affected), absent reflexes in legs with upgoing plantars (mixed UMN/LMN), dysarthria, scoliosis, pes cavus, hypertrophic CARDIOMYOPATHY (leading cause of death), diabetes, hearing/visual loss.

TREATMENT

Disease-modifying and supportive therapy?
Show model answer
  • OMAVELOXOLONE (Skyclarys) — FDA-approved 2023 — first disease-modifying therapy.
  • - Mechanism: Nrf2 pathway activator → increases antioxidant gene expression → mitigates oxidative stress.
  • - MOXIe trial showed modest but significant improvement in mFARS scores over 48 weeks.
  • Other adjunctive (less evidence): idebenone (Q10 analogue), antioxidants.
  • CARDIOLOGY surveillance: annual echo + ECG (arrhythmia and HF risk).
  • Diabetes management.
  • Multidisciplinary care: physiotherapy, orthopaedics (scoliosis, foot deformities), audiology, ophthalmology.
  • Genetic counselling: AR with 25% recurrence; sibling testing (presymptomatic identification).
  • Avoid mitotoxic drugs.
Case 220 — Infant with progressive macrocephaly and intractable seizures HARD
A 6-month-old, born to consanguineous parents, presents with progressive macrocephaly (HC 99th centile, crossing 2 lines upward), severe developmental regression, refractory myoclonic seizures, and progressive hypertonia. MRI: bilateral, symmetric, T2-hyperintense white matter swelling with vacuolation/spongy degeneration. Urine: markedly elevated N-acetylaspartic acid (NAA).

DX

Diagnosis and the enzyme deficit?
Show model answer
  • Canavan disease — autosomal recessive (more common in Ashkenazi Jewish population).
  • Aspartoacylase (ASPA) deficiency → cannot hydrolyse N-acetylaspartate (NAA) → NAA accumulates in CNS → spongiform vacuolar leukodystrophy.
  • MRS hallmark: very tall NAA peak (paradoxical — most leukodystrophies have REDUCED NAA reflecting neuronal loss).
  • Differential of macrocephaly + leukodystrophy: Alexander disease (more frontal predominance, basal ganglia involvement, periventricular rim, GFAP astrocytopathy), megalencephalic leukoencephalopathy with subcortical cysts (MLC1/HEPACAM mutations), glutaric aciduria type 1 (different pattern, bat-wing).

TREATMENT

Current options and future directions?
Show model answer
  • No disease-modifying therapy currently approved.
  • Gene therapy with AAV-delivered ASPA in active clinical trials (e.g., BBP-812) — early results promising.
  • Supportive: seizure management (broad-spectrum AEDs), nutrition (PEG), spasticity, anticipatory care.
  • Multidisciplinary including palliative care from early stages.
  • Family genetic counselling: 25% recurrence; carrier screening in Ashkenazi Jewish couples (panel screening is standard).
  • Prenatal/preimplantation testing available for at-risk pregnancies.
  • Most children die in first decade; severe phenotype the rule.
Case 221 — Adolescent with eye-movement abnormalities and progressive parkinsonism HARD
A 14-year-old presents with 8 months of clumsiness, dysarthria, splenomegaly, parkinsonism, dystonic posturing, and a peculiar finding: cannot voluntarily look up or down on command but reflex vertical eye movements (doll's eye) are preserved. Filipin staining of cultured fibroblasts: positive.

DX

Diagnosis and the eye-movement abnormality name?
Show model answer
  • Niemann-Pick disease type C (NPC) — autosomal recessive.
  • Mutations in NPC1 (95%) or NPC2 — both encode proteins involved in cholesterol trafficking from late endosomes/lysosomes.
  • Pathology: lysosomal accumulation of unesterified cholesterol and other lipids.
  • VERTICAL SUPRANUCLEAR GAZE PALSY (VSGP) — impaired voluntary vertical saccades (especially DOWN-gaze) with preserved reflex movements (oculocephalic) — highly characteristic feature.
  • Other features: progressive ataxia, dystonia, dysarthria, dysphagia, cognitive decline, gelastic cataplexy (loss of tone with emotion), splenomegaly (often present from infancy), neonatal cholestasis (some forms), seizures.
  • Filipin staining of cultured skin fibroblasts shows abnormal cholesterol storage (gold-standard test before molecular era; molecular testing now primary).

TREATMENT

Disease-modifying therapy?
Show model answer
  • MIGLUSTAT (Zavesca) — substrate-reduction therapy; inhibits glucosylceramide synthase → reduces glycosphingolipid accumulation.
  • - Approved in Europe and many other countries (off-label in US).
  • - Slows neurological progression; doesn't reverse established damage.
  • Hydroxypropyl-β-cyclodextrin (HPβCD) — investigational; intrathecal delivery has shown some benefit.
  • Arimoclomol — HSP induction; in clinical trials.
  • Supportive: seizure management, dystonia treatment, nutrition, palliative care.
  • Genetic counselling: AR with 25% recurrence; cascade testing.
  • Wide phenotypic spectrum — late-infantile, juvenile, adult-onset forms with different progression rates.
Case 222 — Newborn with respiratory failure and hypoglycaemia after fasting HARD
A 5-day-old term infant presents with hypoglycaemia (1.2 mmol/L) after 8 hours without feeding. Now: severe encephalopathy, hypothermia, cardiomyopathy on echo, hepatomegaly. Ammonia mildly elevated. Acylcarnitine profile: marked elevation of long-chain species C14, C14:1, C16, C16:1, C18, C18:1; low free carnitine.

DX

Diagnosis and the metabolic block?
Show model answer
  • Very long-chain acyl-CoA dehydrogenase (VLCAD) deficiency — autosomal recessive.
  • VLCAD catalyses the first step of β-oxidation of long-chain fatty acids (C12–C20) in mitochondria.
  • Defect → cannot oxidise long-chain fatty acids during fasting/stress → energy deficit + accumulation of toxic long-chain acylcarnitines.
  • Three phenotypes:
  • - Neonatal/severe: cardiomyopathy, hepatic failure, encephalopathy, hypoketotic hypoglycaemia, sudden death.
  • - Hepatic (childhood): episodic hypoglycaemia, hepatomegaly during intercurrent illness.
  • - Myopathic (adolescent/adult): exercise-induced rhabdomyolysis, muscle pain.
  • Newborn screening detects elevated C14:1 — most cases now identified pre-symptomatically.

TREATMENT

Acute and chronic dietary management?
Show model answer
  • ACUTE: IV dextrose 10–12 mg/kg/min to maintain glucose > 4 mmol/L and suppress lipolysis.
  • Avoid prolonged fasting (provides substrate for energy when lipolysis is harmful).
  • Treat cardiomyopathy and arrhythmias supportively.
  • L-carnitine supplementation (debated benefit) — replaces depleted carnitine, removes toxic acylcarnitines.
  • CHRONIC dietary therapy:
  • - Restrict long-chain triglycerides (LCT); limit dietary fat to 10–25% of calories.
  • - Substitute medium-chain triglycerides (MCT) — bypass VLCAD blockade (medium-chain FA oxidation uses different enzymes).
  • - Frequent feeds; avoid prolonged fasting.
  • - Uncooked cornstarch at bedtime in older children.
  • TRIHEPTANOIN — anaplerotic odd-chain triglyceride; FDA-approved for LC-FAOD; provides intermediates for Krebs cycle.
  • EMERGENCY ACTION PLAN: 10% dextrose IV during any illness or vomiting; family education and medical alert.
  • Genetic counselling and cascade testing.
Case 223 — Infant with neonatal seizures and severe ketotic hyperammonaemia HARD
A 7-day-old term infant presents with poor feeding from day 4, lethargy, vomiting, and now severe encephalopathy with seizures. Ammonia 350 µmol/L. Severe metabolic ACIDOSIS with anion gap 28. Ketones strongly positive in urine. Lactate elevated. Plasma amino acids: elevated glycine. Urine organic acids: elevated propionic acid, methylcitric acid, 3-hydroxypropionic acid.

DX

Diagnosis and the metabolic defect?
Show model answer
  • Propionic acidemia (PA) — autosomal recessive.
  • Propionyl-CoA carboxylase deficiency (PCCA or PCCB genes; biotin-dependent enzyme).
  • Cannot convert propionyl-CoA → methylmalonyl-CoA → succinyl-CoA in Krebs cycle.
  • Toxic accumulation of propionate, 3-OH-propionate, methylcitrate.
  • Classic presentation: neonatal severe metabolic decompensation with high anion gap acidosis, ketotic hyperammonaemia (vs UCDs which are non-ketotic), neurologic deterioration.
  • Glycine often elevated (so called 'ketotic hyperglycinemia' — historical term for organic acidemias).
  • Other major organic acidemias: methylmalonic acidemia (MMA — MUT or vitamin B12 metabolism defects), isovaleric acidemia (IVA — characteristic 'sweaty feet' odour from isovaleric acid), maple syrup urine disease (MSUD — branched-chain).

TREATMENT

Acute emergency management?
Show model answer
  • 1) STOP all protein intake immediately.
  • 2) IV dextrose (D10) 10–12 mg/kg/min + insulin if needed for normoglycaemia → suppresses catabolism.
  • 3) Intralipid as caloric source (does NOT worsen PA — short-chain FA).
  • 4) Carnitine 100–200 mg/kg/day IV — replaces depleted carnitine, removes propionyl-CoA as propionylcarnitine.
  • 5) Biotin trial (5–10 mg/day) — for biotin-responsive forms.
  • 6) Metronidazole — reduces intestinal propionate production by gut flora.
  • 7) HEMODIALYSIS for severe hyperammonaemia (> 250 µmol/L) or persistent acidosis despite medical management — most rapid clearance of organic acids and ammonia.
  • 8) Treat hyperammonaemia: nitrogen scavengers (Ammonul) often used (debate about effectiveness in organic acidemias — propionic acid inhibits urea cycle).
  • 9) Sodium bicarbonate to correct acidemia.
  • 10) After stabilisation: low-protein diet (limit isoleucine, valine, methionine, threonine — propionogenic amino acids), special metabolic formula, frequent feeds, sick-day rules, liver transplant for refractory severe disease.
  • 11) Family education, emergency action plan.
  • 12) Genetic counselling (AR, 25% recurrence).
Case 224 — Child with newly diagnosed cancer and back pain after lumbar puncture HARD
A 7-year-old with newly diagnosed ALL had a diagnostic lumbar puncture under anaesthesia 2 hours ago. On waking, complains of severe back pain, then unable to move legs. Examination: bilateral flaccid paraplegia with sensory level at T10, urinary retention. MRI cord: large epidural haematoma extending from T8 to L2.

DX

Diagnosis and predisposing factor?
Show model answer
  • Iatrogenic spinal epidural haematoma from lumbar puncture.
  • Predisposing factors: COAGULOPATHY (most important — newly diagnosed leukaemia with thrombocytopenia or coagulopathy is high risk), recent anticoagulation, traumatic taps, multiple attempts, anatomical abnormalities.
  • Platelet target for safe LP in pediatric oncology: ≥ 50 (sometimes ≥ 30 with experienced operator; protocols vary). INR < 1.5. No therapeutic anticoagulation within window.
  • This is the reason pediatric oncology protocols mandate platelet/coagulation thresholds before LP, often with platelet transfusion premedication if necessary.

NEXT STEP

Time-critical management?
Show model answer
  • 1) EMERGENCY MRI cord to characterise haematoma extent.
  • 2) URGENT NEUROSURGICAL DECOMPRESSION (laminectomy + clot evacuation) — outcome correlates strongly with time to decompression; ideally < 8 hours from symptom onset for best neurological recovery.
  • 3) Reverse coagulopathy: platelet transfusion to > 100, FFP/PCC if indicated.
  • 4) High-dose dexamethasone may be considered (extrapolation from cord compression).
  • 5) Catheterise bladder (urinary retention).
  • 6) Hemodynamic and respiratory monitoring (high cord injuries with spinal shock).
  • 7) Pressure-area care.
  • 8) Multidisciplinary: neurosurgery, neurology, oncology, rehabilitation, ICU.
  • 9) Family communication; institutional incident review.
  • 10) Document and learn from event — institutional policies on LP safety should be reviewed.
Case 225 — Schoolchild with subacute hearing loss and vertigo after URI MOD
A 9-year-old developed an upper respiratory infection 2 weeks ago. Now: progressive right-sided sensorineural hearing loss over 3 days, severe vertigo, vomiting, ataxia. Examination: nystagmus toward the left, sensorineural deafness on the right, but normal otoscopy. MRI: enhancement of right vestibulocochlear nerve and inner ear structures.

DX

Diagnosis and the differentials to consider?
Show model answer
  • Acute labyrinthitis with vestibulocochlear involvement (vestibular neuritis if hearing spared, labyrinthitis if both vestibular and cochlear affected).
  • Often post-viral (most common cause in children): viral inflammation of the inner ear / 8th nerve.
  • Differentials to consider:
  • - Meniere disease (recurrent vertigo + fluctuating hearing loss + tinnitus + aural fullness — rare in pediatric).
  • - Vestibular schwannoma (unilateral progressive hearing loss + vertigo — rare in children; if bilateral, NF2).
  • - Migraine-associated vertigo / vestibular migraine.
  • - Posterior circulation stroke / brainstem lesion (acute, with other CN/long tract signs).
  • - Autoimmune inner ear disease.
  • - Lyme disease (especially endemic areas).
  • - Cholesteatoma with labyrinthine fistula.

TREATMENT

Management of acute labyrinthitis?
Show model answer
  • Symptomatic: antiemetics (ondansetron preferred — meclizine or dimenhydrinate cause more drowsiness and aren't ideal in young children), antivertiginous, IV fluids if dehydrated.
  • Brief course of CORTICOSTEROIDS (prednisolone or dexamethasone tapered) — controversial but commonly used for hearing preservation if sudden SNHL component.
  • Antivirals (acyclovir/valacyclovir) considered if HSV/VZV suspected (e.g., visible vesicles for Ramsay Hunt).
  • Vestibular rehabilitation as recovery progresses.
  • Audiologic follow-up — assess for permanent hearing loss, consider hearing amplification, cochlear implant in severe cases.
  • Multidisciplinary: ENT, audiology, neurology.
  • Most children recover vestibular function (central compensation); hearing recovery less predictable.
Case 226 — Adolescent with progressive proximal weakness and elevated CK MOD
A 14-year-old has 6 months of progressive bilateral hip-girdle weakness — difficulty climbing stairs, rising from chair, Gowers' sign positive. CK 12 000 (very elevated). Calf hypertrophy and contractures developing. Family history: mother's brother died at age 23 of muscular dystrophy.

DX

Most likely diagnosis and inheritance pattern?
Show model answer
  • Duchenne muscular dystrophy (DMD) — X-linked recessive.
  • Mutations in DMD gene (Xp21) — out-of-frame deletions/duplications/point mutations → absent or severely reduced dystrophin protein.
  • Becker muscular dystrophy (BMD) = same gene, but in-frame mutations → partially functional dystrophin → milder, later-onset.
  • This presentation (14 yo with progressive weakness and very elevated CK with X-linked family history) suggests BMD (DMD typically presents 3–5 years; loses ambulation by 12).
