  • Distinguish DMD from BMD by clinical course, dystrophin testing (immunohistochemistry, Western blot), genetic testing (large deletion testing then sequencing).

TREATMENT

Multidisciplinary management?
Show model answer
  • CORTICOSTEROIDS (prednisone or deflazacort): standard of care — slow disease progression, extend ambulation; significant side effects (weight gain, bone loss, mood).
  • VAMOROLONE (dissociative steroid): FDA-approved 2023; fewer side effects.
  • EXON-SKIPPING THERAPIES for specific mutations:
  • - Eteplirsen, golodirsen, viltolarsen, casimersen (antisense oligonucleotides for specific exon skipping).
  • GENE THERAPY: ELEVIDYS (delandistrogene moxeparvovec) — AAV-delivered micro-dystrophin (FDA-approved 2023 for ambulatory boys 4–5 years; broader approval 2024).
  • CARDIAC SURVEILLANCE: yearly echo from age 6, transition to cardiac MRI; treat cardiomyopathy with ACE inhibitor and β-blocker.
  • RESPIRATORY surveillance: spirometry, sleep study; nocturnal NIV when FVC drops; cough assist.
  • ORTHOPAEDICS: scoliosis management (spinal fusion when significant), contracture management.
  • NUTRITION: bone health (calcium, vitamin D), weight management.
  • GENETIC COUNSELLING: X-linked; mother is usually carrier; affect male offspring 50%; daughters 50% carriers; cardiomyopathy in some carrier women.
  • Multidisciplinary clinic with physiotherapy, occupational therapy, psychology, education support.
Case 227 — Newborn with continuous seizure-like movements and stridor MOD
A 2-week-old infant presents with frequent episodes of exaggerated startle response, generalised stiffening on noise or touch (hyperekplexia), and severe stridor on inspiration. No autonomic features. EEG normal. Nose tap (forehead percussion) elicits prominent stiffening; clonazepam dramatically improves symptoms.

DX

Diagnosis and the receptor involved?
Show model answer
  • Hereditary hyperekplexia (startle disease, stiff-baby syndrome).
  • Caused by mutations in glycine receptor (most commonly GLRA1 — α1 subunit, autosomal dominant; GLRB — β subunit, autosomal recessive) or glycine transporter SLC6A5.
  • Hyperekplexia = pathologically exaggerated startle response to unexpected stimuli, often with sustained tonic stiffening.
  • Glycine is the major INHIBITORY neurotransmitter in brainstem and spinal cord (counterpart to GABA elsewhere); reduced glycinergic inhibition → motor hyperexcitability.
  • Vesna manoeuvre (head flexion) can relieve sustained stiffening in apnoeic episodes.
  • Differential: epileptic startles (EEG correlate, postictal), drug-induced (anticholinergic, withdrawal), Sandifer syndrome, neonatal jitteriness.

TREATMENT

Acute management?
Show model answer
  • Clonazepam — drug of choice; potentiates GABA-A inhibition → restores inhibitory tone. Often dramatically effective.
  • Other benzodiazepines (diazepam, midazolam) also helpful.
  • Education: VIGEVANO MANOEUVRE (forced flexion of head and limbs toward trunk) to abort sustained stiffening apnoea — important for parents to learn.
  • Trigger avoidance (especially in neonates — sudden noise, temperature changes).
  • Most patients improve dramatically with treatment and many can wean off medication later in childhood (residual exaggerated startle into adulthood).
  • Genetic counselling: depends on inheritance pattern.
  • Differential from sporadic glycine receptor antibody-mediated PERM (Progressive Encephalomyelitis with Rigidity and Myoclonus) which is acquired autoimmune disease in older patients.
Case 228 — Schoolchild with painful bilateral vision loss and brainstem signs HARD
A 9-year-old presents with rapid-onset bilateral severe visual loss with pain on eye movement over 1 week, then dysphagia, dysarthria, ophthalmoplegia. MRI: bilateral optic nerve and brainstem T2/FLAIR hyperintensities; longitudinally extensive cervical spinal cord lesion. Serum AQP4-IgG negative, MOG-IgG strongly positive.

DX

Diagnosis and the antibody-specific phenotype?
Show model answer
  • MOG antibody-associated disease (MOGAD) — pediatric presentation often with simultaneous optic neuritis (frequently BILATERAL — vs typically unilateral in MS), brainstem involvement, and LETM.
  • MOGAD distinct from MS (typically OCB-positive, periventricular lesions, smaller cord lesions) and NMOSD (AQP4-IgG, attacks more devastating, treatments differ).
  • Pediatric MOGAD presentations include: bilateral simultaneous optic neuritis (most common), ADEM-like illness, LETM, area postrema syndrome (less common than NMOSD), cortical encephalitis with seizures (FLAMES — FLAIR-hyperintense Lesions in Anti-MOG-associated Encephalitis with Seizures).
  • Most children have monophasic course; ~ 30–40% relapse.

TREATMENT

Acute management and long-term considerations?
Show model answer
  • ACUTE: high-dose IV methylprednisolone 30 mg/kg/day × 3–5 days followed by PROLONGED ORAL TAPER (over 3 months) — abrupt taper triggers relapse.
  • PLEX or IVIG for severe attacks or steroid-refractory disease.
  • LONG-TERM IMMUNOTHERAPY for relapsing patients:
  • - Rituximab (B cell depletion).
  • - IVIG monthly.
  • - Mycophenolate mofetil.
  • - Azathioprine.
  • - Tocilizumab, inebilizumab in development.
  • AVOID interferon-β and natalizumab (worsen MOGAD, as in NMOSD).
  • Distinguishing MOGAD vs NMOSD vs MS matters for therapy:
  • - NMOSD-specific drugs (eculizumab, satralizumab) NOT for MOGAD.
  • - MS DMTs (interferons, natalizumab) NOT for MOGAD.
  • Repeat MOG-IgG at 6 months — persistent positivity favours relapsing course.
  • Multidisciplinary: neurology, ophthalmology, rehab.
Case 229 — Infant with severe encephalopathy and pinpoint pupils after oral medication HARD
A 14-month-old toddler is found unresponsive 2 hours after the parents' bottle of clonidine was discovered open near him. Vitals: HR 50, BP 70/40, RR 6, GCS 6, pinpoint pupils, hypothermia (35.0°C). Naloxone 0.1 mg/kg IV given — no response. Cardiac monitor shows sinus bradycardia.

DX

Most likely toxidrome and the specific drug class effects?
Show model answer
  • Clonidine toxicity (also similar pattern with other α2-agonists: dexmedetomidine, tetrahydrozoline, oxymetazoline nasal sprays — all can be lethal in children even at small ingestions).
  • Classic 'sympatholytic' toxidrome: CNS depression (coma), respiratory depression, bradycardia, hypotension, miosis (mimics opioid toxidrome — major clinical confusion), HYPOTHERMIA.
  • Mechanism: central α2-agonist activity → reduced sympathetic outflow + sedation. Some peripheral α1 effects can cause initial transient hypertension before hypotension dominates.
  • Critically, may mimic opioid toxicity → naloxone trial reasonable but should not be expected to definitively reverse clonidine (some literature describes partial responses to high-dose naloxone, but evidence weak).
  • Onset within 30–60 minutes; effects can persist 12–24 hours.

TREATMENT

Supportive care priorities?
Show model answer
  • 1) ABCs: intubate for airway protection if GCS < 8 or hypoventilation; support ventilation.
  • 2) IV fluids for hypotension (often responds to volume).
  • 3) Atropine for severe bradycardia.
  • 4) Vasopressors (norepinephrine) for refractory hypotension.
  • 5) Naloxone trial — controversial but typically tried at higher doses (some clinicians use 0.1 mg/kg, repeated).
  • 6) Active rewarming for hypothermia.
  • 7) Activated charcoal if recent ingestion (within 1 h) AND airway protected — but absorption is rapid, often too late.
  • 8) NO specific antidote — supportive care until drug clears.
  • 9) Disposition: ICU admission until vital signs and mental status return to baseline (often 12–24 hours of observation).
  • 10) Investigate exposure: home medication safety, intentional ingestion, child protection assessment in young child.
  • 11) Family education about medication safety post-discharge.
Case 230 — Adolescent with severe muscle stiffness after psychiatric admission HARD
A 16-year-old with first psychotic episode was started on haloperidol 5 days ago. Now: T 40.0°C, severe muscle rigidity ('lead pipe'), altered mental status, autonomic instability (BP 175/105 ↔ 95/55, HR 140, profuse sweating), elevated CK 35 000, leukocytosis. Mental status: stuporous.

DX

Diagnosis and the differentials to distinguish?
Show model answer
  • Neuroleptic malignant syndrome (NMS).
  • Onset typically 4–14 days after starting neuroleptic, or after dose increase.
  • Tetrad: hyperthermia, severe muscle rigidity ('lead-pipe'), altered mental status, autonomic instability.
  • Lab: elevated CK (rhabdomyolysis), leukocytosis, elevated LDH/AST, AKI possible.
  • Pathophysiology: dopamine D2 receptor blockade → hypothalamic thermoregulation failure + extrapyramidal rigidity → muscle hyperactivity → hyperthermia + rhabdomyolysis.
  • DIFFERENTIALS:
  • - SEROTONIN SYNDROME (serotonergic drugs; hyperreflexia, clonus, myoclonus more prominent; less rigidity; onset within hours of drug exposure).
  • - MALIGNANT HYPERTHERMIA (anaesthetic-related; minutes-hours after exposure to volatile or succinylcholine; family history; RYR1 mutations).
  • - Anticholinergic toxidrome (dry skin, normal/low CK).
  • - Status dystonicus.
  • - Heat stroke (no rigidity).
  • - Sepsis with concurrent EPS.
  • NMS vs serotonin syndrome: subtle clinical differences and TIMING/drug history are key.

TREATMENT

Specific management and antidotes?
Show model answer
  • 1) STOP all neuroleptics immediately.
  • 2) Aggressive supportive care: cooling (cold water immersion if T > 40°C, ice packs to neck/axillae/groin, evaporative methods), IV fluids, electrolyte management, AKI prevention.
  • 3) Manage rhabdomyolysis: aggressive IV fluids targeting urine output 1–3 mL/kg/h, urinary alkalinisation with bicarbonate if myoglobinuria/AKI risk.
  • 4) SPECIFIC PHARMACOLOGY:
  • - DANTROLENE 1–2.5 mg/kg IV q6h — direct skeletal muscle ryanodine receptor inhibitor; reduces muscle hyperactivity and heat production.
  • - BROMOCRIPTINE 2.5–5 mg PO q6–8h — dopamine agonist; restores dopaminergic tone.
  • - AMANTADINE — alternative dopamine agonist.
  • - BENZODIAZEPINES (lorazepam, midazolam) — useful for agitation and milder cases.
  • 5) ICU admission for continuous monitoring.
  • 6) Mortality 5–20% with treatment, lower with early recognition.
  • 7) Disposition: avoid re-exposure to causative neuroleptic; if antipsychotic needed, choose atypical (lower D2 affinity, lower risk).
  • 8) Resume neuroleptics if essential — wait ≥ 2 weeks after recovery, use lower-potency atypical agent, monitor closely.
Case 231 — Teen with strict diet and seizures after recovery from anorexia MOD
A 17-year-old with severe restrictive anorexia (BMI 13) is admitted for refeeding. Day 4 of careful refeeding: develops confusion, ophthalmoplegia, ataxia, and a generalised seizure. Phosphate dropped from 1.2 to 0.4 mmol/L. Thiamine had NOT been given prior to nutrition initiation.

DX

Two complications to recognise here?
Show model answer
  • 1) REFEEDING SYNDROME — characteristic electrolyte derangement during reintroduction of nutrition in malnourished patient. Severe HYPOPHOSPHATAEMIA is the hallmark (also hypokalaemia, hypomagnesaemia, thiamine deficiency, fluid overload).
  • Pathophysiology: glucose load → insulin release → drives K, PO4, Mg into cells; consumes thiamine; depletes existing low stores → cardiac arrhythmia, congestive heart failure, neurologic complications.
  • 2) WERNICKE ENCEPHALOPATHY — acute thiamine deficiency. Classic triad: encephalopathy + ophthalmoplegia + ataxia. Often misdiagnosed (only ~ 30% have full triad).
  • Both extremely high-risk in severe restrictive eating disorders — preventable but devastating.
  • Mortality of refeeding syndrome up to 80% in untreated cases.

TREATMENT

Prevention and acute management?
Show model answer
  • PREVENTION (the optimal strategy):
  • - Always give THIAMINE 100 mg IV/IM/oral BEFORE any glucose load in malnourished patient (and continue daily).
  • - Start refeeding SLOWLY: typically 10–20 kcal/kg/day initially, advance over days.
  • - Replete phosphate, potassium, magnesium PROPHYLACTICALLY based on body weight and risk score.
  • - Daily electrolytes (PO4, K, Mg, Ca), glucose monitoring.
  • - Cardiac monitoring during initial refeeding.
  • - Multivitamin supplementation.
  • ACUTE refeeding syndrome management:
  • - SLOW or STOP refeeding temporarily.
  • - Aggressive electrolyte repletion (phosphate, potassium, magnesium).
  • - Thiamine 500 mg IV TID × 3 days (high dose for Wernicke) then maintenance.
  • - Cardiac monitoring (arrhythmias from hypokalaemia, hypomagnesaemia).
  • - Treat seizures (correct electrolytes simultaneously).
  • - Resume careful, slower refeeding once electrolytes stable.
  • WERNICKE management: IV thiamine 500 mg TID for 3 days, then 250 mg daily for 5 days, then oral maintenance.
  • Multidisciplinary: ICU, eating disorders team, dietitian, psychiatry; family involvement.
  • Long-term sequelae: Korsakoff syndrome (anterograde amnesia) can be permanent if untreated.
Case 232 — Schoolchild with bilateral facial weakness and tachycardia HARD
An 8-year-old returns from a camping trip in eastern Tennessee with 3 days of headache, then ascending paralysis over 12 hours: cannot walk, then weak grip, then bilateral facial droop, then bulbar weakness. No fever now (had fever 3 days ago). Parents removed an attached tick from his scalp this morning.

DX

Most likely diagnosis given the geography and tick exposure?
Show model answer
  • TICK PARALYSIS (American dog tick — Dermacentor variabilis in eastern US; D. andersoni in northwest; Ixodes holocyclus in Australia).
  • Toxin (holocyclotoxin or related) secreted by gravid female tick during prolonged attachment (typically > 4–7 days).
  • Toxin blocks presynaptic ACh release at neuromuscular junction (some say also impairs sodium channel function in axon).
  • Classic presentation: ascending flaccid paralysis (mimics GBS), often cranial nerve involvement, NO sensory loss (vs GBS which has sensory features).
  • Reversal within hours-days of tick removal — pathognomonic feature.
  • Differentials: GBS (typically slower onset, follows infection by weeks; CSF protein elevated; sensory often involved; doesn't reverse with anything quickly), botulism, organophosphate, snake envenomation.

TREATMENT

Critical management?
Show model answer
  • 1) THOROUGHLY SEARCH for the tick — examine entire scalp, behind ears, neck, axillae, groin, between toes. Often more than one tick.
  • 2) Remove the tick(s) COMPLETELY — use fine forceps, grasp close to skin, pull straight out without twisting (mouthparts should come out intact).
  • 3) Supportive care during recovery: airway support if respiratory weakness, mechanical ventilation if needed.
  • 4) Reversal usually begins within hours of tick removal, complete within 24–72 hours.
  • 5) Australian tick paralysis (Ixodes holocyclus) is more severe and may continue to worsen for 24–48 hours after tick removal — antitoxin (canine-derived) available in Australia, not US.
  • 6) Check for co-infections in tick-borne illness regions: Rocky Mountain spotted fever (doxycycline), Lyme disease, ehrlichiosis, babesiosis, anaplasmosis. May treat empirically with doxycycline if RMSF risk significant in some regions.
  • 7) Tick prevention education for family: tick checks, protective clothing, DEET/permethrin, prompt removal.
  • 8) Pearl: TICK PARALYSIS = GBS until you find the tick. Look thoroughly.
Case 233 — Schoolchild with new-onset right facial pain and decreased corneal sensation MOD
A 12-year-old presents with 3 weeks of severe shooting right-sided facial pain in V2 distribution, triggered by chewing, brushing teeth, light touch on cheek. Now: decreased corneal sensation on right, weakness of right masticatory muscles. MRI brain: 2 cm enhancing mass in the right cerebellopontine angle, indistinguishable from an enhancing tail.

DX

Most likely diagnosis and the syndromic association in children?
Show model answer
  • Cerebellopontine angle (CPA) mass — given pediatric age and trigeminal nerve involvement, most likely vestibular schwannoma OR meningioma.
  • UNILATERAL pediatric vestibular schwannoma: rare; investigate further.
  • BILATERAL vestibular schwannomas = NEUROFIBROMATOSIS TYPE 2 (NF2) — autosomal dominant, NF2/merlin gene on 22q12.
  • Other NF2 features: meningiomas, ependymomas, schwannomas of other cranial and spinal nerves, juvenile posterior subcapsular lens opacities, retinal hamartomas.
  • Differential CPA mass: vestibular/trigeminal schwannoma, meningioma, epidermoid cyst, arachnoid cyst, metastasis (rare in children).

NEXT STEP

Workup and management?
Show model answer
  • 1) MRI BRAIN AND ENTIRE NEURAXIS with contrast — assess for additional schwannomas, meningiomas, ependymomas (NF2 surveillance).
  • 2) Ophthalmology — slit-lamp for cataracts, fundoscopy for retinal hamartomas.
  • 3) Audiology — pure-tone, speech audiometry, ABR.
  • 4) Genetic counselling — NF2 gene testing.
  • 5) Family screening if NF2 confirmed.
  • 6) MULTIDISCIPLINARY MANAGEMENT of the mass:
  • - Observation with serial imaging for small, asymptomatic lesions.
  • - Stereotactic radiosurgery (gamma knife) for selected cases.
  • - Microsurgical resection — for larger or progressive lesions; balance against hearing/facial nerve preservation.
  • - BEVACIZUMAB (anti-VEGF) — effective in NF2-related vestibular schwannomas, particularly for hearing preservation.
  • 7) Long-term surveillance: annual MRI brain + spine, audiology, ophthalmology.
  • 8) AVOID radiation when possible in NF2 (concern for secondary tumours).
Case 234 — Infant with rapidly progressive macrocephaly and 'sunset' eyes MOD
A 4-month-old infant presents with HC crossing percentiles from 50th to 99th over 2 months, full and tense anterior fontanelle, dilated scalp veins, downward conjugate gaze deviation when reaching for objects, vomiting, and increasing irritability. Cranial ultrasound: marked dilatation of lateral and 3rd ventricles with normal 4th ventricle.

DX

Diagnosis and the level of CSF obstruction?
Show model answer
  • Obstructive (non-communicating) hydrocephalus at the AQUEDUCT OF SYLVIUS.
  • Pattern: dilatation of lateral and 3rd ventricles with NORMAL 4th ventricle = aqueductal obstruction.
  • Causes in infancy:
  • - Congenital aqueductal stenosis (idiopathic, or X-linked L1CAM mutation with corpus callosum agenesis + adducted thumbs in male infants).
  • - Post-haemorrhagic (post-IVH of prematurity).
  • - Tectal plate glioma (slow-growing).
  • - Web/membrane in the aqueduct.
  • 'Sunset eyes' = tonic downward conjugate gaze due to pressure on dorsal midbrain (pretectal area) — sign of raised ICP in infants with open sutures (akin to Parinaud syndrome in older patients).

TREATMENT

Surgical options and how to choose?
Show model answer
  • Two main options: VP SHUNT vs ENDOSCOPIC THIRD VENTRICULOSTOMY (ETV).
  • ETV preferred for non-communicating hydrocephalus (aqueductal obstruction) in children > 1 year — creates direct communication from 3rd ventricle into basal cisterns, bypassing the obstruction.
  • ETV success score (Kulkarni): based on AGE, AETIOLOGY, PRIOR SHUNT — higher scores = better candidate.
  • For infants < 1 year (and especially < 6 months): consider ETV + choroid plexus cauterisation (ETV/CPC, Warf protocol) — improves success.
  • VP SHUNT: works for ANY hydrocephalus, but lifetime risk of:
  • - Mechanical malfunction (highest in first year: 30–40%).
  • - Infection (5–10%).
  • - Overshunting / slit-ventricle syndrome.
  • - Need for revisions through life.
  • Modern programmable valves reduce some complications.
  • For this infant (presumed aqueductal stenosis): ETV would ideally be tried; if fails, VP shunt; intra-operative endoscopy can assess aqueductal anatomy.
Case 235 — Adolescent with unilateral throbbing headache and reversible aphasia MOD
A 14-year-old has 3 years of episodic severe right-sided throbbing headaches lasting 4–24 hours with photophobia, phonophobia, nausea, vomiting. Sometimes preceded by 30 min of expressive aphasia or left-sided hand paraesthesias that resolve completely. Family history: maternal aunts with similar episodes. Brain MRI normal.

DX

Diagnosis and classification?
Show model answer
  • Migraine with aura (formerly classical migraine) — by ICHD-3 criteria.
  • Definition: recurrent disorder of fully reversible aura symptoms (visual, sensory, speech/language, motor, brainstem, retinal) developing gradually over ≥ 5 min, lasting 5–60 min, followed by (or accompanied by) headache.
  • Most common aura: visual (90%) — fortification spectra, scintillating scotoma. Then sensory (paraesthesias), speech (aphasia/dysarthria), motor (hemiplegic migraine — distinct variant), brainstem (vertigo, ataxia, diplopia, tinnitus).
  • This case has speech and sensory aura.
  • Differential to consider in adolescent: TIA (would be expected to leave deficit or have recurring stereotyped pattern; rare in absence of vascular abnormality), focal seizures, MELAS (stroke-like episodes), CADASIL (rare in adolescents).

TREATMENT

Acute and preventive treatment?
Show model answer
  • ACUTE:
  • - First-line: NSAIDs (ibuprofen, naproxen), acetaminophen.
  • - Add antiemetic (ondansetron, metoclopramide).
  • - Triptans: FDA-approved in adolescents (almotriptan, rizatriptan, sumatriptan nasal spray, zolmitriptan nasal spray) — safe in migraine with typical aura but CAUTION/AVOID in hemiplegic migraine and migraine with brainstem aura.
  • - CGRP antagonists (gepants): rimegepant, ubrogepant — FDA-approved for adults; pediatric trials ongoing.
  • - Rest in quiet dark room; IV fluids if dehydrated.
  • PREVENTIVE (≥ 4 disabling attacks/month, or severe attacks):
  • - First-line in adolescents: topiramate (FDA-approved 12+), propranolol.
  • - Amitriptyline (low dose).
  • - Magnesium, riboflavin (B2), coenzyme Q10 — supplements with modest evidence.
  • - Onabotulinum toxin for chronic migraine (adolescents with chronic disease).
  • - CGRP monoclonal antibodies (erenumab, fremanezumab, galcanezumab, eptinezumab) — FDA-approved for adults; in development for adolescents.
  • LIFESTYLE: sleep hygiene, regular meals, hydration, exercise, stress management, identify and avoid triggers (food triggers controversial — individualised).
  • School accommodations.
Case 236 — Teen with severe headache 2 weeks after viral illness, papilloedema MOD
A 15-year-old developed an EBV mononucleosis-like illness 3 weeks ago. Now: progressive headache, transient visual obscurations, pulsatile tinnitus, diplopia on right gaze. Fundoscopy: bilateral papilloedema. MRI brain: empty sella, no mass; MRV: occlusion of the right transverse sinus and partial occlusion of left transverse sinus. LP opening pressure 38 cmH₂O.

DX

Diagnosis with the broader differential to recognise?
Show model answer
  • Cerebral venous sinus thrombosis (CVST) following EBV — with secondary intracranial hypertension.
  • This is NOT primary idiopathic intracranial hypertension (IIH/pseudotumor) — the venous sinus thrombosis is the cause.
  • Important distinction: when MRV shows sinus thrombosis, treat the CVST (anticoagulation) AND manage raised ICP separately.
  • CVST often presents subacutely; differential ICP and seizure threshold can develop over days-weeks.
  • Predisposing factors: dehydration, infection (sinusitis/mastoiditis/EBV-mediated immune effects), OCP, thrombophilia, malignancy.

TREATMENT

Combined management?
Show model answer
  • 1) ANTICOAGULATION for CVST: therapeutic LMWH (enoxaparin 1 mg/kg q12h) → bridge to oral anticoagulation; treat for 3–6 months minimum, longer if persistent thrombophilia.
  • 2) MANAGE RAISED ICP:
  • - Acetazolamide (carbonic anhydrase inhibitor) — reduces CSF production.
  • - Serial therapeutic LPs to remove CSF.
  • - If vision threatened: optic nerve sheath fenestration OR CSF diversion (LP shunt, VP shunt).
  • - Endovascular venous sinus stenting in refractory cases with persistent sinus stenosis after recanalisation.
  • 3) Thrombophilia workup at completion of anticoagulation: factor V Leiden, prothrombin G20210A, protein C/S, antithrombin, antiphospholipid antibodies, JAK2 etc.
  • 4) Avoid future risk: stop OCP if applicable; prophylactic anticoagulation in high-risk states (surgery, immobility) if thrombophilia identified.
  • 5) Repeat MRV at 3 months to assess recanalisation; persistent occlusion may not require continued anticoagulation if asymptomatic but should be discussed.
  • 6) Visual fields, OCT, ophthalmology follow-up.
Case 237 — Newborn with cleft lip-palate, microcephaly, and bilateral 2-3 toe syndactyly MOD
A newborn boy has microcephaly, ptosis, anteverted nares, cleft palate, ambiguous genitalia (small phallus, hypospadias), 2-3 toe syndactyly bilaterally. Plasma cholesterol low; 7-dehydrocholesterol elevated.

DX

Diagnosis and the cholesterol biosynthesis defect?
Show model answer
  • Smith-Lemli-Opitz syndrome (SLOS) — autosomal recessive.
  • Mutations in DHCR7 gene (chromosome 11q13.4) — 7-dehydrocholesterol reductase deficiency.
  • Final step of cholesterol biosynthesis blocked → low cholesterol + elevated 7-dehydrocholesterol (toxic precursor).
  • Cholesterol essential for sonic hedgehog signalling, cell membranes, steroidogenesis, myelination, lipid rafts.
  • Features (SLOS phenotype):
  • - Distinctive facial features: microcephaly, ptosis, anteverted nares, micrognathia, cleft palate.
  • - Limbs: 2-3 toe syndactyly (highly characteristic), polydactyly, short proximal phalanges.
  • - Genitalia: ambiguous in males (hypospadias to female phenotype with XY chromosomes), undescended testes.
  • - Multi-organ: cardiac defects, GI (Hirschsprung, pyloric stenosis), renal, adrenal insufficiency.
  • - CNS: microcephaly, agenesis of corpus callosum, cerebellar hypoplasia, holoprosencephaly spectrum.
  • - Behavioural: autism spectrum, intellectual disability, self-injury.

TREATMENT

Management approach?
Show model answer
  • CHOLESTEROL SUPPLEMENTATION — provides exogenous cholesterol (since endogenous synthesis impaired).
  • - Egg yolks, cholesterol-rich foods, pharmaceutical cholesterol crystals.
  • - Improves growth and some biochemical parameters; less clear effect on neurodevelopmental outcomes.
  • SIMVASTATIN — controversial; theoretically reduces 7-DHC accumulation by inhibiting HMG-CoA reductase upstream; small benefit in some studies, harm in others — generally NOT recommended.
  • Multidisciplinary care: cardiology (CHD), GI (feeding issues), urology (genital surgery if needed, hormonal management), orthopaedics (cleft repair, syndactyly), neurology, behavioural support, developmental services.
  • Genetic counselling: AR, 25% recurrence; carrier screening offered in some populations.
  • Prenatal diagnosis: elevated 7-DHC in amniotic fluid; molecular testing of DHCR7.
  • Severity correlates with degree of enzyme activity — wide phenotypic spectrum.
Case 238 — Adolescent with episodic stupor, sometimes ataxia, normal between MOD
A 13-year-old has had 4 episodes over 6 months of sudden-onset hypersomnia lasting 7–14 days each, during which he sleeps 18–20 hours/day, eats voraciously when awake, exhibits hypersexual behaviour, irritability, and a sense of derealization. He returns to baseline completely between episodes. EEG during episode: generalised slowing. MRI normal.

DX

Diagnosis?
Show model answer
  • Kleine-Levin syndrome (KLS) — recurrent hypersomnia of unknown aetiology.
  • Rare; predominantly affects adolescent males (M:F ~ 2–3:1).
  • Clinical features (in episodes): excessive sleep (often > 18 h/day), hyperphagia, hypersexuality, derealization/altered cognition, irritability.
  • Episodes typically last 1–3 weeks; recur every few months; complete return to baseline between.
  • Often triggered by viral illness, alcohol use, sleep deprivation, stress.
  • Self-limited course in most (median 8–14 years from onset to resolution).
  • Differential: depression with atypical features, bipolar (manic features can resemble during episode), drug intoxication, encephalitis (MRI/CSF usually normal in KLS), pseudotumor.

TREATMENT

Management options?
Show model answer
  • NO proven disease-modifying therapy.
  • Mood stabilisers (LITHIUM most commonly tried, valproate) — some reduce episode frequency in retrospective series.
  • Stimulants during episodes (modafinil, methylphenidate) — modest benefit for sleep.
  • Antiepileptics, lamotrigine — variable evidence.
  • SUPPORTIVE: ensure safety during episodes (driving, working at heights), educate family, school accommodations.
  • Counselling about prognosis: usually self-limited, episodes become less frequent and less severe over years.
  • Multidisciplinary: neurology, sleep medicine, psychiatry.
  • Distinguish from narcolepsy (chronic vs episodic), atypical depression (mood phenomenology), recurrent viral encephalitis (would have MRI/CSF abnormalities).
Case 239 — Toddler with episodic stiffening triggered by emotion HARD
A 4-year-old has episodes since infancy of brief 'episodes' where she becomes still, falls limp without losing consciousness (continues to track and respond), triggered by surprise, laughter, or excitement. They last 30–60 seconds. She also has hyperkinetic movement of trunk and face when active. Family history: father has similar episodes.

DX

Diagnosis and the related phenomenon?
Show model answer
  • Episodic CATAPLEXY-like episodes — but in a 4-year-old with non-narcolepsy phenomenology suggests CATAPLEXY in setting of specific syndromes.
  • Could represent: idiopathic cataplexy, Niemann-Pick type C with cataplexy/'gelastic cataplexy' (especially associated with laughter), or familial cataplexy variants.
  • Cataplexy without other narcolepsy features (no hypocretin deficiency, no daytime sleepiness, no sleep paralysis) is uncommon — workup is broader.
  • Pediatric cataplexy can be subtle: facial flaccidity, head drop, knee buckling, often without complete fall.
  • If associated with developmental issues, splenomegaly, vertical gaze palsy → consider Niemann-Pick C.
  • Status cataplecticus — prolonged cataplexy, especially when withdrawing from anti-cataplectic medications.

NEXT STEP

Workup?
Show model answer
  • 1) Comprehensive HISTORY: episode triggers, duration, frequency, family history, daytime sleepiness, sleep architecture, growth/development.
  • 2) Physical exam: organomegaly (Niemann-Pick), eye movements (vertical gaze palsy), neurological exam.
  • 3) Polysomnography + Multiple Sleep Latency Test if narcolepsy suspected.
  • 4) CSF hypocretin-1 measurement if narcolepsy with cataplexy considered.
  • 5) For Niemann-Pick C suspicion: oxysterol biomarkers (cholestane-3β,5α,6β-triol, 7-ketocholesterol — sensitive screening test), then molecular testing NPC1/NPC2; filipin staining of fibroblasts confirmatory.
  • 6) MRI brain.
  • 7) Ophthalmology for vertical gaze palsy.
  • 8) Genetic testing as guided.
  • 9) Treatment varies by cause: narcolepsy → sodium oxybate, modafinil, TCAs; Niemann-Pick C → miglustat; idiopathic → individualised.
Case 240 — Schoolchild with chronic intermittent intoxications and aciduria MOD
A 7-year-old presents during a flu illness with vomiting, confusion, ataxia, distinctive 'sweaty feet' or 'old cheese' body odour. He had similar episodes during illnesses at age 2 and 4 with full recovery. Urine organic acids during episode: markedly elevated isovaleryl-glycine and 3-hydroxyisovaleric acid.

DX

Diagnosis and the metabolic step affected?
Show model answer
  • Isovaleric acidemia (IVA) — autosomal recessive.
  • Isovaleryl-CoA dehydrogenase deficiency (IVD gene) — third step in leucine catabolism.
  • Accumulation of isovaleric acid → metabolic acidosis + 'sweaty feet' or 'old cheese' odour.
  • This patient's intermittent presentation suggests later-onset, milder phenotype (compared to severe neonatal form which presents in first days of life with encephalopathy + acidosis + bone marrow suppression).
  • Newborn screening detects via elevated C5 (isovalerylcarnitine).
  • Other classic organic acidemias to know: propionic acidemia (propionyl-CoA carboxylase), methylmalonic acidemia (methylmalonyl-CoA mutase or B12 metabolism), maple syrup urine disease (BCKAD — branched-chain α-ketoacid dehydrogenase).

TREATMENT

Acute decompensation and chronic management?
Show model answer
  • ACUTE:
  • - STOP protein intake.
  • - IV D10 high glucose infusion rate to suppress catabolism.
  • - GLYCINE supplementation (250 mg/kg/day) — conjugates with isovaleryl-CoA to form non-toxic isovalerylglycine (renally excreted).
  • - L-CARNITINE 100 mg/kg/day — replaces depleted carnitine, conjugates with isovaleryl-CoA to form isovalerylcarnitine (renally excreted).
  • - Sodium bicarbonate for severe acidemia.
  • - Hemodialysis for very severe / refractory cases.
  • CHRONIC:
  • - Leucine-restricted diet (limit natural protein, special metabolic formula).
  • - Oral glycine and L-carnitine maintenance.
  • - Sick-day rules: high-calorie low-protein diet during illness; emergency action plan; early IV glucose if not tolerating orally.
  • - Multidisciplinary metabolic clinic follow-up.
  • - Genetic counselling (AR, 25% recurrence); newborn screening flags subsequent siblings.
  • Generally good outcomes with timely treatment of intermittent form; severe neonatal form has worse prognosis.
Case 241 — Adolescent with progressive proximal weakness and elevated CK during exercise MOD
A 16-year-old presents with recurrent episodes of brown urine and severe muscle pain after vigorous exercise. CK rises to 20 000 during episodes, returns to mildly elevated baseline (2 000) between. Forearm ischaemic exercise test: NO rise in lactate, BUT exaggerated rise in ammonia. Muscle biopsy: subsarcolemmal glycogen vacuoles; absent myophosphorylase staining.

DX

Diagnosis and the enzyme involved?
Show model answer
  • McArdle disease (glycogen storage disease type V, myophosphorylase deficiency) — autosomal recessive PYGM gene mutations.
  • Myophosphorylase is the rate-limiting enzyme for glycogenolysis in muscle.
  • Defect → inability to access glycogen as energy substrate during intense exercise → accumulates glycogen + muscle ischemic damage during anaerobic exercise.
  • Classic features: exercise intolerance (especially anaerobic — sprints, isometric), 'second-wind' phenomenon (improvement after brief rest as muscle switches to FFA/blood glucose fuel), exercise-induced rhabdomyolysis, myoglobinuria, mild persistent CK elevation.
  • Forearm ischaemic exercise test: failure of lactate rise (cannot generate lactate from glycogen due to myophosphorylase deficiency) WITH normal/exaggerated ammonia rise = classic finding distinguishing from myoadenylate deaminase deficiency (where ammonia DOESN'T rise).

TREATMENT

Management strategy?
Show model answer
  • 1) EDUCATION about disease and exercise prescription — most important.
  • 2) WARM-UP and gradual increase in exercise intensity — facilitates 'second wind' (transition to FFA oxidation).
  • 3) AVOID intense isometric exercise and unaccustomed strenuous exertion.
  • 4) Carbohydrate intake BEFORE exercise (oral sucrose 75 g 30 min before) improves exercise tolerance (provides blood glucose for muscle when glycogenolysis impaired).
  • 5) Aerobic training programmes — improves substrate utilisation, increases mitochondrial enzymes.
  • 6) MEDICAL ALERT bracelet — risk during anaesthesia (use caution with succinylcholine — can trigger rhabdomyolysis).
  • 7) During rhabdomyolysis: aggressive IV fluids targeting urine output 1–3 mL/kg/h, urinary alkalinisation if myoglobinuria with rising creatinine.
  • 8) Monitor renal function — AKI from myoglobinuria is the major acute complication.
  • 9) GENETIC counselling — AR with 25% recurrence in carrier parents.
  • 10) Multidisciplinary: neuromuscular clinic, exercise physiology, sports medicine.
  • Most patients have stable course; progressive weakness in late adulthood in some.
Case 242 — Schoolchild with progressive headache and vomiting on rising HARD
A 7-year-old developed 6 weeks of early-morning headache with vomiting on rising. Now: ataxia, head tilt to right, intermittent diplopia. Fundoscopy: bilateral papilloedema. MRI: 4 cm enhancing mass in posterior fossa midline, partly cystic, with mural nodule, marked supratentorial hydrocephalus.

DX

Most likely diagnosis given imaging and age?
Show model answer
  • Cerebellar pilocytic astrocytoma (WHO Grade I).
  • Most common pediatric brain tumour overall.
  • Classic imaging: cystic mass with enhancing mural nodule, often in cerebellum (vermian or hemispheric).
  • Differential of posterior fossa tumour in 5–10 yo: medulloblastoma (more midline, solid, restricted diffusion), ependymoma (often through foramina, calcification), atypical teratoid/rhabdoid tumour (younger children), brainstem glioma.
  • BRAF fusion (KIAA1549-BRAF) common in pilocytic astrocytomas.

NEXT STEP

Immediate management?
Show model answer
  • 1) Treat hydrocephalus emergently: EVD or endoscopic 3rd ventriculostomy.
  • 2) Dexamethasone to reduce oedema.
  • 3) Avoid lumbar puncture (herniation risk).
  • 4) Pre-op spinal MRI to stage (though pilocytic rarely metastasises).
  • 5) Maximal safe surgical resection — often curative for pilocytic astrocytoma.
  • 6) If incompletely resected: observe vs targeted therapy (BRAF/MEK inhibitors for BRAF-altered tumours; chemotherapy for younger children where radiation avoided).
  • 7) Radiation reserved for refractory/recurrent disease in older patients.
  • 8) Long-term follow-up MRI; endocrine surveillance if any sellar/parasellar component.
Case 243 — Adolescent with new diabetes insipidus and visual field defect MOD
A 14-year-old presents with 3 months of polyuria and polydipsia (10–15 L/day), confirmed central diabetes insipidus. Visual field testing: bitemporal hemianopia. MRI: 3 cm enhancing suprasellar mass with cystic and solid components, peripheral calcifications.

DX

Most likely diagnosis and the classic features?
Show model answer
  • Craniopharyngioma — adamantinomatous subtype most common in pediatrics.
  • Arises from embryonic remnants of Rathke pouch.
  • Imaging triad: solid component + cystic component + calcifications (~ 90% have calcium on imaging).
  • Bitemporal hemianopia from suprasellar compression of optic chiasm.
  • Hypothalamic-pituitary axis involvement: diabetes insipidus, growth hormone deficiency, hypogonadism, hypoadrenalism, hypothyroidism — full hypopituitarism common.
  • Two histologic subtypes: adamantinomatous (children, more aggressive, CTNNB1 mutations) and papillary (adults, BRAF V600E).

TREATMENT

Management considerations?
Show model answer
  • Stress-dose hydrocortisone BEFORE surgery (adrenal insufficiency likely).
  • Volume status management (DI causes massive fluid losses) — DDAVP and fluid balance.
  • Maximal safe surgical resection — endoscopic endonasal approach often preferred.
  • RADIATION (especially proton beam in pediatric centres) for subtotal resection or recurrence.
  • BRAF/MEK inhibitors for papillary subtype with BRAF mutation.
  • Post-op hypothalamic obesity (highly characteristic complication) — multidisciplinary management with endocrine, nutrition, behaviour.
  • Lifelong hormone replacement: cortisol, T4, GH, sex steroids, DDAVP.
  • Visual surveillance, neurocognitive support, school accommodations.
  • Recurrence common (20–40%); long-term follow-up.
Case 244 — Infant with severe macrocephaly and split sutures from birth MOD
A neonate at birth has macrocephaly (HC > 99th centile), separated sutures, bulging fontanelle, downward eye deviation. Antenatal US at 32 weeks showed enlarging ventricles. MRI postnatally: huge supratentorial ventricles + 4th ventricle dilation; cerebellar vermian hypoplasia with cystic dilation of 4th ventricle communicating with posterior fossa.

DX

Diagnosis and the malformation triad?
Show model answer
  • Dandy-Walker malformation.
  • Classic triad: (1) hypoplasia or agenesis of the cerebellar vermis, (2) cystic dilation of the 4th ventricle filling the posterior fossa, (3) enlarged posterior fossa with elevated tentorium and torcular Herophili.
  • Hydrocephalus develops in 80% (obstruction of 4th ventricle outflow or aqueduct).
  • Associated CNS anomalies: agenesis of corpus callosum, neuronal migration disorders, encephaloceles.
  • Systemic associations: cardiac defects, facial dysmorphism, renal anomalies, genetic syndromes (PHACE syndrome, Walker-Warburg, etc.).
  • Dandy-Walker VARIANT (or spectrum): less severe vermian hypoplasia with smaller posterior fossa cyst.

TREATMENT

Management?
Show model answer
  • CSF diversion for hydrocephalus: VP shunt or cystoperitoneal shunt (drains the posterior fossa cyst). Some require BOTH (combined shunting).
  • Endoscopic 3rd ventriculostomy with caution given anatomic distortions.
  • Imaging surveillance for shunt function and brain development.
  • Multidisciplinary care: neurosurgery, neurology, developmental paediatrics, genetics.
  • Genetic evaluation for syndromic associations.
  • Cardiac, renal screening.
  • Long-term: neurocognitive outcomes variable depending on associated anomalies and shunt complications.
  • Family genetic counselling (multifactorial in most; specific syndromes have specific inheritance).
Case 245 — Newborn with seizures and bilateral globus pallidus T1 hyperintensity MOD
A late-preterm newborn born at 35 weeks to a G6P5 mother (poor prenatal care) develops dystonic posturing, opisthotonus, hypertonia, high-pitched cry on day 6. Blood unconjugated bilirubin 32 mg/dL. MRI on day 10: bilateral T1 hyperintensity in globus pallidus and subthalamic nuclei.

DX

Diagnosis and the principal site of injury?
Show model answer
  • Acute bilirubin encephalopathy / kernicterus.
  • Severe unconjugated hyperbilirubinaemia → bilirubin crosses BBB → preferential deposition in basal ganglia, especially globus pallidus and subthalamic nuclei (high lipid content + susceptible neurons).
  • Also affects hippocampus, brainstem auditory nuclei (sensorineural deafness), oculomotor nuclei (gaze palsy).
  • Risk factors: prematurity, dehydration/poor feeding (especially exclusively breastfed without supplementation), haemolytic disease (ABO/Rh incompatibility, G6PD, hereditary spherocytosis), sepsis, hypoalbuminaemia.
  • Phases: phase 1 (lethargy, hypotonia, poor feed), phase 2 (hypertonia/retrocollis/opisthotonus/fever — current), phase 3 (chronic — choreoathetoid CP, sensorineural deafness, gaze palsy, dental dysplasia).

TREATMENT

Acute management?
Show model answer
  • 1) AGGRESSIVE phototherapy: multiple banks, intensive, maximum skin exposure.
  • 2) DOUBLE-VOLUME EXCHANGE TRANSFUSION when bilirubin exceeds exchange threshold per AAP 2022 guidelines (varies by gestational age, hours of life, risk factors). For this severe presentation with neurological signs, exchange is INDICATED REGARDLESS of bilirubin level.
  • 3) IVIG 0.5–1 g/kg for haemolytic disease (immune-mediated; reduces ongoing haemolysis).
  • 4) Aggressive hydration to dilute bilirubin and reduce enterohepatic circulation.
  • 5) Albumin infusion to bind free bilirubin (controversial).
  • 6) Treat underlying cause: sepsis workup, ABO/Rh testing, Coombs test, G6PD screen — do not delay treatment.
  • 7) Long-term surveillance: ABR for hearing, developmental assessment, MRI follow-up; high risk of choreoathetoid cerebral palsy, gaze palsy, dental enamel hypoplasia, intellectual deficits.
  • 8) Prevention: pre-discharge bilirubin screening per AAP, early follow-up for breastfeeding jaundice, parental education.
Case 246 — Child with episodic flank pain, haematuria, and microcephaly HARD
A 6-year-old has microcephaly, mild developmental delay, dystonic movements. Now presents with severe flank pain, gross haematuria, and ultrasound showing bilateral renal stones. Urine: hypercalciuria + hyperoxaluria. Serum uric acid normal. Genetic testing: AGT mutation.

DX

Diagnosis and the enzyme defect?
Show model answer
  • Primary hyperoxaluria type 1 (PH1) — autosomal recessive.
  • Mutations in AGXT gene → alanine-glyoxylate aminotransferase (AGT) deficiency.
  • AGT is a peroxisomal enzyme that converts glyoxylate to glycine; without it, glyoxylate is oxidised to oxalate → massive oxalate overproduction.
  • Oxalate deposition: kidneys (recurrent calcium oxalate stones, nephrocalcinosis, progressive kidney injury → ESKD), then systemic (oxalosis — heart, bone, retina, peripheral nerves, CNS).
  • Microcephaly + dystonia in this child may reflect AGXT-related oxalosis affecting CNS, but most PH1 children have isolated renal disease.

TREATMENT

Specific therapy and renal management?
Show model answer
  • HIGH-DOSE PYRIDOXINE (B6) 10–20 mg/kg/day — > 50% of PH1 patients are responders (especially specific AGXT mutations like G170R, F152I).
  • HIGH FLUID INTAKE (3 L/m²/day) — prevents stone formation and crystal deposition.
  • ORAL CITRATE OR MAGNESIUM — inhibits crystal formation.
  • LUMASIRAN (Oxlumo) — siRNA targeting HAO1 (glycolate oxidase) → reduces oxalate production. FDA-approved 2020 — game-changer for PH1.
  • NEDOSIRAN — alternative siRNA in development.
  • Stone management: medical expulsive therapy, ESWL, ureteroscopy as needed.
  • END-STAGE KIDNEY DISEASE: combined liver-kidney transplant (liver replaces the missing enzyme, kidney replaces failed organ) — definitive treatment historically; lumasiran may reduce need.
  • Hemodialysis ALONE inadequate (cannot keep up with oxalate generation in oliguric ESKD).
  • Genetic counselling: AR with 25% recurrence; family screening.
Case 247 — Schoolchild with progressive ataxia and reduced reflexes MOD
A 10-year-old has 18 months of progressive gait ataxia, dysarthria, dysmetria, absent ankle reflexes, vibration sense impaired, vitamin E undetectable. No GI symptoms. Family history of consanguineous parents. Liver enzymes normal.

DX

Diagnosis and the underlying defect?
Show model answer
  • Ataxia with vitamin E deficiency (AVED) — autosomal recessive.
  • Mutations in TTPA (α-tocopherol transfer protein) gene — defective hepatic incorporation of α-tocopherol into very-low-density lipoproteins → very low circulating vitamin E.
  • Clinically RESEMBLES Friedreich ataxia (progressive ataxia, areflexia, dysarthria, sensory loss) but caused by treatable vitamin deficiency.
  • Differential of low vitamin E: AVED (genetic), abetalipoproteinaemia (lipid malabsorption, acanthocytosis, retinitis pigmentosa, low cholesterol), hypobetalipoproteinaemia, fat malabsorption (CF, biliary atresia, cholestatic liver disease, short gut).
  • This is treatable — distinguishing from Friedreich is critical.

TREATMENT

Treatment principles?
Show model answer
  • HIGH-DOSE ORAL VITAMIN E (α-tocopherol) 800–1200 mg/day in adults; weight-based in children.
  • Aim for vitamin E levels in normal range (often need very high doses given defective transfer).
  • Treatment STOPS PROGRESSION and may partially reverse symptoms (especially if started early).
  • Lifelong therapy.
  • Concurrent vitamins A and K (other fat-soluble vitamins may be affected if absorption issue) — check levels.
  • Genetic counselling: AR with 25% recurrence; sibling screening (early treatment can prevent symptoms).
  • Monitor levels and clinical response.
  • Multidisciplinary: neurology, metabolic, physiotherapy, ophthalmology (retinal changes possible).
Case 248 — Newborn with seizures and severe lactic acidosis after surgery HARD
A 3-day-old infant on prolonged propofol infusion (60 µg/kg/min for 4 days) during post-op cardiac surgery develops bradycardia, hypotension, severe metabolic acidosis (pH 7.05, lactate 16), rhabdomyolysis (CK 80 000), hyperkalaemia, AKI. Triglycerides 8 mmol/L.

DX

Diagnosis?
Show model answer
  • PROPOFOL INFUSION SYNDROME (PRIS).
  • Risk factors: propofol infusion > 4 mg/kg/h (> 67 µg/kg/min) for > 48 hours, especially in young children (pediatric ICU sedation should not use propofol infusion for prolonged sedation).
  • Mechanism: propofol disrupts mitochondrial fatty acid oxidation and mitochondrial respiratory chain → impaired ATP production + accumulation of free fatty acids → multi-organ failure.
  • Classic constellation: metabolic acidosis, rhabdomyolysis, lipidaemia (triglycerides ↑), cardiac dysfunction (bradycardia, heart block, asystole), AKI.
  • Mortality 30–50% even with treatment.
  • This is the reason propofol is contraindicated for prolonged ICU sedation in pediatric patients (acceptable for short procedures, brief infusions).

TREATMENT

Immediate management?
Show model answer
  • 1) STOP PROPOFOL IMMEDIATELY — replace with alternative sedation (midazolam, dexmedetomidine, ketamine).
  • 2) Aggressive supportive care: vasoactive support for cardiac dysfunction, treat hyperkalaemia, manage acidosis with bicarbonate (controversial — lactic acidosis), aggressive volume management.
  • 3) Renal support: continuous renal replacement therapy (CRRT) — removes propofol metabolites, manages acidosis, manages fluid balance.
  • 4) ECMO for refractory cardiac dysfunction.
  • 5) Manage rhabdomyolysis: hydration, urinary alkalinisation.
  • 6) Glucose-insulin to support cells; correct hypoglycaemia.
  • 7) PREVENTION: avoid prolonged high-dose propofol in pediatrics; use lowest dose for shortest duration; daily review of need; monitor for early signs (rising lactate, triglycerides).
  • 8) Multidisciplinary ICU care; family communication.
Case 249 — Schoolchild with sudden episodes of severe vertigo and vomiting MOD
A 6-year-old has 3 years of episodes of sudden onset, severe rotational vertigo lasting 1–2 minutes with vomiting, pallor, sometimes nystagmus, occurring 4–6 times per year. Between episodes completely normal. No hearing loss. Audiology normal. Family history: mother has migraine. Episodes seem to occur in mild grouping.

DX

Diagnosis and the broader spectrum it belongs to?
Show model answer
  • Benign paroxysmal vertigo of childhood (BPV) — most common cause of vertigo in young children.
  • Migraine equivalent — considered part of the 'periodic syndromes of childhood that may be precursors to migraine'.
  • Other periodic syndromes: cyclic vomiting syndrome, abdominal migraine, benign paroxysmal torticollis of infancy.
  • BPV: brief episodes (minutes) of severe vertigo with no warning, often with autonomic features (pallor, vomiting), normal between, no hearing loss or tinnitus (distinguishes from Meniere disease — adult disease).
  • Typically resolves by adolescence; many later develop migraine.

NEXT STEP

Workup and management?
Show model answer
  • 1) Confirm diagnosis clinically: history (episode characteristics, family migraine history) + normal exam + normal audiometry.
  • 2) Consider MRI brain if atypical features: persistent symptoms, cerebellar signs, headache, deafness, age > 12.
  • 3) ECG to exclude cardiac syncope causes if any atypical features.
  • 4) Management:
  • - Reassure family — benign self-limited.
  • - Acute: keep child still in darkened room during episode; antiemetic (ondansetron) if vomiting.
  • - No specific medication needed for short episodes.
  • - If frequent/disabling: migraine prophylaxis (propranolol, cyproheptadine, magnesium).
  • 5) School support during episodes.
  • 6) Follow-up to monitor evolution to typical migraine syndromes.
  • 7) Identify and manage other periodic syndromes if present.
Case 250 — Newborn with progressive lethargy and metabolic alkalosis HARD
A term newborn male presents on day 3 with poor feeding, lethargy, hypothermia. Plasma ammonia 720 µmol/L. ABG: pH 7.52, PaCO₂ 22 (primary respiratory alkalosis from ammonia stimulating brainstem). Glucose normal. Urea low. Urinary orotic acid normal. Plasma amino acids: low citrulline.

DX

Most likely UCD given the biochemistry?
Show model answer
  • Carbamoyl phosphate synthetase 1 (CPS1) deficiency — first step of urea cycle.
  • Autosomal recessive.
  • Distinguishing biochemistry:
  • - CPS1 deficiency: ammonia elevated, urea low, citrulline LOW, urinary orotic acid NORMAL (because carbamoyl phosphate isn't being made to overflow into the pyrimidine pathway).
  • - OTC deficiency: ammonia elevated, urea low, citrulline LOW, urinary orotic acid ELEVATED (carbamoyl phosphate accumulates → spills into pyrimidine pathway → orotic acid increased).
  • - Citrullinaemia type 1 (ASS1): citrulline HIGH, urinary orotic acid elevated.
  • - ASL deficiency: citrulline ELEVATED, argininosuccinic acid present.
  • - Arginase deficiency: arginine elevated, milder phenotype (often spastic diplegia rather than neonatal crisis).
  • NAGS deficiency: similar to CPS1 (normal orotic acid, low citrulline) — distinguish by enzyme assay or NAGS mutation testing.

TREATMENT

Emergency hyperammonaemia management?
Show model answer
  • 1) STOP all protein.
  • 2) Reverse catabolism: IV D10 + insulin as needed for normoglycaemia; intralipid for additional calories.
  • 3) NITROGEN SCAVENGERS IV: sodium phenylacetate + sodium benzoate (Ammonul) load + maintenance.
  • 4) ARGININE HCl IV (replenishes urea cycle — needed for all UCDs except arginase deficiency).
  • 5) HEMODIALYSIS (preferred over CRRT) if ammonia > 250 µmol/L, rising despite scavengers, or in coma — fastest clearance.
  • 6) Treat cerebral oedema (head up 30°, normocapnia, careful fluid balance); AVOID mannitol routinely.
  • 7) After stabilisation: low-protein diet, oral nitrogen scavengers (phenylbutyrate, glycerol phenylbutyrate), arginine supplementation, frequent calorie-dense feeds.
  • 8) Liver transplant evaluation for severe CPS1 / OTC deficiency (curative, eliminates risk of future hyperammonaemic crises).
  • 9) Genetic counselling: CPS1 autosomal recessive (25% recurrence); OTC X-linked (50% recurrence in males if mother carrier).
  • 10) Long-term: chronic management, frequent metabolic clinic visits, emergency action plan, medical alert.
Case 251 — Schoolchild with chronic mouth movements and tongue thrusting MOD
An 8-year-old had been on risperidone for 18 months for autism-related aggression. Risperidone was discontinued 3 months ago. He now exhibits continuous involuntary movements: tongue thrusting, lip smacking, choreiform finger movements. Movements are abolished by voluntary action but return at rest.

DX

Diagnosis and pathophysiology?
Show model answer
  • Tardive dyskinesia (TD) — delayed-onset persistent movement disorder following chronic dopamine receptor blockade.
  • Caused by long-term antipsychotic exposure (typical > atypical, but BOTH cause TD); also metoclopramide, prochlorperazine.
  • Pathophysiology: chronic D2 blockade → postsynaptic D2 receptor upregulation and supersensitivity → dyskinetic movements when drug is withdrawn or reduced (or sometimes even continued).
  • Manifestations: oro-bucco-lingual dyskinesia (most common — chewing, lip smacking, tongue thrusting), choreiform movements of limbs/trunk, dystonia (tardive dystonia).
  • Pediatric TD is less common than adult but increasingly recognised with widespread antipsychotic use in autism, behaviour disorders.
  • Withdrawal dyskinesias = similar but transient (resolve within months).
  • TARDIVE = persistent (months-years, sometimes permanent).

TREATMENT

Management?
Show model answer
  • 1) DISCONTINUE offending agent if possible (or reduce to lowest effective dose) — paradoxically may worsen TD initially (withdrawal-emergent dyskinesia) before improving.
  • 2) Switch to LOWER-potency atypical with less D2 affinity (clozapine, quetiapine) — caution: may mask TD without treating it.
  • 3) VMAT2 INHIBITORS — newer FDA-approved for TD:
  • - Valbenazine.
  • - Deutetrabenazine.
  • - Pediatric data limited but use is increasing.
  • - Tetrabenazine — older alternative.
  • 4) Other agents tried: vitamin E, branched-chain amino acids, amantadine, clonazepam — modest evidence.
  • 5) For tardive dystonia: botulinum toxin for focal dystonia, DBS for severe refractory.
  • 6) Multidisciplinary: psychiatry, neurology, family/school support.
  • 7) Prognosis: variable — some resolve over months-years after stopping the drug; others persist indefinitely.
  • 8) PREVENTION: use atypicals at lowest effective dose, shortest duration; avoid in mild presentations of irritability/aggression; consider behavioural interventions first.
Case 252 — Adolescent with severe explosive headaches during sex MOD
A 15-year-old male presents with sudden onset of thunderclap headache during sexual activity that lasted hours. He had two similar episodes over the past month, all triggered by exertion or sex. CT plain: no haemorrhage. CTA: mild irregular narrowing of distal cerebral arteries. CSF: normal. MRI shows no parenchymal abnormality.

DX

Diagnosis to consider?
Show model answer
  • Reversible cerebral vasoconstriction syndrome (RCVS) — thunderclap headache from segmental vasoconstriction.
  • OR primary headache associated with sexual activity (HAS) — thunderclap subtype.
  • Differential of thunderclap headache:
  • - SAH (aneurysmal) — ALWAYS rule out first (CT then LP if CT negative and > 6 h).
  • - RCVS (recurrent thunderclap, often triggered by exertion/sex/vasoactive substances).
  • - Cervical artery dissection.
  • - Cerebral venous sinus thrombosis.
  • - Pituitary apoplexy.
  • - Primary thunderclap headache (diagnosis of exclusion).
  • - Primary cough/exertional/sexual headaches.
  • Repeat angiography 1–4 weeks may show evolution (RCVS develops segmental narrowing over time).

NEXT STEP

Workup?
Show model answer
  • 1) Confirm SAH excluded (CT first, then LP if > 6 h post-onset and CT negative).
  • 2) CTA / MRA — assess for aneurysm, dissection, RCVS.
  • 3) MRV — exclude CVST.
  • 4) Detailed history for vasoactive substances (cannabis, decongestants, SSRIs, triptans).
  • 5) Repeat MRA at 4–6 weeks if RCVS suspected.
  • 6) Management:
  • - Avoid Valsalva and triggering activities during active phase.
  • - Calcium channel blockers (nimodipine, verapamil) for RCVS.
  • - Beta-blocker (propranolol) for primary exertional/sexual headache.
  • - Avoid further vasoactive substances.
  • - Indomethacin may help primary cough/exertional headaches.
  • 7) Education and reassurance after workup negative — most cases self-limited.
  • 8) Catheter angiography reserved for cases where CTA/MRA inconclusive.
Case 253 — Toddler with first afebrile seizure and brief postictal sleep MOD
A 4-year-old previously well boy has a witnessed generalised tonic-clonic seizure lasting 2 minutes during nighttime sleep. Postictal sleep 45 minutes; otherwise full recovery. No fever, no head injury, no toxin exposure. Examination normal. Family history: paternal cousin with childhood epilepsy. EEG day after: normal awake; sleep deprivation EEG: centrotemporal spikes activated by drowsiness/sleep.

DX

Most likely diagnosis given the EEG?
Show model answer
  • Self-limited (benign) epilepsy with centrotemporal spikes (SeLECTS) — formerly benign rolandic epilepsy (BRE) or benign childhood epilepsy with centrotemporal spikes (BECTS).
  • Most common pediatric idiopathic epilepsy syndrome.
  • Onset: usually 3–13 years; resolves by adolescence (~ 16 years) in most.
  • Classic semiology: focal motor seizures of face/oropharynx (drooling, unable to speak, twitching of one side of face), often during sleep, may secondarily generalise.
  • EEG: characteristic high-amplitude centrotemporal sharp/spike waves, activated by drowsiness and sleep — pathognomonic.
  • Normal interictal exam, normal MRI (no need to image in classical cases), good cognitive outcome.
  • Genetic: complex; GRIN2A mutations in some.

TREATMENT

To treat or not to treat?
Show model answer
  • SHARED DECISION-MAKING with family.
  • Many cases do NOT require AED — single nocturnal seizure with classic EEG, no daytime seizures, mild infrequent events — observation reasonable.
  • AED indicated for:
  • - Frequent seizures.
  • - Daytime seizures.
  • - Generalised secondarily generalised seizures.
  • - Family concern.
  • First-line AEDs: levetiracetam (most common first choice in pediatrics), oxcarbazepine, carbamazepine.
  • AVOID drugs that worsen related syndromes (e.g., atypical evolution to CSWS, ESES): carbamazepine in some atypical variants may worsen language.
  • Some variants (atypical BECTS, CSWS — continuous spike-wave during slow sleep) have worse cognitive outcomes and require aggressive treatment.
  • AED continued until 2 years seizure-free typically.
  • Reassure family — excellent long-term prognosis in classical SeLECTS; full resolution in adolescence; normal cognition.
  • Safety counselling: bathing supervision, swimming buddy, sleep safety (especially given nocturnal predominance).
Case 254 — Infant with severe encephalopathy and respiratory compromise after vaccination HARD
A 4-month-old infant received routine DTaP / Hib / IPV / PCV13 / rotavirus vaccinations 6 hours ago. Now: high-pitched crying for 4+ hours, hypotonia, decreased responsiveness, no fever, no fontanelle bulging. Recovers gradually over 12 hours. Repeat episode after second vaccination dose at 6 months prompts admission.

DX

Diagnosis and the broader vaccine-safety context?
Show model answer
  • Hypotonic-hyporesponsive episode (HHE) — temporally associated with vaccination but with controversial causality.
  • Defined as sudden onset of hypotonia, hyporesponsiveness, pallor or cyanosis within 48 hours of vaccination, lasting 1 minute to 48 hours, with spontaneous recovery.
  • Historically associated with whole-cell pertussis (DTwP); much rarer with acellular pertussis (DTaP/DTPa) but still reported.
  • Mechanism uncertain — possibly cytokine release, vasovagal, transient encephalopathy from immune activation.
  • NOT a contraindication to future vaccinations per CDC and most national guidelines — benefits of vaccination far outweigh risks; many infants tolerate subsequent doses.
  • Differentials: vasovagal episode, anaphylactic shock (would have other features), sepsis (febrile), apparent life-threatening event (ALTE)/brief resolved unexplained event (BRUE), infantile spasms, post-vaccine fever-related apnoea in preterm infants.

NEXT STEP

Management and counselling?
Show model answer
  • 1) Confirm event characteristics, exclude differentials.
  • 2) Document with VAERS (US) or equivalent national vaccine adverse event reporting.
  • 3) Reassure family — HHE is rare, almost always self-limited, no long-term sequelae documented.
  • 4) Decision on subsequent vaccinations: shared decision-making with family.
  • - CDC: HHE is NOT a contraindication to future doses of DTaP.
  • - Most infants tolerate subsequent doses without recurrence.
  • - If family chooses to continue: may consider observation in clinic for 1–2 hours post-vaccination.
  • - If family declines additional pertussis-containing vaccines: ensure other vaccinations administered; counsel about pertussis risk especially for newborns/elderly in household.
  • 5) Pertussis-specific considerations: cocooning family members (parents, caregivers) for vaccination to protect infant if their pertussis schedule is incomplete.
  • 6) Differentials in subsequent presentation: if child develops developmental concerns or seizures, full neurological workup.
  • 7) Encephalopathy WITHIN 7 days of pertussis-containing vaccine is the only NEUROLOGICAL contraindication to future pertussis vaccines (very rare).
Case 255 — Adolescent with severe HSV encephalitis treated, then new symptoms HARD
A 14-year-old was treated for biopsy-confirmed HSV-1 encephalitis with 21 days of IV acyclovir, with substantial recovery. Six weeks after discharge: new behavioural change, complex partial seizures, orofacial dyskinesias, dysautonomia. CSF: HSV PCR NEGATIVE; anti-NMDAR antibody POSITIVE.

DX

Diagnosis and recognised phenomenon?
Show model answer
  • POST-HSV anti-NMDAR encephalitis — well-recognised entity.
  • Up to 25–30% of HSV encephalitis survivors develop CNS autoantibodies (most commonly anti-NMDAR; less commonly anti-AMPA, anti-GABA-B, etc.).
  • Pathogenesis: HSV-induced cell damage exposes intracellular antigens → triggers autoimmune response → secondary autoimmune encephalitis weeks to months later.
  • Time course: typically 1–3 months after acute HSV encephalitis.
  • Clinical: relapse-like presentation with new psychiatric/movement/seizure features in patient previously stable.
  • CSF HSV PCR usually NEGATIVE at this point (acute viral infection resolved); antibody testing reveals autoimmune cause.
  • This is NOT viral relapse — important distinction; treatment is immunotherapy, not antivirals.

TREATMENT

Management approach?
Show model answer
  • 1) Confirm with anti-NMDAR antibodies in CSF + serum.
  • 2) Confirm HSV PCR negative (no active viral replication).
  • 3) FIRST-LINE immunotherapy:
  • - High-dose IV methylprednisolone (30 mg/kg/day × 5 days).
  • - IVIG 2 g/kg.
  • - Plasma exchange for severe cases.
  • 4) SECOND-LINE for refractory:
  • - Rituximab.
  • - Cyclophosphamide.
  • 5) Resume acyclovir prophylaxis is not required (PCR negative); some clinicians give brief course empirically if any uncertainty about viral reactivation.
  • 6) Symptomatic: AEDs for seizures, low-dose antipsychotics for psychiatric/dyskinesia (with caution — these patients are sensitive to side effects), supportive care.
  • 7) Recovery often slower than primary anti-NMDAR encephalitis; full recovery may take months-years.
  • 8) Counselling family about this recognised post-HSV phenomenon — not relapse, but new disease.
  • 9) Long-term immunotherapy may be needed for prevention.
  • 10) Surveillance follow-up: cognition, neuropsychology, repeat antibody levels.
Case 256 — Toddler with persistent regression and 'butterfly' brainstem pattern HARD
A 3-year-old has progressive regression of skills (lost ability to walk, speak), prominent dystonia, severe failure to thrive over 12 months. MRI: bilateral symmetric T2 hyperintensities of putamen, caudate, substantia nigra, periaqueductal grey ('butterfly' pattern). Lactate elevated. Genetic: SURF1 mutation.

DX

Diagnosis and the underlying defect?
Show model answer
  • Leigh syndrome from SURF1 mutation (complex IV / cytochrome c oxidase, COX deficiency).
  • Leigh syndrome = subacute necrotising encephalomyelopathy — heterogeneous mitochondrial disorder with characteristic MRI findings.
  • SURF1 is one of many genetic causes (also: MT-ATP6 (m.8993T>G), NDUFV1, NDUFS1-S4, MT-ND mutations, PDHA1).
  • SURF1: nuclear-encoded assembly factor for complex IV (cytochrome c oxidase).
  • Phenotype: usually severe infantile/early childhood onset, characteristic MRI, progressive neurodegeneration, often fatal in early childhood.
  • Other clinical: ophthalmoplegia, optic atrophy, ataxia, dystonia, hypotonia, respiratory failure (brainstem involvement), seizures, cardiomyopathy in some forms.

TREATMENT

Symptomatic management and what to AVOID?
Show model answer
  • No curative therapy.
  • 'Mitochondrial cocktail' (limited evidence, varies by centre):
  • - Coenzyme Q10 / idebenone.
  • - Riboflavin (B2).
  • - Thiamine (B1).
  • - Levocarnitine.
  • - Creatine.
  • - α-lipoic acid.
  • For pyruvate dehydrogenase deficiency-related Leigh: ketogenic diet + thiamine.
  • Seizure management: AVOID valproate (mitotoxic).
  • Manage intercurrent illnesses aggressively (catabolic stress decompensation).
  • Multidisciplinary: neurology, metabolism, cardiology (cardiomyopathy surveillance), GI/nutrition, ophthalmology, audiology, palliative care.
  • AVOID mitotoxic drugs:
  • - Valproate (especially POLG mutations — fulminant hepatic failure).
  • - Aminoglycosides (cumulative ototoxicity).
  • - Linezolid (peripheral and optic neuropathy).
  • - Statins.
  • - Propofol prolonged infusion (PRIS).
  • Family genetic counselling (per inheritance pattern of the specific mutation).
  • Reproductive options: prenatal diagnosis, mitochondrial replacement therapy in some jurisdictions.
Case 257 — Adolescent with rapidly progressive dystonia after viral illness HARD
A 14-year-old developed influenza A 3 weeks ago. Now: rapidly progressive generalised dystonia developing over 2 weeks — initially involving left hand, spreading to whole body. MRI brain: bilateral basal ganglia T2 hyperintensity. CSF: mild lymphocytic pleocytosis. Anti-basal ganglia antibodies positive. ASO titre normal.

DX

Diagnosis and the proposed mechanism?
Show model answer
  • Post-infectious autoimmune basal ganglia encephalitis (or post-infectious chorea/dystonia).
  • Spectrum that overlaps with Sydenham chorea (post-streptococcal) and PANDAS, but here the trigger is influenza.
  • Mechanism: molecular mimicry between viral/microbial antigens and basal ganglia neurons → autoantibody-mediated dysfunction.
  • Antibodies to basal ganglia neuronal antigens may be detected (research test).
  • Differential: Sydenham chorea (recent strep), anti-NMDAR encephalitis (more global encephalopathy, classic features), drug-induced dystonia, Wilson disease (Kayser-Fleischer rings, low ceruloplasmin), neurodegeneration with brain iron accumulation (NBIA), GNAO1-related dystonia, post-streptococcal dystonia.

TREATMENT

Treatment principles?
Show model answer
  • 1) IMMUNOTHERAPY: high-dose IV methylprednisolone (often 30 mg/kg/day × 3–5 days), IVIG 2 g/kg, plasma exchange for severe disease.
  • 2) Refractory: rituximab, cyclophosphamide.
  • 3) SYMPTOMATIC dystonia management:
  • - Trihexyphenidyl (anticholinergic; start low, increase).
  • - Baclofen (oral; or intrathecal for severe).
  • - Tetrabenazine (VMAT2 inhibitor).
  • - Clonazepam (GABA enhancement).
  • - Botulinum toxin for focal components.
  • 4) PICU admission if STATUS DYSTONICUS develops (autonomic instability, hyperthermia, rhabdomyolysis, AKI, respiratory compromise).
  • 5) Multidisciplinary: neurology, movement disorder, immunology, rehab.
  • 6) Long-term immunotherapy may be needed to prevent relapse.
  • 7) Many improve significantly with treatment; some have residual deficits.
  • 8) Surveillance for recurrence with subsequent infections.
Case 258 — Infant with severe encephalopathy and persistent lactic acidosis HARD
A 6-month-old infant has progressive hypotonia, severe developmental delay, intractable seizures, hepatomegaly with elevated transaminases (AST 850, ALT 600), persistent lactic acidosis (lactate 8). Plasma alanine elevated. Genetic: PDHA1 mutation.

DX

Diagnosis and the metabolic defect?
Show model answer
  • Pyruvate dehydrogenase complex (PDC) deficiency — most commonly PDHA1 (X-linked, on Xp22).
  • PDC converts pyruvate to acetyl-CoA at entry point of Krebs cycle.
  • Deficiency → pyruvate accumulates → cannot enter Krebs cycle → energy deficit + diverted to lactate (lactic acidosis) and alanine.
  • Most severe form: neonatal/infantile severe lactic acidosis, encephalopathy, structural brain abnormalities (agenesis of corpus callosum, cerebral atrophy), seizures.
  • Milder forms (especially in females due to X-linked + skewed X-inactivation): episodic ataxia, dystonia, exercise intolerance.
  • Imaging: similar to Leigh syndrome (bilateral basal ganglia and brainstem T2 hyperintensity).

TREATMENT

Specific therapy and dietary management?
Show model answer
  • KETOGENIC DIET — high fat, low carbohydrate; provides ketones as alternative fuel that bypass the PDC blockade.
  • - Often produces dramatic improvement in lactic acidosis and energy status.
  • - Best for milder forms; less effective in severe neonatal phenotype.
  • HIGH-DOSE THIAMINE (B1) 200–500 mg/day — cofactor for PDC; some patients are thiamine-responsive.
  • DICHLOROACETATE — activates PDC by inhibiting PDH kinase; modest benefit; neuropathy side effect limits use.
  • Supportive: AEDs for seizures (AVOID valproate — mitotoxic), nutrition, multidisciplinary care.
  • Genetic counselling: X-linked dominant; PDHA1 in males generally severe, in females variable.
  • Family screening; preconception counselling.
  • Prognosis variable: severe forms life-limiting; milder forms can have reasonable quality of life with ketogenic diet.
Case 259 — Teen post-cardiac surgery with new-onset hemichorea MOD
A 14-year-old, 7 days post-Ross procedure (replacement of aortic valve with pulmonary autograft + RV-PA conduit), develops new left-sided hemichorea / hemiballismus and dysarthria. Examination: irregular, flowing, large-amplitude involuntary movements of left limbs. CT: subtle T2 hyperintensity in right subthalamic nucleus / striatum.

DX

Diagnosis and the lesion location?
Show model answer
  • Post-cardiac-surgery hemichorea-hemiballismus from focal subthalamic / basal ganglia injury (typically ischaemic from emboli or hypoperfusion during cardiopulmonary bypass).
  • Classic neuroanatomy: contralateral subthalamic nucleus (Luys body) lesion → hemiballismus (large-amplitude); contralateral striatum lesion → hemichorea (smaller amplitude, more flowing).
  • Hemiballismus historically associated with subthalamic stroke; clinical lesion-localisation correlation now less strict (basal ganglia network disruption).
  • Differential of post-cardiac surgery hyperkinesias: chorea-encephalopathy from CPB (more diffuse), drug-induced (cyclosporine, tacrolimus), metabolic (hypoglycaemia), embolic stroke (atrial thrombus, calcific/vegetative emboli).

TREATMENT

Management?
Show model answer
  • 1) MRI brain to confirm lesion location and assess for embolic source.
  • 2) Investigate embolic source: TTE/TEE for atrial thrombus, vegetation, prosthetic valve dysfunction.
  • 3) Anticoagulation as appropriate (per cardiac surgery and embolic source).
  • 4) Symptomatic treatment of chorea/ballismus:
  • - Tetrabenazine (VMAT2 inhibitor) — first-line.
  • - Atypical antipsychotics (olanzapine, risperidone) — D2 blockade reduces chorea.
  • - Benzodiazepines for severe agitation.
  • - Refractory: DBS of GPi or thalamic VIM.
  • 5) Safety: padded environment, prevent injury from violent movements.
  • 6) Most cases improve substantially over weeks to months as adaptation/recovery occur.
  • 7) Multidisciplinary: neurology, cardiology, rehabilitation.
  • 8) Long-term: stroke prevention strategy (continued anticoagulation as indicated by Ross/conduit), ongoing cardiac surveillance.
Case 260 — Adolescent with progressive cognitive change and family history of dementia HARD
A 16-year-old presents with 18 months of progressive cognitive decline (declining grades, personality change), then onset of chorea, dystonia, gait abnormalities. Mother developed similar symptoms in her late 30s. Paternal grandfather had movement disorder and died in his 50s. Genetic testing: HTT gene CAG repeat expansion: 65 repeats.

DX

Diagnosis and the inheritance phenomenon?
Show model answer
  • Juvenile Huntington disease (Westphal variant).
  • Autosomal dominant; HTT gene CAG repeat expansion (chromosome 4p16.3).
  • Normal: < 27 CAG repeats. Incomplete penetrance: 27–35. Penetrant: 36+. Juvenile-onset: typically 50+ repeats (especially > 60).
  • JUVENILE HD (onset < 21) — phenotypically different from adult HD:
  • - PARKINSONIAN (rigid, bradykinetic, dystonia) > choreatic.
  • - Severe cognitive decline often early.
  • - Seizures common.
  • - Cerebellar features.
  • - Faster progression.
  • ANTICIPATION: increased repeat length in subsequent generations, EARLIER and MORE SEVERE onset in offspring — most marked in PATERNAL transmission (sperm meiosis instability).
  • This case: paternal grandfather (lower repeat, later onset) → mother (intermediate repeat, late-30s onset) → adolescent (large repeat, juvenile onset).

TREATMENT

Disease-modifying and symptomatic management?
Show model answer
  • 1) No disease-modifying therapy currently approved for HD.
  • 2) Investigational: huntingtin-lowering therapies (antisense oligonucleotides — Tominersen, branaplam — large clinical trials, but TOMINERSEN trial paused due to safety; smaller trials ongoing). Gene therapy approaches in early stages.
  • 3) SYMPTOMATIC management:
  • - Chorea: tetrabenazine, deutetrabenazine, atypical antipsychotics.
  • - Dystonia: trihexyphenidyl, baclofen, botulinum toxin.
  • - Parkinsonism: dopamine replacement (limited benefit).
  • - Cognitive/behavioural: SSRIs for depression, atypical antipsychotics for agitation, AEDs for seizures.
  • 4) Multidisciplinary care: neurology, psychiatry, neuropsychology, rehab, palliative care.
  • 5) NUTRITION: high-calorie diet (catabolic state); PEG when dysphagia.
  • 6) GENETIC COUNSELLING is critical — predictive testing of at-risk family members, reproductive options.
  • 7) Psychosocial support for adolescent and family — including teaching about anticipation phenomenon and implications for siblings/future generations.
  • 8) End-of-life planning and palliative care integration early.
Case 261 — Infant with severe dystonia after newborn period of normal development HARD
A 14-month-old infant developed normally until 8 months, then progressive abnormal movements: dystonia, choreoathetosis, oculogyric crisis. Now: severe generalised dystonia, hypotonia, intellectual disability, autonomic dysfunction (excessive sweating, hypothermia, hypotension). CSF neurotransmitter analysis: low homovanillic acid (HVA), low 5-HIAA, very low 3-O-methyldopa.

DX

Diagnosis and the metabolic block?
Show model answer
  • AADC (aromatic L-amino acid decarboxylase) deficiency.
  • Autosomal recessive DDC gene mutations.
  • AADC is required for synthesis of DOPAMINE (from L-DOPA), SEROTONIN (from 5-HTP), NOREPINEPHRINE, EPINEPHRINE.
  • Severe combined neurotransmitter deficiency → profound motor + autonomic dysfunction.
  • Classic features: severe hypotonia, dystonia, oculogyric crises (sustained upward eye deviation, distressing, lasting hours), autonomic dysfunction (sweating, ptosis, miosis), feeding/sleep disturbance, developmental delay.
  • CSF NEUROTRANSMITTER METABOLITES are diagnostic:
  • - Low HVA (dopamine metabolite).
  • - Low 5-HIAA (serotonin metabolite).
  • - LOW or undetectable 3-O-methyldopa (a marker of decarboxylation).
  • - L-DOPA, 5-HTP elevated (substrates accumulate).
  • Plasma AADC enzyme activity reduced.

TREATMENT

Therapy?
Show model answer
  • SYMPTOMATIC pharmacotherapy:
  • - DOPAMINE AGONISTS (pramipexole, rotigotine, ropinirole) — replace dopaminergic effect.
  • - VITAMIN B6 (pyridoxal-5-phosphate — cofactor for AADC) at high doses; modest benefit.
  • - MAO INHIBITORS (selegiline) — slow degradation of any dopamine.
  • - Trihexyphenidyl for dystonia.
  • - Anticholinergics for sweating.
  • NOT GIVEN: L-DOPA (need AADC enzyme to convert it to dopamine — won't work).
  • GENE THERAPY: AAV2-hAADC (eladocagene exuparvovec, Upstaza) — FDA/EMA approved 2022/2024. Direct putaminal injection delivering AADC gene. Has shown dramatic improvement in motor function and quality of life.
  • Multidisciplinary management: neurology, metabolism, rehab, family support.
  • OCULOGYRIC CRISIS management: benzodiazepines (clonazepam, lorazepam), antihistamines, sleep induction.
  • Genetic counselling: AR with 25% recurrence.
  • Prognosis improving with gene therapy era; previously severe with poor outcomes.
Case 262 — Toddler with episodic stridor and severe dysphagia MOD
A 2-year-old has 6 months of progressive feeding difficulties, recurrent aspiration pneumonia, drooling, hoarse voice, and recently abrupt episodes of stridor with cyanosis lasting 30 seconds, recurring multiple times daily.
