MRI: cerebellar tonsils 14 mm below the foramen magnum; small posterior fossa; syrinx in cervical cord; ventricular enlargement.

DX

Diagnosis and the neuroanatomic explanation?
Show model answer
  • Chiari II malformation (formerly Arnold-Chiari II) — strong association with myelomeningocele (open spinal dysraphism).
  • NEUROANATOMY: small posterior fossa + downward herniation of cerebellar vermis, brainstem (medulla), and 4th ventricle through foramen magnum; tectum 'beaked'; corpus callosum dysgenesis; aqueductal stenosis.
  • This patient may have undiagnosed myelomeningocele (often closed surgically at birth) or other neural tube defect.
  • BRAINSTEM dysfunction from compression / dysgenesis:
  • - DYSPHAGIA (cranial nerve nuclei compressed).
  • - STRIDOR (vocal cord paralysis from CN X dysfunction; can be life-threatening).
  • - CENTRAL APNOEA.
  • - ASPIRATION RISK.
  • SYRINGOMYELIA — from CSF dynamics distortion.
  • HYDROCEPHALUS in > 90%.

NEXT STEP

Management priorities?
Show model answer
  • 1) ENT consult — direct laryngoscopy to evaluate vocal cord function (vocal cord paralysis common, may be uni- or bilateral; bilateral paralysis → airway emergency).
  • 2) Sleep study for central apnoea.
  • 3) Swallow study (VFSS) and aspiration evaluation.
  • 4) Neurosurgical assessment for POSTERIOR FOSSA DECOMPRESSION (suboccipital craniectomy with C1 laminectomy ± duroplasty) — for symptomatic brainstem compression (especially apnoea, severe stridor, dysphagia).
  • 5) SHUNT FUNCTION assessment — if existing VP shunt, ensure it's functional (shunt malfunction can worsen Chiari symptoms).
  • 6) Nutrition: NG/PEG feeding to prevent aspiration.
  • 7) Tracheostomy may be needed for severe bilateral vocal cord paralysis.
  • 8) Multidisciplinary: neurosurgery, ENT, neurology, urology, orthopaedics, rehab, developmental paediatrics.
  • 9) Long-term spina bifida clinic follow-up.
  • 10) Family genetic counselling — sporadic (low recurrence) typically, though folate supplementation is preventive for future pregnancies.
Case 263 — Schoolchild with progressive painful neuropathy and burning feet MOD
A 10-year-old has 18 months of progressive burning pain in bilateral feet, especially at night. Now: hyperhidrosis, episodic abdominal pain, characteristic skin lesions in 'bathing trunk' distribution (small red angiokeratomas on lower abdomen and thighs), proteinuria. Father died of stroke at age 35.

DX

Diagnosis and the storage product?
Show model answer
  • Fabry disease (Anderson-Fabry disease).
  • X-linked deficiency of α-galactosidase A (GLA gene on Xq22).
  • Accumulation of GLOBOTRIAOSYLCERAMIDE (Gb3) in lysosomes throughout body — vascular endothelium especially.
  • Classic features (males affected primarily; female carriers may have milder disease):
  • - Painful peripheral neuropathy (small fiber, burning pain, often nocturnal).
  • - Hypohidrosis (no sweat) → heat intolerance.
  • - Angiokeratomas (red-purple skin lesions, especially around umbilicus, groin, thighs).
  • - Cornea verticillata (whorled cornea on slit lamp).
  • - Renal: proteinuria, progressive CKD.
  • - Cardiac: hypertrophic cardiomyopathy, arrhythmia.
  • - Vascular: premature stroke (often in 30s-40s; father's history fits).
  • - GI: abdominal pain (Gb3 in mesenteric vessels).

TREATMENT

Disease-modifying therapy?
Show model answer
  • ENZYME REPLACEMENT THERAPY:
  • - Agalsidase alfa or agalsidase beta — recombinant α-galactosidase A, IV every 2 weeks.
  • - Slow disease progression; better outcomes with early treatment.
  • MIGALASTAT — oral pharmacological chaperone for amenable GLA mutations (those causing misfolded but partially functional enzyme); FDA-approved.
  • GENE THERAPY — in clinical trials (ex vivo HSCT or in vivo AAV).
  • SYMPTOMATIC management:
  • - Neuropathic pain: gabapentin, pregabalin, carbamazepine.
  • - Cardiac: ACE inhibitor, anti-arrhythmic, ICD if indicated.
  • - Renal: ACE/ARB for proteinuria, eventual dialysis/transplant for ESKD.
  • - Stroke prevention: antiplatelet for those with cerebrovascular involvement.
  • Multidisciplinary: nephrology, cardiology, neurology, ophthalmology, genetics, pain management.
  • Family genetic counselling: X-linked; daughters of affected males all carriers; mothers of affected males may be carriers (de novo also possible).
Case 264 — Adolescent with new-onset severe headaches after head injury 1 year ago MOD
A 16-year-old sustained a sports concussion 12 months ago, with brief LOC, treated conservatively and gradually returned to play. Now: 6 months of chronic daily headaches, cognitive complaints (poor concentration, memory issues), mood disturbance, sleep dysfunction, light/noise sensitivity, gradually worsening despite no further injury.

DX

Diagnosis and the differential to recognise?
Show model answer
  • Post-concussion syndrome (PCS) — symptoms persisting beyond expected recovery period (typically 1–3 months) after mild TBI.
  • Cluster of physical (headache, dizziness, fatigue, sleep), cognitive (memory, concentration), and emotional (mood, irritability) symptoms.
  • Prevalence: ~ 30% have symptoms beyond 1 month; ~ 10–20% beyond 3 months.
  • Risk factors for prolonged recovery: female sex, history of migraine, anxiety/depression, multiple concussions, severity of acute symptoms, premorbid learning/attention issues.
  • Differentials to consider:
  • - Migraine (may be triggered/worsened by concussion).
  • - Cervicogenic headache (post-injury neck strain).
  • - Vestibular dysfunction (BPPV from head trauma).
  • - Convergence insufficiency / oculomotor dysfunction (very common post-concussion).
  • - Sleep disorder.
  • - Mood / anxiety disorder (may be primary or post-traumatic).

TREATMENT

Multimodal management?
Show model answer
  • 1) EDUCATION: realistic expectations, normal recovery trajectory, validation of symptoms.
  • 2) GRADED RETURN TO ACTIVITY (not strict rest — old paradigm; current evidence supports early sub-threshold aerobic exercise):
  • - Berlin/Amsterdam consensus stepwise return-to-school and return-to-play protocols.
  • - Sub-symptom-threshold aerobic exercise improves recovery.
  • 3) HEADACHE MANAGEMENT:
  • - Limit analgesic overuse (medication-overuse headache common in PCS).
  • - Triptans for migraine-type headaches.
  • - Preventive: tricyclics, topiramate, propranolol.
  • 4) VESTIBULAR / OCULOMOTOR REHABILITATION — often dramatically helpful.
  • 5) NEUROCOGNITIVE REHABILITATION: school accommodations, gradual cognitive demands.
  • 6) SLEEP HYGIENE; treat insomnia.
  • 7) Address mood/anxiety: counselling, CBT, mindfulness; SSRIs if depression/anxiety persistent.
  • 8) Multidisciplinary CONCUSSION CLINIC where available — best outcomes.
  • 9) Long-term: most patients improve over months; some have persistent symptoms warranting ongoing management.
  • 10) Counsel about FUTURE CONCUSSION RISK (especially in contact sports) — repeat concussion before recovery → second-impact syndrome (rare but catastrophic), increased risk of prolonged recovery.
Case 265 — Schoolchild with paroxysmal episodes of cold-induced weakness MOD
A 12-year-old has episodes of generalised stiffness when going out in the cold, when surprised, or with exertion. Episodes last seconds to minutes, do not involve LOC. Examination after eating cold ice cream: percussion of thenar eminence produces sustained muscle contraction (myotonia). Family history: father has similar problem; relieved by exercise.

DX

Diagnosis and the channel involved?
Show model answer
  • Myotonia congenita — non-dystrophic myotonia.
  • Two forms:
  • - Thomsen type: autosomal dominant CLCN1 mutations.
  • - Becker type: autosomal recessive CLCN1 mutations (more severe).
  • CLCN1 encodes the muscle CHLORIDE CHANNEL (ClC-1).
  • Loss-of-function → reduced chloride conductance → membrane hyperexcitability → sustained muscle contraction after voluntary or stimulated activation = myotonia.
  • 'Warm-up' phenomenon: stiffness IMPROVES with repeated movement / exercise (helpful for diagnosis).
  • Cold worsens, surprise/sudden movement triggers.
  • No weakness between episodes; muscle bulk often hypertrophic ('Herculean' appearance).
  • Differential: paramyotonia congenita (SCN4A — cold-induced, paradoxical worsening with exercise), myotonic dystrophy (multi-system, distal weakness, cataracts, etc., DM1/DM2 expansions), HypoPP, hyperkalaemic periodic paralysis.

TREATMENT

Management options?
Show model answer
  • 1) ACTIVITY MODIFICATION: warm up before exercise; avoid cold exposure.
  • 2) SODIUM CHANNEL BLOCKERS:
  • - Mexiletine — first-line; FDA-approved for non-dystrophic myotonia. Significantly reduces stiffness and improves quality of life. Watch for QT prolongation (monitor ECG).
  • - Lamotrigine — alternative.
  • - Carbamazepine, ranolazine — used in some cases.
  • 3) AVOID:
  • - Depolarising muscle relaxants (succinylcholine) — can trigger severe sustained myotonia and rhabdomyolysis; use non-depolarising relaxants.
  • - β-blockers can worsen.
  • - Cold environments.
  • 4) ANAESTHESIA CONSIDERATIONS: pre-warm OR; avoid sux; use rocuronium/vecuronium; manage potential rhabdomyolysis post-op.
  • 5) Family genetic counselling (Thomsen AD vs Becker AR — different recurrence).
  • 6) Multidisciplinary: neurology/neuromuscular, anaesthesia consult before procedures, physiotherapy.
  • 7) Generally normal lifespan; quality of life improves with treatment.
Case 266 — Adolescent with sudden severe pain in shoulder and arm weakness MOD
A 16-year-old developed sudden severe right shoulder/scapular pain 10 days ago, then over subsequent days right arm weakness — winging of the right scapula (long thoracic nerve), weakness of shoulder abduction (deltoid), elbow flexion (biceps). No trauma. He had a viral URI 3 weeks ago. EMG: patchy denervation in muscles supplied by the upper trunk of the brachial plexus.

DX

Diagnosis and the natural history?
Show model answer
  • Parsonage-Turner syndrome (neuralgic amyotrophy, idiopathic brachial plexitis).
  • Presumed autoimmune attack on brachial plexus, often post-infectious (viral URI, vaccination, surgery, stress).
  • Classic presentation:
  • - SEVERE acute shoulder/scapular pain (often described as worst pain ever; lasts days-weeks).
  • - Then as pain subsides, WEAKNESS develops in muscles supplied by affected nerves.
  • - Most commonly affects UPPER TRUNK of brachial plexus.
  • - Long thoracic nerve, suprascapular nerve, axillary nerve, musculocutaneous nerve frequently involved (any peripheral nerve possible).
  • - Sensory loss in distribution of affected sensory nerves.
  • Natural history: weakness may progress for weeks; recovery over months-years; most have functional improvement but residual weakness common.
  • Hereditary form: HNA (hereditary neuralgic amyotrophy, SEPT9 mutations) — recurrent, family history, dysmorphic features sometimes.

TREATMENT

Management approach?
Show model answer
  • 1) DIAGNOSIS confirmed by EMG/NCS (patchy denervation in plexal distribution), MR neurography (T2 hyperintensity of plexus), exclude differentials.
  • 2) ANALGESIA: opioids often needed acutely; consider gabapentin/pregabalin for neuropathic pain.
  • 3) CORTICOSTEROIDS: oral prednisone (short course, e.g., 1 mg/kg/day with rapid taper over 2 weeks) — some evidence for reducing pain duration and possibly improving motor recovery IF GIVEN EARLY (within 2 weeks of pain onset).
  • 4) IVIG controversial — used in severe / refractory cases.
  • 5) Physical therapy / occupational therapy — early gentle range of motion, then progressive strengthening as weakness recovers.
  • 6) Bracing as needed (scapular stabiliser for serratus weakness, sling for severe upper-arm weakness).
  • 7) Nerve transfer / tendon transfer surgery for persistent severe weakness (consider after 6–12 months of conservative management).
  • 8) Education: prolonged recovery expected; most improve substantially.
  • 9) Recurrence: 1–25%; if recurrent, investigate HNA.
  • 10) Multidisciplinary: neurology, peripheral nerve surgery, pain management, rehab.
Case 267 — Toddler with sudden weakness after URI and asymmetric cord lesion HARD
A 4-year-old developed a febrile URI 1 week ago. Now: acute onset left arm weakness over 24 hours, progressing to left leg weakness with sensation preserved. Reflexes absent in the affected limbs. MRI spine: T2 hyperintensity restricted to grey matter (anterior horns) of the cervical cord, asymmetric, no enhancement. CSF: lymphocytic pleocytosis, normal glucose, mildly raised protein.

DX

Diagnosis and the most common viral association?
Show model answer
  • Acute flaccid myelitis (AFM) — a polio-like illness affecting anterior horn cells of the spinal cord.
  • Most cases occur in late summer/autumn in temporal clusters (every 2 years in recent surveillance).
  • Strongest association: ENTEROVIRUS D68 (also enterovirus A71, West Nile virus, adenovirus, others).
  • Hallmark imaging: longitudinal T2 hyperintensity restricted to the grey matter (anterior horns) of the spinal cord — gives 'H' shape on axial.
  • Distinguishes from transverse myelitis (which involves the whole cord on axial), and from GBS (areflexic but normal cord MRI; nerve roots may enhance).

TREATMENT

Acute management?
Show model answer
  • 1) Supportive care is the mainstay — there is no specific antiviral therapy proven for AFM.
  • 2) IVIG often used empirically (case-series evidence; no RCT proof of benefit).
  • 3) Corticosteroids — controversial; theoretical concern they may worsen viral infection; used in some protocols, avoided in others.
  • 4) Plasma exchange in severe/refractory disease.
  • 5) Aggressive respiratory monitoring — bulbar/diaphragmatic involvement may necessitate intubation; cough assist, BiPAP.
  • 6) Early rehabilitation: physical and occupational therapy, swallow/speech evaluation.
  • 7) Nerve and tendon transfer surgery considered later (after ≥ 6 months) for persistent severe focal weakness.
  • 8) Public health reporting; investigate cluster context; preventive measures.
  • 9) Long-term: prognosis variable; many have persistent focal weakness.
Case 268 — Schoolchild with seizures and lactic acidosis after diet change HARD
An 8-year-old with refractory focal epilepsy was started on a ketogenic diet 3 weeks ago. Now: vomiting, abdominal pain, lethargy, tachypnoea. ABG: pH 7.18, HCO3 12, lactate 5. Serum β-hydroxybutyrate 7 mmol/L. Glucose 3.2. Investigation reveals: low free carnitine, elevated C8–C10 acylcarnitines on dried blood spot.

DX

Diagnosis and the clinical lesson?
Show model answer
  • Decompensation of an underlying medium-chain acyl-CoA dehydrogenase (MCAD) deficiency precipitated by the ketogenic diet.
  • The patient has an unrecognised fatty-acid oxidation disorder (FAOD) — the ketogenic diet forces reliance on fatty acid oxidation that the patient cannot perform.
  • Result: hypoglycaemia, hypoketotic state (in MCAD), accumulation of toxic intermediates, metabolic acidosis, encephalopathy.
  • LESSON: ketogenic diet is contraindicated in fatty-acid oxidation defects, primary carnitine deficiency, pyruvate carboxylase deficiency, porphyria, and other specific metabolic disorders.
  • BEFORE STARTING KETO DIET: screen for these disorders (acylcarnitine profile, urine organic acids).

TREATMENT

Acute management and what to do about the diet?
Show model answer
  • 1) STOP the ketogenic diet immediately.
  • 2) IV D10 + sodium chloride to provide glucose and suppress lipolysis.
  • 3) IV carnitine if low (100 mg/kg/day).
  • 4) Correct acidosis if pH < 7.1.
  • 5) Treat seizures with appropriate AEDs.
  • 6) Confirm MCAD diagnosis with genetic testing (ACADM gene).
  • 7) Discharge plan: avoid prolonged fasting; sick-day rules; emergency action plan; carnitine supplementation; medical alert bracelet.
  • 8) Alternative epilepsy therapies: AED optimisation, VNS, surgical evaluation, immunomodulation if relevant.
  • 9) Family screening and newborn screen review (this should have been picked up at birth if screening was done; gap to investigate).
Case 269 — Newborn with seizures and bilateral hypoglycaemia-pattern injury on MRI MOD
A term newborn fed only breast milk on the first 36 h had two documented blood glucose values of 1.4 mmol/L on day 1, since corrected. Develops focal seizures on day 3. MRI day 4: bilateral parieto-occipital cortical and subcortical T2/FLAIR hyperintensity with DWI restriction.

DX

Diagnosis and the typical MRI pattern?
Show model answer
  • Neonatal hypoglycaemic brain injury.
  • Severe or prolonged neonatal hypoglycaemia → injury preferentially in the parieto-occipital cortex and subcortical white matter (the most metabolically vulnerable in the neonatal brain).
  • Distinguishes from HIE pattern (which favours basal ganglia/thalami in severe HIE, watershed cortex in milder HIE).
  • Long-term sequelae: cortical visual impairment (most characteristic), occipital seizures, developmental delay, executive dysfunction.

TREATMENT

Acute and surveillance management?
Show model answer
  • 1) Correct hypoglycaemia urgently if recurring: D10 bolus 2 mL/kg, then maintenance infusion to keep glucose > 2.6 mmol/L (3.0 in symptomatic neonates).
  • 2) Investigate persistent hypoglycaemia: hyperinsulinism (paired insulin/glucose; if insulin detectable during hypoglycaemia → hyperinsulinism), inborn errors of metabolism, hypopituitarism, adrenal insufficiency.
  • 3) Seizure management with phenobarbital first-line.
  • 4) Continuous EEG when seizures suspected.
  • 5) Feeding support to maintain euglycaemia.
  • 6) Long-term follow-up: developmental assessment, visual evoked potentials, ophthalmology for cortical visual impairment, neurology.
  • 7) Prevention: identify at-risk neonates early (IDM, IUGR, late preterm, sick), establish hypoglycaemia protocols, frequent feeding, glucose monitoring.
Case 270 — Adolescent with abdominal pain and progressive weakness HARD
A 16-year-old presents with recurrent episodes of severe abdominal pain, vomiting, then progressive proximal weakness, autonomic instability (labile BP, tachycardia), confusion, anxiety, seizures. Hyponatraemia. Urine is dark red on standing. Sister had a similar presentation. No fever.

DX

Diagnosis and the metabolic step affected?
Show model answer
  • Acute intermittent porphyria (AIP) — autosomal dominant deficiency of hydroxymethylbilane synthase (HMBS, also called porphobilinogen deaminase).
  • Third enzyme in the haem biosynthesis pathway.
  • Accumulation of ALA and porphobilinogen (PBG) → neurotoxic, cause attacks.
  • Classic tetrad: abdominal pain + autonomic dysfunction + peripheral neuropathy + neuropsychiatric features.
  • Hyponatraemia common (SIADH from hypothalamic involvement).
  • Dark/reddish urine on standing (porphobilinogen oxidises to porphobilin).
  • Triggers: drugs (CYP-inducing — anticonvulsants, barbiturates, sulfa, OCPs), fasting, hormonal changes (menstruation), stress, alcohol, infection.

TREATMENT

Acute attack management?
Show model answer
  • 1) Remove triggers — stop offending drugs; check the porphyria drug-safety list (e.g., the Drug Database for Acute Porphyria or American Porphyria Foundation list).
  • 2) HEMIN (Panhematin) IV 3–4 mg/kg/day × 4 days — supplies exogenous haem, feedback-suppresses ALA synthase, reduces ALA/PBG production.
  • 3) HIGH-CARBOHYDRATE LOADING with IV dextrose (D10) 300–500 g/day — also suppresses ALA synthase via glucose effect.
  • 4) Analgesia: opioids are SAFE in porphyria (no porphyrinogenicity); avoid NSAIDs if AKI risk; benzodiazepines safe (lorazepam, midazolam).
  • 5) Correct hyponatraemia carefully (slow, avoid CPM).
  • 6) Antihypertensives: β-blockers (propranolol) safe; AVOID barbiturates and many anticonvulsants.
  • 7) For seizures: bromides historically; gabapentin and levetiracetam considered safe; AVOID phenytoin, phenobarbital, carbamazepine.
  • 8) GIVOSIRAN (Givlaari) — siRNA targeting ALAS1, FDA-approved 2019, reduces recurrent attacks by suppressing the ALA synthase upstream of the block.
  • 9) Long-term: trigger avoidance, dietary counselling (avoid prolonged fasting), monthly preventive haem infusions in severe cases.
  • 10) Genetic counselling: AD with variable penetrance; family testing.
Case 271 — Adolescent with refractory headache and visual blurring after head trauma MOD
A 14-year-old had a minor head injury 6 weeks ago. Since then: progressive holocranial headache (worse upright), nausea, intermittent blurred vision, posterior neck pain. MRI: diffuse smooth dural enhancement; brain 'sagging' with downward displacement; small bilateral subdural collections; tonsillar descent 5 mm.

DX

Diagnosis and the key imaging features?
Show model answer
  • Spontaneous intracranial hypotension (SIH) from a CSF leak.
  • Postural headache (worse upright) is the cardinal feature.
  • SEEPS imaging mnemonic: Subdural collections, Enhancement of pachymeninges (diffuse, smooth, no nodularity), Engorgement of venous structures (intracranial venous sinuses), Pituitary enlargement (hyperaemia), Sagging of the brain (with apparent Chiari-like tonsillar descent that resolves with patching).
  • Causes: CSF leak from dural tear (often along nerve root sleeves), CSF-venous fistula (an under-recognised cause), trivial trauma, idiopathic.
  • Distinguishes from primary Chiari I (no postural headache, no SEEPS findings).

TREATMENT

Stepwise management?
Show model answer
  • 1) Conservative: bed rest, hydration, caffeine, oral analgesia. Helps a minority.
  • 2) Localise the leak: dynamic CT myelography, MR myelography, or digital subtraction myelography to find CSF-venous fistulas.
  • 3) Epidural blood patch (EBP) — first-line intervention: autologous blood injected into epidural space. Often given lumbar then targeted if needed.
  • 4) Fibrin glue patch or repeat EBP for refractory cases.
  • 5) Surgical repair for persistent leak with identified site.
  • 6) Subdural collections related to SIH usually resolve once the leak is sealed; AVOID immediate surgical drainage of small SDHs in SIH (often re-accumulate; treat the leak first).
Case 272 — Newborn with sustained generalised seizures and pyridoxal-5'-phosphate testing HARD
A term newborn presents day 1 with refractory tonic-clonic seizures unresponsive to phenobarbital (40 mg/kg loaded). IV pyridoxine 100 mg trial — no response. Glucose, calcium, magnesium, electrolytes normal. EEG: burst-suppression. IV pyridoxal-5'-phosphate (PLP) 30 mg/kg/day initiated — seizures stop within hours.

DX

Diagnosis and the defect?
Show model answer
  • PNPO (pyridox(am)ine 5'-phosphate oxidase) deficiency — autosomal recessive.
  • PNPO converts pyridoxine and pyridoxamine into the active PLP cofactor.
  • Without functional PNPO, even with abundant dietary pyridoxine, the active form (PLP) cannot be generated.
  • Hence pyridoxine alone is INEFFECTIVE; PLP supplementation works.
  • Related condition: pyridoxine-dependent epilepsy (PDE) from ALDH7A1 mutations — these patients respond to pyridoxine itself.
  • Both should be considered in neonatal refractory seizures with normal metabolic workup; trial pyridoxine first, then PLP if no response.

TREATMENT

Long-term management?
Show model answer
  • 1) Lifelong PLP supplementation 30–50 mg/kg/day divided 4–6 times daily.
  • 2) Folinic acid (5-formyltetrahydrofolate) supplementation as adjunct (some patients benefit).
  • 3) Monitor: clinical seizures, EEG, hepatic transaminases (PLP can cause transaminitis at high doses).
  • 4) Adjust dose for growth and breakthrough seizures.
  • 5) Genetic counselling: AR, 25% recurrence in carrier parents; cascade testing of siblings.
  • 6) Neurodevelopmental follow-up — outcomes variable; some have normal development, others significant disability despite seizure control.
  • 7) Family education about emergency action plan during illness/missed doses.
  • 8) Avoid AEDs that interfere with vitamin B6 metabolism if possible.
Case 273 — Schoolchild with mid-line lower limb dystonia worsening through the day MOD
A 9-year-old has 18 months of gait disturbance: walking is normal in the morning but progressively worsens through the day with foot inversion, toe-walking, ankle dystonia. Symptoms dramatically relieved by sleep. No regression. Family history: aunt has similar problem responsive to low-dose levodopa.

DX

Diagnosis and the metabolic basis?
Show model answer
  • Dopa-responsive dystonia (DRD; Segawa disease).
  • Autosomal dominant GCH1 mutations — GTP cyclohydrolase 1 deficiency.
  • Reduces tetrahydrobiopterin (BH4), the cofactor for tyrosine hydroxylase → reduced dopamine synthesis.
  • Classic clinical features:
  • - Childhood-onset lower-limb dystonia.
  • - DIURNAL FLUCTUATION (worse in evening, better with sleep).
  • - Dramatic, sustained response to low-dose L-DOPA.
  • - Often misdiagnosed as cerebral palsy.
  • Differentials: idiopathic generalised dystonia, autosomal recessive tyrosine hydroxylase deficiency (more severe, neonatal/infantile onset, broader phenotype), juvenile parkinsonism.

TREATMENT

Treatment principles?
Show model answer
  • L-DOPA / carbidopa — first-line and diagnostic.
  • Start low (1–2 mg/kg/day) and titrate; typical maintenance 4–10 mg/kg/day divided 2–3 doses.
  • Response is dramatic and sustained over years to decades (unlike Parkinson disease — no long-term motor fluctuations).
  • Lifelong therapy.
  • Adjuncts rarely needed; some patients add anticholinergics (trihexyphenidyl) for residual dystonia.
  • Avoid dopamine antagonists (neuroleptics, metoclopramide).
  • Genetic counselling: AD with reduced penetrance (~30%); cascade testing offered.
  • Excellent long-term prognosis with treatment.
Case 274 — Child with severe ataxia after upper respiratory illness and rash MOD
A 6-year-old developed an erythematous truncal rash 2 weeks ago (presumed enteroviral). Now acute onset truncal ataxia, dysmetria, dysarthria, fine intention tremor. Cognition preserved. No fever. MRI brain: mildly increased cerebellar T2 signal.

DX

Diagnosis and differential?
Show model answer
  • Acute post-infectious cerebellitis (acute cerebellar ataxia of childhood).
  • Most common cause of acute ataxia in children < 5 years.
  • Self-limited, post-viral autoimmune process; varicella was historically the most common trigger (now less common since vaccination); other triggers include EBV, mycoplasma, enterovirus, influenza.
  • Excellent prognosis — most recover over weeks-months.
  • Differential to exclude carefully: posterior fossa tumour, drug intoxication (anti-histamines, anticonvulsants, alcohol), ADEM, OMS (opsoclonus-myoclonus syndrome — paraneoplastic in 50%), Miller Fisher syndrome variant of GBS, Wernicke encephalopathy (rare in children).

NEXT STEP

Workup and management?
Show model answer
  • 1) MRI brain to rule out tumour and ADEM.
  • 2) Toxin screen (urine drug screen for benzodiazepines, antihistamines, alcohol).
  • 3) Consider CSF: typically normal or mild lymphocytic pleocytosis in post-infectious cerebellitis; if more severe pleocytosis or marked protein, think ADEM or infection.
  • 4) Look for eye movement abnormalities — opsoclonus would prompt neuroblastoma workup (urine catecholamines, MIBG scan, imaging).
  • 5) Most cases: supportive only (physical therapy, fall prevention).
  • 6) Severe cases: high-dose corticosteroids (atypical features, prolonged course).
  • 7) IVIG for severe / refractory.
  • 8) Most children fully recover in 4–8 weeks; longer recovery (> 3 months) warrants additional workup.
Case 275 — Adolescent with episodic stupor and abnormal smell during seizure MOD
A 15-year-old has 14 months of episodes characterised by a sudden 'rotting' smell aura, followed by staring, lip-smacking, dystonic posturing of the right arm, postictal confusion. MRI: subtle right hippocampal atrophy with T2/FLAIR hyperintensity; loss of internal architecture. EEG: right temporal interictal spikes.

DX

Diagnosis and the imaging finding?
Show model answer
  • Right mesial temporal lobe epilepsy with mesial temporal sclerosis (MTS).
  • Most common substrate for drug-resistant focal epilepsy.
  • MRI features of MTS:
  • - Hippocampal atrophy (unilateral or markedly asymmetric).
  • - T2/FLAIR hyperintensity of the hippocampus.
  • - Loss of internal architecture.
  • - Atrophy of adjacent structures (amygdala, parahippocampal gyrus, mammillary body).
  • Classical semiology: olfactory or epigastric aura → automatisms (oral/manual) → dystonic posturing of contralateral hand → contralateral version → postictal confusion.
  • Often history of complex febrile seizures in infancy (debated causal vs marker).

TREATMENT

Treatment options including surgery?
Show model answer
  • 1) First-line AEDs: levetiracetam, oxcarbazepine, carbamazepine, lamotrigine.
  • 2) Establish drug-resistance: failure of 2 appropriately chosen, dosed, tolerated AEDs (ILAE definition).
  • 3) PRE-SURGICAL evaluation when drug-resistant: video-EEG, neuropsychology, fMRI for language lateralisation, ictal SPECT, PET, sometimes intracranial EEG.
  • 4) Standard ANTERIOR TEMPORAL LOBECTOMY (ATL) or SELECTIVE AMYGDALOHIPPOCAMPECTOMY (SAH): 60–70% achieve seizure-freedom (Engel I); much higher than continued AED.
  • 5) MRI-GUIDED LASER INTERSTITIAL THERMAL THERAPY (LITT) of the mesial temporal lobe — less invasive, faster recovery, slightly lower seizure-free rate than ATL.
  • 6) Responsive neurostimulation (RNS) for non-surgical candidates.
  • 7) Neuropsychological consideration: dominant-hemisphere resection can cause verbal memory decline; non-dominant generally well-tolerated.
  • 8) Surgery shows clear superiority over continued medical therapy in randomised trials (Wiebe NEJM 2001; pediatric data extrapolated).
Case 276 — Infant with frequent episodes of crying and arching MOD
A 2-month-old infant presents with multiple daily episodes of sudden 'crying out', arching of the back, extending all four limbs in stiff posture for 5–15 seconds, repeating in clusters, especially after feeds. Mother is breastfeeding. Episodes occur with apparent reflux of food.

DX

Most likely diagnosis and the differentials to consider?
Show model answer
  • Sandifer syndrome — gastro-oesophageal reflux disease (GORD) with associated stereotyped dystonic/posturing movements.
  • Distinct entity in infants/young children: characteristic torticollis, opisthotonus, dystonic posturing of trunk and limbs ASSOCIATED WITH REFLUX EPISODES.
  • Classically misdiagnosed as seizures (especially infantile spasms) — important to consider this benign condition.
  • Clue: temporal association with feeds, posturing relieved by sitting up, no postictal phase, normal development between episodes, video-EEG normal.
  • Differentials: infantile spasms (West syndrome — EEG hypsarrhythmia), benign myoclonus of infancy, tonic seizures, hyperekplexia, dystonia, paroxysmal torticollis of infancy.

TREATMENT

Management approach?
Show model answer
  • 1) Confirm with video-EEG (normal ictal recording) and clinical reflux assessment.
  • 2) Treat the underlying GORD:
  • - Feeding modifications: upright feeding, smaller more frequent feeds, thickening (if appropriate), maternal diet modification for breastfed.
  • - Positional therapy: upright after feeds (NOT prone for sleep — SIDS risk).
  • - Proton pump inhibitors (PPIs) — used cautiously in infants given evolving safety data.
  • - H2 blockers as alternative.
  • 3) Resolves with GORD improvement (often by 12–18 months).
  • 4) Fundoplication rarely needed in severe refractory cases (often with feeding tube).
  • 5) Family reassurance — benign condition, full recovery expected.
  • 6) Multidisciplinary: paediatric GI, neurology consultation if diagnosis uncertain, feeding therapy.
Case 277 — Newborn with progressive hypotonia, hepatic failure, and stroke-like episodes HARD
A term newborn presents on day 5 with poor feeding, hypotonia, jaundice (AST 1200, ALT 950), elevated INR, hypoglycaemia, lactic acidosis (lactate 8). On day 8: focal seizure. MRI: bilateral subcortical white-matter T2 hyperintensities. Genetic testing: POLG mutation (homozygous).

DX

Diagnosis and the spectrum it belongs to?
Show model answer
  • Neonatal-onset POLG-related mitochondrial DNA depletion syndrome — also known as ALPERS-HUTTENLOCHER SYNDROME (severe end of POLG disease spectrum).
  • POLG encodes the catalytic subunit of mitochondrial DNA polymerase γ — essential for mitochondrial DNA replication.
  • Loss of function → progressive mitochondrial DNA depletion → multi-organ mitochondrial failure.
  • POLG disease spectrum (autosomal recessive in most severe forms):
  • - Alpers-Huttenlocher (severe pediatric — hepatic failure, encephalopathy, intractable seizures).
  • - Childhood myocerebrohepatopathy (MCHS).
  • - MEMSA (myoclonic epilepsy, myopathy, sensory ataxia).
  • - Sensory ataxic neuropathy + dysarthria + ophthalmoparesis (SANDO).
  • - Adult progressive external ophthalmoplegia.

TREATMENT

Critical management and what to AVOID?
Show model answer
  • 1) SUPPORTIVE CARE — no curative therapy.
  • 2) AVOID VALPROATE absolutely — triggers FULMINANT HEPATIC FAILURE in POLG mutations (unlike in other patients where valproate hepatotoxicity is dose-dependent and rarer).
  • 3) Seizure management: levetiracetam (preferred), topiramate, clobazam — AVOID valproate.
  • 4) Hepatic support: vitamin K, FFP for coagulopathy, dextrose for hypoglycaemia.
  • 5) Liver transplant has been controversial — historically considered futile given multi-organ disease and inevitable CNS progression; selected cases may benefit.
  • 6) Mitochondrial cocktail: CoQ10, riboflavin, thiamine, levocarnitine — limited evidence.
  • 7) Multidisciplinary: neurology, metabolism, hepatology, palliative care from early stages.
  • 8) Aggressive treatment of intercurrent illness (catabolic decompensation).
  • 9) Family genetic counselling: AR 25% recurrence; carrier screening; prenatal options.
  • 10) Prognosis: severe neonatal form generally fatal in early childhood from progressive neurodegeneration + intractable seizures + hepatic failure.
Case 278 — Schoolchild with myoclonus and progressive ataxia HARD
A 9-year-old has 18 months of progressive myoclonic jerks (worse with action), generalised tonic-clonic seizures, gait ataxia, mild dementia. EEG: generalised polyspike-and-wave + photoparoxysmal response at low frequencies (1–3 Hz). MRI: cerebellar atrophy. Skin biopsy: characteristic curvilinear inclusion bodies (lipofuscin) in sweat glands and eccrine ducts.

DX

Diagnosis and the inheritance/genetic basis?
Show model answer
  • Progressive myoclonic epilepsy (PME) — most likely neuronal ceroid lipofuscinosis (NCL) given age and skin biopsy findings.
  • NCL is a group of lysosomal storage diseases with characteristic curvilinear / fingerprint / granular osmiophilic deposits on electron microscopy.
  • Multiple genetic subtypes (CLN1-CLN14) — autosomal recessive mostly.
  • Juvenile NCL (CLN3, Batten disease) — most common pediatric onset around 5–10 years: visual loss (often first symptom), seizures, cognitive decline, behavioural changes.
  • Other PMEs to consider:
  • - Unverricht-Lundborg disease (EPM1, cystatin B) — Baltic origin, less severe dementia.
  • - Lafora disease (EPM2A/EPM2B) — teenage onset, severe and rapid progression, Lafora bodies on skin biopsy.
  • - Mitochondrial (MERRF — ragged-red fibres, m.8344A>G).
  • - Sialidosis type I (alpha-NEU1).
  • - Dentatorubral-pallidoluysian atrophy (DRPLA — Asian populations, ATN1 CAG repeat).

TREATMENT

Management?
Show model answer
  • 1) Symptomatic seizure control:
  • - Broad-spectrum AEDs: valproate, levetiracetam, clobazam, zonisamide.
  • - AVOID phenytoin, carbamazepine, oxcarbazepine, gabapentin, vigabatrin (worsen myoclonic seizures).
  • - Avoid valproate in mitochondrial-related PME (POLG mutations).
  • 2) Disease-modifying:
  • - CLN2 (TPP1) deficiency: cerliponase alfa (Brineura) — intracerebroventricular enzyme replacement; FDA-approved 2017.
  • - Other NCL subtypes: gene therapy and substrate reduction in trials.
  • 3) Vision support, mobility aids, swallow management.
  • 4) Multidisciplinary: neurology, ophthalmology, palliative care early integration.
  • 5) Genetic counselling per inheritance and specific subtype.
  • 6) Most forms are progressive and life-limiting; CLN2 treatment slows progression but doesn't reverse.
Case 279 — Adolescent with new-onset severe muscle pain and exercise intolerance MOD
A 15-year-old has exercise intolerance and recurrent episodes of muscle pain, cramping, and dark urine following anaerobic exercise (sprints, weight lifting). Symptoms have been present since childhood but worsening. Forearm ischaemic exercise test: ABSENT lactate rise; normal ammonia rise. Muscle biopsy: marked subsarcolemmal glycogen vacuoles; absent myophosphorylase staining.

DX

Diagnosis and the metabolic block?
Show model answer
  • McArdle disease (glycogen storage disease type V, myophosphorylase deficiency) — autosomal recessive PYGM gene mutations.
  • Myophosphorylase is the rate-limiting enzyme for glycogenolysis in muscle.
  • Cannot break down glycogen → cannot access major energy substrate during intense exercise.
  • Forearm ischaemic test: failure of lactate rise (cannot generate lactate from glycogen) with normal/exaggerated ammonia rise = classic.
  • 'Second wind' phenomenon: improved tolerance after a brief rest as muscle switches to FFA/blood glucose fuel.
  • Persistent mild CK elevation between episodes; severe rises with rhabdomyolysis episodes.

TREATMENT

Activity modification and exercise prescription?
Show model answer
  • 1) Education about disease and the 'second wind' phenomenon.
  • 2) Warm-up gradually before exercise; allow second wind to develop.
  • 3) Avoid intense isometric/anaerobic exercise.
  • 4) Sucrose loading (75 g) 30 minutes before exercise — improves tolerance by providing blood glucose for muscle.
  • 5) Aerobic conditioning programme — improves mitochondrial enzymes and substrate utilisation.
  • 6) Avoid muscle-toxic drugs (statins).
  • 7) Medical alert bracelet; anaesthesia precautions (succinylcholine can trigger severe rhabdomyolysis — use rocuronium).
  • 8) During rhabdomyolysis: aggressive IV fluids, urine output target 1–3 mL/kg/h, urinary alkalinisation; monitor renal function.
  • 9) Genetic counselling: AR 25% recurrence; carrier siblings asymptomatic.
  • 10) Most patients have stable adult course.
Case 280 — Newborn with severe cardiomyopathy and macroglossia HARD
A 4-month-old infant presents with progressive feeding difficulties, weak cry, hypotonia, and respiratory distress. Examination: macroglossia, hepatomegaly, severe cardiomyopathy on echo (LVH with LVEF 30%). CXR: cardiomegaly. CK markedly elevated. Genetic: GAA mutation.

DX

Diagnosis and the enzyme deficiency?
Show model answer
  • Pompe disease (glycogen storage disease type II, infantile-onset) — autosomal recessive.
  • Acid α-glucosidase (GAA) deficiency — lysosomal enzyme.
  • Glycogen accumulates in lysosomes → cell damage in muscle (cardiac and skeletal), liver, and CNS.
  • Infantile-onset Pompe (IOPD): severe phenotype — cardiomyopathy with severe LVH, hepatomegaly, macroglossia, hypotonia ('floppy baby'), respiratory failure.
  • Late-onset Pompe (LOPD): progressive proximal myopathy + respiratory weakness, no cardiomyopathy.
  • Without treatment, IOPD fatal in first year from cardiorespiratory failure.

TREATMENT

Enzyme replacement and adjunctive?
Show model answer
  • 1) ENZYME REPLACEMENT THERAPY: alglucosidase alfa (Myozyme/Lumizyme) — recombinant human GAA, IV every 2 weeks.
  • 2) AVALGLUCOSIDASE ALFA (Nexviazyme) — newer ERT with improved lysosomal uptake; FDA-approved 2021.
  • 3) Start ERT as early as possible — best outcomes in infants treated within 6 months of life and before significant muscle damage.
  • 4) CROSS-REACTIVE IMMUNOLOGIC MATERIAL (CRIM) status critical:
  • - CRIM-positive: have some residual enzyme; usually respond well to ERT.
  • - CRIM-negative: produce no enzyme; develop high-titre antibodies to ERT that neutralise it; need IMMUNE TOLERANCE INDUCTION (ITI) with rituximab/methotrexate/IVIG before/during ERT.
  • 5) CARDIAC management: medications for HF (ACE inhibitor, β-blocker), arrhythmia surveillance.
  • 6) RESPIRATORY: BiPAP, eventually invasive ventilation; cough assist.
  • 7) NUTRITION: G-tube feeding often required.
  • 8) Multidisciplinary: neuromuscular clinic, cardiology, pulmonology, GI/nutrition, anaesthesia consult before procedures.
  • 9) Newborn screening for Pompe in many regions allows pre-symptomatic ERT — best outcomes.
  • 10) Gene therapy in clinical trials.
Case 281 — Schoolchild with seizures and severe sleep dysregulation MOD
An 8-year-old has refractory focal epilepsy with frequent night-time motor seizures (multiple per night). Daytime: poor school performance, behavioural problems, severe daytime sleepiness, dropping cognition over 18 months. EEG awake: occasional centrotemporal spikes. SLEEP EEG: continuous bilateral spike-and-wave activity present > 85% of NREM sleep (CSWS pattern).

DX

Diagnosis and the EEG phenomenon?
Show model answer
  • Encephalopathy with continuous spike-and-wave during slow sleep (CSWS) — also called electrical status epilepticus during sleep (ESES).
  • Defined by epileptiform discharges occupying ≥ 85% of NREM sleep on overnight EEG.
  • Associated with regression in cognitive, behavioural, language domains during the active phase.
  • Related entity: Landau-Kleffner syndrome (acquired epileptic aphasia) — language regression-dominant variant of CSWS spectrum.
  • Underlying causes: idiopathic, structural lesions (perinatal stroke, malformations), genetic (GRIN2A mutations in some).
  • Crucial because EEG abnormalities during sleep cause neurocognitive regression — treatment of EEG abnormalities improves cognitive outcome.

TREATMENT

Treatment approach?
Show model answer
  • 1) AEDs: valproate, ethosuximide, clobazam, levetiracetam.
  • 2) AVOID carbamazepine, oxcarbazepine, phenytoin — may worsen CSWS.
  • 3) HIGH-DOSE CORTICOSTEROIDS (oral prednisone or pulsed methylprednisolone) — often dramatically improves EEG and cognitive function. Duration: months to years.
  • 4) ACTH alternative steroid approach.
  • 5) IVIG in selected cases.
  • 6) Ketogenic diet for refractory cases.
  • 7) Surgical evaluation for focal cause (cortical dysplasia, perinatal infarct) — disconnection or resection can be transformative.
  • 8) Multiple subpial transections for non-resectable eloquent cortex involvement.
  • 9) Multidisciplinary: neurology, neuropsychology, speech/language therapy, school support.
  • 10) Long-term: cognitive recovery often incomplete even with EEG normalisation; earlier treatment = better outcomes.
  • 11) Repeat sleep EEG to monitor treatment response.
Case 282 — Adolescent with chronic vegetative state and shoulder/limb posturing MOD
An 18-year-old has been in a prolonged disorder of consciousness for 4 months following severe TBI. Initially vegetative state (eyes open, no awareness). Now: reproducible visual fixation on objects, intermittent localising to sound, occasional command following ('squeeze my hand'). Coma Recovery Scale-Revised (CRS-R) score 12.

DX

Defining the state and how it differs from vegetative state?
Show model answer
  • Minimally conscious state (MCS) — clinical state distinct from vegetative state (VS) / unresponsive wakefulness syndrome (UWS).
  • MCS defined by: reproducible BUT INCONSISTENT evidence of awareness — examples include visual fixation/pursuit, response to noxious stimulation with appropriate movement, intelligible verbalisation, simple command following.
  • VS/UWS: no behavioural evidence of awareness despite preserved arousal (eyes open, sleep-wake cycles).
  • MCS+ (plus): with language preserved (yes/no responses, intelligible verbalisation).
  • MCS– (minus): without language.
  • Coma Recovery Scale-Revised (CRS-R) = standardised assessment tool — includes auditory, visual, motor, oromotor, communication, arousal subscales.
  • Prognosis better in MCS than VS — more likely to recover further, though long-term disability common.

TREATMENT

Approach to prolonged disorders of consciousness?
Show model answer
  • 1) CAREFUL multidisciplinary assessment with validated scales (CRS-R) repeated over time — single assessment risks misclassification.
  • 2) Identify and treat confounders: pain, infection, hydrocephalus, drug effects (especially benzodiazepines), seizures (continuous EEG).
  • 3) Pharmacological trial of AMANTADINE — modest but significant evidence of improved recovery in subacute TBI; pediatric extrapolation.
  • 4) Apomorphine, modafinil, zolpidem (paradoxical responder phenomenon) explored in some.
  • 5) Intrathecal baclofen for severe spasticity (may improve interaction).
  • 6) Rehabilitation: sensory stimulation, positioning, contracture prevention, family engagement.
  • 7) NUTRITION via PEG; bowel/bladder management; skin care.
  • 8) Functional neuroimaging (fMRI motor imagery paradigm, EEG response to commands) may reveal 'covert awareness' in some patients classified as VS — Owen / Cruse / Naci studies.
  • 9) Multidisciplinary: neurology, rehab, neuropsychology, palliative care; family-centred decision-making.
  • 10) Pediatric prognostication generally better than adults — recovery from MCS over months-years possible.
Case 283 — Schoolchild with seizures and adventitious choreiform movements MOD
A 7-year-old has chorea and dystonia of orobuccal and limb muscles, episodic stiffening triggered by sudden movement, and brief atypical absence episodes. EEG: generalised spike-wave. MRI normal. Family: father has episodic stiffening 'when surprised' since childhood. Genetic: PRRT2 mutation.

DX

Diagnosis and the genetic basis?
Show model answer
  • PRRT2-related disorders — paroxysmal kinesigenic dyskinesia (PKD) with benign familial infantile epilepsy spectrum.
  • PRRT2 mutations (autosomal dominant; chromosome 16) cause a spectrum of paroxysmal disorders:
  • - Benign familial infantile epilepsy (BFIE) — typically resolves in childhood.
  • - Paroxysmal kinesigenic dyskinesia (PKD) — sudden movement-triggered brief episodes of dystonia/chorea.
  • - Infantile convulsions with choreoathetosis (ICCA) — combination phenotype.
  • - Other paroxysmal movement disorders.
  • Mechanism: PRRT2 codes for a presynaptic protein involved in neurotransmitter release; mutations cause neuronal hyperexcitability.
  • Strong family history typical (high penetrance).

TREATMENT

Therapy and prognosis?
Show model answer
  • 1) LOW-DOSE CARBAMAZEPINE or OXCARBAZEPINE — first-line and dramatically effective for both PKD attacks and seizures.
  • 2) Phenytoin, lamotrigine alternative.
  • 3) Levetiracetam — for the epilepsy component.
  • 4) Generally excellent response and prognosis.
  • 5) Some patients become attack-free by adulthood without ongoing treatment.
  • 6) Genetic counselling: AD with high penetrance; cascade testing of relatives.
  • 7) Distinguish from:
  • - Paroxysmal non-kinesigenic dyskinesia (PNKD, MR1 gene; longer attacks, triggered by alcohol/caffeine/stress, not movement).
  • - Paroxysmal exercise-induced dyskinesia (PED, GLUT1 SLC2A1; attacks after prolonged exercise).
  • - Episodic ataxia type 1 / type 2.
  • - Hyperekplexia (glycine receptor).
  • - Tics, Tourette syndrome.
Case 284 — Toddler with rapidly developing severe symmetrical leukodystrophy HARD
An 18-month-old infant developed normally until 12 months. Then over 6 months: progressive macrocephaly (HC +3 SD), severe truncal hypotonia with limb spasticity, regression of milestones (lost speech, sitting), nystagmus, swallowing difficulty. MRI: bilateral, symmetrical, extensive white-matter T2 hyperintensities with cystic vacuolation, BASAL GANGLIA and CEREBELLUM relatively spared. Urine NAA: normal. Genetic: TUBB4A mutation.

DX

Diagnosis and the protein affected?
Show model answer
  • H-ABC (Hypomyelination with Atrophy of Basal Ganglia and Cerebellum) syndrome, due to TUBB4A mutation.
  • However TUBB4A mutation can present with various phenotypes including DYT4 (whispering dysphonia) and H-ABC.
  • The MRI in this case (symmetric WHITE-matter changes with CYSTIC degeneration but BG/cerebellar SPARED) actually more typical of VANISHING WHITE MATTER (VWM) disease — caused by EIF2B1-5 mutations affecting eIF2B (initiation factor for translation).
  • VWM characteristic features: episodes of deterioration triggered by febrile illness, minor head trauma, or stress (rapid progression); chronic progressive course.
  • MRI hallmark: diffuse cerebral white-matter rarefaction → cystic degeneration ('vanishing'); relative sparing of cortex and deep grey nuclei.
  • MRS: low or absent NAA, choline, creatine in affected white matter.

TREATMENT

Management?
Show model answer
  • 1) No disease-modifying therapy currently approved.
  • 2) Avoid TRIGGERS:
  • - Aggressive antipyresis with any fever.
  • - Helmet for head injury prevention.
  • - Avoid significant stressors when possible.
  • 3) Vaccination encouraged (vaccines themselves don't trigger; the febrile illnesses they prevent do); plan for fever management post-vaccination.
  • 4) Low threshold for hospitalisation with intercurrent illness to manage temperature, hydration, and nutrition.
  • 5) Supportive: seizure management, spasticity (oral baclofen, ITB if severe), nutrition (PEG often needed), respiratory support.
  • 6) Multidisciplinary with palliative care integration early.
  • 7) Genetic counselling: AR; cascade testing.
  • 8) Therapies in development: small-molecule eIF2B activators (e.g., ISRIB analogues) under investigation.
  • 9) Prognosis variable — childhood-onset forms often progress over years; adult-onset milder.
Case 285 — Adolescent with severe asthma needing intubation and resulting weakness MOD
A 14-year-old admitted to PICU for severe asthma exacerbation, intubated and ventilated for 9 days requiring continuous infusion of midazolam, fentanyl, and intermittent vecuronium for ventilator dyssynchrony. Now extubated 3 days. He is awake but has profound bilateral proximal limb weakness, can barely lift arms off bed, reflexes diminished, CK 1800.

DX

Most likely diagnosis and the contributing factors?
Show model answer
  • ICU-acquired weakness (ICUAW) — likely combination of critical illness myopathy (CIM) and critical illness polyneuropathy (CIP).
  • Risk factors:
  • - Sepsis, SIRS, multiorgan failure.
  • - Prolonged immobilisation.
  • - Hyperglycaemia.
  • - CORTICOSTEROIDS — high-dose pulse therapy (very relevant in severe asthma).
  • - NEUROMUSCULAR BLOCKADE for prolonged periods (especially aminosteroid agents like vecuronium, rocuronium combined with corticosteroids → severe quadriparesis).
  • - Mechanical ventilation duration.
  • Mechanism: combination of muscle protein breakdown, mitochondrial dysfunction, denervation-like changes, electrical inexcitability of muscle membrane.
  • EMG/NCS: low CMAP amplitudes; needle EMG shows fibrillations (denervation pattern in CIP) or myopathic units (in CIM).

TREATMENT

Management and prevention?
Show model answer
  • 1) Avoid further contributors: minimise/stop corticosteroids and NMBA when possible.
  • 2) Aggressive glycaemic control (target normoglycaemia per current evidence, avoiding both hyper- and hypoglycaemia).
  • 3) EARLY MOBILISATION — strongest preventive evidence; passive then active range-of-motion, sitting, standing as tolerated even during mechanical ventilation.
  • 4) Daily SEDATION INTERRUPTION / TARGETED LIGHTER SEDATION (minimise benzodiazepines specifically).
  • 5) NUTRITION: adequate protein delivery; enteral preferred.
  • 6) Physical and occupational therapy; long process — recovery often takes weeks-months, some have persistent weakness.
  • 7) Diaphragmatic involvement may delay weaning from ventilator.
  • 8) Multidisciplinary: ICU, rehab, neurology, family education.
  • 9) Prevention bundle: ABCDE (Awakening, Breathing trials, Choice of sedation, Delirium monitoring, Early mobility) shown to reduce ICUAW.
  • 10) Long-term sequelae: protracted weakness, deconditioning, return to function may take months-years; some develop chronic pain and fatigue.
Case 286 — Schoolchild with severe head trauma and post-op fever and pus HARD
A 12-year-old with severe TBI had an EVD placed 7 days ago. Now: T 39.4°C, neck stiffness, declining LOC. CSF from EVD: WBC 1800 with 90% neutrophils, glucose 1.0 mmol/L (low), protein 3.5 g/L (high), Gram stain: gram-positive cocci in chains.

DX

Diagnosis and likely organisms?
Show model answer
  • EVD-associated ventriculitis / meningitis (healthcare-associated, post-neurosurgical).
  • Incidence: 5–15% of EVDs; risk increases with duration of catheter, CSF sampling, leaking system, breaks in sterility.
  • Common organisms:
  • - Coagulase-negative staphylococci (most common — biofilm-forming).
  • - Staphylococcus aureus including MRSA.
  • - Cutibacterium acnes (formerly Propionibacterium acnes).
  • - Gram-negative bacilli including resistant nosocomial flora.
  • - Enterococci.
  • Gram-positive cocci in chains on Gram stain in this case suggests streptococci or enterococci.

TREATMENT

Antibiotic regimen and catheter management?
Show model answer
  • 1) EMPIRICAL antibiotics covering MRSA + Gram-negatives:
  • - IV VANCOMYCIN (loading dose 15–20 mg/kg; trough 15–20 µg/mL) + IV CEFEPIME or MEROPENEM (for Gram-negative including pseudomonas).
  • 2) Narrow based on cultures and susceptibilities.
  • 3) DURATION: typically 10–14 days from first sterile CSF culture (often 21 days total for ventriculitis).
  • 4) INTRAVENTRICULAR / INTRATHECAL antibiotics for refractory infection or specific resistant organisms — vancomycin 10–20 mg, gentamicin 5–10 mg via EVD (after dose clamp EVD for 30–60 min then drain).
  • 5) REMOVE INFECTED CATHETER as soon as feasible; replace if continued CSF drainage needed (some advocate immediate replacement at different site; others wait until CSF sterile).
  • 6) Repeat CSF cultures to confirm clearance.
  • 7) Multidisciplinary: ID, neurosurgery, microbiology.
  • 8) Consider risk factors: CSF leak around insertion site, multiple sampling, breaks in sterility.
  • 9) Prevention: chlorhexidine prep, antimicrobial-coated catheters, closed system, minimise sampling, remove EVD ASAP when no longer needed.
Case 287 — Newborn with respiratory failure and characteristic facial features MOD
A term newborn presents at 6 hours with respiratory failure, severe generalised hypotonia, weak cry, and characteristic features: long face, tented upper lip, ptosis, talipes equinovarus, undescended testes. Mother has clinical evidence of facial weakness and grip myotonia. CK normal.

DX

Most likely diagnosis and the inheritance pattern?
Show model answer
  • Congenital myotonic dystrophy type 1 (DM1) — autosomal dominant; almost always inherited from AFFECTED MOTHER (maternal anticipation phenomenon — repeat expands during oogenesis).
  • CTG repeat expansion in DMPK gene (chromosome 19q13).
  • Normal: < 35 repeats. Premutation: 35–49. Mild DM1: 50–150. Adult: 100–1000. Congenital: usually > 1000-2000 repeats.
  • CONGENITAL DM1 features: severe neonatal hypotonia, respiratory failure, feeding difficulties, characteristic facial features (long face, tented mouth — 'inverted V upper lip'), arthrogryposis (especially talipes), high mortality in neonatal period (up to 25%).
  • Survivors of neonatal period: cognitive impairment, motor delay, eventual development of classic myotonic dystrophy features (myotonia, distal weakness, cataracts, cardiac conduction disease).
  • MOTHER usually has milder adult-onset disease (grip myotonia, ptosis, facial weakness, fronto-temporal balding in males) that may be undiagnosed before her affected child is born.

TREATMENT

Management and counselling?
Show model answer
  • 1) NEONATAL: respiratory support (often prolonged mechanical ventilation), nutrition (NG/PEG feeding), thermal regulation, treat aspiration pneumonia.
  • 2) Confirm with genetic testing (CTG expansion in DMPK).
  • 3) Maternal evaluation: cardiac (conduction abnormalities), endocrine (insulin resistance, hypothyroidism), MUSCULAR symptoms.
  • 4) Long-term:
  • - Cognitive support / education.
  • - Cardiac surveillance (ECG, Holter, echo; conduction blocks require pacemaker).
  • - Cataract surveillance.
  • - Endocrine surveillance (DM, hypothyroidism, hypogonadism).
  • - Anaesthesia precautions (lifelong) — sensitivity to sedatives, opioids, sux can trigger myotonia, post-op respiratory failure.
  • 5) Genetic counselling: AD with anticipation; risk of affected offspring up to 100% if mother is affected; risk of congenital DM1 in offspring of affected mother ~50%.
  • 6) Pre-conception counselling for affected women.
  • 7) Multidisciplinary: neuromuscular clinic, cardiology, endocrine, ophthalmology, anaesthesia review before any procedures.
Case 288 — Adolescent with persistent encephalopathy after stopping anticonvulsant MOD
A 16-year-old on long-term phenobarbital for epilepsy is admitted with sudden cessation of phenobarbital (lost prescription for 2 weeks). Now: confusion, anxiety, tremor, T 38.8°C, HR 130, BP 165/95, generalised tonic-clonic seizure on arrival.

DX

Diagnosis and the mechanism?
Show model answer
  • Barbiturate (phenobarbital) withdrawal syndrome.
  • Long-term phenobarbital → adaptive changes in GABA-A receptors and other CNS systems → tolerance and dependence.
  • Abrupt discontinuation removes the chronic GABAergic enhancement → withdrawal hyperexcitability.
  • Features parallel alcohol/benzodiazepine withdrawal: anxiety, tremor, autonomic hyperactivity (tachycardia, hypertension, sweating, hyperthermia), agitation, hallucinations, seizures (rebound).
  • Phenobarbital withdrawal can be severe and life-threatening; seizures may occur even in patients without prior epilepsy.
  • Risk increases with longer duration of use and higher doses.

TREATMENT

Acute management?
Show model answer
  • 1) RESTART PHENOBARBITAL — load with IV dose if seizing (15–20 mg/kg), then maintenance.
  • 2) Aim to re-achieve therapeutic level.
  • 3) For symptomatic management of withdrawal: benzodiazepines (lorazepam, diazepam) for breakthrough symptoms.
  • 4) Supportive care: IV fluids, cooling for hyperthermia, monitor airway.
  • 5) ICU admission for severe presentations (seizures, autonomic instability).
  • 6) If patient is to stop phenobarbital (e.g., AED change): SLOW TAPER over weeks to months — typically 10–25% dose reduction every 2 weeks.
  • 7) Cross-taper with replacement AED (e.g., levetiracetam, lacosamide) for those on phenobarbital for epilepsy.
  • 8) Address adherence issues: medication safety, refill systems, simplify regimen.
  • 9) Long-term: counsel about importance of medication continuity, never abrupt cessation; medical alert bracelet.
  • 10) Education for family and adolescent — addiction is NOT the issue; physiological dependence to control seizures is the framing.
Case 289 — Toddler post-vaccination with sudden floppy spells MOD
A 6-month-old infant received routine 6-month vaccinations 3 days ago (DTaP, Hib, hepatitis B, rotavirus, PCV13). Now: cluster of episodes — sudden flexor spasm of the trunk and arms, with the head nodding forward, lasting 1–2 seconds, occurring in clusters of 10–20 per cluster, multiple clusters per day. EEG: hypsarrhythmia.

DX

Diagnosis and the EEG pattern?
Show model answer
  • Infantile epileptic spasms syndrome (IESS) — formerly West syndrome.
  • Triad: (1) infantile spasms (brief flexor/extensor spasms in clusters, often on awakening), (2) hypsarrhythmia (chaotic, high-amplitude, asynchronous EEG with multifocal epileptiform discharges), (3) developmental regression or arrest.
  • Peak onset 3–7 months.
  • Causes (50–70% identifiable):
  • - Structural (tuberous sclerosis, cortical malformations, perinatal stroke, AHT).
  • - Genetic (ARX, STXBP1, CDKL5, KCNQ2, many others).
  • - Metabolic (PKU, pyridoxine-dependent, biotinidase, NKH).
  • - Idiopathic / cryptogenic (worse prognosis if no cause found and developmentally normal).
  • Timing relative to vaccination is COINCIDENTAL — peak age of West syndrome coincides with routine vaccination schedule; vaccines do NOT cause West syndrome.

TREATMENT

Urgent treatment?
Show model answer
  • 1) TIME-CRITICAL — early treatment dramatically improves cognitive outcome. Initiate within days of recognition.
  • 2) FIRST-LINE options (depend on suspected aetiology):
  • - For TSC: VIGABATRIN 100–150 mg/kg/day (uniquely effective in TSC); monitor for visual field constriction.
  • - For non-TSC: high-dose ACTH OR high-dose oral corticosteroids OR vigabatrin. United Kingdom Infantile Spasms Study (UKISS) showed hormonal therapy + vigabatrin combination superior to monotherapy.
  • 3) Goals: complete cessation of spasms AND resolution of hypsarrhythmia (must verify with repeat EEG).
  • 4) If first-line fails: switch to alternative first-line, or escalate to ketogenic diet, other AEDs.
  • 5) Evaluate aetiology in parallel: brain MRI, genetic testing (next-generation sequencing panel), metabolic screen (urine organic acids, plasma amino acids, lactate, ammonia, CSF if dysmorphic features).
  • 6) Refractory cases: surgical evaluation for focal cause (especially TSC tubers, focal cortical dysplasia).
  • 7) Multidisciplinary care: neurology, developmental paediatrics, ophthalmology (vigabatrin visual surveillance), genetics.
  • 8) Family education about emergency seizure plan; medical alert.
  • 9) Long-term: many evolve to Lennox-Gastaut syndrome; cognitive outcomes vary; some achieve near-normal development with early effective treatment, especially in cryptogenic cases.
Case 290 — Adolescent with severe sleep disturbance, hallucinations, sleep paralysis MOD
A 14-year-old has 8 months of excessive daytime sleepiness (sleeping 14+ hours/day), vivid dream-like hallucinations as he falls asleep, brief episodes of being unable to move on waking, and recently 1 episode of sudden loss of muscle tone making him drop to one knee when surprised by his sister. CSF hypocretin-1 (orexin-A) 12 pg/mL (markedly low).

DX

Diagnosis and the underlying pathology?
Show model answer
  • Narcolepsy type 1 (NT1) — narcolepsy WITH CATAPLEXY and hypocretin deficiency.
  • Caused by autoimmune destruction of hypothalamic hypocretin (orexin)-producing neurons.
  • Strong HLA-DQB1*06:02 association (necessary but not sufficient).
  • Pentad of features (not all present in every patient):
  • 1) Excessive daytime sleepiness (universal, often first symptom).
  • 2) Cataplexy — sudden bilateral loss of muscle tone triggered by strong emotion (laughter, surprise, anger); consciousness preserved; pathognomonic.
  • 3) Hypnagogic / hypnopompic hallucinations (vivid dream-like experiences at sleep onset/offset).
  • 4) Sleep paralysis (transient inability to move on waking).
  • 5) Fragmented night sleep.
  • Diagnostic: CSF hypocretin-1 < 110 pg/mL is highly specific; or polysomnography + MSLT showing mean sleep latency < 8 min + ≥ 2 sleep-onset REM periods (SOREMPs).

TREATMENT

Pharmacotherapy approach?
Show model answer
  • 1) EDS treatment:
  • - Modafinil, armodafinil — wakefulness-promoting agents.
  • - Methylphenidate, dextroamphetamine — alternatives if inadequate.
  • - Pitolisant — histamine H3 receptor antagonist; newer non-stimulant.
  • - Solriamfetol — newer agent.
  • 2) CATAPLEXY treatment:
  • - Sodium oxybate (γ-hydroxybutyrate) — most effective; FDA-approved; addresses cataplexy AND consolidates sleep AND treats EDS.
  • - Antidepressants suppressing REM (clomipramine, fluoxetine, venlafaxine) — alternative.
  • 3) SCHEDULED NAPS during the day (improve symptoms).
  • 4) Sleep hygiene; avoid alcohol/sedatives; school accommodations.
  • 5) MEDICAL ALERT and DRIVING SAFETY counselling.
  • 6) Multidisciplinary: sleep medicine, mental health support.
  • 7) Family genetic counselling — multifactorial; HLA-DQB1*06:02 background risk but no simple inheritance.
  • 8) Investigate for autoimmune comorbidities.
Case 291 — Adolescent with rapidly progressive painless vision loss bilaterally HARD
A 16-year-old male presents with painless central vision loss in the right eye over 2 weeks, then identical loss in left eye 6 weeks later. No pain on eye movement (distinguishing from optic neuritis). Fundoscopy: telangiectatic microangiopathy of the optic disc initially, then optic atrophy. Maternal uncle developed similar visual loss as a young adult.

DX

Diagnosis and the inheritance pattern?
Show model answer
  • Leber hereditary optic neuropathy (LHON).
  • Mitochondrial DNA disease — three primary mutations: m.11778G>A (most common, 50%, complex I subunit ND4), m.14484T>C, m.3460G>A. All affect complex I.
  • MATERNAL inheritance (mitochondrial DNA — passed only through ova).
  • Heteroplasmy and incomplete penetrance — only some carriers develop disease (~ 50% of male carriers, ~ 10% of female carriers; sex-specific modifiers including X-linked locus suspected).
  • STRONG MALE PREDOMINANCE (~ 80–90% of affected are male).
  • Onset typically adolescence/young adulthood.
  • Painless, subacute, sequential bilateral visual loss is classic.

TREATMENT

Treatment options?
Show model answer
  • 1) IDEBENONE (synthetic ubiquinone analogue, Raxone) — approved in Europe and Asia for LHON; modest evidence for partial improvement, especially with m.11778 mutation.
  • 2) AVOID smoking, alcohol — strongly worsen LHON penetrance and progression.
  • 3) AVOID mitotoxic medications: linezolid, aminoglycosides, certain antiretrovirals (zidovudine), chloramphenicol.
  • 4) GENE THERAPY: lenadogene nolparvovec (Lumevoq) — AAV-delivered ND4 in intravitreal injection for m.11778; in clinical trials, some encouraging data.
  • 5) Coenzyme Q10, vitamin supplementation often advised (weak evidence).
  • 6) Visual rehabilitation, low-vision aids, school/work accommodations.
  • 7) Family counselling: maternal-line relatives at risk; cascade testing offered.
  • 8) Differentials:
  • - Bilateral optic neuritis (painful, demyelinating workup).
  • - Nutritional / toxic optic neuropathy (B12, B1 deficiency; methanol; ethambutol).
  • - Other hereditary optic atrophies (OPA1 dominant optic atrophy, OPA3 Costeff).
  • 9) Many patients retain peripheral vision and learn to function; visual recovery (especially partial) occurs in some, particularly with m.14484.
Case 292 — Schoolchild with chronic chest pain and progressive scoliosis HARD
An 11-year-old with severe scoliosis (Cobb angle 65°) presents after a syncopal episode. Echocardiogram: severe restrictive cardiomyopathy. Physical: pes cavus, hammertoes, distal weakness, areflexia at ankles, sensory ataxia, reduced vibration. GAA repeat expansion homozygous (700/720) in FXN gene.

DX

Confirmed diagnosis and the implications of repeat size?
Show model answer
  • Friedreich ataxia (FRDA) — autosomal recessive.
  • GAA trinucleotide repeat expansion in intron 1 of FXN (frataxin) gene on chromosome 9q21.
  • Reduces frataxin expression (mitochondrial protein for iron-sulfur cluster assembly).
  • Repeat-size correlation:
  • - Normal: 5–33 repeats.
  • - Pre-mutation: 34–65.
  • - Disease range: 66–1700.
  • - Larger repeats correlate with EARLIER onset, FASTER progression, EARLIER cardiomyopathy, EARLIER diabetes, MORE SEVERE motor disability.
  • This patient (700/720 repeats) has large repeats predicting severe phenotype.
  • Compound heterozygotes (one expansion + one point mutation) ~ 4% of cases.

TREATMENT

Surveillance and disease-modifying therapy?
Show model answer
  • 1) CARDIOLOGY surveillance:
  • - Annual echo + ECG (often shows HCM evolving to dilated cardiomyopathy; arrhythmia common).
  • - Cardiac MRI for tissue characterisation.
  • - Heart failure management (ACE inhibitor, β-blocker).
  • - ICD if sustained ventricular arrhythmia or EF severely reduced.
  • - Heart-failure-related death is leading cause of mortality.
  • 2) ORTHOPAEDIC: spine surgery for severe progressive scoliosis (can compromise respiratory function); foot surgery for severe deformities.
  • 3) ENDOCRINE: annual HbA1c — diabetes develops in 30%.
  • 4) Audiology, ophthalmology.
  • 5) DISEASE-MODIFYING:
  • - OMAVELOXOLONE (Skyclarys) — FDA-approved 2023 for FRDA in patients ≥ 16; Nrf2 activator reduces oxidative stress.
  • - MOXIe trial showed modest mFARS improvement at 48 weeks.
  • - Limited cardiac data; primarily neurological benefit.
  • 6) Investigational: gene therapy with AAV9-frataxin (clinical trials), other compounds.
  • 7) Multidisciplinary clinic: neurology, cardiology, endocrinology, orthopaedics, rehab, genetic counselling.
  • 8) Family genetic counselling: AR with 25% recurrence; sibling testing offered (presymptomatic diagnosis allows monitoring/early intervention).
Case 293 — Infant with progressive deformity and joint contractures MOD
A 4-month-old infant presents with multiple congenital joint contractures (clubfoot bilaterally, knee and elbow contractures, hip dislocations), polyhydramnios history, weak cry, poor suck, characteristic 'flexed posture' since birth, normal facies. Maternal anti-AChR antibody positive in maternal serum.

DX

Diagnosis and the pathology?
Show model answer
  • Arthrogryposis multiplex congenita (AMC) — likely with neuromuscular cause; given maternal AChR antibody positivity, NEONATAL TRANSIENT MYASTHENIA GRAVIS (now improved) or FETAL ACETYLCHOLINE RECEPTOR INACTIVATION SYNDROME (FARIS) potential.
  • AMC is a clinical syndrome (not a single disease) — congenital multiple joint contractures from in-utero reduced fetal movement.
  • Causes:
  • - Neurogenic (spinal cord/anterior horn — most common): SMA, congenital infections, cord malformation.
  • - Myopathic: congenital myopathy, myotonic dystrophy, congenital muscular dystrophy.
  • - Neuromuscular junction: maternal myasthenia transferring antibodies through placenta (transient if mother has MG); FARIS (severe form with permanent joint contractures from in-utero blockade of fetal AChR).
  • - Connective tissue, fetal crowding, oligohydramnios.
  • - Genetic syndromes (distal arthrogryposis).

NEXT STEP

Workup?
Show model answer
  • 1) Detailed family history (maternal myasthenia, other neuromuscular).
  • 2) Maternal evaluation if myasthenia not previously diagnosed: AChR antibody, MuSK antibody.
  • 3) Infant workup: neuromuscular examination (tone, reflexes, ventilation), CK, EMG/NCS, genetic testing panel for congenital arthrogryposis/myopathy/dystrophy/SMN1 deletion (SMA), MRI brain/spine, swallow study, sleep study.
  • 4) Cardiac and respiratory monitoring (especially for myasthenia and myotonic dystrophy).
  • 5) For transient neonatal myasthenia: typically resolves in 2–4 weeks; supportive care during this period (NG feeds, respiratory support, sometimes pyridostigmine, occasionally IVIG/PLEX).
  • 6) For FARIS: severe disability often permanent; multidisciplinary care.
  • 7) Multidisciplinary management of contractures: physiotherapy, orthopaedic surgery (releases, osteotomies), serial casting.
  • 8) Mother needs ongoing management if she has myasthenia.
  • 9) Pre-conception counselling for future pregnancies: maternal MG plan, possible IVIG/PLEX in pregnancy.
Case 294 — Adolescent with stroke after thrombolysis-related haemorrhage HARD
A 16-year-old received IV alteplase 3 hours after onset of right hemiparesis from suspected ischaemic stroke (no contraindications identified at the time). 90 minutes after tPA: sudden severe headache, vomiting, declining LOC, fixed left pupil. Repeat CT: large left hemispheric haematoma with 8 mm midline shift.

DX

Diagnosis and immediate management?
Show model answer
  • Symptomatic intracranial haemorrhage (sICH) after tPA — rate ~ 6% in adult ischaemic stroke; risk factors include large infarct, hypertension, age, prior anticoagulation, NIHSS severity.
  • Time-critical reversal protocol applies.

NEXT STEP

Reversal and reversal agents?
Show model answer
  • 1) STOP alteplase infusion immediately.
  • 2) URGENT laboratory studies: fibrinogen, INR, aPTT, platelet count, type and screen.
  • 3) REVERSAL of fibrinolysis:
  • - CRYOPRECIPITATE 10 units (or 0.15 unit/kg) — repletes fibrinogen depleted by tPA (target fibrinogen > 150 mg/dL).
  • - TRANEXAMIC ACID 10–15 mg/kg IV — antifibrinolytic; reduces ongoing thrombolytic effect.
  • - Or aminocaproic acid as alternative.
  • - Platelet transfusion only if thrombocytopenic.
  • 4) BLOOD PRESSURE control — target SBP < 140 acutely (avoid extremes).
  • 5) NEUROSURGICAL evaluation for clot evacuation, decompressive craniectomy if mass effect and herniation.
  • 6) ICP management: head up 30°, normocapnia, normothermia, hyperosmolar therapy (3% saline or mannitol) as bridge to surgery.
  • 7) Intubate for airway protection if GCS < 8 or worsening.
  • 8) Family communication and shared decision-making.
  • 9) ICU admission.
  • 10) Long-term outcome variable; significant disability/mortality with large ICH post-tPA.
  • 11) Pediatric tPA use remains controversial — Stroke trials in adolescents extrapolated from adult data; centres vary in practice; mechanical thrombectomy increasingly preferred for LVO without lytic risk.
Case 295 — Adolescent with stupor on lithium and recent thiazide diuretic HARD
A 15-year-old with bipolar disorder on lithium 900 mg/day was started on hydrochlorothiazide for hypertension 2 weeks ago. Now: confusion, tremor, ataxia, hyperreflexia, dysarthria, T 39.0°C, mild AKI. Serum lithium 3.8 mEq/L (toxic; target 0.6–1.2).

DX

Diagnosis and the drug interaction?
Show model answer
  • Lithium toxicity precipitated by thiazide diuretic-induced volume depletion.
  • Thiazides (HCTZ) and other diuretics (loop diuretics also) reduce sodium delivery to distal tubule → increased proximal Na reabsorption → also increases LITHIUM reabsorption proximally (lithium handled similarly to Na in proximal tubule) → rising lithium levels.
  • NSAIDs and ACE inhibitors also raise lithium levels via reduced renal clearance.
  • Lithium toxicity classification:
  • - Mild (1.5–2.5): tremor, GI symptoms, fatigue.
  • - Moderate (2.5–3.5): coarse tremor, dysarthria, ataxia, confusion.
  • - Severe (> 3.5): seizures, coma, arrhythmia, AKI, may be fatal.
  • Chronic toxicity often presents at lower levels with more severe neurologic features than acute overdose.

TREATMENT

Management approach?
Show model answer
  • 1) STOP LITHIUM IMMEDIATELY.
  • 2) STOP HCTZ; manage hypertension differently (e.g., amlodipine, ACE inhibitor with renal monitoring — but caveat lithium interaction).
  • 3) IV FLUIDS (0.9% NaCl) — restore volume, enhance renal clearance.
  • 4) CARDIAC MONITORING — lithium can prolong QT, cause AV conduction abnormalities, arrhythmias.
  • 5) HEMODIALYSIS indications:
  • - Level > 4 mEq/L regardless of symptoms.
  • - Level 2.5–4 with severe symptoms (seizures, coma, severe AKI).
  • - Refractory toxicity or hemodynamic instability.
  • - Beware POST-DIALYSIS REBOUND — lithium redistributes from intracellular to extracellular compartment; repeat session often needed.
  • 6) AVOID activated charcoal (does NOT bind lithium effectively).
  • 7) Consider polyethylene glycol whole-bowel irrigation for sustained-release ingestion within 1 hour.
  • 8) Treat seizures with benzodiazepines.
  • 9) After recovery: address ongoing bipolar treatment; alternative mood stabiliser may be needed if recurrent toxicity; if continuing lithium, careful drug interaction review, dose reduction, regular monitoring.
  • 10) Long-term sequelae: nephrogenic diabetes insipidus, chronic kidney disease, hypothyroidism, cognitive effects.
Case 296 — Newborn with congenital adrenal hyperplasia and severe acidosis crisis MOD
A 7-day-old infant (newborn screening pending) presents with vomiting, profound dehydration, lethargy, hypotonia. Na 121, K 7.8, glucose 1.8. Ambiguous genitalia in genotypic female. Renal salt-wasting suspected. ABG: pH 7.0, severe metabolic acidosis.

DX

Diagnosis and the principal enzyme deficient?
Show model answer
  • Congenital adrenal hyperplasia (CAH) — most likely 21-hydroxylase deficiency (CYP21A2 mutations; over 90% of CAH).
  • Two main forms:
  • - SALT-WASTING (severe, classic): complete loss of aldosterone (no Na retention) → hyponatraemia, hyperkalaemia, dehydration, shock; complete loss of cortisol → hypoglycaemia, vascular instability.
  • - SIMPLE-VIRILISING (less severe).
  • Females: ambiguous genitalia from elevated adrenal androgens in utero.
  • Males: normal-appearing genitalia at birth → may present catastrophically with salt-wasting crisis in 2nd week of life if not picked up on newborn screen.
  • Newborn screen: elevated 17-hydroxyprogesterone.

TREATMENT

Acute management?
Show model answer
  • 1) IV STRESS-DOSE HYDROCORTISONE 50–100 mg/m² immediately (do not wait for confirmation if clinical picture fits) — replaces cortisol AND has some mineralocorticoid activity at high dose.
  • 2) IV 0.9% NaCl 20 mL/kg bolus, repeat as needed for shock; then maintenance with 5% dextrose + 0.9% NaCl.
  • 3) Treat hyperkalaemia: calcium gluconate, insulin/glucose, sodium bicarbonate, salbutamol; rarely Kayexalate (resin) — and the fluids/cortisol address underlying mechanism.
  • 4) Treat hypoglycaemia with dextrose bolus + ongoing infusion.
  • 5) Once stable: add FLUDROCORTISONE (mineralocorticoid replacement) once enteral feeding established — 0.05–0.2 mg/day.
  • 6) Long-term: lifelong hydrocortisone + fludrocortisone; sodium chloride supplementation in infancy.
  • 7) STRESS DOSING for illness, surgery, trauma: increase hydrocortisone 2–3× (or IM/IV if vomiting).
  • 8) GENITAL surgery for females: increasingly delayed and individualised (avoid neonatal surgery where possible; informed consent at later age).
  • 9) Multidisciplinary: paediatric endocrine, urology, psychology, genetic counselling.
  • 10) Family genetic counselling: AR with 25% recurrence; prenatal diagnosis offered.
  • 11) Medical alert bracelet, parental education on stress dosing and emergency injection.
Case 297 — Schoolchild with hereditary spastic paraplegia features MOD
A 9-year-old has 4 years of progressive spasticity of bilateral lower limbs: stiff toe-walking gait, contractures of ankles, hyperreflexia with bilateral Babinski signs, intact upper extremity strength, normal cognition. No sensory loss. Father has milder similar phenotype. Genetic: SPAST (SPG4) mutation.

DX

Diagnosis and the inheritance pattern?
Show model answer
  • Hereditary spastic paraplegia (HSP) — pure form, autosomal dominant.
  • SPG4 (SPAST gene) is the most common AD form (40% of pure AD HSP).
  • SPAST encodes spastin — involved in microtubule remodeling.
  • 'Pure' HSP: pyramidal tract degeneration causing spastic paraplegia ONLY.
  • 'Complicated' HSP: spastic paraplegia plus additional features (cognitive impairment, optic atrophy, peripheral neuropathy, retinopathy, intellectual disability).
  • Multiple genetic forms (> 80 genes): AD, AR, XL.
  • Other common: SPG3A (atlastin, AD; childhood-onset), SPG7 (paraplegin, AR; complicated), SPG11 (AR; thin corpus callosum, cognitive impairment), L1CAM (XLD, MASA syndrome).
  • Pure HSP — gradually progressive; usually retains ambulation into adulthood; lifespan typically normal.

TREATMENT

Management approach?
Show model answer
  • 1) No disease-modifying therapy.
  • 2) SPASTICITY MANAGEMENT:
  • - Oral baclofen (titrate up).
  • - Tizanidine.
