  • - Benzodiazepines (diazepam, clonazepam).
  • - Botulinum toxin for focal spasticity (especially calves for toe-walking).
  • - Intrathecal baclofen pump for severe disease.
  • 3) PHYSIOTHERAPY: stretching, range of motion, gait training, mobility aids (canes, walker, eventually wheelchair).
  • 4) ORTHOPAEDIC: heel cord lengthening, orthoses, contracture management.
  • 5) ADAPTIVE EQUIPMENT and home modifications.
  • 6) Surveillance for urinary symptoms (urgency, incontinence) — common in HSP; urology.
  • 7) Multidisciplinary: neurology, physical therapy, occupational therapy, orthopaedics, urology.
  • 8) Genetic counselling: AD with variable expression and penetrance; cascade testing.
  • 9) Pre-conception counselling for affected adults — 50% recurrence per pregnancy.
  • 10) Activity encouragement; school accommodations.
Case 298 — Newborn with seizures and elevated branched-chain amino acids HARD
A term newborn presents day 4 with poor feeding, vomiting, lethargy. Maple syrup odour in urine and earwax. Plasma amino acids: leucine 2400 µmol/L (very elevated; normal < 200), isoleucine 800, valine 600. Urine ketones +++. MRI: bilateral diffuse white-matter T2 hyperintensity ('MSUD oedema' pattern).

DX

Diagnosis and the metabolic block?
Show model answer
  • Maple syrup urine disease (MSUD) — autosomal recessive.
  • Defect in branched-chain α-ketoacid dehydrogenase (BCKAD) complex — encoded by multiple genes (BCKDHA, BCKDHB, DBT, DLD).
  • Cannot decarboxylate branched-chain α-keto acids of leucine, isoleucine, valine.
  • Accumulation of leucine and branched-chain α-keto acids → neurotoxic.
  • LEUCINE is the most neurotoxic; correlates with severity of encephalopathy.
  • Diagnostic: characteristic maple-syrup/burnt-sugar odour (from sotolon, a metabolite of isoleucine derivative); elevated plasma BCAAs (especially leucine), urine ketones, presence of alloisoleucine (pathognomonic).
  • Newborn screening detects elevated leucine.

TREATMENT

Emergency and chronic management?
Show model answer
  • ACUTE:
  • - STOP all protein intake.
  • - HIGH-glucose IV (D10) + insulin if needed to suppress catabolism.
  • - INTRALIPID for additional calories.
  • - ISOLEUCINE and VALINE supplementation (paradoxical — these are not the toxic ones, and their accumulation actually competes with leucine transport into brain).
  • - HEMODIALYSIS if severe (leucine > 1500, encephalopathy, refractory) — most rapid clearance of branched-chain amino acids and α-keto acids.
  • - Aim to reduce leucine to < 1000 quickly.
  • CHRONIC:
  • - Special MSUD formula (branched-chain amino acid–restricted).
  • - Strict protein restriction with careful titration of natural protein (leucine tolerance varies by individual).
  • - Frequent monitoring of BCAAs.
  • - Sick-day rules: low-protein high-calorie diet, emergency action plan with early hospitalisation.
  • - Thiamine trial (10–20 mg/kg/day) — < 5% are thiamine-responsive.
  • - Liver transplant for selected severe / poorly controlled cases — curative for metabolic disease.
  • - Multidisciplinary metabolic clinic, dietitian.
  • - Genetic counselling (AR with 25% recurrence; carrier screening offered in some populations).
Case 299 — Adolescent with new-onset psychosis and visual hallucinations HARD
A 17-year-old male presents with 3 weeks of progressive paranoid delusions, command auditory hallucinations, visual hallucinations of distorted faces, disorganised thought, insomnia. No fever. No substance use. CSF: 12 WBC (lymphocytes), normal protein, oligoclonal bands NEGATIVE; serum anti-NMDAR antibody POSITIVE on cell-based assay; CSF anti-NMDAR PENDING.

DX

Most likely diagnosis and the limits of serum vs CSF antibody testing?
Show model answer
  • Anti-NMDA receptor encephalitis presenting predominantly as a psychiatric syndrome.
  • Initial psychiatric prodrome (psychosis, behavioural change, agitation, insomnia) is the most common presentation in adolescents/young adults — many initially admitted to psychiatry before encephalitis recognised.
  • CSF antibody testing has higher SPECIFICITY than serum:
  • - Serum-only positive: 14% false-positive rate (other autoimmune, infectious, healthy).
  • - CSF positive: gold standard.
  • - For diagnosis, ALWAYS test BOTH serum and CSF; trust CSF.
  • If serum positive only with low CSF pleocytosis but characteristic clinical picture, treat empirically and rule out other causes.
  • Typically progresses from psychiatric prodrome → seizures → dyskinesias → autonomic instability → catatonia → coma (1–4 weeks).

TREATMENT

Management strategy?
Show model answer
  • 1) Confirm with CSF testing; rule out other infectious encephalitis (HSV PCR), other autoimmune (paraneoplastic panel).
  • 2) TUMOUR SCREENING in parallel — pelvic US/MRI for ovarian teratoma in females (most common association); testicular US in males; chest/abdomen imaging.
  • 3) FIRST-LINE immunotherapy:
  • - High-dose IV methylprednisolone 30 mg/kg/day × 5 days.
  • - IVIG 2 g/kg.
  • - Plasma exchange for severe cases.
  • 4) SECOND-LINE for refractory/severe:
  • - Rituximab 375 mg/m² weekly × 4 doses.
  • - Cyclophosphamide 500–750 mg/m²/month.
  • 5) Tumour removal if found — accelerates and improves recovery.
  • 6) SYMPTOMATIC: AEDs for seizures (levetiracetam, valproate), antipsychotics for agitation (CAUTION — these patients are sensitive to neuroleptic side effects, may worsen movement disorder), benzodiazepines for catatonia.
  • 7) Supportive ICU care: ventilation if respiratory compromise, autonomic support, nutrition.
  • 8) Recovery is slow (months-years); residual cognitive/behavioural issues common.
  • 9) Long-term immunosuppression to prevent relapse (~ 12–20% relapse rate).
  • 10) Multidisciplinary: neurology, psychiatry, rehab, neuropsychology, family support.
Case 300 — Adolescent post-vaccination with bilateral motor weakness and autonomic features HARD
A 16-year-old developed bilateral leg weakness 14 days after a routine seasonal influenza vaccination. Over 7 days: ascending weakness with areflexia, mild facial weakness, autonomic features (labile BP, intermittent ileus), CSF showed albuminocytologic dissociation (protein 1.2 g/L, WBC 4). NCS: demyelinating pattern with conduction blocks.

DX

Diagnosis and the recognised association?
Show model answer
  • Guillain-Barré syndrome (GBS) — typical AIDP variant — temporally associated with influenza vaccination.
  • Risk attributable to influenza vaccination: ~ 1–2 additional cases per million doses; risk of GBS from influenza ILLNESS itself is HIGHER (so vaccination is net protective).
  • 1976 swine flu vaccine had a clear association (~ 1 per 100,000) — modern vaccines much safer.
  • Other recognised vaccine-associated GBS triggers (rare): rabies (older nervous tissue vaccines), some pandemic vaccine formulations.
  • Typical clinical features: progressive ascending weakness, areflexia, autonomic dysfunction, peak by 4 weeks, plateau, recovery over months-years.
  • NCS: demyelinating (AIDP) most common; axonal (AMAN, AMSAN) variants more common after Campylobacter.

TREATMENT

Management priorities?
Show model answer
  • 1) ASSESS severity and progression:
  • - FVC, NIF, ability to count to 20 on single breath, swallowing, cough.
  • - Indications for ICU: FVC < 20 mL/kg, NIF less negative than –30, autonomic instability, bulbar dysfunction, rapid progression.
  • 2) IMMUNOTHERAPY: IVIG 0.4 g/kg/day × 5 days OR plasma exchange (5 sessions) — equivalent efficacy.
  • 3) DO NOT use steroids alone (no benefit in GBS; may even worsen).
  • 4) Combining IVIG + PLEX has no additional benefit.
  • 5) AUTONOMIC management: cardiac monitoring (arrhythmias, BP swings — treat conservatively to avoid worsening), avoid drugs that worsen autonomic instability.
  • 6) MECHANICAL ventilation if respiratory failure (typically prolonged).
  • 7) PAIN management (neuropathic — gabapentin, pregabalin; some require opioids acutely).
  • 8) VTE prophylaxis (LMWH).
  • 9) Nutrition (NG/PEG if dysphagia).
  • 10) Pressure-area care, contracture prevention.
  • 11) Early REHABILITATION as recovery starts.
  • 12) Long-term: most recover substantially over 6–12 months; ~ 20% have residual disability.
  • 13) Report to VAERS or equivalent vaccine adverse event database.
  • 14) Counselling about future vaccinations: subsequent doses generally not contraindicated (decision individualised by severity and aetiology); benefits of preventing influenza usually outweigh small risk.
